        FOREWORD: THE QUANTUM FICTION
      The story of the development of physics in the 20th century has been
told by several authors from different points of view, addressing different
audiences. Although the treatments differ in detail, they converge in
according to quantum mechanics the pride of place in the hierarchy of
conceptual revolutions. Ushering in a paradigm shift in the Kuhnian sense,
in the early decades of the past century, quantum mechanics has been
reaping one success after another in explaining known phenomena and
predicting new ones. Till then, physics had been revolving around themes
like causality, certainty and continuity (the three Cs.). Towards the end of
the 19th century, a host of puzzling problems had cropped up in the area of
experimental physics, which resisted solutions employing the classical
physics of Newton, Maxwell and others. Phenomena, such as black body
radiation, atomic spectra, photoelectric effect and specific heats of solids,
among others, required the introduction of the concept of quantization, a
totally strange and ad hoc technique into physics. Einstein's theory of
relativity also appeared on the scene, but it didn't solve many problems
raging at that time. The word ‘quantum' means a ‘unit or definite portion;'
it was applied for the first time by Max Planck (1900) in his quantum
theory of black body radiation. The key assumption he employed was the
discrete nature of radiation energy. It was revolutionary in the sense of
violating for the first time the sacred myth of continuous variations of
energy. Later on, the discreteness hypothesis was extended to angular
momentum by Sommerfeld. As a result of the application of the
quantization hypothesis, a set of ad hoc rules emerged which came to be
called ‘the old quantum theory.'

     In the 1920s, there arose two fundamental theories that justified the
results of the old-fashioned quantum theory, but went beyond it, not only
in respect of the application strategy but also in predicting new properties
and phenomena. These were known as matrix mechanics and wave
mechanics which produced identical results in spite of their different
mathematical and physical foundations. That they are examples of
different mathematical foundations was soon established, and there began
an era of hectic applications of the unified approach which was named
‘quantum mechanics.' The mathematical apparatus of quantum mechanics
made ample use of the theory of linear operators in Hilbert space. Physical
or measurable quantities are represented by the so-called hermitian
operators that possess real eigen values corresponding to experimentally
measurable values. The set of eigen values of an operator is called its
‘spectrum.' In atomic physics, for example, it turns out that the spectrum
of energy eigen values of an atom is discrete, giving rise to the observed
frequencies of spectral lines. A spectral line corresponds to a transition
between two energy states. The discovery of spin preceded the formulation
of a quantum mechanical theory of the electron in relativistic terms by
Paul Dirac. He subsequently developed an abstract method of quantization
of discrete as well as continuous systems. The electromagnetic field, a
continuous system, was quantized by Dirac following his general method.
The difference between quantum mechanics and quantum field theory is
that the former describes as single (discrete) particle while the latter
describes a many particle system. Thus in the quantum field theory of
electromagnetic field, there are particles of light called photons in the
(quantized) field carrying it.

     The most outstanding, fundamental development in quantum
mechanics was the discovery of the uncertainty principle by Heisenberg in
1927. In fact, this is the key principle that distinguishes the strange world
of quantum physics from the familiar one of classical physics. It states
that it is impossible to determine precisely and simultaneously the values
of certain pairs of physical quantities like position and momentum. Both
these quantities are simultaneously required to define a path for a particle,
and therefore, it is impossible to predict the future behaviour of an atom or
electron. The uncertainty principle explains why probability enters
quantum physics in a fundamental way. Since determinism is lost, the
classical role of causality is also suspect, as far as quantum mechanism is
concerned. It also implies the breakdown of the notion of a continuous
path for quantum systems. The energy of a bound system like an atom is
discrete for small values of what are called quantum numbers, but
continuous for very large values of the numbers. It may be noted that if a
system is in a bound state, its total energy will be negative (e.g. hydrogen
atom with an electron bound to it), but in an unbound state (e.g. the
electron released from the hydrogen atom) the total energy will be positive
and continuous, giving rise to the continuous part of its spectrum.

     Among the innumerable applications of quantum mechanics, the most
spectacular and brilliant ones are in particle physics, resulting in the
development of the standard model of elementary particles. Quantum field
theory is the platform on which it has been launched. The basic premises
of quantum field theory are simple. A quantum or quantized field is a
continuous system obeying quantization rules which imply the existence
of particles of definite physical properties such as mass, spin, charge,
helicity and so on. In nature four fundamental forces are believed to exist,
and these are described by quantum fields. All except the gravitational
field have been successfully quantized. These are the fields carrying the
forces like weak, electromagnetic and strong forces. Relativity permits the
creation and destruction of particles. A significant break through in the
understanding of the fundamental forces is the development of the unified
theory of weak and electromagnetic interactions, later supplemented by
the emergence of quantum theory of strong interaction. The resulting
theory is called SU(2) x U(1) x SU(3) colour gauge theory of quarks,
leptons and gauge fields. These are the force fields carrying massless
photons, massive bosons and fermions.

     Quantum field theory is eminently successful in accounting for
transitions involving any of those interactions just mentioned. So far no
one has formulated a quantum field theory of gravitation. A currently
emerging view is that gravity is not a fundamental force, but an ‘emergent
force,' derived from the three other forces. The model of particles and
their interactions built on the basis of the concept of quantum gauge fields
interacting with particles is the so-called Standard Model. However, very
recently, some experimental results seem to point towards the possible
existence of a fifth force. This is very much at a speculative stage.
      Joseph Mathew has done a brilliant job in producing a popular
synthesis of different strands of quantum physics. This includes not only a
survey of historical developments of its framework, but also controversial
aspects, such as quantum cosmology, measurement theory and role of the
observer. Some aspects of this grand theory may sound just like surreal
fiction. I have immense pleasure in introducing before intelligent and
curious people this beautiful work. The author is already well known for
his contributions to philosophical theology and particularly for his book
on cosmology. I wish him the very best.


                                      Prof. Dr. K. Babu Joseph
                              PROLOGUE
       It is a measure of the revolution brought about by the advent
       of the quantum theory that every one of these claims [of
       Newtonian mechanics] has been challenged. What is in volved
       here is no small alteration of the details of our general con
       ception of reality but a revolution in the very foundations of
       that conception, touching every aspect of our understanding of
       the world, ourselves, and the relations between the two.

               -Clifford A. Hooker, “The Nature of Quantum
                            Mechanical Reality”

     The author's book In Search of Cosmic Origins: The Great Saga of
the Universe narrates the fascinating wonders of the megacosm, of the
origin and evolution of the universe, of galaxies, stars and solar system.
The present work The Strange World of Quantum Physics contemplates the
breathtaking world of the microcosm, of subatomic particles, such as
photons, electrons, protons, neutrons and quarks, and of different fields
like, electromagnetic field. This quantum world is poles apart from the
Newtonian world of our common sense experience. Certainly, Einstein's
theory of relativity also is different from Newton's physics. However, the
theory of relativity retains some of the fundamental concepts of Newton's
theory, such as continuum, causality and determinism. But quantum
physics overturns all the basic concepts of Newtonian mechanics. It is the
science of the ‘discrete' world - of the ‘quantum,' of waveparticle duality,
uncertainty principle, wave function, non-locality, quantum vacuum and
the like. Moreover, unlike the other two theories, quantum physics is a
foundational theory in so far as it takes us to the ultimate depths of
physical reality. Truly, it is ‘meta-physics' in the literal sense of the word.1
“Once you get down to the quantum randomness level, no further
explanation is possible. You can't go deeper because physics stops here.”2
Indeed, as our voyage into microcosm progresses from quantum physics
proper to quantum field theories, thence to the quantum creation and
destruction of the universe, one gets the uncanny feeling that one is diving
deeper and deeper to reach the rock bottom of reality.

      Such eerie reactions are compounded by the fact that the quantum
world cannot be pictured or visualized in its weirdness and strangeness,
though it is not unintelligible; but its understanding demands the use of
very abstract and complex mathematics. In formulating their theories,
quantum physicists have invented mathematical systems very remote from
our ordinary modes of thinking. The strange quantum world is made
intelligible employing highly abstract mathematical concepts. Werner
Heisenberg's matrix mechanics and Ervin Schrödinger's wave mechanics
are such imposing mathematical frameworks. Although quantum physics
is couched in highly symbolic language of abstract mathematics, its
mathematical objects do refer to some physical entities. “Physics should
be more than a set of formulas that predict what we will observe in an
experiment; it should give a picture of what reality is.”3 Hence, in the last
analysis, quantum physicists have to provide conceptual clarifications of
the mathematical formalisms they employ in their theories. They have to
tell us and themselves what the mathematical concepts mean or refer to.
Quantum physicist Heinz Pagels speaks about physicists pondering over
“the equations and their meaning for the real world, developing an
interpretation which departed radically from naïve realism.”4 In fact, apart
from inventing their theories, great physicists have engaged themselves in
such conceptual elucidations. The debates, often bitter and harsh, between
Niels Bohr and Albert Einstein make up famous chapters in the history of
quantum theory. As we shall see in the second chapter, these disputes
concern the basic conceptual problems of quantum physics, such as its
in/completeness, in/determinism and non/locality. Although these
discussions often employ mathematical formalisms, in the final analysis
they have to do with the conceptual frameworks of the theories in
question.

     In fact, philosophical description and synthesis are excellent tools for
the exposition of the conceptual structures of scientific theories - in the
present context, of quantum physics. Many scientists themselves have
undertaken such projects, and even in the title of their works, they use
words, like ‘philosophy,'       ‘philosophical,'    ‘metaphysics,'   and
‘metaphysical.' We mention a few works of the authors who have
employed philosophical methods in their presentations of scientific
theories. Edwin Burtt (The Metaphysical Foundations of Modern Physical
Science), David Bohm (Causality and Chance in Modern Physics;
Wholeness and Implicate Order), Milic Capek, (Bergson and Modern
Physics; The Concepts of Space and Time; The New Aspects of Time; The
Philosophical Impact of Contemporary Physics), Paul Davies (The
Accidental Universe; The Forces of Nature; Other Worlds; Quantum
Mechanics; Space and Time in the Modern Universe), John C.
Polkinghorne (One World; The Particle Play; The Quantum World; Reason
and Reality).

     Conceptual clarifications are all the more important in the case of
quantum physics because the microworld it deals with is so bizarre and
remote from our ordinary experience. Hence, we have to clarify what it is
all about. The present work The Strange World of Quantum Physics is such
a text on the conceptual framework of quantum physics - its important
concepts and their interrelations. In this project, we have employed a
philosophical method and a literary style which we call ‘narrative
synthesis.' This is a method we have developed over the years in writing
our books and articles. Our long years of experience in teaching
philosophy also have contributed to a great extent in employing the
method of narrative synthesis. This method is essentially a narrative; it is
a narration, a presentation, or a discourse about a particular subject. But it
is more than that; it is a narrative which aims at a synthesis. The method is
in fact a narrative which is a synthesis, and a synthesis which is a
narrative. In a word, it is the method of story telling, not about some
imaginary world, but in the present case, about the quantum world.5 The
reviewers of the author's previous books have in fact acknowledged this
style of narrative synthesis. In the review of In Search of Cosmic Origins:
The Great Saga of the Universe, Poulose Mangai writes:

       The billions of years old history of the universe and its future
       possibilities are presented to us in the form of a most
       interesting and exciting saga. It is a saga of creation and
       evolution with plots and subplots, with numerous events in a
       logical and intelligible sequence without leaps or gaps, and
       with many and varied characters. All this is unified and it is
       one single saga. And it is for the real, not imagined.6

Similarly, in the review of author ' s From Cosmos to Theos: Towards a
New Philosophical Theology, Augustine Pamplany refers to the method of
narrative synthesis employed in the book: “It requires an extra ingenuity
to carry a profound scientific field like cosmology to such a narrative
style. Certainly, the long decades of teaching experience of the author have
significantly contributed to the literary style of the book.”7

     The reader will witness the method of narrative synthesis unfolding,
not only in the present book as a whole, but in each chapter, section and
subsection. From prologue to epilogue, The Strange World of Quantum
Physics develops a narrative with many plots and numerous subplots, all
connected in logical sequences without leaps or gaps. Since we are telling
a story, we have left out some sub-plots, like critiques of different
theories, so as not to interrupt the development of the main plot except in
some cases where it is needed for the progress of the story itself. The
logical flow of ideas is ensured from the beginning of the work to its end.
Readers will find the book profusely dotted with citations and references -
- many of them from great physicists, like Albert Einstein, Max Planck,
Niels Bohr, Werner Heisenberg, Louis de Broglie, Nobel Laureates like
Eugene Wigner, Steven Weinberg and Ilya Prigogine, eminent physicists
like John von Neumann, David Bohm, John Wheeler, Stephen Hawking
and John Polkinghorne, and reputed mathematicians, like Hermann Weyl
and Roger Penrose. This is done intentionally in order to substantiate the
statements in the text by the authority of these great men of science. We
have also inserted a number of figures and tables in order to illustrate the
concepts being discussed. Unavoidably, some theories had to be repeated
in different contexts. However, we have taken care that the repeated
passages are adapted to the different themes explored. The present volume
can be best used as introductory and complementary reading for quantum
physics, and as reference book, especially by college and university
students. Hopefully they will find the abstract mathematical formalisms
and constructs they learn in their courses on quantum physics taking on
flesh as concepts.

      The book is divided into six chapters. The first chapter, “Towards
Quantum Physics,” is introductory. It starts with a comparison between the
major themes of Newtonian mechanics and quantum theory. This is
followed by a historical outline of quantum physics. The second chapter,
“An Overview of Quantum Physics,” is an exposition of the important
concepts of quantum theory, such as wave-particle duality, Heisenberg's
uncertainty principle, wave function and non-locality. The third chapter
entitled, “The Quantum Field Theories,” is an exploration of the
unification of quantum theory and the theory of relativity. Quantum field
theories, such as quantum electrodynamics, electroweak theory and
quantum chromodynamics are introduced. The proposals for quantum
theories of gravity and quantum vacuum are also discussed. The fourth
chapter, “The Standard Model of Particle Physics,” presents the current
picture of quantum physics. The fifth chapter, “The Quantum World and
the Observer,” deals with what is known as the ‘measurement problem.'
The Copenhagen interpretation of quantum physics affirms the role of
observer in the process of measurement/observation. The last chapter,
“The Quantum Cosmology,” has as its theme the quantum creation and
destruction of the universe. The “Epilogue” concludes The Strange World
of Quantum Physics highlighting the weird, bizarre and exotic dimensions
of quantum mechanics.

     A word is in place about the author's credentials to write the present
book which demands a good grasp of the theories of physics, especially
Newtonian mechanics, theory of relativity and quantum theory itself.
Although I have had almost no formal training in theoretical physics, I
have at my disposal a very valuable tool to probe the conceptual depths of
quantum physics - that is, philosophical analysis and synthesis. I had my
formal training in philosophy at the Institute of Philosophy, University of
Louvain, Belgium. During my studies at the Institute, an American
professor Kenneth Gallagher introduced one of the best books written on
the philosophical dimensions of modern physics, The Philosophical
Impact of Contemporary Physics by Milic Capek.8 It is an excellent
comparative study of Newtonian physics and Einstein's theory of
relativity. It is this book that introduced me to the world of physics,
especially Newtonian mechanics and the theory of relativity. Then my
readings and researches took me to quantum physics. Besides, I spent a
year as post-doctoral student at the University of Louvain. I had also
opportunities to do research at the University of Oxford and also at the
Catholic University of America, Washington, D. C. During my sojourn at
these Universities, I have read hundreds of books and articles on physics.
My previous books In Search of Cosmic Origins (2014) and From Cosmos
to Theos (2017), both based on physics, have inspired me to write the
present book. I have published also a number of articles on scientific
subjects in different journals. The experience of teaching philosophy of
science for decades hopefully has contributed in great measure to express
the concepts of quantum mechanics with clarity. The synthesis in the
present volume is the outcome of all my reading and writing on Newtonian
mechanics, theory of relativity and quantum physics.

     Last, but not least, as we pass from theory to theory, deeper into the
mysteries of the quantum world, we are overwhelmed by the grandeur of
the great gift granted to great scientists - the capacity to comprehend the
ultimate depths of physical reality. Paul Davies' observation is pertinent.
He says: “Yet I feel compelled to believe that, through science [here,
quantum mechanics], we really are being allowed to glimpse the rational
foundations of physical existence.”9 Eminent quantum physicist Eugene
Wigner acknowledges: “The miracle of the appropriateness of the
language of mathematics for the formulation of the laws of [quantum]
physics is a wonderful gift which we neither understand nor deserve.”10
Spontaneously, our thought rises to the ‘Wonderful Giver' who graciously
grants gifts to us humans who do not deserve them. Sentiments of
gratitude well up in our mind to this Wonderful Giver of gifts for granting
us a ‘glimpse of the rational foundations of physical existence.' A hymn of
thanksgiving springs forth from the depth of our being. “I thank you for
the wonder of my being, for the wonders of all your creation.” (Psalm:
139,14).

                                                Joseph Mathew
                                    1
              TOWARDS QUANTUM PHYSICS
       On that day, [when the quantum was introduced into science]
       the vast and grandiose edifice of classical physics found itself
       shaken to its very foundations, without anyone very clearly
       realizing it at first. In the history of the intellectual world
       there have been few upheavals comparable to this.

               -Louis De Broglie, The Revolution in Physics

     The present chapter is obviously introductory. It presents the thematic
and historical settings of quantum physics. This great theory did not
appear all on a sudden on the contemporary scientific horizon. In a real
sense, it has its antecedents in Newtonian mechanics. To be sure, no
adequate comprehension of quantum theory is possible without at least
some understanding of Newton's physics. Hence, in the present chapter we
introduce the major themes of both theories in comparison. Hopefully, this
would impart to the reader an overall ‘feel' of quantum physics in contrast
to Newtonian theory. Here we follow the method of the eminent quantum
physicist Louis de Broglie. He compares and contrasts the concepts of
quantum physics with those of Newton's mechanics throughout his great
work The Revolution in Physics. As we deal with the important notions of
quantum mechanics in the following chapters, we invariably refer also to
the corresponding concepts in Newton's physics. In most cases, we can
comprehend the ideas of quantum theory adequately only by comparing
them with related conceptions in Newtonian mechanics. The present
chapter traces also the historical development of quantum physics.

1.1. From Newtonian Mechanics to Quantum Physics

     Newtonian mechanics and quantum physics present a very interesting
study in comparison and contrast just because the scientific revolution
brought about by quantum theory is very spectacular, and it affects the
very foundations of Newton's physics. Clifford Hooker says:

       It is a measure of the revolution brought about by the advent
       of the quantum theory that every one of these claims [of
       Newtonian mechanics] has been challenged. What is involved
       here is no small alteration of the details of our general
       conception of reality but a revolution in the very foundations
       of that conception, touching every aspect of our understanding
       of the world, ourselves, and the relations between the two.1

     Renowned quantum physicist David Bohm suggests that the way
quantum revolution challenged Newton's mechanics goes much deeper
than the manner in which Einstein's theory of relativity confronted
Newton's theory: “The quantum theory, however, actually overturned
[Newtonian] mechanism in a much more thoroughgoing way than did the
theory of relativity.”2 Peter Coveney and Roger Highfield likewise
comment: “In attempting to describe the world at this microscopic level,
we find that Newton's mechanics breaks down. Newton's failure here is
even more spectacular than his failure to cope with the fast and highly
massive objects dealt with by relativity.”3 In order to comprehend the great
scientific paradigm shift from Newtonian mechanics to quantum physics,
we compare and contrast three major themes from each, namely, the
composition of physical objects, their cause-effect relations and the role of
the observer in observing these objects and their relations.

1.1.1. Newtonian Mechanics

     Newtonian mechanics is actually the physics of the ordinary world in
which we live. De Broglie says: “Classical mechanics and physics were
built up in order to give an account of phenomena which operate on an
ordinary, human scale and they are also valid for larger scales, the
astronomical ones.”4 Its conceptual framework embodies many aspects of
the experience of the world around us. Oxford scientist Arthur Peacocke
tells us that the Newtonian perspective “corresponds so well with
intuitions derived from our senses -- the stuff of the world, matter, [which]
possesses energy, and is located in space at a particular time.”5 These
concepts represent, not only the dimensions of the physical world; they
form even part of our conceptual apparatus for perceiving and
understanding the world. “The Newtonian categories of space, time, matter
and causality are so deeply ingrained in our whole perception of reality
that they colour every aspect of the way that we think about life, and we
can't easily imagine a world which mocks their reality.”6 For this reason,
these basic concepts of Newton's mechanics are even believed to be a
priori - given before our experience of the world rather than derived from
experience. Peacocke observes: “The concepts of space, time, matter and
energy continued to appear to be ‘given', self-evident features of the
world, a priori concepts essential to our thinking.”7 In fact, whenever we
engage in any physical activity, we are aware of these dimensions of
physical objects. For instance, each time we drive a car from one place to
another we are, to some extent, conscious of the space between the two
places, and the time it takes to travel between them. The simple act of
opening and closing a door makes us aware of the physical reality of both
the door and our hand, and of cause-effect relation between them.

     Newton's world view can be summarized in the following statement
which refers to the basic components of physical reality, namely, space,
time, matter and motion: “In this receptacle of absolute space and time
there moves material elements or particles, the small, solid, and ultimately
indestructible objects out of which all matter is made.”8 According to
Newtonian mechanics, the physical objects we experience are constituted
out of solid particles of matter or their aggregates which exist or move in
space and time in accordance with fixed physical laws of motion. The
Newtonian model of matter is atomistic; atoms are the basic building
blocks of physical bodies. Everything in the universe, including living
beings, is made up of these atoms and their composites. The most
important property of atoms is occupying space. Hence, we cannot speak
of material atoms without also referring to its spatio-temporal
dimensions.9 In the words of the great philosopher of science Hans
Reichenbach: “Speaking of particles means attributing to them a definite
place and a definite velocity for each time point.”10 Hooker explains in
detail:
       Thus, in the Newtonian ideal, the world consisted of objects
       having precise spatial boundaries and spatiotemporal
       locations, and which were further characterized by a finite
       number of attributes (mass, velocity, acceleration, orientation,
       shape, size, and position). The addition of the electromagnetic
       theory meant that these same Newtonian objects now
       possessed another property — charge.11

     From this point of view, a material particle or a physical object is at a
particular place and at a particular time; and if it is moving, it has a
definite speed. “Classically, a particle — that is, a material object whose
dimensions are small compared to the other relevant spatial distances — is
characterized by a sharp spatial definition and a definite trajectory through
space in time.”12 Further, not only the notion of matter, but also the
concept of motion entails space and time. Edwin Burt suggests that “the
mathematical study of the how of motion, inevitably thrusts into
prominence the concept of space and time.”13

      Although Newtonian mechanics refers to space and time when
speaking of physical objects and motions, this does not mean that they are
intrinsically related to them. Rather, the three-dimensional space and one-
dimensional time function merely as the background where these objects
exist and interact. De Broglie writes: “In prerelativistic physics, space
forms a fixed framework in which the physical phenomena observed by all
imaginable observers are going to take place, a single universal and
absolute time impressing its rhythm on all these observers.”14 Space is
thought to be an empty container of bodies, independent of physical
objects. Like the material stuff, Newton's absolute space is the space of
our everyday experience. Obviously, there is one and only one such space.
It is the space shared by all humanity, the Sun, all planets and stars. We
move through this space in our own ways and at our own speeds; and
regardless of our state of motion, we experience space in the same way.
Space gives us the sense of length, breadth and height. According to the
Newtonian view, length, breadth and height are intrinsic properties of
physical objects; they remain unchanged whether the objects or the
observer move or not.
     Moreover, all changes and motions in the physical world are
described in terms of a separate dimension, namely, time. It has three
dimensions, namely, past, present and future. Newton's concept of time
relies heavily on the notion of simultaneity; all events throughout the
universe are believed to be simultaneous. Simultaneity means that to every
event on Earth at a given moment correspond events in distant regions of
the universe. The propagation of the speed of light (186,000 or 300,000 per
sec) is thought to be instantaneous. Hence, the actual ‘now' and the ‘seen-
now' are regarded as simultaneous, and they are considered synonymous
terms. The philosopher of science Milic Capek says: “In truth, the cosmic
‘Now' is nothing but an enormous extrapolation of our limited
macroscopic experience of ‘Seen now.'”15 Further, according to Newtonian
physics, time passes at the same rate whatever be the speed or position of
the observer.16 Both Newtonian space and time constitute the setting of all
events in the universe, unchanged by the state (motion or rest) of physical
objects or of the observer.

      Now, physical objects interact, and so they are related by causeeffect
links. Newtonian concept of causality affirms deterministic cause-effect
relations in the interactions between physical objects. According to this
view, all physical phenomena in the universe can be reduced to the motion
of material particles or their aggregates, caused by their mutual attraction,
that is, by the force of gravity. The effect of this force is described
mathematically by Newton's equations of motion which are ultimately
based on his three laws of motion.17 These are considered fixed laws
according to which all physical objects move, and they are believed to
account for all changes in the universe. Given the initial conditions of a
moving body and the laws of motion, all its future states can be accurately
determined. Hence, all that happened have definite causes; and whatever
happens will give rise to definite effects. The future of any part of the
system can in principle be predicted with absolute certainty if its state at
any time is known in all details. According to this law of causality, not
only is it possible to predict the future of a physical system in detail, but
even its past can be reconstructed completely. This is the deterministic
interpretation of causality in which prediction becomes the hallmark of
scientific enterprise. Thus, according to Newtonian mechanics, cause-
effect relations are rigorously deterministic.

     Such systems of causes and effects in the universe interact
independently of being observed by observers. From the perspective of
Newtonian mechanics, physical objects exist in their own right with the
properties of mass, length, position and velocity, independently of the
observer. Even though the importance of observation is stressed by
classical science, the role of observer in scientific investigation is not
taken into account. The observer is set apart from the physical system
which is being examined. The function of the observing scientist is to
investigate the world as objectively as possible. Nobel Laureate Ilya
Prigogine and Isabelle Stengers testify that “in the classical view the only
‘objective' description is the complete description of the system as it is,
independent of the choice of how it is observed.”18 Hence, according to
Newtonian mechanics, physical objects and systems exist independently of
observers who examine them without in any way influencing them.
Physical objects would be what they are whether they are being observed
or not.19

1.1.2. Quantum Physics

     The theory of relativity and quantum physics have indeed
accomplished radical paradigm shifts in physics. These two great theories
have challenged the foundations of the classical world of Newtonian
mechanics. In no other science we witness such far-reaching scientific
revolutions taking place. In fact, a double paradigm shift has occurred:
from Newtonian physics to the theory of relativity, and from Newton's
theory to quantum mechanics. These were profound scientific
transformations which have questioned the foundations of Newtonian
physics. Capek remarks about the impact of these scientific upheavals that
have taken place in physics since the year 1900:

       [N]ot only did its [of Newtonian physics] roof collapse, but
       even the very foundations had to be rebuilt. All fundamental
       classical concepts had to be either given up or profoundly
       revised: space, time, matter, motion, causality. It is very
       difficult to deal separately with each of these concepts since a
       change of one is related to the changes in the others.20

      Physicist Brian Greene shares a similar view on the breathtaking
transformations that have taken place with the coming of the theory of
relativity and quantum physics:

       One measure of the depth of a physical theory is the extent to
       which it poses serious challenges to the aspects of our
       worldview that had previously seemed immutable. By this
       measure, quantum mechanics and the theories of relativity are
       deep beyond anyone's wildest expectations: Wave functions,
       probabilities, quantum tunneling, the ceaseless roiling energy
       fluctuations of the vacuum, the smearing together of space
       and time, the relative nature of simultaneity, the warping of
       the space-time fabric, black holes, the big bang. Who could
       have guessed that the intuitive, mechanical, clockwork
       Newtonian perspective would turn out to be so thoroughly
       parochial - that there was a whole new mind-boggling world
       lying just beneath the surface of things as they are ordinarily
       experienced?21

     De Broglie remarks about the paradigm shift occasioned by the
coming of quantum mechanics: “On that day, [when the quantum was
introduced into science] the vast and grandiose edifice of classical physics
found itself shaken to its very foundations, without anyone very clearly
realizing it at first. In the history of the intellectual world there have been
few upheavals comparable to this.”22 Similarly, Harold Schillling suggests:
“The fact is that almost none of the fundamental concepts that have been
so potent in macromechanics - such as size, or position and distance, or
time and time interval, mass, velocity, momentum - can be used with
respect to the microworld without radical modification of their meanings
and of the rules for their use.”23
     Quantum mechanics is the physics of subatomic world, also called
‘quantum world' or ‘microcosm.' It is the science of the world of
subatomic particles, such as photons, protons, electrons, neutrons, hadrons
and quarks, and of the four forces, namely, gravitational, electromagnetic,
strong nuclear and weak nuclear forces. Although this quantum world is
rationally comprehensible, it cannot be visualized like the Newtonian
world of everyday experience. That is not because the subatomic particles
are very small, but because the visual conventions we adopt from the
world of ordinary objects and from Newtonian mechanics do not apply to
quantum entities. For example, we can visualize a stone which is both at
rest and at a precise place. But it is meaningless to speak of a quantum
particle such as an electron resting at a point in space. In order to speak
about such weird quantum objects, physicists employ imposing
mathematical systems, like Heisenberg's matrix mechanics and
Schrödinger's wave mechanics which are very remote from our ordinary
conceptions and modes of thinking.

     Hopefully we get a general idea of quantum physics by discussing
briefly its important concepts in comparison with the corresponding
notions in Newtonian mechanics; these are quantum objects, cause-effect
link among them, and relation of the observer to the observed quantum
system. Peacocke refers to these important concepts of quantum
mechanics when he says:

       Furthermore, under the impact of the need to adopt wave
       particle complementarity for small particles and the
       acceptance of the Heisenberg Uncertainty Principle, the ideal
       of classical physics, of the objective observer who rationally
       describes the properties of the world as they exist
       independently of his observations, had become untenable.24

     According to quantum physics, physical objects cannot be analyzed
into solid, indestructible, material particles existing or moving in space
and time as held by Newton. Rather, subatomic particles, such as photons
and electrons, have the characteristics of both particles and waves. In
some contexts, they behave as particles and in others as waves. For
example, photons which are particles of light behave as particles under
appropriate conditions, and as waves in other situations. Further, we
cannot think of elementary particles as existing like physical objects of
our ordinary experience. Quantum physicists tell us that a quantum
particle such as an electron exists or evolves as a ‘superposition' of all its
possibilities. We find here the greatest contrast between Newtonian solid
particles and quantum wave-particles which are an ensemble of
possibilities rather than a collection of actually existing properties.

     Moreover, Newtonian concepts of space and time are not adequate to
describe quantum objects. They have been radically modified in quantum
mechanics. De Broglie argues:

       Without doubt general relativity has already taught us to
       consider the local properties of space-time as being dependent
       on the distribution of matter in the universe. But the
       modification that the presence of quanta requires is even
       much more profound and no longer permits us to represent the
       motion of a physical unit by a line in space-time (world-line);
       . . .25

In a sense, a quantum object operates ‘outside time' manifesting itself as a
discontinuous succession of instantaneous transitions. Thus, the quantum
jump of an electron from one orbit to another does not occur as a
continuous passage through intermediate positions; it does not have a
trajectory. In a way, it occurs ‘outside (ordinary) space,' by local
occupations without journeys between them. In the quantum world, we
must, as Niels Bohr says, ‘transcend' the usual frameworks of space and
time.26 Physicist Lee Smolin aptly remarks: “This state space, where the
quantum states live, is very different from the three-dimensional space in
which we seem to live.”27 Hence, quantum events cannot be said to take
place in the space of our ordinary experience. This oddity of quantum
objects and events is wrought about by Heisenberg's uncertainty principle.
De Broglie tells us: “[T]he existence of these uncertainties [of Heisenberg]
warns us that the space and time of classical physics, well-defined and
perfectly usable on the macroscopic level, cease to be perfectly adequate
for the description of physical reality on the level of atoms and
electrons.”28 Smolin explains:

       Why doesn't quantum theory allow us a simple and intuitive
       picture of phenomena, as things happening in ordinary space?
       The reason is that it seems intrinsically impossible for one
       observer to have all of the information that would be
       necessary to give a complete description of any quantum
       system in nature. It seems that, whatever part of the world we
       are studying, we are allowed to know at most half of the
       information that would be necessary to fully describe it. This
       limitation is called Heisenberg Uncertainty Principle, and it is
       this, above everything else, that makes the real quantum world
       different from our Newtonian imaginings.29

     Furthermore, Heisenberg's uncertainty principle that effects the
modification of space and time, wreaks havoc with the Newtonian concept
of causality. In quantum physics, causality appears to have collapsed. Paul
Davies asserts: “The central feature of quantum physics is the
disintegration of the cause-effect link.”30 In another context he says:

       Quantum physics wrecked the orderly, yet sterile Laplacian
       scheme. Physicists learned that at the atomic level matter and
       motion are vague and unpredictable. Particles can behave
       erratically, rebelling against rigidly prescribed motions,
       turning up in unexpected places without discernible reason
       and even appearing or disappearing without warning.31

     Cause-effect relation in the quantum world is governed by
Heisenberg's uncertainty principle. This principle affirms fundamental
uncertainty in the behaviour of quantum objects. Uncertainty relation
expresses a trade-off in the degree of certainty about the values of
conjugate quantities, such as position and its complementary quantity,
velocity. Thus, the position and velocity of an electron cannot be
simultaneously determined with certainty. By the same token, quantum
physics in effect abandons the classical, deterministic concept of causality.
Ruth Reyna clarifies:

       That the values of conjugated parameters [like position and
       velocity] when measured simultaneously are indeterminate is
       now an accepted law of particle physics, and the deterministic
       conception of causality is given up as a result of this
       uncertainty in our data. In so far as knowledge is concerned,
       causality in the intuitive sense becomes meaningless. But the
       classical theory of deterministic causality (if it has ever held)
       is replaced by a statistical conception of event.32

     However, causality is not altogether absent in quantum physics, but it
has taken a different modality; that is, individual quantum events do not
always have well-defined causes. If an atom, for example, is excited by a
collision with another atom, it will usually return quickly to its lowest
energy state by emitting a photon. Such emitting of photon is naturally an
outcome of the atom's excited state. We can certainly say that the
excitation caused the production of photon. In that sense, there is a cause-
effect relation. Nevertheless, the actual moment of the production of
photon is unpredictable; it can happen at any moment. Physicists can
predict only the probable appearance of photon, but they cannot know for
certain when this event will actually take place.33

     Now, in such causal interactions of quantum objects, the observer
appears to be crucially involved. In fact, quantum mechanics has radically
modified the classical, Newtonian assumptions about the relation between
the observer and the observed physical system. According to this view, the
physicist cannot merely be a spectator of the quantum world s/he is
observing. Physicist Richard Schlegel contrasts the roles of observer in
classical physics and in quantum mechanics:

       The classical physicist who could sit, as it were, on one side of
       a translucent screen with his thoughts and experiences,
       viewing the world he studied on the other, is now the
       impossible spectator. For much of physics the dividing screen
       is lost, and cannot be replaced. Especially on the level of
       individual atomic processes the scientist now finds that he in
       fact has a role in the creation of the world that he is
       describing. It is not . . . that his emotions bias his results, but
       rather that his act of observation participates in forming the
       natural world. . . . even in so traditionally a model of an
       objective science as physics the completely uninvolved
       spectator has been shown to be an impossibility.34

     The observer is no longer a spectator of the world ‘out there,' but is
involved in the measurement/observation of subatomic particles. Physicist
Heinz Pagels opines: “What is actually going on in the quantum world
depends on how we decide to observe it. The world just isn't ‘there'
independent of our observing it; what is ‘there' depends in part on what we
choose to see — reality is partially created by the observer.”35 Pagels
comments on the dependence of an electron on the observer: “The electron
seems to spring into existence as a real object only when we observe it!”36
As we shall see later, eminent physicists, like Niels Bohr, Heisenberg,
Eugene Wigner, John Von Neumann and John Wheeler, have strongly
affirmed the fundamental role of the observer in the quantum world.

     The observer's involvement in the subatomic world takes on two
modalities: the measurement of quantum objects and the production of
their properties.37 Many physicists affirm that the observer is intimately
linked to the process of measurement of quantum objects. These entities
do not exist like the objects of our ordinary experience. An unobserved
electron, for instance, exists only as an indeterminate set of all its
possibilities. None of these possibilities has any actuality unless it is
observed. This means that possibilities become actual when a
measurement is taken or an observation is made. Moreover, some
physicists further suggest that even the properties of quantum objects do
not exist unless they are observed. In classical physics, properties of
physical objects, such as mass and other physical dimensions, are
considered their inherent attributes. But in quantum mechanics, properties
of quantum objects are not intrinsic to the objects, but they are the
outcome of interactions between the observed objects and the observer.
Hence, the observer is necessary, not only to measure/observe quantum
objects, but even to define their properties; and so we cannot speak about
the properties of a quantum object as such. They are meaningful only in
the context of the object's interaction with the observer.38 Pagels contrasts
the classical view of the objectivity of the world with that of quantum
physics:

       The Copenhagen interpretation of the new quantum theory
       ended the classical idea of objectivity — the idea that the
       world has a definite state of existence independent of our
       observing it. This contrasts with our ordinary experience of
       the world, which supports the classical view of objectivity that
       the world goes on even if we do not perceive it. Wake up in
       the morning and the world exists much as you left it. But the
       Copenhagen interpretation maintains that if we look closely at
       the world -- at the level of atoms — then its actual state of
       existence depends in part on how we observe it and what we
       choose to see. . . .The atomic world simply does not exist in a
       definite state until we actually set up an apparatus and observe
       it.39

     We wind up our discussion with a quote from Peacocke about the
extent of the modification of classical concepts brought about by quantum
physics:

       After such radical transformation of the basic classical
       concepts of space, time, matter, and motion, very little is left
       of the traditional corpuscular-kinetic scheme of nature. It is
       natural that classical determinism of the Laplacian type is
       threatened when the scheme on which it was based is
       disintegrating. Not a single constituent concept of this
       Laplacian model of the universe remains unaffected; what has
       been left intact by the relativity theory was challenged by the
       theory of quanta or wave mechanics.40

To trace the history of this great revolution in physics, we now turn.
1.2. A Historical Overview of Quantum Physics

     The contemporary history of quantum physics can be broadly divided
into three periods: the age of discoveries, the period of mathematical
formulation and later developments. The first stage includes the discovery
of wave-like behaviour of light by Thomas Young, its discrete nature by
Max Planck and its particle-like character by Einstein. During the period
of mathematical formulation, quantum mechanics was developed as a
theory of physics by great physicists, like Louis de Broglie, Werner
Heisenberg, Erwin Schrödinger, Niels Bohr, Max Born and Pascual Jordan.
Later developments comprise quantum field theories including the
proposed quantum theory of gravity.

1.2.1. The Age of Discoveries in Quantum Physics

     Quantum mechanics has its origin in the scientific investigations on
the nature of light: whether a light beam consists of a stream of particles
like pellets fired from a gun, or it is a continuous, unbroken wave akin to
waves on the surface of an ocean.41 Isaac Newton held the particle
hypothesis: that light is composed of particles which were called
‘corpuscles.' His particle view prevailed until the year 1800. The first
staunch proponent of the wave theory of light was Newton's contemporary,
the Dutch scientist Christiaan Huygens. In 1801, Thomas Young's famous
double-slit experiment conclusively proved that light has a wave-like
character. He showed that light produces interference patterns which are
clear indications of the wave nature of light. James Clerk Maxwell in 1865
proposed the theory of electromagnetism which predicted also the wave
character of light.

     The giant step towards the invention of quantum theory was taken by
Max Planck in the year 1900. He is one of the founders of quantum
mechanics. Planck discovered that energy comes in ‘packets' or ‘bundles.'
He arrived at this concept of discreteness of energy by examining the
phenomenon of black body radiation.42 Planck's investigations confirmed
that light is emitted in discrete units or packets of energy. He called each
packet a ‘quantum.' Further, Planck discovered that the energy of a light
particle is proportional to its frequency. Thus, high frequency light such as
violet light has short wavelength and high energy; and low frequency light
like red light has long wavelength and low energy.43 Although Planck's
proposals broke with the Newtonian past, it was not radical enough. Most
physicists, including Planck, thought that light was a wave-like
phenomenon in accordance with the view of nature as continuum.44
Actually, his work on black body radiation showed that light behaves as if
it consists of particles because light is emitted or absorbed as discrete
packets of energy. However, Planck hesitated to admit that these bundles
of energy could be identified with particles.

      It was up to Einstein to complete the theory of light. He went beyond
Planck to make the radical assumption that light itself consists of
particles. Einstein was led to this insight by his investigations of the
phenomenon of photoelectric effect. When a beam of light falls on a
metallic surface, the beam ejects electrons from the metal, precisely one at
a time as though individual particles of light (individual photons) were
hitting electrons and knocking them out of the metal's surface one by one.
Einstein suggested that in photoelectric effect, light behaves as if it
consists of individual particles, each of which carries a definite amount of
energy; and he formulated the law of photoelectric effect. But unlike
Planck's theory about the discreteness of energy, which was immediately
tested experimentally, there was simply no way to confirm Einstein's
photoelectric equation by experiment. Hence, Einstein's hypothesis of the
particle nature of light was not readily accepted by his contemporaries;
and there was great resistance from the part of scientific community at
large. Most physicists including Planck believed that light is a continuous
electromagnetic wave in accordance with the view of nature as continuum.
It was not until 1923 that there was sufficient experimental evidence for
photoelectric theory. Thus, Planck and Einstein ushered in a new era in
physics. Together, they accomplished a radical transformation of classical
physics. Planck's idea of the quantum of energy, further developed by
Einstein as particle, implied that nature cannot be thought of as
continuum. Matter and energy were shown to be discrete.
      The next step forward in the development of quantum mechanics was
taken by Niels Bohr in 1913. His contribution relates to the structure of
atoms. Using the concept of the quantum, he was able to describe the way
in which electrons are arranged within atoms, and to explain the pattern of
light emitted by atoms like hydrogen. Bohr speculated that the electron's
orbits are arranged at specific distances from the nucleus of atom; and that
when it absorbs energy (photons), electrons ‘jump' outward from the orbit
closest to nucleus (‘ground state' of atom) by absorbing energy and
eventually return to this orbit, in the process emitting energy (photons)
equal to the energy that it absorbed in jumping outward. Bohr suggested
that when a little energy is available (low heat), only a small amount
(packet) of energy is absorbed by electrons, and they do not jump out very
far from nucleus. When they return to their ground state, they emit a small
quantity (packet) of energy like that of red light. But when more energy is
available (high heat), larger amount of energy is absorbed by electrons;
they make bigger jumps outward and, on returning, they emit larger
quantities of energy like those of blue and violet light. Therefore, over low
heat, a metal piece glows red, and over high heat, it glows blue-white.

     By this time, most of the quantum physicists had accepted the
hypothesis that light particles or photons behave sometimes as waves and
sometimes as particles. But they did not realize that subatomic particles,
like electrons or protons, might also similarly behave as waves or
particles. This proposal was the next step in the development of quantum
physics. Louis de Broglie suggested that the wave-particle concept can be
applied, not only to light but to all matter particles as well. He reasoned by
analogy that if light which is a wave, could sometimes behave like a
stream of particles (photons), then an electron, clearly a particle, could
also sometimes behave like a wave. De Broglie's ideas were presented in
two papers in September 1923, in which he deduced the wavelength of
electron. He suggested that his idea could be experimentally confirmed if
electrons showed interference phenomena like waves. In the mid-1920s,
experiments conducted by Clinton Davisson and Lester Gerner confirmed
that electrons do exhibit interference patterns associated with waves.
Hence, electrons embody wave-like character together with their more
familiar picture as particles. Later experiments showed that other
particles, like protons and neutrons, also have similar wavelike nature.
Thus, we have experimental confirmation that all matter particles have
wave-like character.

1.2.2. The Period of Mathematical Formulation

     However, it was not until the late 1920s with the work of Werner
Heisenberg and Erwin Schrödinger that the concepts of light and matter as
both waves and particles were mathematically formulated. Heisenberg in
the year 1925 proposed a mathematical formalism called ‘matrix
mechanics.' In matrix mechanics, the physical properties of a particle,
such as its position and momentum, energy and time, were represented by
mathematical objects called ‘matrices' which are generalizations of the
idea of simple numbers. Simple numbers obey the Boolean commutative
law of multiplication; that is, the result of multiplication does not depend
on the order in which it is carried out. For instance, (3 x 6) = (6 x 3) = I8.
But matrix multiplication depends on the order in which it is carried out.
For example, if A and B are matrices, then A x B does not have to equal B
x A. The main idea of matrix mechanics is that physical variables like
position q and momentum p of a particle are not simple numbers but
matrices. Max Born and Pascual Jordan wrote a paper extending
Heisenberg's ideas, pointing out the importance of matrix algebra for
describing atomic energy transitions. Indeed, matrices rather than simple
numbers represent the correct language for describing the behaviour of
subatomic particles. Matrix mechanics was the first logically consistent
formulation of quantum physics; it completely describes all phenomena of
the quantum world. Matrix mechanics interprets the physical properties of
subatomic particles as matrices that evolve in time. It is equivalent to
Schrödinger's formulation of quantum theory called ‘wave mechanics.'

     Schrödinger's paper on wave mechanics appeared in 1926. He
expanded on de Broglie's idea that a certain type of wave could be
associated with subatomic particles. Since the equation which Schrödinger
developed is based on the physics of waves, his formulation is known as
‘wave mechanics.' In this mathematical formalism of quantum theory, the
behaviour of all quantum systems is described in terms of ‘wave function'
which evolves according to the law called ‘Schrödinger wave equation.' It
is applied to all sorts of quantum phenomena. Wave function is an abstract
mathematical ‘representation' of the state of a quantum system. Both
Heisenberg's matrix mechanics and Schrödinger's wave mechanics provide
correct descriptions of all quantum phenomena. In fact, Schrödinger
himself demonstrated that wave mechanics and matrix mechanics are
mathematically equivalent. A physicist working on a problem in quantum
physics can now choose any one of the two mathematical formalisms
which is applicable to his work. These mathematical frameworks of
quantum theory are essentially the same as those which are still in use
today. Thus, by the end of 1926 quantum mechanics had two apparently
different mathematical formalisms – Heisenberg's matrix mechanics and
Schrödinger's wave mechanics.

     With these theoretical developments, quantum theory finally
provided an answer to the question, whether a quantum object, such as a
photon or an electron, is a wave or a particle. The mathematics of quantum
theory permitted both wave-like and particle-like representations of
quantum objects. But obviously these two concepts are opposed and are in
conflict with each other. The problem is, how can we talk about an object
in terms of two such incompatible ideas? It was Niels Bohr's turn to
handle this problem. He suggested that the function of quantum physics is
not to represent what quantum objects are, but how we can think and talk
about them. Bohr emphasized that when we are putting a question to
nature, we must also specify the experimental arrangement that we would
use to determine the answer. In one experiment, an electron may exhibit
wave-like properties, and in another it may behave as a particle.

     In other words, it is the kind of experimental arrangement that
enables one to observe the quantum object as particle or as wave. Bohr
called wave and particle ‘complementary concepts' which are different
representations of the same object. Bohr's principle of complementarity
states that the same quantum object possesses complementary properties,
and that the knowledge of the one excludes the knowledge of the other. We
may therefore describe an object like an electron in ways which are
mutually exclusive — as wave or as particle — without logical
contradiction provided we also realize that the experimental arrangements
that determine these descriptions are similarly also mutually exclusive.
Bohr proposed the principle of complementarity in 1927. It was around
this time that Heisenberg invented uncertainty principle. It states that two
conjugate quantities of a quantum object, such as position and velocity,
cannot be simultaneously measured with equal precision. If its position is
measured with certainty, then its velocity cannot be measured
simultaneously with the same certainty, and vice versa. The same
uncertainty principle is applicable also to other conjugate quantities, such
as energy and time.

     Thus, Heisenberg and Bohr, each in his own style made new
breakthroughs in quantum physics. Bohr's principle of complementarity
and Heisenberg's uncertainty principle together constitute the foundations
of what is known as the ‘Copenhagen interpretation' of quantum
mechanics. This view has convinced most of the physicists about the
correctness of quantum physics. The Copenhagen interpretation
magnificently revealed the internal consistency of quantum theory -- a
consistency which was purchased at the price of renouncing causal
determinism and objective knowledge of the quantum world.45 Quantum
mechanics reached its full flowering in the late 1920s. Ilya Prigogine and
Isabelle Stengers remark about the theoretical developments of this great
theory during the period we are considering:

       Thus we reach the decisive years 1925-27, a ‘golden age' of
       [quantum] physics. During this short period, Heisenberg,
       Born, Jordan, Schrödinger, and Dirac made quantum physics
       into a consistent new theory. This theory incorporates
       Einstein's and de Broglie's wave-particle duality in the
       framework of a new generalized form of dynamics: quantum
       mechanics.46

    Further, Richard Feynman has made significant contributions to
quantum mechanics in the 1940s. His formulation of quantum theory is
known as ‘sum-over-histories'/‘sum-over-paths' or ‘path integral
formalism.' This method is a tool for computing the quantum mechanical
probabilities, for instance, of the ‘flight' of quantum particles from one
point to another. It is contrasted with the classical method of computation
which considers a single, unique trajectory of the motion of a particle, say,
from location A to site B. Based on the initial conditions of the particle,
and the forces acting on it, classical physics predicts with certainty its path
every moment, and finally the time of its arrival at the destination. (See
Figure 1) There is only just one unique, linear trajectory and only one
space-time framework for the motion of the particle.47




  Figure 1. The classical trajectory of a particle from A to B
     Feynman's path integral formalism replaces this classical notion of a
unique trajectory of a particle with an infinity of quantum mechanically
possible paths. This is a formulation of quantum physics in which particles
are believed to travel from one point to another along all possible paths
between them. Feynman's method considers all possibilities of a particle
travelling from, say, location A to site B. The quantum particle traverses
every possible trajectory between source and destination, simultaneously
moving through countless loops and making numerous diversions. (See
Figure 2) Each one of these paths will have a certain probability associated
with it. The crucial point here is that these different probabilities have a
wave-like character. Hence, if we sum over all the probabilities of all
paths, then the different probabilities will reinforce or cancel each other,
and so the only path which survives this interference process is the one
that the particle actually follows. Greene comments: “Feynman showed
that he could assign a number to each of these paths in such a way that
their combined average yields exactly the same result for the probability
calculated using the wave-function approach.”48 The resulting sum tells us
the probability of detecting the particle that started out at location A
reaching the location B at the specified time.49
Figure 2. Six possible paths of a quantum particle from A to
                              B
     With the mathematical formulation of quantum mechanics in place,
physicists saw new visions on the scientific horizon - the unification of
quantum physics with Einstein's theory of relativity. The ultimate goal of
particle physicists in this project is the Theory of Everything which is
often called the ‘Holy Grail of Physics.'50 We now pass on to a short
history of this great search.

1.2.3. Later Developments in Quantum Physics

      Once the mathematical formulation of quantum mechanics was more
or less complete, it became evident to physicists that for a comprehensive
theory of subatomic particles and forces of nature, quantum physics must
be incorporated with the theory of relativity. Such is the origin of
relativistic quantum field theories which attempt to unify special theory of
relativity and quantum mechanics. Important steps towards the unification
of these two theories were taken by Heisenberg, Born and Jordan in the
years 1926-28, and by Eugene Wigner and Wolfgang Pauli in 1929-30. The
new concept advanced for this purpose was that of ‘quantum field.' It was
proposed that there are different kinds of fields, like electromagnetic field
and gravitational field, and that each field has an associated particle.
Fields are the fundamental entities which manifest themselves as particles.
This is the basic postulate of quantum field theory. Its fundamental laws
were in place by the 1930s.

     Since then, quantum field theory has been enhanced, amplified and
applied to quantum particles and fields. It attempts to interpret the four
forces of nature (gravitational, electromagnetic, strong nuclear and weak
nuclear forces) as particle interactions mediated by ‘virtual particles;'
these function as ‘exchange particles' or ‘force-carriers.' The theory of
virtual particles was worked out in the 1940s by J. Schwinger, Richard
Feynman and S. Tomonaga. Each of the four fundamental forces of nature
has an associated exchange particle. The force-carrier associated with
electromagnetic interaction is called ‘virtual photon;' the electromagnetic
interaction between two charged particles is attributed to the exchange of
these virtual photons. This quantum field theory is called ‘quantum
electrodynamics' or QED for short. The electroweak theory is the
unification of electromagnetism and weak interaction. The quantum field
theory of strong nuclear interaction incorporating special theory of
relativity is called ‘quantum chromodynamics' or QCD. The success of the
unification of electromagnetic and weak interactions led to a number of
attempts to combine electroweak theory with quantum chromodynamics
into a Grand Unified Theory or GUT.

     Further developments in quantum field theory attempt to bring
gravity into the fold, and formulate a fully unified field theory. For this,
the first step is the invention of a quantum theory of gravity. That is,
quantum theory has to be unified with the general theory of relativity
which is first and foremost a theory of gravity. Quantum gravity governs
the Planck regime where space-time curvature supposedly becomes
enormous - at the Planck time of 10-43 sec, and the Planck length of 10-33
cm, with the density of mass/energy at 1093 gm/cc, and temperatures above
1032 degree Kelvin.51 The quantum theory of gravity supposedly would
provide the theoretical framework for a satisfactory description of space-
time domain at the Planck magnitudes. But in spite of the strenuous work
of some of the finest particle physicists in the world, an adequate quantum
theory of gravity has not yet emerged. Nevertheless, reputed physicists,
like Stephen Hawking, James Hartle and John Wheeler, have dared to
make some bold speculations regarding the space-time structure
prevailing at the Planck dimensions. At these scales, there is no longer a
time dimension, but only four space-like dimensions. Such a state of
physical reality is called ‘quantum vacuum' or ‘superspacetime.' The
essential property of quantum vacuum is its fluctuation. This consists in
particles and anti-particles appearing out of the vacuum and disappearing
in an extremely short time, permitted by Heisenberg's uncertainty
principle.

     Quantum cosmology is a sequel to quantum field theories. Physicists,
like Andrei Linde, Alex Vilenkin and Edward Tryon, have put forward
theories, known as ‘chaotic inflation' and ‘eternal inflation' which suggest
that our universe originated as a result of some primordial quantum
vacuum fluctuation. The theories of quantum creation of the universe are
complemented by the theory of quantum destruction. Sydney Colman and
Frank de Lucia have theorized about the destruction of the universe as a
result of the unpredictable behaviour of quantum vacuum.52

    We conclude the present chapter with an ‘outrageous' quote on
quantum mechanics by Richard Feynman and a comment by Greene.
Feynman wrote in 1965:

       There was a time when the newspapers said that only twelve
       men understood the theory of relativity. I do not believe that
       there ever was such a time. There might have been a time
       when only one man did because he was the only guy who
       caught on, before he wrote his paper. But after people read the
       paper a lot of people understood the theory of relativity in one
       way or other, certainly more than twelve. On the other hand I
       think I can safely say that nobody understands quantum
       mechanics.53

     Green observes that although Feynman expressed this view in 1965, it
applies equally well today. What Feynman meant was that although the
special and general theories of relativity require a drastic revision of
previous ways of understanding the universe, nevertheless when one fully
accepts the basic principles underlying them, the new and unfamiliar
implications for space and time follow directly by careful logical
reasoning. Quantum mechanics is different. Just as special and general
theories require dramatic changes in our world view when things are
moving very fast or when they are very massive, quantum mechanics
reveals that the universe has equally, if not more, startling properties when
examined on atomic and subatomic scales. To be sure, many of the
mathematical formalisms and rules of quantum mechanics were in place
by the year 1928; and ever since, they have been used to make stunningly
precise and successful predictions. Nevertheless, the scientists who
employ these formulae and rules do so without really understanding why
these laws work or what they really mean. Greene concludes: “Unlike
relativity, few if any people ever grasp quantum mechanics at a ‘soulful'
level.”54
                       2
        AN OVERVIEW OF QUANTUM PHYSICS
       The discovery of quantum theory produced the most profound
       modification of Newtonian physics that has occurred since the
       publication of the Principia [Mathematica].

                 –John Polkinghorne, The Quantum World

     The present chapter is in fact a sequel to the last chapter, “Towards
Quantum Physics.” The latter introduced quantum physics in contrast to
Newtonian mechanics; the historical survey presented the developments of
the important concepts of quantum theory in a chronological order. In the
present text, we deal with most of these notions thematically. It starts with
the idea of the quantum itself, that is, discreteness at the foundations of
physical reality. The twin pillars of quantum physics are wave-particle
duality and Heisenberg's uncertainty principle; and its foundation is the
wave function. The wave function is believed to collapse when quantum
objects/events are observed. In fact, these are all counter-intuitive ideas
that are contrary to classical/Newtonian physics and our common sense
perception and understanding of the world. Quantum physics thus presents
a concept of physical reality that is strange or ‘ab-normal' in the literal
sense of the word. In spite of this fact, we discuss finally some proposals
made by great physicists on the nature of quantum reality.

2.1. The Quantum World

     We start our discourse on quantum physics at the very beginning with
the quantum itself. The meaning of the quantum can be best clarified by
contrasting it with the continuum, the hallmark of classical mechanics,
both Newtonian and Einsteinian. The continuum (continuity) and the
quantum (discreteness) are the two overarching concepts which
differentiate between classical physics and quantum mechanics
respectively.
2.1.1. The Continuum and the Quantum

      The classical physics - the Newtonian mechanics and the theory of
relativity -- is characterized by the continuum. It is the foundational
concept of these two great systems of physics. The continuum is in fact a
simple, ordinary concept. “A continuum is something whose parts are so
close together, so ‘arbitrarily small,' that the continuum really cannot be
broken down into them. There are no breaks in a continuum. It is called a
continuum because it flows continuously.”1 Basically, continuum is a
mathematical concept; a line, a surface and a piece of metal are examples
of continuum. Thus, a line drawn on a paper is one-dimensional continuum.
The line flows continuously from one end to the other. Similarly, the
surface of a wall is twodimensional continuum. All points on its surface are
in contact with other points. A three-dimensional continuum is any object
in three dimensions, like a table or a chair. Space is a three-dimensional
continuum with three dimensions of length, breadth and depth. We
ordinarily think that physical objects of the world around us, and the
framework of space and time in which they exist and move have the
character of continuum.

     Before Max Planck proposed the idea of the quantum, most of the
physicists conceived of the world of nature as a continuum. “They thought
of the forms of matter blending into one another in a smooth, continuous
way. Various physical quantities like energy, momentum, and spin were
continuous and could take on any value.”2 Newtonian mechanics is the
science of the world of our ordinary experience, and its fundamental
concepts represent continuous quantities. David Bohm says: “Newton's
basic concepts of absolute space and time, and matter and motion imply
continuity or homogeneity which is the hall mark of Newtonian
mechanism.”3 Thus, according to Newtonian physics, objects are said to
exist in a continuum of space and time. Their actions and movements are
also regarded as continuous quantities. Consider, for instance, a person
shooting with a gun at a target 20 meters away. If s/he is a sharp shooter,
the target is directly hit. The whole operation takes place within a
continuum. The gun and the bullet -- the initial conditions -- are whole and
entire together, and function as one continuous unit.4 Further, the action of
shooting is executed within the co-ordinates of space and time which
constitute a continuous framework. The point here is not merely that the
bullet flies continuously to the target. Of course, it does. But more than
that, it is the space-and-time continuum that makes possible the execution
of such an act. The causal action of the bullet hitting the target is
accomplished within the continuum of space and time. Physicist Paul
Davies writes: “Central to this belief [causeeffect relation] is the
assumption that space and time are continuous, so that events which occur
in one place and time can influence those that occur at other places and
times.”5 That is to say, the cause-effect link is made possible by the space-
and-time continuum.

      Moreover, the continuum is the fundamental concept, not only in
Newtonian mechanics, but also in Einstein's theory of relativity. As a
classical physicist, Einstein firmly held on to the postulate of continuum in
physics. He says: “It is further characteristic of these physical objects that
they are thought of as arranged in a spacetime continuum.”6 Bohm suggests
that “relativity theory requires continuity, strict causality (or determinism)
and locality. On the other hand, quantum theory requires non-continuity,
non-causality and non-locality. So the basic concepts of relativity and
quantum theory directly contradict each other.”7 Thus, in the special theory,
space-time structure functions as the continuous background of uniform
motions of physical objects. Space-time continuum constitutes the
background of all motions in the world; and it stays on as the universal
setting even if all mass/energy in the universe is removed. But in the
general theory, space-time-mass-energy is itself the ultimate continuum. It
is the fundamental matrix out of which all physical objects and events in
the universe emerge.

     The concept of continuum has thus validity in the classical physics of
Newtonian mechanics and in the theory of relativity. It functions as the
fundamental postulate of the physics of macrocosm of our every day
experience where Newton's theory is valid, and also of the megacosm of
galaxies and stars, where the theory of relativity is applicable. But the
concept of continuum has some fundamental limitations: it cannot be
applied to the Big Bang regime and to the subatomic world of quantum
mechanics. The continuum thus does not apply to the whole spectrum of
physical reality. Hawking speaks about the inapplicability of the continuum
in the Big Bang domain: “All our theories of science are formulated on the
assumption that space-time is smooth [continuous] and nearly flat, so that
they would all break down at the big bang singularity, where the curvature
of space-time is infinite.”8 Thus, ‘the wall of Planck time at 10-43 second'9
severely constrains the applicability of continuum. In fact, the Big Bang
singularity is the ultimate consequence of applying uncritically the general
theory of relativity (which presupposes the continuum) to the curvature of
space-time ‘beyond' the Planck time, all ‘the way down' to the origin of the
universe. Moreover, the concept of continuum has no validity in the
