quantum world of subatomic particles. As we saw, this was precisely
Planck's discovery: that energy is not emitted continuously, but in discrete
quantities. Indeed, the continuum is an invalid concept in the subatomic
world.

     In stark contrast, the quantum discreteness is the fundamental
postulate of the microcosm -- the world of subatomic particles, like
photons, protons, electrons, neutrons and their anti-particles, and of the
four forces, namely, gravitational, electromagnetic, strong nuclear and
weak nuclear forces. The most fundamental character of this quantum
world is discreteness or discontinuity. “Quantum theory is a theory of
discontinuity -- its very nature makes it so.”10 The concepts of continuum
and quantum (discreteness) are in complete contrast, and are incompatible.
Philosopher J. R. Lucas suggests that the doctrine of the continuum denies
“on the one hand the discreteness and on the other hand the gappiness of
space and time.”11 The concept of discreteness can be clarified with a
simple example. Suppose we are talking about the increase or decrease in
the population of a city. The smallest amount which the number of people
can increase or decrease is by one person. It cannot increase by .7 person. It
can increase or decrease by fifteen people, but not by 15.27 people. The
population can get larger or smaller only in jumps, and the smallest jump
that it can make is one whole person; hence, it is evident that population
can fluctuate only by a whole number of people. In the language of physics,
a population can change only in discrete amounts or discontinuously. In
quantum mechanics, we talk of the discreteness of energy. The smallest
possible amount or unit of discrete energy is called the ‘quantum.'12 It was
Planck, we saw, who introduced this concept of discreteness in physics; he
showed that energy exists as discrete packets called ‘quanta.' That is,
energy is ‘quantized.'

     Indeed, discreteness is the overarching concept in quantum mechanics.
Discreteness defines the quantum world. Quantum objects are nothing if
they are not discrete. Everything in the subatomic world is discrete quanta.
All actions, all motions, all processes in the microcosm come in discrete,
indivisible units. Bohm contrasts the discreteness of action with the
classical continuity of movement:

       Action of every kind is of this discrete indivisible nature
       (whether of particles or of fields). The apparent continuity that
       is commonly observed arises because the individual quanta are
       very small. An ordinarily visible movement therefore has a
       very large number of discrete jumps, each too small to be
       perceptible (except possibly with the most sensitive of
       instruments). So all apparently continuous large-scale (i.e.,
       classical) motions are to be understood as constituted of
       discrete steps. Such a notion certainly contradicts the older
       classical concept of continuity of movement, which is at the
       very basis of the mechanistic ideas of Newtonian physics.13

As Bohm rightly points out, the discreteness of the quantum world appears
to be at odds with the continuity of Newtonian mechanics, and of the world
we observe around us. The discrete character of the physical objects of our
common sense world is not perceptible through our senses because their
discreteness is exceedingly small. For example, if we look at a huge pile of
wheat from a distance, it appears to be a continuous, smooth hillock. But
upon closer inspection, we recognize that it is made up of tiny grains which
are the discrete units in the pile of wheat. Photographs in newspapers are
also examples of the discreteness of continuous objects. Viewed from afar,
a newspaper photo appears as a continuous picture. But if we look closely
at it, it is seen as consisting of many tiny dots which are its discrete
elements.
     Truly, discreteness is at the foundation of the quantum world.
Physicists have pointed out this fundamental character of discreteness in
various facets of the subatomic world. De Broglie speaks of the ‘quantum
of action.'14 Similarly, the space-time of quantum world would have to be
discrete in contrast to the continuous space and time framework of classical
physics. R. Blanché contends: ‘‘We must expect to see even space and time
returned to the granular [discrete] structure of elementary quanta.''15 He
continues: “If we so choose, however, there is nothing to prevent us from
bringing space and time down to this elementary level; but then space and
time themselves will acquire a discrete character.”16 Physicist and
mathematician James Jeans attributes the break down of causality in the
quantum world to its discrete nature: “As discontinuity [discreteness]
marched into the world of phenomena through one door, causality walked
out through another.”17 As we shall see later, the greatest difficulty in
constructing the final theories of physics, like the quantum theory of
gravity and the Theory of Everything, is in fact ‘quantization' at the Planck
magnitudes. This involves the quantization of gravity -- that is, rendering
gravity discrete. This in turn has to do with quantizing space-time
curvature since according to the general theory of relativity, gravity is
nothing but space-time warpage in the presence of mass/energy. Similarly,
space-time would have to be ‘quantized' also in the Theory of Everything.
Cambridge Astrophysicist John Gribbin states: “That [Theory of
Everything] implies that spacetime itself must be, on an appropriate very
short-range level, quantised into discrete lumps, not smoothly
continuous.”18 In order to refer to the extremely discrete character of space-
time at the Planck scales, Princeton physicist John Wheeler uses the phrase
‘space-time foam.'19

     Niels Bohr proposed that possible energy levels of atoms are
‘quantized,' that is, they are fixed and discrete. He assumed that an electron
has certain sets of possible, discrete orbits; and that it jumps from one orbit
to another without continuously crossing intervening space. An electron
cannot gain or lose energy in a continuous way, spiralling to higher or
lower orbits; it takes in, or throws off, packets (quanta) of energy and leaps
from one orbit to another like a rabbit disappearing and reappearing
discontinuously. For instance, in hydrogen atom which has one electron in
orbit, if a quantum packet of light energy - a photon -- is absorbed, the
electron jumps to a higher orbit. Whether it jumps to the next orbit or six
orbits farther depends on how large quantum of energy it takes in. If the
electron is in an outer orbit and emits (gives off) a quantum of energy, it
leaps discontinuously (like a magical rabbit) down to a lower orbit. In
either direction, the electron can only occupy fixed, discrete orbits, nothing
in between.

      As we wind up our discussions on the two fundamental postulates of
modern physics -- the continuum and the quantum -- we refer to some
interesting comparisons and contrasts between these concepts and other
notions related to them. Thus, the continuum implies some important
themes of classical physics, like causal determinism and locality. This
means that what is continuous is characterized by determinism and locality,
and vice versa. We can make similar statements about determinism and
locality too. What is deterministic has the character of the continuum, and
also has the property of locality.20 Similarly, the concept of discreteness
also entails some important notions of quantum physics, such as
indeterminism and non-locality. Each of these notions is correlated with
each other. What is discrete is thus indeterminate and non-local. We can
make similar statements also about indeterminism and non-locality. What
is indeterminate is discrete and non-local. Interestingly, each of these
characteristics of classical physics contrasts with its correlated, opposite
property of quantum mechanics. Hence, we have the opposing pairs of
continuity versus discontinuity, determinism versus indeterminism, and
locality versus non-locality. Bohm contrasts the concept of continuity and
its related notions in the theory of relativity with the postulate of
discreteness and its cognate ideas in quantum physics: “For relativity [and
also Newton's physics] requires strict continuity, strict determinism, and
strict locality, in the formulation of its laws, while quantum theory requires
discontinuity, indeterminism and non-locality in such formulations.”21

     We conclude our narrative on the continuum and the quantum with a
perceptive quote from Hooker on the impact of quantum theory on the
logical structure of classical physics:
       The logical structure of classical descriptions permitted,
       indeed demanded for completeness, the simultaneous
       application of all of the classical categories of description,
       thus: mass, charge, position, time, velocity, acceleration,
       momentum, angular momentum, energy, and so on. . . .

       In its fundamental aspect, then, quantum theory presents itself
       as demanding a weakening of the logical structure of physical
       descriptions. It imposes restrictions on the joint applicability
       of classical descriptive categories. The maximally informative,
       consistent descriptions of quantum theory pertaining to a
       single situation are logically weaker than those of classical
       theory, since they contain fewer components. The tightly
       woven logical structure of classical descriptions -- tightly
       woven because all physical descriptive categories are
       demanded simultaneously for a complete description -- is rent
       apart, as it were, as a (conceptual) consequence of the
       discovery of the (physical) quantum of action, to form a more
       weakly connected descriptive framework in which only some
       of the possible physical descriptive categories are applicable at
       any one time.22

Indeed, ‘the tightly woven logical structure of the descriptions of classical
physics,' demanding ‘for completeness the simultaneous application of all
of the classical categories of description,' is the outcome of the
foundational concept of the continuum. By contrast, the weakening of this
logical structure of physical descriptions in quantum mechanics, imposing
‘restrictions on the joint applicability of classical descriptive categories,' is
the ‘consequence of the discovery of the (physical) quantum of action,' --
of the overarching concept of discreteness.

2.1.2. The Quantum Itself

     As we have seen,23 it was Max Planck, one of the founders of quantum
mechanics, who proposed the concept of the quantum – of discreteness of
energy - in the year 1900. Before Planck's time, physicists thought of
nature as a continuum.24 They believed that various physical quantities, like
energy, mass and momentum, and also space and time, were continuous and
smooth. However, James Maxwell's assumption in his theory of
electromagnetism that energy comes only in continuous quantities led to
serious conceptual problems that rocked the foundations of classical
physics in the last decade of the nineteenth century. The basic idea of
Planck's quantum hypothesis is that this continuous view of the world must
be replaced by a discrete one. Planck's work thus broke with the concept of
the continuum in nature. De Broglie comments on the great significance of
Planck's momentous discovery:

       It is probable, that at the time when Planck wrote his
       fundamental papers on the theory of black body radiation,
       contemporary physicists did not at once comprehend the
       importance of the revolution which had just been
       accomplished. The hypothesis of Planck without doubt must
       have seemed to them an ingenious means of improving the
       theory of an interesting but, all in all, a particular phenomenon,
       and not a brilliant idea destined to upset all the classical
       conceptions of physics. But, little by little, the fundamental
       importance of the idea of Planck became apparent. The
       theorists perceived that the discontinuity introduced by the
       hypothesis of quanta is incompatible with the general ideas
       which served up to then as the bases of physics and demanded
       a complete revision of these ideas. We can not admire too
       much the intuition of genius which permitted Planck, by the
       study of a particular physical fact, to perceive at a glance one
       of the most fundamental and mysterious laws of nature. More
       than forty years [now more than a century] have passed since
       this marvelous discovery and we are still far from having
       achieved a comprehension of all its import, and exhausting all
       its consequences. In the history of the progress of the human
       mind, the conquest of the constant of Planck must remain a
       memorable date.25
     In effect, Planck discovered that the basic structure of physical reality
is discrete, granular or discontinuous. He replaced the concept of
continuous amount energy with the idea of ‘packet' or ‘bundle' of energy
which he called ‘quantum;' its definition involves a universal constant, now
called ‘Planck's constant.' The idea that such a constant existed, or that
quanta were real, was so ‘unorthodox' that when it was first proposed, not
even Planck himself could make this leap of faith. However, confirming
evidence gradually accumulated, and by the year 1913, the ‘quantum
heresy' was generally accepted: indeed, quanta - discrete units of energy --
were deemed to be real.

     Planck arrived at the concept of the discreteness of energy as he was
attempting to solve a simple problem: he wanted to know how objects glow
brighter as they get hotter, and change colour when their temperature is
increased or decreased. Classical physics had no proper explanation for this
commonplace occurrence. In order to arrive at a solution to this problem,
Planck decided to examine the phenomenon of ‘black body radiation.' It
was Gustav Kirchoff who in 1859 suggested black body as a medium for
studying the behaviour of light and electromagnetic phenomena. We see an
object when light that has fallen on it, is reflected by its surface and
reaches our eyes. However, not all light falling on an object is reflected;
much of it is absorbed by the object itself. For instance, a red rose flower
appears red because it reflects only red light. White light falling on red rose
contains all colours of the spectrum; red colour is reflected, and the
remaining colours are absorbed. Moreover, the nature of light reflected
from an object, such as red light reflected from rose flower, depends also
on the properties of reflecting surface, like its roughness, smoothness,
temperature and so on.

     Kirchoff suggested that the way out of these problems was to define an
ideal body which absorbs all light falling upon it and reflects none of it
back. He called such an object a ‘black body.' “Black body radiation is the
radiation that comes from a nonreflecting, perfectly absorbing, flat
(nonglossy) black body.”26 By investigating black bodies, Kirchoff
assumed, physicists would be able to examine the radiation which is
naturally emitted by them, and which depends only on their temperature;
they can examine black bodies without bothering about problems arising
from the properties of reflecting surface. Since black is the absence of
colour (no light is reflected or emitted), black bodies have no colour unless
we heat them. If a black body is glowing a certain colour, we know that it is
because of the energy that we have added to it, and not because it reflects
or emits that colour spontaneously. As a body is heated, the radiation it
emits becomes increasingly of shorter wavelength until the body becomes
visible and successively glows red, yellow, blue and finally white.27

     Before the year 1900, physicists assumed that electrons of excited
atoms from a metallic surface radiate energy continuously and smoothly
until their energy is exhausted and dissipated. But Planck discovered that
they emit energy only in specific quantities. Instead of radiating energy
continuously and smoothly as a clock spring runs down, atoms give off
their energy in spurts, dropping to lower energy levels after each spurt until
they stop emitting energy altogether. Planck's investigations of black body
radiation led to the revolutionary theory that light and electromagnetic
radiation are emitted in discrete, separate units or ‘packets' of energy, not
continuously and smoothly, like a continuous, unbroken wave, as classical
physics claimed. He postulated that energy is given off in discrete
quantities -- in whole number(s). A radiating body gives off one, two, three
or any number of units of energy. But this number is never fractional, such
as one and a half, or one and a quarter; that is to say, when it comes to
energy, no fractions are allowed. Planck called each discrete unit or packet
of energy a ‘quantum.' He specified the amount of discreteness of energy --
the size of a single quantum by the symbol ‘h’ (pronounced ‘h-bar') which
was later called ‘Planck's constant.' It is the fundamental parameter in
quantum mechanics, and it determines the size of discrete units of energy.
Planck's constant is an extremely small unit, and if it were set to zero, then
the nature of the world would appear continuous. 28

     Furthermore, Planck discovered a proportion between the energy of a
light particle (photon) and its frequency. We know that the higher the
frequency of a light wave is, the shorter its wavelength; and the lower its
frequency, the longer its wavelength. Hence, high-frequency light like
violet has short wavelength and low-frequency light like red has long
wavelength. Planck found that there is a relation between light energy and
frequency: the higher the frequency is (shorter wavelength), the higher the
energy, and the lower the frequency is (longer wavelength), the lower the
energy. Hence, the energy of a light particle is directly proportional to its
frequency, and inversely proportional to its wavelength. Consequently,
high-frequency light, like violet light has short wavelength and high
energy; and low-frequency light, like red light, has long wavelength and
low energy. This means that long wavelength radiation is intrinsically less
energetic than short wavelength radiation.29 De Broglie formulates Planck's
postulate: “Matter can emit radiant energy only in finite quantities
proportional to the frequency.”30 This simple relation between energy and
frequency is central to quantum mechanics.

     Planck's quantum hypothesis explains this relation between energy and
frequency. He found that high and low frequencies of light comprise
varying sizes of energy ‘packets.' But each packet of each colour
(frequency) has the same amount of energy. Accordingly, each packet of red
light has the same amount of energy, each packet of green light has the
same quantity of energy, and each packet of violet light has the same
amount of energy. However, energy packets of different colours have
different amounts of energy. Thus, the energy packets of violet light are
‘larger' than those of green light, and the energy packets of green light are
‘larger' than those of red light. In other words, the size of energy packets of
low-frequency light (longer wavelength) like red is ‘smaller' than those of
high-frequency light (shorter wavelength) like violet.31 This explains why
hot objects radiate energy as they do. When a black body is slowly heated,
the first colour it glows is red because the energy packets of red light are
the smallest in the visible spectrum of light. As the heat is increased, more
energy is available and bigger energy packets produce higher-frequency
colours, such as blue and violet. With ever increasing size of these packets
of energy appear colours of higher frequencies.32 This is indeed the solution
to the problem Planck set out to unravel: how objects glow brighter as they
get hotter, and change colour when their temperature is increased or
decreased.
     The following figure exhibits the whole spectrum of electromagnetic
radiation which extends from gamma rays with shortest wavelength of one
millionth of a nanometer to radio waves many kilometers long. X-rays
range from 0.01 to 10 nanometers in wavelength. Visible light has
wavelengths extending from four hundred to seven hundred nanometers.




             Figure 3. The electromagnetic spectrum
    (Courtesy: Paul Davies, The Goldilocks Enigma). ‘nm' stands for
nanometer which is 10-9 of a meter. ‘pm' refers to micrometer which is 10-6
                               of a meter.

     The foregoing discussions show that wavelength, frequency and
energy are all related terms. Hopefully the following explanations further
clarify these relationships and proportions. Wavelength is the distance
between successive crests or successive troughs of waves.33 Closer crests
and closer troughs in a wave mean shorter wavelength, and distant crests
and distant troughs imply longer wavelength. (See Figures 3 and 4)




     Figure 4. Low frequency (low energy) light with long
                         wavelength




    Figure 5. High frequency (high energy) light with short
                         wavelength
     Frequency in general means how often something happens. The
frequency of a wave refers to the number of up-and-down cycles of
oscillation that a wave completes every second. (See Figures 3 and 4) The
concepts of wavelength, frequency and energy are intimately related. The
longer the wavelength is, the lower the frequency; and the shorter the
wavelength is, the higher the frequency. Thus, the relation between
wavelength and frequency is one of inverse proportion. Again, the longer
the wavelength, lesser the energy, and shorter the wavelength, higher the
energy; here again, there is an inverse proportion between wavelength and
energy. Finally, there is a direct proportion between frequency and energy:
higher frequency means higher energy, and the lower frequency, the lower
energy.34 (See Figures 3 and 4)

      The relationships between wavelength, frequency and energy can be
illustrated by the following example. Take a long rope, tie its one end on a
pillar, and hold the other end in one's hand. To generate a long wavelength,
we leisurely shake our end of the rope up and down. The frequency of
waves matches the number of cycles per second, which our arm goes up
and down; and it is fairly low. We need not expend much energy to
accomplish this feat; it is a low energy affair. Here we execute an operation
which involves long wavelength, less frequency and low energy. In order to
produce short wavelengths of the rope, we have to shake it more frequently,
and this yields higher-frequency waves of the rope. For this, we have to
exert more - higher - energy, and the rope too gets more energy. Here we
perform an act which entails short wavelength, higher frequency and higher
energy. The term ‘amplitude' is also used in relation to waves; it describes
the maximum height or depth of a wave. In fact, there is a proportion
between energy and amplitude of a wave: the lower the amplitude, the
lower the energy, and higher the amplitude, the higher the energy. More
specifically, the energy transported by a wave is directly proportional to the
square of its amplitude.

     As we close our narrative on the quantum, we observe that Planck's
monumental discovery of the discreteness of energy put an end to the
classical concept of the continuum in nature. Literally, the quantum has
‘pulverized' (reduced to dust or grains) the continuum.35 We conclude with
Pagels' comment on Planck's discovery of the quantum:

       Then with an incredible leap of intuition, Planck made the
       quantum hypothesis, which in his own words he described as
       ‘an act of sheer desperation.' . . . Energy exchange was not
       continuous but discrete. Completely without precedent, this
       idea was one of the great leaps of the rational imagination, and
       Planck spent the remainder of his long life attempting to
       reconcile his radiation law with the continuous picture of
       nature.36
2.2. The Quantum ‘Wavicles'

      The quantum world is essentially characterized by discreteness, we
said. The most important ‘ingredients' of this world are quantum ‘stuff'
(wave-particle) and quantum interactions (Heisenberg's uncertainty
relation). These follow from the fundamental concept of discreteness. We
start our narrative with the quantum ‘stuff' which is sometimes dubbed
‘wavicle' (wave-particle). Quantum objects, like photons and electrons,
possess complementary properties of both wave and particle. The double-
slit experiment demonstrates their wave-like property and photoelectric
effect confirms their particlelike character.

2.2.1. The Quantum Wave

     Quantum mechanics appeared on the modern scientific horizon with
Thomas Young's double-slit experiment in the year 1801. In a sense, it is
the foundational experiment in quantum physics. “[Richard] Feynman was
fond of saying that all of quantum mechanics can be gleaned from carefully
thinking through the implications of this single experiment, so it's well
worth discussing.”37 In fact, the double-slit experiment accomplished a
paradigm shift in the conception of quantum objects. Classical physicists
including Newton believed that light is a stream of particles. That belief
was completely overturned by this experiment which demonstrated that
light is also a wave.

     The double-slit experiment can be best understood by comparing it
with ‘single-slit' experiment.38 Greene says: “The [double-slit] experiment
consists of comparing the images on photographic plates that result when
either or both of the slits in the barrier are kept open and the light source is
turned on.”39 For both single-slit and double-slit experiments, the
arrangements are the same except that for single-slit experiments, one of
the slits (left or right) is open, and the other slit (right or left) is kept
closed; the experimental setting consists of a light source like a flashlight,
a barrier screen with two slits and an image screen, like a photographic
plate for recording the arrival of light particles or photons.40 In single-slit
experiments, either left or right slit is covered, leaving right or left slit
open. If the left slit is closed and the right slit is open, then the image will
be impressed on the image screen against the right slit like the one shown
in figure 6. Alternately, if the right slit is covered and the left slit is open,
the image will be found on the screen against the left silt like the one in
figure 7.




       Figure 6. Single-slit experiment with right slit open




                 >

        Figure 7. Single-slit experiment with left slit open
In both cases, since light passes through either the right or the left slit only,
its image will be impressed only either on the right or the left part of the
image screen.

     However, in double-slit experiment, the light beam passes through
both slits and its image is recorded on the image screen. According to
classical physics, since both slits are open, the number of photons passing
through them would be more than those passing through single-slit, thereby
exposing the photographic plate to more total light than in single-slit
experiments. Further, in the Newtonian view because light consists of
corpuscles (little pellets), the particles that pass through two slits would be
impressed on the screen against two areas that line up with two slits. That
is to say, the images would be found against both slits. The image on the
photographic plate would appear like the one in figure 8 which is a
combination of figures 6 and 7.




      Figure 8. The double-slit experiment (classical view)




    Figure 9. The double-slit experiment (with interference
                           pattern)
But the actual image we observe on the screen is something entirely
different and quite amazing too. We find that if both slits are open, the
stream of light produces alternate bands of bright and dark images on the
photographic plate. (See Figure 9) Such a sequence of bright and dark
bands is known as ‘interference pattern' which is typical of wave
phenomena. In bright areas of the image screen, light-wave peaks from
each slit coincide; that is, crests from two beams meet and they enhance
each other, producing bright bands. Whereas in dark areas, light-wave
peaks from one slit coincide with wave troughs from the other, resulting in
cancellation; that is to say, where crests from one light-wave coincide with
troughs from the other, they would cancel out producing dark bands. In
fact, such interference patterns occur in any wave system when two or more
waves come together and overlap: a crest adds with another crest to make a
larger crest, but as a crest meets a trough, they cancel each other.41 Wave
phenomena are thus characterized by such interference patterns.

     As we saw, in single-slit experiments, photons produce images on the
photographic plate, lined up against right or left slit that is open as in the
case of images in figure 6 or 7. However, in doubleslit experiment with
both slits open, we observe an interference pattern -- dark and bright bands
alternating. That is, areas on the photographic plate, which were dark in
single-slit experiments, are now bright in double-slit experiment; and vice
versa, bright areas in single-slit experiments are now dark in double-slit
experiment as in figure 9. Greene describes this puzzling scenario of
interference patterns in detail:

       But when you later examine the image produced [in doubleslit
       experiment], you find that not only are there places on the
       photographic plate that were dark in the first [single-slit]
       experiment and are now bright, as expected, there are also
       places on the photographic plate that were bright in your first
       [single-slit] experiment, but are now dark as in Figure 4.8 [in
       the present text 9]. By increasing the number of individual
       photons that hit the photographic plate, you have decreased the
       brightness in certain areas. Somehow, temporally separated,
       individual, particulate photons are able to cancel each other
       out. Think about how crazy this is. Photons that would have
       passed through the right slit and hit the film [photographic
       plate] in one of the dark bands in Figure 4.8 [in text 9] fail to
       do so when the left slit is opened (which is why the band is now
       dark). But how in the world can a tiny bundle of light that
       passes through one slit be at all affected by whether or not the
       other slit is open?42

Zukav gives an alternate description of the same scenario ending it up with
an almost similar question:

       If both slits are open, there are always alternating bands of
       illuminated and dark areas. This means that there are always
       areas where the photons never go (otherwise there would not be
       any dark areas). If one of the slits is closed, there is no
       interference and the dark bands disappear; the whole wall
       [image screen] becomes illuminated, including those areas
       which previously were dark when both slits were open.

          When we fired our photon and it went through the first slit,
       how did it ‘know' that it could go to an area that must be dark
       if the other slit were open? In other words, how did the photon
       know that the other slit was closed? 43

Indeed, the behaviour of light in double-slit experiment is strange and
astonishing.

     We can observe similar bizarre behaviour of photons in doubleslit
experiment even if we weaken or reduce the intensity of the light beam
until only one photon at a time passes through the slits; and the image
screen records the cumulative effect of many photons arriving one after
another at the screen for a period of time. According to classical physics,
the individual photon would travel through either one slit or the other.
Because the photons are fired individually, there would not be any
interference of waves, and so no wave pattern either. Individual photons
arrive at the image screen one by one and leave their mark. Hence, if we
examine the screen after a number of photons have arrived, according to
classical theory, we should find two images aligned with the two slits.
These images would be like those in figure 8. However, when we actually
observe the image screen, we do not find such a distribution of marks of
photons impressed on the screen; rather we find a wave pattern just as if a
strong stream of photons is fired all at once as in figure 9.

      In other words, if individual photons are fired at the two slits one at a
time and their point of arrival at the image screen is recorded, one observes
the bright and dark interference patterns identical with patterns of the
original double-slit experiment with a strong stream of photons. Stephen
Hawking comments referring to electrons: “Each electron, therefore, must
be passing through both slits at the same time!”44 Similarly, John
Polkinghorne opines: “Since the appearance of the fringes depends
critically upon both slits in the screen being open, one is driven to the
(classically unintelligible) conclusion that the electron ‘went through both
slits'.”45 Greene asks: “How can individual photon particles that
sequentially pass through the screen and separately hit the photographic
plate conspire to produce the bright and dark bands of interfering waves?”46
According to the classical view, each photon passes through either the left
slit or the right slit, and we would therefore find the pattern shown in figure
8. But we do not. The paradox here is that any photon can surely pass
through only one of the slits. Yet the interference pattern requires two
overlapping wave fronts, one from each slit. Since the interference pattern
depends critically upon both slits in the screen being open, one is forced to
conclude that each photon must have passed through both slits at the same
time!

      In the modern version of double-slit experiment, carried out by
Japanese researchers at the end of 1980s, a source of electrons like electron
gun replaces the source of light, magnetic fields plays the role double-slit,
and a detector similar to the screen in a television set functions as screen.
The remarkable thing is that in such an experiment with electrons, one gets
exactly the same kind of interference patterns as those obtained from
experiment with light as source. In the modified version of the experiment,
single electrons are fired through the double-slit system, and their image
impressed on the sensitive surface of a television screen. As more and more
electrons pass through the double-slit system, their impressions on the
screen add up to make a distinctive pattern of light and shade. It is exactly
the interference pattern associated with a strong stream of electrons that
passes through both slits at once en route to the screen. Thus, even when
electrons are fired one at a time, the interference patterns still appear just
as in the case of experiment with shooting photons one by one. But the
cumulative effect of enormous number of electrons will average out the
distribution into a well-defined pattern. This interference pattern is the
same as the one obtained with a strong source of electrons.47

     An alternative description of the flight of a particle, like a photon or
an electron from the source to the image screen is given in terms of
Richard Feynman's path integral formalism. Feynman argues that in
travelling from the source to a given point on the image screen, each
individual photon or electron actually traverses every possible trajectory
simultaneously. The electron tries out every possible path - an infinite
number of them -- between the source and a given position on the screen. It
may travel in an orderly way through left slit; simultaneously it may also
pass through right slit. It may head towards left slit, but may suddenly
change course and move through right slit. The electron may roam back
and forth, and finally pass through left slit. It may even undertake a long
journey to the Andromeda galaxy before turning back and passing through
right slit on its way to the screen! According to Feynman, the electron
simultaneously ‘sniffs' out every possible path from the source to the
screen. In the following figure, a few of the infinity of trajectories of a
single electron travelling from the source to the screen are shown. Notice
that the electron actually goes through both slits.48




Figure 10. A Feynman diagram of an electron trying different
                          paths
    The bizarre behaviour of photons/electrons in double-slit experiment
does raise a number of puzzling questions. Gribbin points out some of the
conundrums:

       It is as if each electron leaves the gun as a particle, turns into a
       wave that travels both ways through the apparatus, then turns
       back into a particle to arrive at a definite spot on the screen.
       Not only that, but it chooses exactly the right spot to make its
       overall contribution to an interference pattern that is built up
       over a long period of time.49

There are still more questions about the strange behaviour of
photons/electrons in the double-slit experiment. How does each individual
particle ‘know' where the others preceding it have hit on the image screen,
and where those following it will land so that it can place itself at the right
location in the interference pattern that builds up from the flow of
thousands or even millions of particles in the experiment? How does a
photon/electron which passes only through one of the slits, ‘know' of the
existence of the other slit and adjust its behaviour accordingly? Could it be
that it passes through both the slits at the same time, only to collapse into a
particle when it arrives on the screen contributing to the interference
pattern?

      Then there is the all-important problem about the nature of the photon/
electron wave itself. Eminent quantum physicists de Broglie and
Schrödinger held that these are ‘matter waves' just as ocean waves are
water waves. According to them, all quantum objects, not just electrons, are
little waves, and all of nature is a great wave phenomenon. Schrödinger
held that electron waves were ‘smeared-out' electrons. This captured some
‘feeling' of an electron wave, but it was too inelegant. When we smear
something out, part of it is here and part of it is there. However, one never
encounters half of an electron or a third of it, or any other fraction, for that
matter. This makes it hard to grasp what a smeared electron actually is. As
an alternative, in 1926 Max Born refined Schrödinger's concept of electron
wave, and it is his interpretation, developed by Bohr and his colleagues,
that is still accepted today. Born held that quantum waves are not material,
but waves of probability. Thus, electron is not a wave like a water wave; it
is not a matter wave, but a probability wave. Physicist Fritjof Capra
explains: “They [quantum particles] are not ‘real' three-dimensional waves
like sound or water waves. They are ‘probability waves,' abstract
mathematical quantities with all the characteristic properties of waves
which are related to the probabilities of finding the particles at particular
points in space and at particular times.”50 Likewise, Pagels says: “What is
behaving like a wave is the probability amplitude for finding electrons —
waves of probability! Although the electron is a particle, the distribution of
these particles on the screen imitates that of a water wave -completely
different from the bullets.”51
     For example, consider the waves of an ocean. If two wave crests
overlap, the height of water wave at that point increases; the amplitudes or
heights of waves add up to produce the total height. But if one wave crest
meets a wave trough at one point, the two waves cancel each other out. This
principle of adding individual wave amplitudes to get the total is called the
‘superposition principle.' Like ordinary waves, the probability waves of
quantum objects obey superposition principle; if there are two probability
waves in some region of space, the amplitudes of these waves add up to
give the total height. But there is a difference: simple addition of
probabilities is not valid in quantum mechanics. Pagels explains: “The
probability for finding a particle at a point in space is not given by the
height of the wave at that point but rather by the intensity of the wave —
the height of the wave squared, gotten by multiplying the height at that
point times itself.”52 Pagels further tells us:

       Because the wave amplitudes add by the superposition
       principle but the probability for finding a particle is given by
       the square of the wave amplitude — its intensity — probability
       in quantum theory are not additive, and there results a
       nonlinear aspect to quantum probabilities, unlike those
       encountered in classical physics.53

Greene expresses the same concept of probability differently: “Places
where the magnitude (a bit more correctly, the square of magnitude) of the
wave is large are places where the electron is more likely to be found;
places where the magnitude is small are places where the electron is less
likely to be found.”54 (See Figure 11)
          Figure 11. Probabilities of finding an electron
Thus, if the probability wave or more precisely, the square of the
probability wave of an electron is twice as large at location A than at
location B, then quantum theory predicts that in a sequence of many
repetitions of an experiment, the electron will be found at location A twice
as often as at location B. The ‘shape' and evolution of such probability
waves are governed by Schrödinger equation.

    We conclude our discourse on ‘The quantum wave' with a quote from
Coveney and Highfield:

       To the extent that quantum mechanics provides the ultimate
       description, no more can be asked about the whereabouts of the
       electron until it impinges on the screen. We are therefore
       forced to conclude that the electron is somehow spread or
       delocalised throughout space and time, passing through both
       slits and interfering with itself before miraculously collapsing
       instantaneously to some spot on the screen in an entirely
       random fashion. It is everywhere and nowhere at the same
       time.55

2.2.2. The Quantum Particle

     The double-slit experiment conclusively proves that light behaves as a
wave. This outcome is in fact surprising because traditionally it was held
by physicists including Newton that light consists of particles. Of course,
we may say that in the double-slit experiment, a photon or an electron
behaves, not only as a wave, but also as a particle; that is, a photon
proceeds as a particle from a light source, spreads out as a wave, and
finally ends up as a particle to design the interference pattern of a wave on
the image screen. However, this is not a definitive proof that light consists
of particles. In order to produce such a proof, a genius like Einstein had to
enter the scene. He started with Planck's concept of the discreteness of
energy. Planck believed that light displays its discrete nature only when it
is being emitted by some physical object. “This meant that when light is
moving in free space it is not quantized but rather retains the wave
characteristics described by Maxwell's equations.”56 Einstein believed that
Planck's revolution was not radical enough. He assumed that light is
quantized, and so it is emitted or absorbed as particles. It is the
phenomenon of photoelectric effect that led him to this insight. Einstein
extended Planck's hypothesis regarding the nature of light, and suggested
that light itself consists of particles. Science writer Gary Zukav says:
“Einstein's theory of light was that it is composed of tiny particles. A beam
of light, said Einstein, is analogous to a stream of bullets. Each bullet is
called a photon. This is similar to what Planck proposed, but actually it is a
leap beyond. . . . Einstein theorized that energy itself is quantized.”57
Greene makes an interesting comment about Einstein's discovery of the
particlenature of light: “But Einstein, the man who brought down Newton's
revered theory of gravity, seems now to have resurrected Newton's particle
model of light by his introduction of photons.”58

     In order to prove his theory, Einstein investigated the phenomenon of
photoelectric effect; and in 1905 he wrote a paper demonstrating the
particle nature of light.59 Employing Planck's concept of the discreteness of
energy, Einstein made the ingenious proposal that in photoelectric effect,
light behaves as if it is made up of individual particles or little packets,
each packet carrying a definite amount of energy. The photoelectric effect
was discovered by the German physicist Heinrich Hertz in 1887. “This
phenomenon consists in this: that a piece of metal, exposed to the action of
radiation with a sufficiently short wavelength, often expels electrons in
rapid motion.”60 When a beam of light strikes a metallic surface, it
relinquishes some of its energy. This transferred energy excites electrons in
the metal, and some of the loosely bound electrons are knocked off the
surface. When a dim beam of light shines on the surface of a metal, the
beam ejects electrons from the metal, one at a time precisely as though
individual particles of light (individual photons) were hitting electrons and
knocking them out of the metal's surface one by one. This becomes
possible because some of the electrons of metals are only loosely bound
within their atoms. They are not tightly bound to any individual atom and
so are relatively free to move around the metal. Light falling on a piece of
metal provides energy for these free electrons, and if they get sufficient
energy, they move fast enough to fly off. This phenomenon is known as
photoelectric effect which works because metals have a lot of loose
electrons. (See Figure 12) In itself, there is nothing particularly remarkable
about this phenomenon.61




                 Figure 12. The Photoelectric effect
     However, the strange features of photoelectric effect become apparent
when one examines in detail the properties of electrons ejected from a
piece of metal as light rays fall on it. Philippe Lenard observed two
peculiar characteristics.62 First, if the energy or frequency (colour) of the
impinging light is kept constant and its intensity is increased (that is, a
stronger light is used; Lenard simply moved his light source closer to the
metal), then the number of ejected electrons increased, but their speed
remained the same. Second, if the frequency, and so the energy, of
impinging light (that is, when light with a shorter wavelength or different
colour is used), is increased, then the speed of ejected electrons also
increases; and equivalently, if the frequency of light is decreased, their
speed also decreases. Greene comments: “For some unknown reason, the
color [frequency] of the impinging light beam — not its total energy
[intensity] — controls whether or not electrons are ejected, and if they are,
the energy they have.”63 De Broglie describes the photoelectric puzzle as
follows:

       The essential characteristic of this phenomenon is that the
       energy of the expelled electrons is solely a function of the
       frequency of the incident radiation and does not depend in any
       way on its intensity. Only the number of the electrons depends
       on the incident intensity. These simple empiric laws made most
       difficult the theoretical interpretation of the elementary
       mechanism at the base of the liberation of the photoelectric
       electrons, or photoelectrons, as they are called today.64

     Einstein based his revolutionary theory on the experimental work of
Lenard. He set out to find solutions to the riddles posed by photoelectric
effect by broadening Planck's concept of discreteness of energy. Zukav
explains in detail:

       Einstein's theory also substantiated Planck's revolutionary
       discovery. High-frequency light, like violet, is made of higher-
       energy photons than low-frequency light, like red. Therefore,
       when violet light, which is made of high-energy photons,
       strikes an electron, it causes the electron to rebound with a
       high velocity. When red light, which is made of low-energy
       photons, strikes an electron, it causes the electron to rebound at
       a low velocity. In either case, increasing or decreasing the
       intensity of the light increases or decreases the number of
       rebounding electrons, but only by changing the color
       [frequency] of the impinging light can we change their
       velocity.

       In short, Einstein demonstrated, using the photoelectric effect,
       that light is made of particles, or photons, and that the photons
       of high-frequency light have more energy than the photons of
       low-frequency light.65

     Thus, basing himself on Lenard's observations on photoelectric effect,
Einstein suggested that Planck's concept of the discreteness of energy
emission can be incorporated into a new description of light as consisting
of particles. As we saw, high-frequency light such as violet light has short
wavelength and high energy, and low-frequency light like red light has long
wavelength and low energy.66 Hence, photons of violet light knock off
electrons from atoms of a metal and send them flying off at a higher speed
than photons of red light because photons of violet light with high-
frequency have more energy than photons of red light with low-frequency.

      Consequently, what determines the energy and speed of ejected
electrons is the frequency (colour) of light beam. This means that if we
increase the frequency (colour) of impinging light (for instance, using
violet light), then electrons will leave metallic surface with greater speed;
but if the frequency of light is decreased (red light is used), then electrons
will be ejected at slower speed. The reason is that the energy packets of
violet light are ‘larger' than those of red light. Thus, violet light has more
energy (high frequency and shorter wavelength) than red light (low
frequency and longer wavelength). This follows from Planck's principle
that the energy of each photon is proportional to its frequency. However, as
the intensity of light of any frequency is increased, the number of photons
impinging on metallic surface increases resulting in an increase in the
number of ejected electrons. Conversely, if the intensity of light is reduced,
the number of photons falling on metal is also decreased, as also the
number of ejected electrons. For instance, reducing the intensity of a beam
of green light decreases the number of photons in the beam. Each
remaining photon, however, still has the same amount of energy as any
other photon of green light. Consequently, when a photon of green light
strikes an electron, it knocks it away with the amount of energy which is
characteristic of green-light photons. But since the intensity of light beam
is reduced, the number of ejected electrons is also decreased. Hence, a light
beam, according to Einstein, should actually be thought of as a stream of
tiny packets — tiny particles of light with definite amount of energy.67 In
the words of Greene:

       The frequency of the light (its color) determines the speed of
       the ejected electrons; the total intensity of the light determines
       the number of ejected electrons. And so Einstein showed that
       Planck's hypothesis of the discrete nature of energy actually
       reflects a fundamental feature of electromagnetic waves: They
       are composed of particles — photons — that are little bundles,
       or quanta of light.68

     Evidently, Einstein's hypothesis accounted for the peculiarities of
photoelectric effect which Lenard observed. We sum up Einstein's
contribution to quantum physics in the words of Glen Kezwer : “He
[Einstein] extended Planck's quantum hypothesis by suggesting that the
light itself is quantized, meaning that the light behaves as if it consists of
individual particles or quanta each of which carries a definite amount of
energy.”69

2.2.3. The Wave-Particle Complementarity

     Thomas Young's double-slit experiment demonstrated that light is
wave-like, and Einstein's interpretation of photoelectric effect, established
that light is particle-like. In some contexts, light behaves as wave and in
others as particle. The double-slit experiment shows that light manifests
the interference properties of waves. The photoelectric effect confirms that
light has particle-like properties. Greene says: “Together they show that
light has both wave-like and particle-like properties. The microscopic
world demands that we shed our intuition that something is either a wave or
a particle and embrace the possibility that it is both.”70 But no single
experiment shows light to be both, and none could, since waves and
particles have mutually exclusive properties. Under no circumstances, it
behaves as both wave and particle. The property of being wave-like, as the
property of being particle-like, is relative to circumstances of
measurement. The complex nature of light can be summarized as follows:
       The description of light must be in terms of incompatible
       properties that cannot be observed together at the same time
       but that together give a complete model of the behavior of
       light. The properties of being a wave and being a particle are
       mutually incompatible, yet the complete description of the
       nature of light must include both. The two properties
       complement each other.71 Because of the wave-like and
       particle-like nature of quantum particles, like photons and
       electrons, they are sometimes called ‘wavicles.'

      However, the concepts of wave and particle are fundamentally
different. They have a number of irreconcilable differences. Particles have
some properties that are contrary to those of waves. Likewise, waves
behave in some ways that particles simply do not and cannot. Particles are
discrete objects, confined to relatively small, well-defined locations in
space and time. They interact with one another, for example, by bouncing
off one another. Waves, on the other hand, are typically spread out over
fairly wide areas. They interact with one another in ways quite different
from those of particles. They are easily split in an enormous variety of
ways, some parts moving in one direction, and some in other directions.
Two waves sometimes interact with one another so as to form a larger
wave; sometimes two waves interact in such a way as to cancel each other
out. These behaviour patterns of waves, we saw, are known as
‘interference.'72

      These general properties of particle and wave are exemplified in the
classical concept of particle and in the quantum mechanical notion of wave.
Classically, a particle is characterized by a sharp spatial position, and if it
is moving, it has a definite trajectory through space in time. It has
momentum and energy, and these quantities are also sharply localized in
space and time. By contrast, a quantum wave cannot be sharply localized.
The energy and momentum of a wave are distributed throughout its spatial
extension, and are also not sharply localized. It follows that a wave has no
definite trajectory through space in time, but rather propagates through a
finite spatial volume in time. Typical characteristics of wave phenomena
are interference and diffraction, none of which are associated with particle
motions. Thus, there is the greatest possible contrast between the concepts
of classical particle and quantum wave.73 Despite these dissimilarities,
however, quantum objects, such as photons, electrons and protons, exhibit
characteristics of both waves and particles.

     If quantum objects behave as waves and particles, which are
apparently contrary entities, how are we to think of their reality? To answer
this question, Niels Bohr proposed the theory of complementarity. He
interpreted wave-particle duality as complementary states of the same
quantum object. Bohr suggested that these seemingly incompatible
properties are complementary rather than contradictory. According to de
Broglie, by complementarity Bohr means that “a complete description of
observable phenomena requires that these concepts [wave and particle] be
employed in turn, but that these concepts nevertheless are in a sense
irreconcilable, the images that they furnish never being simultaneously
applicable in toto to a description of reality.”74 Though the concepts of
particle and wave exclude one another, the wave-particle nature of a photon
or an electron, for instance, can be regarded as complementary aspects of
the same reality like two sides of a coin. An electron can behave sometimes
as a wave and sometimes as a particle, but never as both together, just as a
tossed coin may fall either head or tail up, but never both at once. Wave and
particle are the complementary representations of the same quantum
object. Bohr says that “the evidence obtained under different experimental
conditions cannot be comprehended within a single picture, but must be
regarded as complementary in the sense that only the totality of the
phenomena exhausts possible information about the objects.”75 The
principle of complementarity states that quantum entities are neither pure
waves nor pure particles in their nature, but objects that partake of the
characters of both. Photon is not a wave in the classical sense; it is a wave
which is also a particle. Nor is electron a particle in the old sense; it is a
particle which is also a wave. We may therefore describe an object like an
electron in ways which are mutually exclusive — as wave or as particle —
without logical contradiction.

    However, the experimental arrangements which determine these
complementary descriptions also have to be similarly mutually exclusive.
It is purely a human choice - of the scientist -- which experiment, and so
which description s/he chooses. The role of the scientist in choosing the
experimental set up can be illustrated by modifying the original double-slit
experiment - by setting up little beams of light right behind the two holes.
We can now observe which hole an electron goes through by detecting the
light scattered by the electron as it emerges from a hole. However, the
moment we turn on little light beams, the original experimental conditions
(of the double-slit experiment) is altered and the distribution pattern of
electrons on the image screen changes. If we know exactly which hole each
electron passes through, then the distribution on the screen becomes just
like that of machine-gun bullets — a particlelike distribution. Once we
decide to experimentally check that the electron is a particle going through
the hole, it does behave like a particle passing through the hole. But
suppose we dim the light beams so that we see only a few electrons as they
go through a given hole. Then the particle distribution begins to shift over
to the wave interference pattern in a continuous way. Pagels says:
“Whatever we do, we cannot know which hole the electron passes through
and still retain the wavelike distribution pattern. Mutually exclusive
experimental arrangements -- you can have the light beam or not -give
mutually exclusive results -- the electron behaves like a particle or it does
not.”76

      Hence, whether a photon or an electron behaves as a wave or as a
particle depends entirely on the kind of experiment we perform on it. The
wave-like property or the particle-like property of a quantum object is
relative to the circumstances of measurement/ observation. In other words,
the experimental set up -- the way a photon is measured/observed --
determines in which of the two ways it appears. However, no single
experiment or measurement shows a quantum object to be both, and none
could, since waves and particles have mutually exclusive properties. This
means that it is necessary to include the whole measurement set up in the
description of a quantum object or its property. To define its property alone
without the inclusion of the measuring set up is meaningless. Paul Davies
summarizes Bohr's position on the relation between experimental
arrangements and wave-particle duality:
       Bohr recognized that it is not paradoxical for an electron to be
       both a wave and a particle because the wave-like and
       particlelike aspects are never displayed in a contradictory way
       in the same experiment. Bohr pointed out that one can
       construct an experiment to display the wave-like properties of
       a quantum object, and another to display its particle-like
       properties, but never both together. Wave and particle
       behaviour (and other incompatibilities, such as position and
       momentum) are not so much contradictory as complementary
       aspects of a single reality. Which face of the quantum object is
       presented to us depends on how we choose to interrogate it.77

     Similarly, Pagels says:

       Bohr's principle of complementarity asserts that there exist
       complementary properties of the same object of knowledge,
       one of which if known will exclude knowledge of the other. We
       may therefore describe an object like an electron in ways
       which are mutually exclusive — e.g., as wave or particle —
       without logical contradiction provided we also realize that the
       experimental arrangements that determine these descriptions
       are similarly mutually exclusive. Which experiment -- and
       hence which description one chooses -- is purely a matter of
       human choice.78

According to Bohr, complementarity is not limited to the subatomic world,
but it is a general principle that pertains to all aspects of nature, and even to
other fields of human thought. Bohr himself applied complementarity to
biology.79

     What we said about the complementarity of wave-particle duality and
about the mutually exclusive experimental set up applies also to other
conjugate properties of quantum objects. For example, the properties of
position and momentum of a subatomic particle can be given meaning only
under different experimental arrangements. Thus, the experimental set up
used to measure the position of a particle is not the same as that used to
measure its momentum. If we design our experiment to measure position,
we cannot possibly measure the momentum at the same time with
precision; in extracting information about the one, we destroy our ability to
know about the other. Hence, there are experimental situations in which the
expression ‘position of an electron' can be assigned a definite meaning, and
also other situations in which the expression ‘momentum of an electron'
can be meaningfully employed. However, there is no experimental
arrangement in which the expression ‘position and momentum of an
electron' can be given an experimental sense.

     In his doctrine of complementarity, Bohr thus provided a conceptual
interpretation of wave-particle duality and of the complementary character
of conjugate properties such as position and momentum of quantum
objects. Complementarity is thus an essential trait of the quantum world.
The complex nature of quantum objects, like photon and electron as wave
and as particle can be summarized as follows. These objects are described
in terms of incompatible properties that cannot be observed together at the
same time, but together they give a complete picture of the behaviour of
these objects. The properties of wave and particle are mutually
incompatible, yet the complete description of the nature of quantum objects
includes both. These two properties complement each other, but they are
not simultaneously observable. If we set up an experiment to measure the
wave-like aspect of a quantum object, we lose information about its
particle-like characteristic. In a converse experiment, we cannot get
information about its wave-like character. There is no way, in principle, to
observe both properties at once and get information about both. The
principle of complementarity shows that quantum reality is undetermined
and that our grasp of it can never be complete.80

      We close our discourse on ‘The Quantum Wavicles' with a quote from
Einstein, which succinctly summarizes the meaning of ‘wavicle': “We are
faced with a new kind of difficulty. We have two contradictory pictures of
reality [wave-particle]; separately neither of them fully explains the
phenomena of light, but together they do.”81

2.3. The Quantum Uncertainty
      Wave-particle duality is the first pillar of quantum physics, and
Heisenberg's uncertainty principle is the second. Both of these concepts are
intimately related. John Hawley and Katherine Holocomb remark: “The
fact that only probabilities, and not absolute certainties, can be assigned to
[quantum] states is ultimately a consequence of the wave nature of
particles; this lies at the heart of Heisenberg's famous uncertainty
principle.”82 Indeterminism in the quantum world follows from uncertainty
principle.

2.3.1. The Uncertainty Principle

     Heisenberg's uncertainty principle is the fundamental law which
governs the dynamics of quantum objects - of wave-particles. Interactions
in the quantum world are ruled by uncertainty relation. This law teaches
fundamental uncertainty in the behaviour of quantum systems. The
characteristics of our everyday world — that objects have definite positions
and speeds at particular times, and that they have definite energies at
definite times — are now seen to be utterly meaningless in the quantum
world. According to classical physics, if we know the initial conditions of a
physical system, such as a particle or its aggregate, we can know
simultaneously and with certainty positions and speeds of the future states
of that system. In fact, the classical mechanics requires the simultaneous
knowledge of both these conjugate quantities with certainty for the
prediction of the evolution of physical systems. By contrast, in quantum
mechanics, it is impossible to know simultaneously with precision where a
quantum object is (its position), and what its velocity is (its momentum).
For, these properties of quantum objects are governed by Heisenberg's
uncertainty principle. De Broglie contrasts the positions of classical
physics and quantum mechanics:

       We can realize the difference which exists on this score
       between the old [classical] and the new [quantum] mechanics
       by noting that the elements [position and velocity, for instance]
       whose simultaneous knowledge at the initial instant was
       necessary in classical mechanics in order to be able to predict
       rigorously the evolution of a system, are precisely those whose
       simultaneous determination is impossible according to the
       Uncertainty Relations.83

     In general, the uncertainty principle can be formulated as follows:
“The uncertainty principle states that if the value of some physical quantity
is certain, then the value of a complementary quantity is uncertain.”84 De
Broglie spells out uncertainty principle in detail:

       Indeed, as the fine and profound analyses of Heisenberg and
       Bohr have shown, it follows from the existence of the quantum
       of action that all attempts to measure a characteristic quantity
       of a given system has the effect of changing in an unknown
       fashion the other quantities associated with this system. More
       precisely, any measurement of a quantity which permits the
       exact localization of a system in space and time has the effect
       of changing in an unknown fashion a quantity which is
       conjugate with the first and which serves to specify the
       dynamic state of the system. In particular, it is impossible to
       measure at the same time and with precision any two conjugate
       quantities. . . . .

       From the above it follows that we are never able to know with
       precision more than half the quantities, the knowledge of
       which would be necessary for an exact description of a system
       in accordance with classical ideas. The value of a quantity
       characterizing a system is indeed that much more uncertain
       when that of its conjugate quantity is more exactly known.85

     The uncertainty principle thus expresses a trade-off between the
degree of certainty about the value of one physical quantity, such as
position and its complementary quantity, namely, velocity. The more the
value of one quantity is certain, the less certain is the value of the other.
Consequently, the position and velocity of a subatomic particle, like an
electron, cannot be simultaneously determined with certainty. If position is
accurately calculated, velocity remains uncertain, and vice versa.
Heisenberg emphatically affirms: “Any use of the words ‘position' and
‘velocity' with an accuracy exceeding that given by the uncertainty
equation is just as meaningless as the use of words whose sense is not
defined.”86

     In order to illustrate the behaviour of quantum particles governed by
uncertainty principle, let us imagine a world in which the flight of a tennis
ball cannot be predicted in terms of Newton's laws of motion. In such a
world, the trajectory of the ball would not be determined by the thrust of
the racket; and the laws in this world would give us only probabilities of
the motions of the ball. Although games would be played in accordance
with the rules of tennis, even the most proficient player would not succeed
in sending the ball in the intended direction even if the ball is struck in the
appropriate way. In the description of such a world, the expression ‘ball
with a determinate position and velocity' would not be employed and would
be found meaningless. In this imagined world, tennis balls behave in a
manner in which photons and electrons behave in the quantum world. Their
motions are governed by uncertainty principle.87

     Heisenberg devised a thought experiment in order to clarify the
meaning of uncertainty principle; it is known as ‘gamma ray microscope
experiment.' It is designed to observe the position and velocity of an
electron. Heisenberg suggested gamma ray as the medium for observation
because it has the shortest wavelength. The shorter the wavelength of
radiation used, the more accurate is the determination of position. If we use
such high-frequency gamma rays, we can locate the position of an electron
with greater precision, just as a ruler with millimeter graduations
determines more precisely the length of an object than the one with
centimeter graduations. But the problem here is that gamma rays which
have a much shorter wavelength than visible light, also has much more
energy than visible light. Such energetic rays will impart an unpredictable
amount of energy to the electron disturbing its course. Renowned physicist
Arthur Eddington confirms: “We can improve the determination of the
position with the microscope by using the light of shorter wavelength, but
that gives the electron a greater kick and spoils the subsequent
determination of momentum.”88 In this way, we can measure accurately
only its position, but not its velocity because the gamma ray would cause
an unpredictable change in electron's momentum. Hence, in order to
determine with great precision the velocity of an electron, we need to
disturb it as little as possible. If we use low-frequency (less energetic)
radiation, we minimize the impact on electron's motion and determine its
velocity more precisely since the radiation has comparatively low energy.
But then we sacrifice precision in determining electron's position; that is,
we cannot locate its position accurately. Heisenberg observed an inverse
proportion between the precision with which one measures
electron's/photon's position and the precision with which one measures its
momentum. The more accurately the position is determined, the less
accurately the momentum is measured and vice versa.89

     However, Bohr objected to the presuppositions of Heisenberg's
‘microscope experiment.' The thought experiment assumed that the
electron had an actual path, and that at each instant it had a definite
position and momentum which was then disturbed by observation. It
presumed that the electron really possesses definite properties, and that it is
the interaction with measuring apparatus which prevents us from knowing
them exactly. Bohr argued vehemently against such classical
presuppositions being carried over to quantum physics. For, the electron
has no path; and it has no definite, independent properties, like position and
momentum. Davies comments:

       It is not that quantum mechanics is inadequate to predict the
       precise positions, motions, etc. of particles; it is that a quantum
       particle simply does not possess a complete set of physical
       attributes with well-defined values. It is meaningless to even
       consider an electron, say, to have a precise location and motion
       at one and the same time.90

Gribbin shares a similar view:

       The essential feature of Heisenberg's discovery-- indeed, in
       many ways the essential feature of quantum physics -- is that
       the entity we call ‘an electron' does not possess both a well-
       defined    position    and a       well-defined momentum,
       simultaneously. There is an intrinsic uncertainty, which has to
       do with the way the Universe is put together, and has nothing
       to do with the skill, or otherwise, of human experimental
       physicists.91

     This means that quantum uncertainties cannot be reduced by more
precise measurements; they are irreducible. This is another way of saying
that such uncertainties are inherent to the nature of subatomic particles.
Davies clarifies:

       Most physicists interpret quantum uncertainty to be intrinsic,
       and not merely the result of human ignorance or clumsiness in
       measurement. One might express this by saying that even the
       electron is uncertain of its properties. Thus quantum
       uncertainty cannot be ameliorated by ‘looking harder'. In this
       respect, it stands in contrast to random chance in, say, a game
       of roulette, or movements in the stock market. Changing share
       prices certainly have their causes in something: if they appear
       random and unpredictable it is because humans do not have all
       the information they need to work out how they vary. Quantum
       randomness, by contrast, is irreducible, which is to say that
       quantum processes are in some sense genuinely spontaneous -
       without any specific cause.92

Differently expressed, quantum uncertainty is not a consequence of the
limitations of human knowledge. Rather uncertainty belongs to the very
nature and behaviour of quantum objects themselves. Hence, uncertainty
principle actually says something deeper than merely stating that we cannot
know at any instant both position and momentum of a quantum particle
with certainty; it says that a particle does not possess both a definite
position and a definite momentum simultaneously. Consequently, if we
measure accurately the position, we lose information about the momentum,
and vice versa. Either can be known as precisely as possible, but only at the
expense of the knowledge of the other. Indeed, uncertainty principle states
something essential about the nature of quantum particles.

     This principle is ordinarily stated in terms of position and velocity of
subatomic particles. But it is applicable also to other conjugate quantities,
like energy and time. Just as we cannot know simultaneously the position
and momentum of a quantum particle with precision, so there is a
limitation to the accuracy with which we can measure its energy within a
given interval of time. The uncertainty principle relates these two
quantities in the same way as it links mutual uncertainties of position and
momentum. Thus, if a particle's energy in a quantum state is known with
precision, then the time it spends in that state - its duration -- cannot be
known with certainty. But if its duration in that state is known precisely,
then its energy cannot be known with certainty. In other words, if we know
the state of energy of a particle exactly, then we are not certain about the
amount of time that it is likely to remain in that state. Conversely, if we
know precisely the length of time the particle has been in that state, we
would be uncertain about its energy state. Greene observes: “Quantum
mechanics asserts that you can't say that a particle has precisely such-and-
such energy at precisely such-and-such moment in time.”93

      The effect of uncertainty relation on physical systems is extremely
small in everyday terms since the Planck constant ‘h’ (‘h-bar') is also
exceedingly minute. The more massive the object is, the less the
uncertainty. To get a ‘feel' of how Heisenberg's uncertainty principle has
effect on various objects, we make the following comparisons. For a flying
tennis ball, the uncertainties are only one part in about ten million billion
billion billion (10-34). Hence, a tennis ball obeys the deterministic laws of
classical physics to a high degree of accuracy. Even for a bacterium,
uncertainty effects are only about one part in a billion (10-9), and so it
really does not experience the quantum world. With atoms in a crystal, we
are getting down to the quantum world, and the uncertainties are one part in
a hundred (10-2). Finally, for an electron with a mass of just 10-37 gram, the
uncertainty effect can be significant. Quantum uncertainties completely
dominate these particles and we have entered the true quantum world
governed by uncertainty relations.94

     We conclude our narrative on ‘The Uncertainty Principle,' with an
insightful quote from Greene:

       The uncertainty principle captures the heart of quantum
       mechanics. Features that we normally think of as being so
       basic as to be beyond question — that objects have definite
       positions and speeds and that they have definite energies at
       definite moments — are now seen as mere artifacts of Planck's
       constant being so tiny on the scales of the everyday world. Of
       prime importance is that when this quantum realization is
       applied to the fabric of spacetime, it shows fatal imperfections
       in the ‘stitches of gravity’ and leads us to the third and primary
       conflict physics has faced during the past century.95

2.3.2. The Quantum Indeterminism

     Indeed, the quantum indeterminism follows from Heisenberg's
uncertainty principle. Pagels confirms: “Physicists found that the
uncertainty relations implied indeterminacy.”96 In order to comprehend
indeterminism in quantum physics, we contrast it with the rigorous
determinism of classical physics - both of Newton's mechanics and of
Einstein's theory of relativity. Newton's laws of motion determine
completely the future behaviour of any physical system if its initial
