conditions at any given instant of time, and forces acting on it are fully
known. De Broglie explains the meaning of determinism in classical
mechanics:

       THE EQUATIONS of classical mechanics completely determine the
       motion of a system when the position and state of motion of
       the parts of the system are known at the initial instant. Thus the
       classical motion of a corpuscle is completely predictable when
       its position and velocity are known at a certain initial instant.
       This possibility of predicting in an inexorable way the future of
       a mechanical system when a certain amount of data about its
       present state is known constitutes the determinism of classical
       mechanics.97

As part of classical physics, the theory of relativity also presupposes strict
determinism. Physicist Benjamin Gal-Or states:
       By contrast, the classical space-time of general relativity
       remains fully deterministic at all scales. While it is also a
       dynamic entity which changes according to the distribution of
       mass, energy, and momentum, it nevertheless remains well-
       defined, once that distribution has been defined. It always, at
       any scale, defines a specific time relationship to the concept of
       (strict) causality. In other words, causality is strictly obeyed in
       any formulation of the general theory of relativity.98

One of the reasons why Einstein could not accept quantum physics was his
firm conviction that it eschews such rigorous determinism.

      In contrast to Newtonian and relativistic determinism, quantum
mechanics teaches intrinsic indeterminism in the quantum world.
Heisenberg's uncertainty principle in effect states such indeterminism: that
it is impossible to determine simultaneously the precise values of
conjugate quantities, like position and momentum of quantum objects.
Obviously, such uncertainties are negligible in the case of physical objects
and systems of the ordinary world, and rigorous determinism rules such a
world. But on the subatomic level, uncertainties about the simultaneous
knowledge of conjugate quantities are considerable, and forbid their
deterministic description. Prigogine and Stengers write: “Quantum
mechanics deals with only half of the variables of classical mechanics. As a
result, classical determinism becomes inapplicable, and in quantum physics
statistical considerations play a central role.”99 De Broglie compares the
determinism of classical mechanism with the indeterminism of quantum
physics:

       In the old [classical] physics, the simultaneous knowledge of
       the quantities fixing the position of the parts of a system and of
       the conjugate dynamic quantities permitted, at least in
       principle, a rigorous calculation of the state of the system at a
       later instant. . . . This possibility of a rigorous forecasting of
       future phenomena starting from present phenomena, a
       possibility implying that the future is in some manner
       contained in the present and adds nothing to it, constituted
       what has been called the determinism of natural phenomena.
       But this possibility of a rigorous forecasting requires the exact
       knowledge at the same instant of time of the variables of
       spatial localization and of the conjugate dynamic variables;
       now, it is precisely this knowledge which quantum physics
       considers impossible.100

      We illustrate the contrast between Newtonian determinism and
quantum indeterminism with a standard problem in ballistics. Suppose a
gun is fired, and we know both the position and momentum of the bullet as
it leaves the barrel. Using the laws of classical physics and the knowledge
of bullet's initial conditions, such as its weight, position and momentum,
we can precisely determine its entire future trajectory. However, if we
consider the same problem from the standpoint of quantum mechanics, we
reach a different conclusion. Heisenberg's uncertainty relation implies that
we cannot simultaneously know with precision both the position and
momentum of the bullet at the instant it leaves the barrel. To the extent that
these initial measurements are uncertain, the future trajectory of the bullet
cannot be determined. All we can do is to give a statistical or probabilistic
prediction of the future trajectory of the bullet. For real bullets and tennis
balls, the uncertainty effects are negligible. But for subatomic particles,
like electrons, we can give only a probabilistic description of their future
path. This is quantum indeterminism. Davies spells out:

       In classical mechanics one must know both the positions and
       the momenta of all the particles at the same moment to predict
       the subsequent evolution of the system. In quantum mechanics
       this is forbidden. Consequently there is an intrinsic uncertainty
       or indeterminism in how the system will evolve. Armed even
       with the most complete information permitted about a quantum
       system it will generally be impossible to say what value any
       given quantity (e.g. the position of a particle) will have at a
       later moment.101

    De Broglie finds the ultimate foundations of classical determinism
and quantum indeterminism in their respective conceptions of space-time
framework:

       The manner in which classical physics conceived the absolute
       determinism of physical phenomena rested essentially on the
       manner of thinking about space and time and the theory of
       relativity, while revising so profoundly our ideas relative to
       space and to time, had, however, sufficiently respected them so
       as not to breach classical determinism. It is not the same with
       the quantum theory. The latter, by not allowing us any longer to
       represent the evolution of individual phenomena in a
       continuous manner in the framework of space and time, obliges
       us to abandon determinism completely, or at least to modify
       profoundly the conception we had of it. . . . Indeed, the present
       quantum theory furnishes us only with laws of probability,
       allowing us to say what, the result of the first observation
       being given, is the probability that a later observation will
       furnish us such and such a result. This substitution of laws of
       probability for rigorous laws in the microscopic domain is
       certainly bound up with the non-validity of the concepts of
       space and time in this domain. 102

     However, although quantum mechanics teaches uncertainty and
indeterminism, there is a sense in which it is still a deterministic theory.
Prigogine and Stengers suggest: “If the wave function at a given instant is
known, Schrodinger equation allows it to be calculated for any previous or
subsequent instant. From this viewpoint, the situation is strictly similar to
that in classical mechanics.”103 Davies explains the meaning of
determinism in quantum mechanics:

       In spite of the indeterminism that is inherent in quantum
       physics, a quantum system can still be regarded as
       deterministic in a limited sense, because the wave function
       evolves deterministically. Knowing the state of the system at
       one time (in terms of the wave function), the state at a later
       time can be computed, and used to predict the relative
       probabilities of the values that various observables will possess
       on measurement. In this weaker form of determinism, the
       various probabilities evolve deterministically, but the
       observable quantities themselves do not.104

     Thus in quantum mechanics, determinism consists in the prediction of
the probable state of a quantum system. This means that although the result
of a particular quantum event may not be determined, the relative
probabilities of different outcomes evolve in a deterministic manner. That
is, probability distributions are causally determined, not specific events.
Hawking tells us:

       These quantum theories are deterministic in the sense that they
       give laws for the evolution of the wave with time. Thus if one
       knows the wave at one time, one can calculate it at any other
       time. The unpredictable, random element comes in only when
       we try to interpret the wave in terms of the positions and
       velocities of particles.105

That is to say, physicists can calculate the probability of a quantum event,
such as an atomic nucleus decaying, taking place even though they cannot
know for certain the actual outcome in that particular case. In this sense as
a statistical theory, quantum mechanics is a deterministic theory. That is
why machines like computers and mobiles which depend for their
functioning on the behaviour of innumerable quantum particles like
electrons can still function reliably even though the behaviour of each
individual electron within the machine cannot be predicted. Paul Davies
and John Gribbin aptly remark: “Quantum physics builds chance into the
very fabric of reality, but a vestige of the Newtonian-Laplacian world view
remains.”106

     Although scientists still use deterministic laws of classical mechanics
to deal with events and motions of objects of everyday life, their attitude
toward determinism has been profoundly altered by the new insights
provided by quantum mechanics. Pagels suggests how quantum
indeterminism might influence the perspectives of physicists:
       It [quantum indeterminism] implied the existence of physical
       events that were forever unknowable and unpredictable. Not
       only must human experimenters give up ever knowing when a
       particular atom is going to radiate or a particular nucleus
       undergo radioactive decay, but these events are even unknown
       in the perfect mind of God. 107

Pagels explains why quantum events are undetermined even for God:

       Even an all-knowing mind must support its knowledge with
       experience, and once it tries to experimentally determine one
       physical quantity the rest of the deck of nature gets randomly
       shuffled again. The very act of attempting to establish
       determinism produces indeterminism. There is no randomness
       like quantum randomness. Like us, God plays dice — He, too,
       knows only the odds.108

      Eminent physicists like Born, Eddington, Heisenberg and Bohm have
strongly supported indeterminism in the quantum world. Born frankly
asserts that quantum theory is “in the nature of the case indeterministic,
and therefore the affair of statistics.”109 Likewise, Eddington remarks: “It is
impossible to trap modern physics into predicting anything with perfect
determinism because it deals with probabilities from the outset.”110
According to Heisenberg, quantum indeterminism is the manifestation of a
very fundamental and all-pervasive general principle that governs all of
nature. Bohm comments about Heisenberg's view on indeterminism: “Thus,
rather than consider the indeterminacy relationships primarily as a
deduction from the quantum theory in its current form, he [Heisenberg]
postulates these relationships directly as a basic law of nature and assumes
instead that all other laws will have to be consistent with these
relationships.”111

      In contrast, great physicists, like Einstein and Schrödinger, regarded
indeterminism of the quantum world as an indication of man's limited
ability to penetrate the details of natural processes, not as a description of
how nature works. Einstein was a staunch supporter of classical
determinism, and could not reconcile himself with indeterminism in nature.
Pagels remarks: “The indeterminism of the quantum theory is an issue of
principle, of what is knowable and unknowable, not experimental technique
— that is what distressed Einstein. Even God can give you only the odds
for some events to occur, not certainty.”112 Einstein found the concept of
quantum indeterminism so shocking that he dismissed it with the famous
retort that “God does not play dice with the Universe.” He spent much of
his later years searching the deterministic elements that he thought must lie
hidden beneath the apparently random world of quantum mechanics.113

     We conclude our narrative on ‘The Quantum Uncertainty,' with de
Broglie's comment on the deep impact of quantum indeterminism on
classical determinism:

       Nevertheless, although a great many fundamental reforms still
       seem necessary to us in order to have a clear understanding of
       quantum physics, personally it does not seem probable that we
       will succeed in completely re-establishing the determinism of
       days gone by. The blows that the development of the new
       [quantum] mechanics have [sic] delivered to it [classical
       determinism] seem to us too deep-seated to be easily effaced.
       The wisest course, no doubt, is to hold to this statement: at the
       present time, the physics of phenomena where quanta play a
       part, is no longer deterministic.114

2.4. The Quantum Wave Function and Its Collapse

     Now, the baffling question is, is there something that would
conceivably ‘tame' the quantum ‘beast' with its strange nature of wave-
particle duality governed by uncertainty principle? Is there any overarching
concept that would bring together these bizarre characteristics and
uncertainties of quantum objects? That concept, according to quantum
physics, is the wave function. Whereas wave-particle duality and
uncertainty principle are the two pillars of quantum mechanics, wave
function is truly its foundation. The collapse of the wave function ‘gives
birth' to one of the multiple possibilities conceived in its ‘womb.'
2.4.1. The Quantum Wave Function

     The wave function is the central concept in quantum physics. “The
fundamental theoretical quantity in quantum mechanics is the wave
function.”115 In fact, the word ‘wave function' underlines the importance of
wave-particle duality in quantum mechanics. Prigogine and Stengers
suggest: “The term ‘wave function’ has been chosen to emphasize once
again the wave-particle duality so fundamental in all quantum physics.”116
Polkinghorne explains how the behaviour of an electron in double-slit
experiment is the outcome of its wave function:

       [T]he electron's state is a superposition of the state in which it
       goes through one slit with the state in which it goes through the
       other. The diffraction pattern on the detecting screen
       corresponds to the interference effects between these two
       components, added together to form the single wavefunction
       describing the electron's state.117

Zukav gives a simple description of the concept of wave function:

       In more general terms, it [wave function] describes physical
       reality at the most fundamental level (the subatomic) that
       physicists have been able to probe. According to quantum
       mechanics, the wave function is a complete description of
       physical reality at that level. Most physicists believe that a
       description of the substructure underlying experience more
       complete than the wave function is not possible.118

Physicist Julian Barbour likewise says: “The wave function of a particle is
assumed to be a maximal representation of its physical state. It codes
everything that can ever be deduced about the particle at an instant. Using
it, we can predict the outcome of experiments performed on the
particles.”119

    The wave function thus comprises everything about the state of a
quantum system. It encapsulates everything that can be known about a
quantum object before an observation is made. The wave function is
symbolized by the Greek letter psi (ψ) which represents the mathematical
description of the state of an isolated quantum system.120 Hence, wave
function is called also ‘psi-function.' Here is another description of the
wave function referring to it as psi-function:

       In quantum mechanics, wave function is the variable quantity
       that mathematically describes the wave characteristics of a
       particle. The value of the wave function of a particle at a given
       point of space and time is related to the likelihood of the
       particle's being there at the time. By analogy with waves such
       as those of sound, a wave function, designated by the Greek
       letter psi, ψ , may be thought of as an expression for the
       amplitude of the particle wave (or de Broglie wave), although
       for such waves amplitude has no physical significance. The
       square of the wave function, ψ2, however, does have physical
       significance: the probability of finding the particle described
       by a specific wave function y at a given point and time is
       proportional to the value of ψ2.121

     According to quantum physics, every physical entity, whether it is an
atom or a bullet, has a wave function associated with it. It is not to be
thought of as a material thing like waves in water. The wave function
describes all the possibilities that a quantum system ‘comprises' before it
interacts with an observing system or a measuring device. “It would be a
composite of all the possibilities of the observed system which it
describes.”122 The wave function ‘represents,' not any actual state of a
quantum object, but a superposition of all its possible states. It ‘contains'
all the potential behaviour of a quantum system. The wave function
‘includes' the wave-particle nature of quantum object, and its properties,
such as position and velocity, and energy and time. Coveney and Highfield
suggest: “Accordingly, his equation [Schrödinger's] includes a completely
new mathematical quantity called the wavefunction which takes into
account the Jekyll and Hyde character of tiny particles and maps out all the
possibilities of their behaviour.”123 Further, the wave function is not static,
but it is dynamic and ever-changing. Zukav says: “This strange animal
[wave function] constantly would change with the passage of time. Each
moment it would be different than the moment before.”124

    The concept of wave function can be best clarified by contrasting it
with the idea of initial conditions/actual state in Newtonian mechanics.
Davies explains initial conditions:

       In classical mechanics the state of a system [initial conditions]
       is easily visualized. It is given by specifying the positions and
       velocities (or momenta) of all the particles concerned. The
       system evolves as the particles move about under the influence
       of their mutual interactions and any externally applied forces.
       The physicist can predict this evolution, at least in principle,
       by the use of Newton's laws of motion to compute the paths in
       space of each particle.125

      However, in quantum physics, we speak of wave function rather than
initial conditions of a system as in Newtonian mechanics. “Quantum
mechanics replaces this concrete picture of the state of mechanical system
by an abstract mathematical object called wave function or the state
vector.”126 Similarly, Bohm says: “In other words, the actual and individual
object of classical physics is replaced by a more abstract kind of potential
and statistical object. This latter is said to correspond to the ‘quantum state
of the system', which in turn corresponds to ‘the wave function of the
system’ (or more generally to a vector in Hilbert space).”127 Thus, the state
of the system in classical mechanics is called initial conditions, and the
‘state of the system' in quantum physics is called wave function. But we
hasten to add that this is an understatement, and that it does not tell us the
whole truth. For, according to classical physics, the initial conditions of a
physical object are actual states which comprise its properties, such as
position, velocity, mass and shape, at a given instant of time; whereas in
quantum mechanics, we cannot speak about such actual states apart from
experimental conditions. Bohm observes:

       In classical physics, one thinks that only the actual state of a
       system can be relevant in a physical situation, and that
       probability comes in either because we are ignorant of the
       actual state or because we are averaging over an ensemble of
       actual states that are distributed over a range of conditions. In
       quantum theory it has no meaning to discuss the actual state of
       a system apart from the whole set of experimental conditions
       which are essential to actualize this state.128

     Thus, the actual state of a system, according to classical mechanics,
comprises its initial conditions; and the comparable concept in quantum
physics is the wave function which, however, does not represent the actual
state of a quantum object simply because no such state exists. As far as the
quantum object's actual state is concerned, the wave function has to
collapse in order to ‘produce,' so-to-speak, this state by actualizing one of
its possibilities in the act of measurement. Consequently, the wave function
‘represents' an ensemble of its possibilities, not actual states. For instance,
an electron's character of wave-particle duality and its properties of
position and momentum are not actual states, but are possible ones. There
are also other conjugate properties of an electron, like energy and time
which are also not actualities, but are only possibilities that are described
by its wave function. Hence, the state of a quantum object is a world of
possibilities and probabilities, not actualities, and the wave function is said
to describe such a state. We summarize the concept of wave function in the
words of Bohm:

       Every physical situation is now characterized by a wave
       function (or more abstractly by a vector in Hilbert space). This
       wave function is not directly related to the actual properties of
       an individual object, event, or process. Rather, it has to be
       thought as a description of the potentialities within the
       physical situation. Different and generally mutually
       incompatible potentialities (e.g., for wavelike or particle-like
       behaviour) are actualized in different experimental
       arrangements (so that the wave-particle duality can be
       understood as one of the main forms for the expression of such
       incompatible potentialities). In general, the wave function
       gives only a probability measure for the actualization of
       different potentialities in a statistical ensemble of similar
       observations carried out under specified conditions, and cannot
       predict what will happen in detail in each individual
       observation.129

     The wave function thus describes the possibilities of the state of a
quantum system; and Schrödinger equation calculates their probabilities
becoming actual. This equation predicts probabilities of quantum events
occurring, but cannot predict with certainty what will actually happen in a
given case. This law does not predict, for instance, an electron's actual
location when landing on the image screen in double-slit experiment; but it
only calculates the probabilities of finding electron at many points on the
screen when an observation is made. The reason is that the electron simply
does not possess an actual set of physical properties with well-defined
values, such as position and velocity; these are but potentialities of the
electron.130

2.4.2. The Collapse of the Wave Function

     Now, the question is, how do the possibilities of a quantum object
represented by its wave function achieve actuality? How do these turn into
actual states and get related to the real world in which events definitely
happen or do not happen? Davies answers: “If a quantum system is in a
superposition of states, a definite reality can only be observed if the wave
function collapses on to one of the possible observable states.”131
According to the Copenhagen interpretation of quantum mechanics,
possibilities achieve definiteness and actuality, only if something happens
to collapse the wave function. Prior to this collapse, all possibilities are on
an equal footing. The wave function collapses in the act of measurement/
observation, and one of the possibilities becomes actual. Although the
quantum world is inherently indeterminate, and Schrödinger equation
predicts only probabilities of the wave function, the act of measurement
transforms probability into certainty, and converts one of the possibilities
into actuality by selecting from a range of possibilities. “This
transformation, from the plethora of potential outcomes to the one
observed, is known as the ‘reduction' or the ‘collapse' of the
wavefunction.”132 In the act of measurement/ observation, the wave
function collapses and one of the possibilities become real, and acquires
definiteness and determination. Zukav explains:

       In other words, making a measurement interferes with the
       development in isolation of the observed system. When we
       interfere with the development in isolation of the observed
       system (which is what Schrödinger’s wave equation governs)
       we actualize one of the several potentialities that were a part of
       the observed system while it was in isolation.133

Similarly, Julian Barbour says: “It is not that before the measurement a
particle does have a definite momentum, and we simply do not know it.
Instead, all momenta in the superposition are present as potentialities, and
measurement forces one of them to be actualized.”134

     For example, in the double-slit experiment the wave function of an
electron describes innumerable possibilities of pathways on its way from
the source to the image screen. As the electron is fired from an electron
gun, its wave function would continue to evolve according to the law of
Schrödinger equation. But the equation does not predict the exact location
where the electron will land; it tells us only the probability of the electron
arriving at several points on the screen. This probability of spotting the
position of electron on the screen varies exactly the same way as the
predicted wave intensity differs. It has a small probability of landing at
many points over a wide area and greater probability to land in other
regions. When the electron actually hits an image screen, such as a
photographic plate, and is observed at a particular spot on it, at that instant
one of the possibilities in the wave function would actualize and others
would cease to exist. In other words, the probability of the electron's being
at that point suddenly becomes one, and zero at all other points; that is, its
wave function collapses. When measurement/ observation is stopped, the
electron evolves into a new superposition of possibilities, only to collapse
again, perhaps in a different way in another measurement.135 Coveney and
Highfield illustrate:

       If we adopt the Copenhagen interpretation of quantum
       mechanics, there are an infinite number of possible outcomes
       of which just one becomes actuality when we ‘pop' the
       wavefunction. Imagine you are sitting in a quantum theatre.
       There is an endless profusion of possible plays which might
       take place, from Shakespeare to Coward to Ibsen to Wilde. But
       as soon as the curtains swing apart the theatre wavefunction
       collapses to reveal Agatha Christie's The Mousetrap.136

     However, if a particular measurement is repeated over and over again
in completely identical manner, for instance, for measuring the position of
an electron on the image screen, the same outcome will not be obtained
again and again. Rather, the subsequent repetitions of experiment will yield
a variety of results about the particular position of electron. Barbour
comments: “Every measurement ‘collapses' the wave function. After the
collapse, the wave function, which could have been used to predict
outcomes of alternative measurements, has been changed irrevocably: there
is no going back to the experiment we opted not to perform. It is a very
singular business.”137 Thus, one measurement of the electron's location on
the image screen alters its behaviour irreversibly. In fact, as Coveney and
Highfield point out, a local collapse of a wave function has effect
everywhere in the universe:

       When a measurement is performed at the screen to find out
       where the electron is, the wavefunction collapses. But although
       measurement is made locally, the collapse of the wavefunction
       alters the physical state of the electron everywhere at the same
       instant - remember that the wavefunction contains every
       possibility for that electron in space and time, not just the one
       that emerges when the wavefunction collapses. . . . An
       observation at any given point causes the collapse of a state of
       potentiality (all possible outcomes) to a state of actuality (the
       outcome observed), which is then fixed everywhere in the
       universe at one and the same instant.138

     In order to illustrate the quantum weirdness of the collapse of wave
function, Schrödinger devised a thought experiment in 1935, which is
famously known as “Schrödinger's cat.” It is narrated umpteen times, but
no discourse on quantum physics can ignore its presentation. Imagine that a
live cat is caged inside a closed box. The box contains a flask with poison
(like hydrogen cyanide) inside, a small amount of radioactive material, a
Geiger counter and a hammer. If the hammer is triggered, it would break
open the flask, release poison and the cat would be killed. The hammer will
be triggered only if the Geiger counter detects disintegration of one atom of
radioactive material. The sequence of events would be the following: one
of the atoms of radioactive material decays, Geiger counter detects it,
hammer is triggered and it breaks open the flask, poison is released, and the
cat is killed. (See Figure 13) The radioactive atoms, being quantum objects,
will disintegrate in such a way that the probability of their disintegration
can be calculated according to the law of Schrödinger equation.




                      Figure 13. Schrödingers'cat
     In this thought experiment, we assume that after a specified time, say
one hour, the probability of one atom disintegrating is exactly fifty percent.
This means that there is a fifty percent probability that the flask has been
broken and the cat is killed; and a fifty percent probability that the atom
did not disintegrate and the cat is still alive. We wait for one hour, and then
open the box and look inside. From the common sense point of view, what
we expect to find — and what we will actually find — is that the cat is
either alive or dead. There is a fifty-fifty chance for the atom to decay, and
consequently for the cat to be either alive or dead. But what does the
quantum theory say about the situation prior to opening the box? It states
that before we looked at the cat, it was both alive and dead at the same
time! Or, more precisely, the state of the cat was a mixture: fifty percent
live cat and fifty percent dead cat!! Schrödinger is quoted to have said that
these two states of the cat are “mixed or smeared together by equal
amounts.”139

      According to quantum mechanics, the wave function of the cat in the
box represents a superposition of two possibilities: of the live cat and of the
dead cat. In the one case, the atom decays, Geiger counter detects it,
hammer is triggered and it breaks open the flask, poison is released, and the
cat is dead; and in the other case, which has equal probability, none of these
happens, and the cat remains alive. In the ghostly hybrid state, the cat
cannot be regarded as definitely either dead or alive, but in some strange
way both. For the physicist who accepts the Copenhagen interpretation,
nothing is real unless it is observed. S/he has not opened the box, and has
not peered inside it; and so neither of the two possibilities of radioactive
material and also of the cat, has any actuality. Without the scientist's actual
observation, the atom has neither decayed nor has not decayed, and
consequently the cat has neither been killed nor not been killed. The
radioactive material would be in a superposition of states, and so would be
the cat. However, once the physicist looks inside the box, the atom is either
decayed or not decayed; and the cat is then dead or alive. Hence, if we are
to take the Copenhagen interpretation of quantum mechanics seriously, the
cat in the box exists in some indeterminate state of being alive and dead:
the wave function of the cat comprises the superposition of these two
possible states. Only when the box is opened and the state of the cat is
observed, and the cat's wave function collapses, does it actually ‘become'
either alive or dead. Davies and Gribbin remark: “It is as if nature suspends
judgement on the fate of the poor creature until somebody peeks. But this
raises the obvious question: What is going on inside the box when nobody is
looking?”140

     The cat story is deliberately contrived to highlight the paradoxical
character of the collapse of wave function in a dramatic way, but similar
phenomena occur whenever any quantum event takes place involving
subatomic particles. The oddity of collapse of wave function is that it
seems to depend upon the action of the observer. It appears that if nobody
observes, then the wave function does not collapse. Hence, the behaviour of
a particle, such as an electron, appears to vary according to whether it is
being observed or not. This is deeply disturbing to physicists, but may not
be a big problem for ordinary people. Many physicists are very uneasy
about the view that the wave function of a quantum system collapses when
it is observed by an observer. They are averse to giving a role to conscious
observers in a fundamental physical theory, such as quantum mechanics;
they are wary of the thought that quantum reality ultimately depends on
conscious observer.141

     As an alternative to the Copenhagen interpretation, Hugh Everett III
and Bryce DeWitt proposed the Many Worlds hypothesis. Everett rejected
the idea of the collapse of wave function when a measurement is made by
an observer; in fact, he wanted to eliminate the involvement of conscious
observers in the quantum process. According to Many Worlds hypothesis,
wave function never collapses, as held by Copenhagenists. Instead, all
quantum possibilities represented by wave function actually exist, each in
its own universe. In this perspective, possibility is identical with actuality,
and so infinite possibilities of everything and also of the observer are
somehow actualized. As Polkinghorne puts it, “everything that could
happen does happen.”142 Barbour summarizes Many Worlds hypothesis:

       Collapse does not happen at all: the multiple possibilities
       represented in the entangled state continue to coexist. In each
       possibility the observer, in different incarnations, sees
       something different, but what is seen is definite in each case.
       Each incarnation of the observer sees one of the possible
       outcomes that the Copenhagen interpretation assumes is
       created by collapse.143

     If the wave function does not collapse, how do we experience a
definite, actual world? According to Many Worlds hypothesis, with each
measurement of a quantum system, like measuring the position/velocity of
an electron, the universe splits into a multiplicity of mutually unobservable
and independent domains. When the measurement of a quantum object is
made, suddenly and imperceptibly the universe is divided into an infinite
number of observing subjects in an infinite number of new universes.
Definiteness emerges from infinite possibilities because all these
possibilities are actualized in multiple universes which are all ‘real.'
Conscious observers also divide endlessly into multiple copies, and these
too actualize all possibilities. For a fleeting moment, we observe a
determinate universe. Here the word ‘universe' does not mean ‘all spatio-
temporal reality.' Rather, it refers only to the limited domain of the
observer and the observed system. Only that restricted region of the
universe involved in the measurement of quantum objects divides; but
unobserved regions do not split. Here is an interesting quote from De Witt,
one of co-authors of Many Worlds hypothesis, about his head-reeling
experience upon learning about Everett’s proposal of this theory: “I still
recall vividly the shock I experienced on first encountering this multiworld
concept. The idea of 10100+ slightly imperfect copies of oneself all
constantly splitting into further copies, which ultimately become
unrecognizable, is not easy to reconcile with common sense. Here is
schizophrenia with a vengeance. . .”144

     According to Many Worlds hypothesis, then, all possibilities that the
wave function of a quantum object describes are real, and all of them
actualize in different worlds that co-exist with ours. When a measurement
is taken, the universe divides into these multiple actualities, and we
observe one of them. On the other hand, according to Copenhagen
interpretation, the wave function describes an ensemble of possibilities;
only one of them collapses and actualizes when an observation is made, and
the rest of them continue to evolve perhaps to collapse in another
measurement. Thus, we have two contrasting explanations of the
measurement of a quantum system - the one Copenhagen interpretation and
the other Many Worlds hypothesis.

     We conclude with the words of John Gribbin and Martin Reese who
contrast both theories: “Where Copenhagen theory says that none of the
quantum options has any reality unless it is observed, many Worlds theory
says that all quantum possibilities really do exist, each in its own space and
time, and that each measurement we make simply identifies which branch
of the multi-universe we are in.”145

2.5. The Quantum Reality Itself

      So far in our discourse, we focused on the ‘stuff' of quantum objects,
their properties and their causal interactions. The quantum reality, we saw,
is characterized by wave-particle duality which is governed by
Heisenberg's uncertainty principle; these are subsumed under the concept
of wave function which collapses when a measurement is made. Now, the
question is, what is the kind of reality that such quantum objects have?
Copenhagenists led by Niels Bohr, and EPR (Einstein, Podolsky, Rosen)
team under the leadership of Einstein locked horns over the quantum-
reality problem. Incidentally, EPR paradox highlighted an important
property of subatomic particles, namely, their non-locality.

2.5.1. The Strange Quantum World

      Quantum mechanics has been extremely successful in explaining
countless physical phenomena. Its predictions have been confirmed again
and again during the last century. Its practical applications in technology
have produced many electronic manufactured goods which have become
part of modern life. However, the reality of quantum objects themselves
was/is an enigma even to great physicists, like Bohr, Heisenberg and
Einstein. Moreover, quantum theory contains within itself a number of
conceptual paradoxes which even after over a century of its origin remain
unresolved. According to Cambridge physicist John Polkinghorne “the
greatest paradox about quantum theory is that after more than fifty years of
successful exploitation of its techniques its interpretation still remains a
matter of dispute.”146 Likewise, Pagels comments: “Here, for the first time,
arose the astonishing problem of physically interpreting the mathematics of
the new mechanics, a problem quantum physicists would be struggling with
in the coming years.”147 Physicist Nick Herbert wryly remarks: “One of the
best kept secrets of science is that physicists have lost their grip on
reality.”148 In fact, there were intense or even acrimonious exchanges
between the Copenhagen school led by Bohr and the EPR team with
Einstein as its principal advocate about the strange properties of quantum
reality without ever reaching any conclusion.

     Before we examine the nature of quantum reality, let us recount the
saga of quantum mechanics we have narrated so far. The twin concepts of
quantum physics, namely, wave-particle duality and Heisenberg's
uncertainty principle have shaken the foundations of classical, Newtonian
mechanics and of our common sense world of everyday experience. A
quantum particle, such as a photon or an electron, we saw, behaves both as
a wave and as a particle. Further, according to uncertainty principle, we
cannot measure with certainty the conjugate properties of quantum objects,
like position and momentum at the same time. Uncertainty relation appears
to have wrecked cause-effect relations in the quantum world. Moreover, the
quantum wave function and its collapse add further twist to ‘matter myth,'
as Davies calls it. Furthermore, the observed quantum system and the
observer seem to be intimately linked. Bohm remarks that according to the
Copenhagen interpretation, “in an observation carried out to a quantum-
mechanical level of accuracy, one must regard the entire ensemble
consisting of observing apparatus and observed system as indivisibly
united by the quanta connecting them.”149 The Many Worlds hypothesis
about the measurement of quantum objects is still more bizarre: our
universe is said to be dividing endlessly every time a quantum
measurement/observation is made. Indeed, the quantum world appears
weird and strange when contrasted with the Newtonian universe and our
ordinary world of experience.

     In fact, the quantum mechanics has wilted away the meanings of some
of the fundamental concepts of classical mechanics and of our common
sense language. Thus, what we ordinarily call a ‘thing' or ‘physical object'
appears to have ‘melted away' in the quantum world. R. Blanché writes: “In
brief, the several attributes of the substantial thing, res, are gradually
withdrawn. Reality rests against a background of unreality: the
infrastructure of the microphysical world has no more than a ‘ghostly
existence', its reality ‘dissolving in the shadows' (P. Jordan).”150 Similarly,
Polkinghorne points out: “The existence of such elusive [quantum] objects
clearly modified our notion of reality. One of the perplexities about the
interpretation of quantum mechanics is what, if any, meaning it attaches to
the reality of something as protean as an electron.”151 That is the reason
why many scientists believe that in spite of its extraordinary success in
making predictions and its astonishing achievements in technology,
quantum mechanics does not tell us the whole story about physical reality.
According to eminent physicists, like Einstein, Schrödinger, de Broglie,
Paul Dirac and Bohm, quantum theory, no matter how well it works, is not a
complete theory because it does not provide a comprehensive picture of
reality. On the contrary, Bohr and Heisenberg, the great stalwarts of the
Copenhagen interpretation hold that this theory has no story to tell us about
reality; indeed, it is just incapable of doing it.

     The complexities and weirdness of the quantum world raise many
questions about its reality. Greene asks a number of general questions about
the quantum mystery:

       What are we to make of this? Does it mean that on a
       microscopic level the universe operates in ways so obscure and
       unfamiliar that the human mind, evolved over eons to cope
       with phenomena on familiar everyday scales, is unable to fully
       grasp ‘what really goes on'? Or, might it be that through
       historical accident physicists have constructed an extremely
       awkward formulation of quantum mechanics that, although
       quantitatively successful, obfuscates the true nature of reality?
       No one knows.

       Maybe some time in the future some clever person will see
       clear to a new formulation that will fully reveal the ‘whys' and
       the ‘whats' of quantum mechanics. And then again, may be not.
       The only thing we know with certainty is that quantum
       mechanics absolutely and unequivocally shows us that a
       number of basic concepts essential to our understanding of the
       familiar everyday world fail to have any meaning when our
       focus narrows to the microscopic realm. As a result, we must
       significantly modify both our language and our reasoning when
       attempting to understand and explain the universe on atomic
       and subatomic scales.152

     Further, we can ask also some specific questions about the reality of
quantum objects and about uncertainty principle. Can we assume whether
or not a quantum particle like an electron continues to be at a place and at a
time like an ordinary physical object? Does uncertainty relation affect only
our knowledge, or does it also affect the ‘real' position or ‘real' momentum
of a particle? There are a number of difficulties even in raising such
questions. They may turn out to be meaningless because they presuppose
the concept of classical object, which is not applicable to quantum object.
Renowned philosopher of science Marx Wartofsky observes: “That is, in
the new theory [quantum mechanics] the meaning of particle has changed
and no longer countenances a simultaneous determination of the precise
values of the classical particle.”153 The classical meaning of physical object
is completely changed in quantum theory.

     Then there is the most important question about the status of wave
function, or of the quantum state it ‘represents,' namely, the superposition
of the possibilities of quantum objects and their properties. We cannot
interpret the quantum state described by wave function in any naïve,
ordinary sense of the term. Nevertheless, the wave function is treated by
quantum physicists with a degree of seriousness which accords it a status
going beyond that of mere calculational device. Oxford mathematician
Roger Penrose argues:

       Despite the fact that we are normally only provided with
       probabilities for the outcome of an experiment, there seems to
       be something objective about a quantum-mechanical state
       [wave function]. It is often asserted that the state- vector is
       merely a convenient description of ‘our knowledge' concerning
       a physical system -- or, perhaps, that the state-vector does not
       really describe a single system but merely provides
       information about an ‘ensemble' of a large number of similarly
       prepared systems. Such sentiments strike me as unreasonably
       timid concerning what quantum mechanics has to tell us about
       the actuality of the physical world.154

Similarly, Polkinghorne opines about the ‘frail' reality of the wave
function:

       The wave function is the vehicle of our understanding of the
       quantum world. Judged by the robust standards of classical
       physics it may seem a rather wraith-like entity. But it is
       certainly the object of quantum mechanical discourse and, for
       all the peculiarity of its collapse, its subtle essence may be the
       form that reality has to take on the atomic scale or below.155

Finally, there is the knotty question about the collapse of the wave function.
Polkinghorne asks:

       How does the act of measurement unscramble those
       superposed possibilities and plump for one of them on a
       particular occasion? In the jargon of the subject this perplexity
       is called the problem of ‘the collapse of the wavefunction' --
       probability which was spread out over ‘here', ‘there' and,
       perhaps, ‘everywhere', suddenly is focused on the actually
       realized result of the observation. It is somewhat humiliating
       for the physicist to have to confess that there is no universally
       agreed or wholly satisfactory answer to that reasonable
       question.156

      Faced with the bizarre concepts and principles of quantum mechanics,
quantum physicists soon found themselves divided into two camps. The
one group was led by Niels Bohr who argued that this theory deals only
with ‘phenomena,' presented by the abstract mathematical formalism, and
that it does not, and cannot, provide us a deeper insight into reality; this is
the Copenhagen interpretation. The other camp under the leadership of
Einstein maintained that quantum mechanics should give us a complete
picture of reality, and that in its current formulation, it cannot be regarded a
complete theory in physics; this position is presented by the EPR
paradox.157
2.5.2. The Copenhagen Interpretation and the EPR Paradox

     We have placed the Copenhagen interpretation of quantum mechanics
and EPR paradox in a sequel because the discussions between the
proponents of both these positions bring into bold relief the question about
the nature of quantum reality. The Copenhagen interpretation, we know,
emerges from the work of Bohr and Heisenberg who were the leading
advocates of this view. Their position was countered by Einstein and his
team members, Boris Podolsky and Nathan Rosen; they proposed a
thought-experiment which is famously known as the ‘EPR paradox.'

2.5.2.1. The Copenhagen Interpretation

      The Copenhagen interpretation is in fact the first response to the
question about the nature of quantum reality. According to Bohr, what
quantum mechanics knows is not the reality itself, but the ‘phenomenon'
which “refers exclusively to observation obtained under specific
circumstances, including an account of the whole experiment.”158 The
concept of phenomenon here includes, not only the quantum object itself,
but also the experimental set up which is employed to observe it; and so the
knowledge of quantum reality is determined, not merely by the object
alone, but by the conditions of observation also. In other words, the
experimental arrangement partially determines observational results, and
consequently also the meaning ascribed to observed object. Such a quantum
object with ascribed meaning is called ‘phenomenon.' Thus for instance, if
the double-slit experiment gives us information that electron is wave-like
in its nature, that information is a product, not only of the electron, but also
of the particular experimental set up. Photoelectric effect is a different
experiment, and so it would supply us knowledge about the particle-like
behaviour of the electron. Since the experimental arrangement at least
partially determines the outcome of observing the electron, our knowledge
of it is determined, at least in part, by how we know it. Heisenberg says: “If
one wants to clarify what is meant by ‘position of an object', for example of
an electron, he has to describe an experiment by which the position of an
electron can be measured; otherwise this term has no meaning at all.”159
Hence, we cannot say anything about the position or velocity of an electron
without also specifying how (that is, by means of which experiment) we
determine them. The same applies to any observed property of quantum
objects. The very process of measurement evidently assumes considerable
importance in this view, whereas it had been completely ignored in
classical physics.

      Such a view emphasizes the observer's role in selecting which
property of quantum object is to be measured, for instance, waveaspect or
particle-aspect. Depending upon this choice, knowledge of different
properties of quantum reality is obtained. Consequently, the
Copenhagenists apparently hold that as long as observation decides the
attributes of a subatomic particle, there is no need to refer to a deeper
reality. Bohr and Heisenberg and other prominent physicists who accept the
Copenhagen interpretation, have spoken to this effect. Bohr's statement
sums up the Copenhagen view: “There is no quantum world. There is only
an abstract quantum physical description. It is wrong to think that the task
of physics is to find out how nature is. Physics concerns what we can say
about nature.”160 Likewise Heisenberg says: “What we learn about is not
nature itself, but nature exposed to our methods of questioning.”161
Princeton physicist John Wheeler draws the ultimate consequences of the
Copenhagen interpretation: “No elementary phenomenon is a real
phenomenon until it is an observed phenomenon.”162

     In fact, all these quotes referring to the Copenhagen interpretation
have a distinctively Kantian flavour. They state in effect that we can know
only phenomena - things as they appear in knowledge, not noumena -
things-in-themselves. This is in short the gist of the philosophy of the great
German philosopher Immanuel Kant. In his Critique of Pure Reason, Kant
divided the possible objects of knowledge into phenomena and noumena.163
Phenomena are objects as they appear to the knowing subject, and noumena
are things-in-themselves which supposedly exist independently of the
subject. Herman Philipse summarizes Kant's view on knowledge: “Kant's
transcendental turn implies a distinction on the one hand the objects as we
may experience them (phenomena) which are partly constituted by the
knowing subject, and on the other hand entities as they are in themselves
(noumena).”164 Davies and Gribbin spell out what is meant by phenomena
in the context of quantum physics:

       Quantum mechanics enables us to relate different observations
       made on, say, an atom. The theory is to be regarded as a
       procedure for connecting these observations into some sort of
       consistent logical scheme - a mathematical algorithm. Use of
       the word ‘atom' is just an informal way of talking about that
       algorithm. It is a helpful meaning of encapsulating that
       abstract concept in physical language, but that does not mean
       that the atom is actually there as a well-defined entity with a
       complete set of physical attributes of its own, such as definite
       location in space and definite velocity through space. . . . The
       term ‘atom' itself became merely a code word for a
       mathematical model [phenomenon]. It was not intended to
       represent an independent part of reality.165

     This is indeed a quantum mechanical rendering of the philosophy of
Kant. Bohr and Heisenberg would say that the phenomenon -algorithm (the
mathematical model of atom) - is “partly constituted by the knowing
subject.”166 Bohr himself states as much when he says that phenomenon
“refers exclusively to observation obtained under specific circumstances,
including an account of the whole experiment.”167 Philosopher T. Z. Lavine
explains Kant's concept of phenomenon which is applicable to the
Copenhagen interpretation:

       Nature does not give the human mind its laws, Kant has
       discovered. It is the other way around. The mind gives its own
       laws to nature - its own laws in the form of its own necessary
       concepts which organize all sensory materials. These are the
       concepts which form, organize, and structure all our
       experience, all our knowledge of nature. In Kant's famous
       words, ‘Mind is the law-giver to nature.' Thus the laws of
       nature are dependent upon the concepts of the human mind.
       With the philosophy of Kant, the world has become mind-
       dependent, as the American philosopher C. I. Lewis has said.168
In the present context, we may render the last statement in the quotation as
follows: “With the Copenhagen interpretation of quantum physics, in
particular with the views of Bohr and Heisenberg, the quantum world has
become mind-dependent.” That is to say, the properties of quantum objects
appear to be dependent on the observer.

     If these musings on the Copenhagen interpretation of quantum physics
are taken seriously, then the objectivity of the quantum world is called into
question. Hence, we have no access to ‘raw' quantum reality or
noumenon/thing-in-itself in Kantian terms. Renowned quantum physicist
Babu Joseph argues:

       For, one of the key postulates of quantum mechanics is that the
       process of extracting information or knowledge from a system
       is by performing an experiment or observation, which involves
       the preparation for, plus the execution of, the act. Hence, it is
       often surmised that a ‘raw' system by itself doesn't have a
       physical identity of itself. The composite system plus observer
       or knower alone makes sense and it is this combination or
       ‘complex' which is considered dynamical (capable of
       evolution).169

Likewise, physicist Henry Stapp draws a similar conclusion when he says
that there is no substantive physical world:

       If the attitude of quantum mechanics is correct, in the strong
       sense that a description of the substructure underlying
       experience more complete than the one it provides is not
       possible, then there is no substantive physical world, in the
       usual sense of this term. The conclusion here is not the weak
       conclusion that there may not be a substantive physical world
       but rather that there definitely is not a substantive physical
       world.170

    Zukav too voices a similar opinion:
       The Copenhagen Interpretation of Quantum Mechanics does
       not go so far as to say what reality is ‘really like behind the
       scenes,' but it does say that it is not like it appears. It says that
       what we perceive to be physical reality is actually our
       cognitive construction [phenomenon in the Kantian sense] of
       it. This cognitive construction may appear to be substantive,
       but the Copenhagen Interpretation of Quantum Mechanics
       leads directly to the conclusion that the physical world itself is
       not.171

     The Copenhagen interpretation says in effect that it is unnecessary to
peer ‘behind the scenes' to see what is really happening as long as quantum
mechanics works in different experimental conditions; that is, as long as it
correlates experiments correctly and makes predictions. It is not necessary
to know, for instance, how light can manifest itself both as waves and
particles. It is enough to know that it does, and to use this knowledge to
predict probabilities. But then, there is no comprehension of the reality of
light. Truly, this limitation of our knowledge is inescapable; we can know
only quantum phenomena. Hence, we cannot give a description of quantum
objects as they really exist in themselves - noumena in Kantian terms.
Heisenberg strongly affirms: “The hope that new experiments will lead us
back to objective events in time and space is about as well founded as the
hope of discovering the end of the world in the unexplored regions of the
Antarctic.”172

     We summarize the Copenhagen view in the pithy words of quantum
physicist John Bell. He suggests that “in quantum physics, ‘be-ables'
[noumena] are replaced by ‘observables,' [phenomena] and the verb ‘to be'
becomes ‘to be measured.'”173 The British idealist philosopher George
Berkeley stated as much when he said: esse est percipi -- to be is to
perceived. This view denies the objectivity of human knowledge; more than
that it apparently rejects the existence of an external world independent of
the knowing subject. May we suggest that the Copenhagen view epitomized
by the statements of Bohr, Heisenberg and Bell affirms as much with
regard to the quantum world?
2.5.2.2. The EPR Paradox

     Not all physicists, however, were prepared to accept the Copenhagen
interpretation of quantum mechanics. Foremost among them was Einstein
who relentlessly opposed it. At the outset, he believed that quantum theory
was simply incorrect by being inconsistent. But his repeatedly unsuccessful
skirmishes with Bohr eventually led him to change his critical line of
attack; he came to regard quantum theory, not as incorrect but rather as
incomplete. In some way, the whole structure of quantum mechanics,
especially as interpreted by the Copenhagen school, was completely at odds
with his thinking. Although Einstein was one of the pioneers of quantum
physics with his interpretation of photoelectric effect, in the later
development of the theory in the mid-1920s, he was no longer in the
forefront; and he did not creatively contribute much to the further progress
of quantum physics.

      Einstein expressed his disdain for indeterminism and statistical,
probabilistic character of the laws of quantum mechanics in different
contexts -- in most cases with his famous symbolic expression of
‘throwing/playing the dice.' We are familiar with his celebrated quip, “God
does not play dice.” In the following quotes from Einstein, there is
invariably the reference to the unpredictable dice. In a letter, addressed to
Max Born in 1926, he says: “Quantum mechanics is certainly imposing.
But an inner voice tells me that it is not yet the real thing. The theory says a
lot, but does not bring us any closer to the secret of the Old One [God]. I, at
any rate, am convinced that He does not throw dice.”174 In another letter to
Born, Einstein declared: “You believe in God playing dice and I in perfect
laws in the world of things existing as real objects, which I try to grasp in a
wildly speculative way.”175 In a letter, written much later to James Franck,
Einstein wrote:

       I can, if the worst comes to the worst, still realize that the
       Good Lord may have created a world in which there are no
       natural laws. In short, a chaos. But that there should be
       statistical laws with definite solutions, e.g., laws which compel
       the Good Lord to throw dice in each individual case, I find
       highly disagreeable.176

The great quantum physicist Paul Dirac who contributed much to the
development of quantum theory agrees with Einstein's view:

       I think it might turn out that ultimately Einstein will prove to
       be right, because the present form of quantum mechanics
       should not be considered as the final form. . . . I think that it is
       quite likely that at some future time we may get an improved
       quantum mechanics in which there will be a return to
       determinism and which will therefore justify the Einstein point
       of view.177

      Einstein did not think that quantum theory was incorrect, for it agreed
with experiments; but he believed that it gives an incomplete description of
physical reality, and denies objectivity and determinism in the quantum
world. He says: “I am therefore inclined to believe that the description of
quantum mechanism . . . has to be regarded as an incomplete and indirect
description of reality, to be replaced at some later date by a more complete
and direct one.”178 In another context, he strongly affirms: “I am in fact
firmly convinced that the essentially statistical character of contemporary
quantum theory is solely to be ascribed to the fact that this theory operates
with an incomplete description of physical systems.”179 Einstein's
objections were thus based on the apparent indeterminism and denial of
causality in quantum mechanics. He held on to the classical view of
determinism to the end of his life. For him, it was unthinkable that there
are arbitrariness and chance in the fundamental structure of the universe.
Einstein's vision of the eternal laws of nature that govern all physical
reality left no room for indeterminism and chance. Indeed, the Copernican
interpretation of quantum physics appeared to go against his instincts.

     In order to counter the claims of the Copenhagen interpretation,
Einstein with his colleagues Podolsky and Rosen wrote a paper in 1935,
proposing a thought experiment leading to what is often called ‘the EPR
paradox.'180 The EPR (Einstein, Podolsky, Rosen) paper expressed Einstein's
conviction that the standard Copenhagen interpretation of quantum physics
and the objectivity of quantum world are incompatible. The EPR paradox is
based on the following incompatible stipulations. Quantum mechanics
predicts that once two particles have been related to each other, they
continue to instantaneously affect each other, no matter how far they may
be separated. But we know from the theory of relativity that no signal can
travel faster than light. There is no way in which one particle can relate to
another faster than light. In other words, quantum theory requires that the
two particles affect each other even though they cannot exchange signals
fast enough to causally influence each other. This is the gist of EPR
paradox. Davies describes the EPR experiment in simple words:

       He [Einstein] conceived of an experiment in which two
       particles interact and separate to a great distance. Under these
       circumstances the quantum state of the combined system can
       be such that a measurement performed on one particle
       apparently affects the outcome of measurements performed on
       the other, distant, particle. This he found so unsettling he
       dubbed it ‘spooky action-at-a-distance'.181

The EPR experiment describes a counter-intuitive ‘togetherness-in-
separation' by which quantum entities which have once interacted with each
other retain the capacity of instantaneous, mutual influence, however far
apart they may be.

     The EPR paradox was originally formulated in terms of position and
momentum of an electron. It was later reformulated by David Bohm
employing the concept of spin. Subatomic particles like electrons have the
property called ‘spin.' If the electron spins clockwise on its axis, it is
described as spin-up; if it spins counterclockwise, it is called spin-down.
The crucial property of a spinning electron, which cannot be understood in
classical terms, is the fact that its axis of rotation cannot always be defined
with certainty; electrons merely show tendencies to spin about certain axes.
But whenever a measurement is performed for any axis of rotation, the
electron will be found to spin in one or the other direction about that axis.
In other words, the act of measurement gives the particle a definite axis of
rotation, but before the measurement is taken, it cannot generally be said to
spin about a definite axis; it merely has a certain tendency or potentiality to
do so.

     The EPR experiment involves two subatomic particles, say, electrons
which interact and then separate to a vast distance. An important aspect of
the experiment is that the distance between the two particles can be
arbitrarily large; one particle may be in New Delhi and the other on the
Moon or on a distant star. Now, suppose the two electrons are made to drift
apart by some process that does not affect their spins. They go off in
opposite directions, and when they are separated by a large distance, their
individual spins are measured. According to quantum theory, both electrons
are spinning in a limbo state - ‘up or down' -- until a measurement is made.
Suppose now that the spin of particle 1 is measured and is found to be ‘up.'
Because of the quantum correlation between the two particles, this
measurement tells us that the spin of particle 2 must be ‘down.' Thus, by
measuring the spin of particle 1 we obtain an indirect measurement of the
spin of particle 2 without in any way disturbing that particle. There is no
need for us to make a measurement of the spin of 2; it is known as soon as
we measure the spin of 1 because of the correlation existing between the
two particles. This ghostly action-at-a-distance seems to imply that the two
particles communicate by a physical effect which propagates faster than
light because there is no time for particle 2 to receive information by any
conventional signal.

     The paradoxical aspect of the EPR experiment consists in the fact that
the observer is free to choose the axis of measurement. As we saw, the
spins of two electrons about any axis exist only as tendencies or
potentialities before the measurement is taken. Once the observer has
chosen a definite axis and has performed the measurement, this act will
give both particles a definite axis of rotation. The crucial point is that we
can choose our axis of measurement at the last minute when electrons are
already far apart. At the instant we perform our measurement on particle 1,
particle 2 which may be thousands of miles away, will acquire a definite,
correlate spin along the chosen axis. Now, the question is, how does particle
2 come to know which property of spin has been chosen - ‘up' or ‘down' --
so that it can decide the correlate spin? How can these two particles
communicate so quickly? The problem is that it is impossible for particle 2
to receive such information by any conventional signal because it may be
quite far away from particle 1; and according to the theory of relativity, no
signal can travel faster than light. There is no way a sudden change in
particle 1 can cause a synchronized change in particle 2, or vice versa since
such a signal cannot instantaneously reach from the one to the other. In
other words, since no signal can travel faster than the speed of light, it is
impossible that the measurement performed on the spin of particle 1 will
instantly determine the spin of particle 2 thousands of miles away. Two
correlated particles act in harmony, even though they cannot exchange
signals fast enough to causally influence their harmonious action. This is
the crux of the EPR paradox.

     The EPR solution to the paradox was that the two particles cannot be
considered as one undivided system; and so if particle 2 measures the
correlate spin value of particle 1, it is only because the former possessed
that value before measurement. Since particle 2 could not be physically
affected by the measurement of particle 1, EPR concluded that it must have
had the definite spin value before the measurement on particle 1 was made.
Hence, it cannot be said that particle 2 acquires the correlate spin value as
soon as the spin value of particle 1 is measured. In short, the EPR team
held that both particles objectively possessed the appropriate spin values
before measurement; and thus EPR affirmed the objectivity of the quantum
world.

     However, Bohr completely disagreed with the EPR interpretation.
According to him, the two-particle system is an indivisible whole, even if
the particles are separated by a great distance. Although particle 1 is many
light years away from particle 2, they are nevertheless linked by
instantaneous, nonlocal connections. Such a two-particle system cannot be
analyzed in terms of independent parts. If two particles are correlated with
each other, they continue to instantaneously affect each other even if they
are widely separated. The measurement of the spin of particle 1 influences
the spin (up or down) of particle 2; because of the quantum correlation of
two particles, particle 2 immediately adopts the opposite spin (up or down)
of particle 1. Julian Barbour explains this correlation between the two
particles in terms of wave function: “Provided we know the original wave
function, acquired knowledge about one particle sharpens our knowledge -
instantaneously - about the other.”182 He continues:

       As I have already explained, innumerable interference
       phenomena indicate that, in some sense, the particles are,
       before any measurement is made, simultaneously present
       wherever y extends. Since there is no restriction on the
       distance between the particles, any causal effect on the second
       particle after the first has been observed would have to be
       transmitted instantaneously.183

      As we wind up our discussions on EPR paradox, we note that Einstein
firmly rejected non-local connections among quantum objects which have
once interacted. By opposing the idea of nonlocal influence, he wanted to
demonstrate that quantum mechanics is an incomplete theory. Coveney and
Highfield point out: “The contention of the EPR paradox was to show from
this analysis that quantum theory cannot support an objective description of
reality, and that it is incomplete since it involves such unphysical ‘nonlocal'
correlations.”184 In their paper on EPR paradox, Einstein and his colleagues
caustically referred to non-locality: “No reasonable definition of reality
could be expected to permit this.”185

2.5.3. The Quantum Wholeness

     Indeed, the EPR paradox has unwittingly stumbled upon an important
property of quantum objects, namely, their non-locality/ entanglement.
Davies suggests: “The lesson of EPR is that quantum systems are
fundamentally non-local. In principle, all particles that have ever interacted
belong to a single wave function - a global wave function containing a
stupendous number of correlations.”186 The phrase ‘quantum entanglement'
refers to such global interrelations of all particles.

2.5.3.1. The Quantum Non-locality
      In fact, non-locality follows from the fundamental nature and
properties of quantum particles. The quantum object characterized by
wave-particle duality which is governed by uncertainty principle, and
whose wave function collapses when a measurement is made, would have
the property of non-locality just because such an entity cannot be localized.
The non-local property of quantum objects is so strange that it was missed
at first. Most of the early applications of quantum physics were to systems
with only one particle, which did not disclose the possibility of sharing
their properties among widely separated particles. The EPR thought
experiment focused precisely on the problem of non-locality, albeit
negatively. The EPR paper is Einstein's famous statement of his rejection of
non-locality in quantum physics. By contrast, Niels Bohr, Werner
Heisenberg and the Copenhagen school strongly advocate non-local
connections among quantum objects.

     Non-locality is essentially characterized by action-at-a-distance. “This
[non-local effect] means that something affects something else that is not
within its immediate area.”187 In fact, Einstein was wont to refer to non-
locality as ‘spooky action-at-a-distance.' “In physics, action at a distance is
the concept that an object can be moved, changed, or otherwise affected
without being physically touched (as in mechanical contact) by another
object. That is, it is the nonlocal interaction of objects that are separated in
space.”188 Physicist Menas Kafatos and Robert Nadeau explain the meaning
of quantum non-locality:

       The character of physical reality as predicted by quantum
       physics was non-local, meaning that correlations between
       results would hold in spite of the fact that the regions in space
       in which they would be observed would be too distant from one
       another to allow signals traveling at the speed of light to travel
       between the two points in the time allowed.189

In non-local interaction, one body influences another without the mediation
of bodies between them. That is to say, a non-local interaction
instantaneously travels across void without any medium. Since non-local
influences act instantaneously, the speed of their transmissions is not
limited by the velocity of light. Further, nonlocal effects do not diminish
with distance. They are as powerful at one million miles distance as at one
millimeter.

     An example of non-local interaction is the so-called ‘voodoo' injury.
When a voodoo practitioner sticks a pin in his/her doll, the distant target is
(supposedly) instantly wounded, although nothing actually travels from
doll to victim. Believers in voodoo claim that an action here causes an
effect there. Coveney and Highfield compare voodoo influence with the
non-local effect in observing an electron: “The mere observation of that
electron sends out instantaneous repercussions to the farthest reaches of the
universe, with no more explanation than of how a voodooist may hurt
someone located miles away by sticking pins into a wax effigy.”190 Non-
local influences link up quantum particles at one location with other
particles at different places instantaneously without crossing space; such
interactions are unmediated, unmitigated and immediate. Polkinghorne
says: “Quantum entities exhibit a counterintuitive togetherness-in-
separation, a power once they have interacted to influence each other
however far they subsequently separate.”191 In fact, the EPR paradox is the
clearest statement of non-local influences. Robert Russell observes that
“nature reveals a highly non-local and wholistic character at the quantum
level which is strikingly different from the separability of nature in our
ordinary experience.”192 Reputed physicists like John Bell believe that there
are innumerable non-local influences in the universe. In fact, such
influences are present, not only in rare and exotic circumstances, but they
appear to lie behind all the events of everyday life. They are ubiquitous.193

     The concept of non-locality can be further clarified by comparing it
with the notion of locality which is the hallmark of the classical world of
Newton and Einstein. Bohm contrasts the quantum concept of non-locality
with the classical notion of locality:

       One finds a peculiar new property of non-locality of
       connection, i.e., a close rapport between particles or other
       elements (e.g., fields) that may be distant from each other. This
       violates the classical mechanical requirement of locality of
       connection . . . . This latter requirement is that the basic
       elements constituting the universe (whether particles or fields)
       are strongly connected, only when they are in contact in space,
       or else, infinitesimally close together.194

The property of locality requires that events happening here and now are as
a rule directly caused by events which occur immediately in nearby space
and time. Davies says: “The property of locality has to do with the fact that,
in most cases, the behavior of a physical system is determined entirely by
the forces and influences that arise in its immediate vicinity.”195 For
instance, body A affects body B locally when it either touches B, or touches
C which is in physical contact with B. However, the term ‘local' does not
necessarily mean that the two objects or events are near to each other; they
may be millions of miles part, but may have local interaction as in the case
of gravitational force.

      Obviously, locality is a fact of our every day experience of the world
around us; it refers to the way in which physical objects, such as metals,
stones, apples or people, are connected to, or interact with, one another; it
describes how information is transferred from one object to another. For
example, if some iron filings are spread on a sheet of paper which is placed
on top of a magnet, the filings will arrange themselves in a definite pattern
around the magnet. If a second magnet is brought near the first, then the
filings will be rearranged into a different pattern. Between the magnets and
the filings there is some kind of communication, a signal or a force which
defines the resultant pattern. Locality has to do with the nature of such
communication, that is, how signals are transmitted in the world around us.
Truly, the macroscopic world of our experience runs on the principle of
locality. Physicist John Barrow remarks: “The hallmark of the entire non-
quantum world is that things occurring here and now are directly caused by
events that occurred immediately nearby in space and time: this property
