we term ‘locality' to reflect the fact that it is the most local events that
exert the predominant influence upon us.”196 Until recently, physicists
believed that the universe functions strictly by means of local
communications and influences.
     Newtonian mechanics explains interactions between bodies through
local forces such as gravity. Locality is also one of the fundamental
presuppositions of Einstein's theory of relativity, and he considers it as
being an “absolutely inevitable requirement for any reasonable physical
theory.”197 Pagels comments:

       A principle of physics that he [Einstein] held even more dear
       than determinism was the principle of local causality — that
       distant events cannot instantaneously influence local objects
       without any mediation. What the EPR argument did without
       making any assumption about determinism or indeterminism
       was to show that quantum theory violated local causality. This
       finding startled most physicists, because they too held the
       principle of local causality sacred. 198

The toughest limitation to local interactions is the speed at which they can
travel. According to the theory of relativity, no physical object can travel
faster than light. Hence, the limit of local causality is determined by the
velocity of light. This is the main reason why in the EPR experiment,
Einstein strongly objected to the hypothesis of instantaneous interactions
between two correlated particles which are very distant.

     Although the EPR paradox served to heighten the debate between
Einstein and Bohr on non-local correlations among quantum objects, they
had no conclusive proof, for or against, the property of non-locality. Such a
proof was provided by Bell's inequality theorem. In his paper published in
1964, John Stuart Bell proposed a simple mathematical theorem for testing
the results of EPR experiment. Bell in fact achieved a significant
sharpening of the EPR paradox. “The essence of the original paradox is the
existence of correlations between pairs of quantities -- pairs of positions or
pairs of momenta -- that are always verified if one correlation or the other
is tested.”199 More specifically, it means that independent measurements
performed on widely separated particles which have interacted, yield
correlated results; for instance, correlations of the spin of a particle with
the spin of another particle both of which have interacted. This in itself is
not surprising because if the particles diverged from a common origin, each
will have retained an imprint of their contacts. However, what is important
is the degree of correlation among them. This was investigated by Bell.
Davies writes: “Bell showed that quantum mechanics predicts a
significantly greater degree of correlation than can possibly be accounted
for by any theory that treats the particles as independently real and subject
to locality.”200 This is the core of Bell's inequality theorem.

     Bell's procedure in simple words is the following. First, make a series
of paired measurements on two particles (in practice, photons) whose states
have been linked together by interaction in an appropriate way; and then
study the degree of correlations which exist between the results obtained
with one photon and the results obtained with the other. Second, proceed to
derive certain ‘inequalities' that impose upper limits on the degree of
correlations that the measurements of classical entities (assumed to be
local) could exhibit; tighter correlations would simply be a logical
impossibility. If quantum objects exceed this limit of the degree of
correlations, and thus break ‘Bell inequalities,' it would mean that they
would have the property of non-locality; and EPR thought experiment
would then be proved wrong. Bell demonstrated that quantum objects do
violate these inequalities, and that these objects are more tightly correlated
than any conceivable classical objects. In the words of Polkinghorne: “Bell
showed that, if the underlying theory were purely local, with no strange
effects at a distance, then there was a bound (the Bell inequality) on how
closely correlated the results could prove to be.”201 However, quantum
theory predicted the violation of this bound - the Bell inequality; and so
quantum objects would have the property of non-locality. According to
John Barrow, Bell's theorem “showed that any theory purporting to describe
the reality behind experiments like that proposed by Einstein and his
colleagues must possess non-local features if a simple arithmetical
condition is satisfied. Quantum mechanics meets the Bell conditions for
there to exist non-local interactions.”202 Barrow suggests that unlike other
theorems in physics, there is something unique to Bell's theorem: “What is
most striking, and quite unique, about Bell's proof is that it demonstrates a
necessary logical property of any theory of reality, not just a property of its
observed manifestations as do all other theorems of mathematical physics:
if your view of reality is the right one, then it must be a non-local one.”203
     Bell's theorem can be clarified with the example of a chance event,
like tossing a coin. Tossing a coin and getting head or tail is an ordinary
affair. We may get head or tail alternately, sometimes two heads or two tails
in a row. It is a chance business. Now, we can postulate that the maximum
number of getting heads in a row is five. This postulate would impose a
limit beyond which ordinarily it is not possible to obtain heads in a row.
But suppose that a person tosses a coin and gets heads that exceed this
limit, say, ten heads in a row; and also that this happens now and then. We
are inclined to think that something extra-ordinary is happening. We may
come to attribute some unique property to the coin or some magical power
to the one who tosses it. The extra-ordinary occurrences of obtaining ten
heads in a row do violate the postulate about tossing the coin. Bell's
inequality in this case would then be the postulate of getting five heads in a
row. Violation of this postulate by obtaining ten heads in a row time and
again would represent non-locality.204

     In order to test Bell's inequality theorem, the French physicist Alain
Aspect and his team in 1982 devised a decisive experiment which most
physicists now accept. Their experiment showed that the degree of
correlations of quantum objects actually violated Bell's inequality theorem.
Aspect's experiment thus confirmed non-local connections over distances
up to twenty six meters. This may not appear a great distance, but it is to be
noted that it is 1022 times greater than atomic domain. The experiment
contradicted theories that assume locality, and it confirmed non-locality in
the quantum world. Hence, if Aspect's experiments showed that quantum
objects obey Bell's theorem, Einstein and EPR experiment would be proved
right; but if these objects violated the theorem, Einstein would then be
proved wrong. Aspect's experiment demonstrated the latter alternative.
After Aspects' experiments, several investigators have checked Bell's
theorem experimentally, and the results have been conclusive: quantum
particles interact non-locally. Once two particles have interacted with one
another, they remain linked in some way as parts of the same indivisible
system. Indeed, the quantum world is characterized by non-locality. This
means that if two quantum objects, such as two photons, have interacted,
we cannot separate the description of the properties of one of them from
the description of the properties of the other. Incidentally, Aspect's
experiment “suggests that the space-time of quantum physics is remarkably
different from that of Einstein's relativity [and certainly that of Newton's
mechanics.]”205 This distinguishing mark of spacetime consists in the fact
that “there is no here and there or that here is identical to there. This gives
space and time a property that is called ‘nonlocality.'”206

2.5.3.2. The Quantum Entanglement

     ‘The Quantum Entanglement' is in fact a continuation of, and
complement to, ‘The Quantum Non-locality.' Apparently, both phrases
mean the same. However, it is appropriate to make a distinction:
entanglement is the quantum reality of which nonlocality is the property.207
Physicists ordinarily use the term ‘nonlocality' when they discuss EPR
paradox, Bell's theorem and Aspect's experiment. But the phrase, ‘quantum
entanglement' is used in a global context and in a broader sense than
‘quantum non-locality.' Quantum entanglement has to do with the fact that
all quantum particles which have once interacted belong to a universal
wave function containing innumerable correlations. Quantum entanglement
is thus an all-inclusive quantum phenomenon. “Quantum entanglement is a
physical phenomenon that occurs when a pair or group of particles is
generated, interact, or share spatial proximity in a way such that the
quantum state of each particle of the pair or group cannot be described
independently of the state of the others, including when the particles are
separated by a large distance.”208 Schrödinger testifies about the reality of
quantum entanglement when he says:

       When two systems of which we know the states by their
       respective representatives [wave functions], enter into
       temporary physical interaction due to known forces between
       them, and when after a time of mutual influence the systems
       separate again, then they can no longer be described in the
       same way as before, viz, by endowing each with a
       representative of its own. . . . By the interaction the two
       representatives have become entangled.209
     Great physicists believe that quantum entanglement represents an
essential characteristic of the subatomic world. According to particle
physicists like David Bohm, non-locality is not to be understood merely as
an entanglement between systems of two paired quantum particles. Rather,
it has to be envisaged at a much deeper level, and broader context. That is
to say, quantum entanglement/non-locality opens up a fundamental
dimension in the fabric of quantum reality. Herbert observes:

       What Bell's theorem does do for the quantum reality question
       is to clearly specify one of deep reality's necessary features:
       whatever reality may be, it must be non-local. Since Clauser's
       [and also Aspect's] experimental verification of Bell's theorem,
       we know that any correct model of reality has to incorporate
       explicitly non-local connections. No local reality can explain
       the type of world we live in.210

Schrödinger ascribes so much importance to quantum entanglement that he
calls it “THE characteristic trait of quantum mechanics, the one that
enforces its entire departure from classical ideas of thought.”211 Likewise,
Capra points out: “These nonlocal connections are seen by some physicists
as the very essence of quantum reality.”212 Smolin voices a similar opinion:
“The results of the experiment performed by Aspect and his colleagues
demonstrated that locality is not a principle that is respected by nature.
This means that the entangled nature of the quantum state reflects
something essential in the world.”213

     Now, what are these essential dimensions of the quantum world that
quantum entanglement discloses? First and foremost, it reveals the
wholeness of correlated particle systems. Smolin says: “Quantum theory
says quite generally that whenever two systems have interacted, their
description is tied together in this way no matter how far apart they may be.
This is called the wholeness or the entanglement of the quantum
description.”214 Polkinghorne speaks in a similar vein: “Quantum states
exhibit a surprisingly integrationist view of the relationship of systems
which have once interacted with each other, however widely they may
subsequently separate.”215 This means that an entangled system of
subatomic particles constitutes, and behaves as, one integrated whole.
Hence, the designations, ‘quantum wholeness' and ‘holism' (alternately
spelled ‘wholism') are employed to refer to such systems. These usages
mean that nonlocal influences render correlated multi-particle systems
holistic, that is, organize them into wholes. “Even physicists like Planck
and Einstein, who were greatly discomforted by the prospect of quantum
non-locality, completely understood and embraced holism as an
inescapable condition of our physical existence.”216 However, such holism
is different from the kind of wholeness implied in Newtonian mechanics.
Herbert explains the difference between the two:

       Quantum wholeness is no mere replay of the old so that
       everything is connected to everything else, no twentieth-
       century echo, for instance, of Newton's insight that gravity
       links each particle to every other. . . . Quantum wholeness, on
       the other hand, is a fundamentally new kind of togetherness,
       undiminished by spatial and temporal separation. No casual
       hookup, this new quantum thing, but a true mingling of distant
       beings that reaches across the galaxy as forcefully as it reaches
       across the garden.217

     Moreover, quantum entanglement discloses another essential
characteristic of the quantum world, namely, its inter-connectedness. This
means that the whole which quantum entanglement constitutes, is not made
up of isolated bits and pieces of matter. It does not consist of mere
additions of material particles externally related. Rather these particles are
all intimately related in such a way that their very existence consists in
their relationships with one another. Heisenberg affirms: “The world thus
appears as a complicated tissue of events, in which connections of different
kinds alternate or overlap or combine and thereby determine the texture of
the whole.”218 Zukav remarks about the inter-relations of parts implied by
Bell's theorem: “One of the implications of Bell's theorem is that, at a deep
and fundamental level, the ‘separate parts' of the universe are connected in
an intimate and immediate way.”219 According to Stapp, a particle itself is
constituted by such relationships: “An elementary particle is not an
independently existing unanalyzable entity. It is, in essence, a set of
relationships that reach outward to other things.”220

     Furthermore, such inter-relatedness of particles refers back to
quantum wholeness. The universe itself is believed to be a whole of
interacting relationships. Davies and Gribbin argue:

       We can think of the Universe as a vast network of interacting
       particles, and each linkage binds the participating particles into
       a single quantum system. In some sense the entire Universe
       can be regarded as a single quantum system. Although in
       practice the complexity of the cosmos is too great for us to
       notice this subtle connectivity except in special experiments
       like those devised by Aspect, nevertheless there is a strong
       holistic flavour to the quantum description of the Universe.221

Kafatos and Nadeau share a similar view:

       Since all quanta that interact with one another in a single
       quantum state remain in some sense a part of a ‘single' system,
       this suggests that a universe in which quanta are entangled at
       every level is and remains a single quantum whole. As
       physicists David Bohm and Bernard d'Espagnat suggest, non-
       locality appears to have been an inherent property of the
       universe throughout its history. 222

Indeed, quantum entanglement discloses the essential properties of the
universe - its inter-connectedness and holistic character; it is an inter-
related whole.

     Instances of quantum entanglement abound in the quantum world.
Consider for example, the jump of an electron from one atomic orbit to
another or the decay of a subatomic particle; these may occur without any
single event causing it. We can never foretell when and how such
phenomena will occur; we can only predict the probability of their
happening. This does not mean that atomic events take place in a
completely arbitrary manner; rather it implies that they are not caused by
local influences. It further indicates that the behaviour of any part is
determined by its non-local connections to the whole system. Physicists do
not know these connections precisely, and so they replace the classical
notion of cause and effect with the wider concept of statistical causality.
Hence, according to quantum theory, individual events do not always have
well-defined causes. For, the laws of quantum mechanics are statistical
laws which state that the probabilities of subatomic events are determined
by the dynamics of the whole system. But in the everyday macroscopic
world, nonlocal connections are relatively unimportant, and so we can
speak of separate objects and formulate laws describing their behaviour in
terms of certain causes. Capra aptly states: “But as we go to smaller
dimensions, the influence of nonlocal connections becomes stronger, the
certainties give way to probabilities, and it becomes more and more
difficult to separate any part of the universe from the whole.”223

     However, there is a problem regarding such a conception of
wholeness: that we do not have a holistic experience or knowledge of
things around us; we are not aware of them as wholes. Rather, we find that
things of our everyday life are not intrinsically related wholes although
they may be externally connected. Then, how are we to think of these
unrelated things as wholes? Niels Bohr answers: “Isolated material
particles are abstractions, their properties being definable and observable
only through their interaction with other systems.”224 This means that we
ordinarily think of physical objects and particles by isolating and
abstracting them from the whole of which they are parts. However, as
Bohm and Hiley suggest: “Rather, we say that inseparable quantum
interconnectedness of the whole universe is the fundamental reality, and
that relatively independently behaving parts are merely particular and
contingent forms within this whole.”225 Kafatos and Nadeau go so far as to
say that unrelated, individual objects are mere illusions: “Holism in this
new sense means that the universe on the most fundamental level is an
undissectable whole, and that discreteness of objects must be, in some
sense, a macro-level illusion.”226 Quantum entanglement thus demonstrates
that we cannot decompose the world into independently existing smallest
units, as in classical, Newtonian physics. Bohm and Hiley think that
quantum physics accomplishes a reversal of the classical view regarding
the relation between parts and the whole. They argue:

       One is led to a new notion of unbroken wholeness which denies
       the classical idea of analyzability of the world into separately
       and independently existing parts. . . . We have reversed the
       usual classical notion that the independent ‘elementary parts'
       of the world are the fundamental reality, and that the various
       systems are merely particular contingent forms and
       arrangements of these parts.227

Thus, the phenomenon of quantum entanglement shows that the universe is
not merely a collection of independent physical objects and particles, but
rather an intricate web of relations between the various parts of a unified
whole.

      Nevertheless, there is a basic limitation to the concept of wholeness
implied in quantum entanglement. In a sense, it is abstract and intangible.
This is in fact another aspect of the problem regarding wholeness we have
just discussed. Kafatos and Nadeau spell out:

       Although results of experiments testing Bell's theorem ‘infer'
       wholeness in so far as they show that the conditions for these
       experiments ‘imply' the existence of an unanalyzable and
       undissectable whole, the abstract theory that helps us to
       coordinate the concrete results cannot ‘in principle' disclose
       this wholeness. Since the abstract theory can deal only in
       complementary aspects of the complete reality disclosed in the
       act of measurement, that reality is not itself, in fact or in
       principle, disclosed. . . . The distinction we are trying to make
       here is, in our view, a critical one. On the one hand, nonlocality
       as a fact of nature clearly ‘infers' that wholeness is a
       fundamental aspect of nature. Yet, very importantly, neither
       theory nor experiment can ‘disclose' and, thereby, ‘prove' the
       existence of this indivisible and undissectable whole.228
This is in contrast to the classical scenario. According to classical physics,
the nature of the whole can be inferred from the analysis of its parts.
Kafatos and Nadeau refer to a reversal of positions from classical
mechanics to quantum physics:

       That belief [the classical belief in locality] allowed one to
       assume that the whole could be described as the sum of its
       parts, and thus the ultimate extension of theory in the
       description of all the parts would ‘be' the whole. To put it
       differently, it was presumed that reductionism was valid, and,
       therefore, that one could analyze the whole into parts and
       deduce the nature of the whole from its parts. With the
       discovery of non-locality that picture is reversed; it is the
       whole which discloses ultimately the identity of the parts.229

      All the same, the fact remains that quantum entanglement co-exists
with local influences among elementary particles and physical objects, and
that these influences can only be transferred locally. It is also true that it is
impossible for energy to be transferred, or information to be
communicated, faster than light as presumed in EPR paradox.
Consequently, one cannot actually use quantum entanglement to transport
energy or to transmit information. Nevertheless, the situation is very
surprising. Once two or more quantum particles have interacted, we cannot
separate any description of the properties of the one from the properties of
the other. Smolin points out the extent of the influence of quantum
entanglement: “Given any one electron, its properties are entangled with
those of every particle it has interacted with, from the moment of its
creation, indeed quite possibly from the moment of the creation of our
universe.”230

     We conclude with a quote from Kafatos and Nadeau about the
profound impact of non-locality/quantum entanglement on our ordinary
understanding of reality:

       But no previous discovery has posed more challenges to our
       sense of everyday reality than the discovery that non-locality is
       a fact of nature. Non-locality is a shocking discovery because it
       appears to subvert the bias that the world is composed most
       fundamentally of individual objects and their non-relational
       properties.231

2.5.4. Quantum Reality as Potentiality

     Indeed, the strange, weird concepts of quantum mechanics, like wave-
particle duality, uncertainty principle, wave function and non-locality, have
left great physicists like Einstein, Bohr and Heisenberg in two opposite
camps regarding the nature of quantum reality. Many other scientists as
well as ordinary mortals like us are aghast, and are in a state of utter
confusion and consternation. The Copenhagenists, we saw, apparently hold
on to a sort of skepticism (or idealism?) about the knowledge of the
subatomic world. However, the EPR view does not go so far, but affirms
only that quantum mechanics is an incomplete theory because it does not
provide us an objective knowledge of quantum reality. Einstein and his
team defended robust realism in quantum physics; that is, it has to give us a
comprehension of the quantum world. In other words, it must provide us
knowledge of microworld as it is in itself. Bell's theorem which affirms
non-locality of particles sheds some light on the reality of the subatomic
world. “Those who would deny any deep reality were put on the defensive
by the proof of Bell's theorem, however, so that most now do affirm an
objective reality on a global scale, distinct though not separate from the
cognizing observer.”232

     In the present context, we attempt at an understanding of the nature of
quantum reality by reflecting on the notion of wave function. Let us recall
our narrative on this concept.233 It is the foundational postulate in quantum
theory. It describes physical reality at the most fundamental level. The
wave function is a complete description of physical reality at that level.
Quantum physics teaches that every physical entity has a wave function
associated with it. It describes all the possibilities of a quantum object
before it interacts with an observing system. The wave function does not
signify any actual state of a particle, but a superposition of all its possible
states, like its character of wave-particle duality, and properties, such as
position and momentum, energy and time and so on.
       In between acts of measurement a quantum mechanical system
       is represented by a smoothly evolving wave function, which is
       an expression of potentiality (that is to say, it contains within
       itself the possibility of various outcomes), rather than the
       specification of actuality (which would imply a precise and
       pictureable representation of where the system is and what it is
       doing).234

      Thus, wave function describes the potentialities of a quantum object.
It is not directly related to the actualities of the system. We can speak about
the actuality of wave function only in the context of measuring/observing
the system. That is to say, according to the Copenhagen interpretation,
when a subatomic particle is observed, its wave function collapses, and one
of its possibilities/potentialities achieves actuality.235

     These reflections on wave function throw some light on the nature of
quantum reality. It is all about possibilities and potentialities. Wave
function does not represent any actual state of quantum reality, but it
describes the superposition of all its possibilities. Reputed scientists have
supported this view. David Bohm has this to say:

       This wave function is not directly related to the actual
       properties of an individual object, event, or process. Rather, it
       has to be thought of as a description of the potentialities within
       the physical situation. Different and generally mutually
       incompatible potentialities (e.g., for wavelike or particle-like
       behaviour) are actualized in different experimental
       arrangements (so that the wave-particle duality can be
       understood as one of the main forms for the expression of such
       incompatible potentialities).236

Zukav expresses it differently: “The wave function, according to this view,
is not quite a thing yet it is more than an idea. It occupies that strange
middle ground between idea and reality, where all things are possible, but
none are actual. Heisenberg likened it to Aristotle's potentia.”237 Thus in
quantum physics, the wave function serves as the matrix of all potentiality.
      The great physicist Heisenberg is a staunch proponent of this view.
Polkinghorne comments: “Heisenberg encouraged the thought of the
wavefunction as being the carrier of potentiality (the superposition of a
variety of outcomes) in contrast to its representing the instantiation of a
definite state of affairs.”238 Heisenberg has expressed this position umpteen
times in different contexts. He clearly states: “In the experiments about
atomic events we have to do with things and facts, with phenomena that are
just as real as any phenomena in daily life. But the atoms or the elementary
particles themselves are not as real; they form a world of potentialities or
possibilities rather than one of things or facts.”239 Heisenberg cautioned
that “the properties of quantum systems are real, but only as potentialities,
until measurement actualized them.”240 According to him, “unmeasured
quantum attributes are just, what they are, objective though unrealized
possibilities.”241 Polkinghorne remarks: “Heisenberg believed that quantum
entities do not possess precise positions and momenta but only
potentialities for these properties, which then only become actual when this
is forced by an act of measurement.”242 Referring to the central role
accorded to potentiality in quantum physics, Heisenberg says that it is
indeed “the restoration of the concept of potentiality.”243 He explains the
meaning of potentiality: “It meant a tendency for something. It was a
quantitative version of the old concept of ‘potentia' in Aristotelian
philosophy.”244 In the middle ages the idea of potentiality referred to the
tendency of an entity to develop in a particular way. Heisenberg does not
accept such an Aristotelian interpretation of potentiality as a striving to
attain a future purpose, but he does suggest that the possibilities described
by wave function refer to tendencies in nature.

      Now, all these views are in stark contrast with classical positions.
Classical physics, both Newtonian mechanics and Einstein's theory of
relativity, do not deal with merely possible objects, but with actual entities.
These systems are concerned with actually existing/ moving physical
objects which occupy definite space at a definite time. Potentiality has no
important role to play in classical systems. According to Bohm, classical
physics “has no place in it to give the notion of potentiality such a
fundamental role.”245 Quantum physics replaces actual entities of classical
physics with possible objects. Polkinghorne opines:
       In contrast to the mechanistic view and also our common
       sense, according to quantum theory, material reality does not
       exist with certainty at definite places, with definite velocity,
       but it manifests itself as ‘tendencies to exist'; and atomic
       events do not occur with certainty at definite times and in
       definite ways, but rather show ‘tendencies to occur.' These
       tendencies are expressed as probabilities; they are not ‘real'
       three dimensional objects.246

Capra interprets the concept of potentialities as ‘tendencies to exist' in
terms of probabilities: “In the formalism of quantum theory, these
tendencies [to occur] are expressed as probabilities and are associated with
mathematical quantities which take the form of waves.”247 Thus, according
to quantum mechanics, at the fundamental level, physical reality has to be
thought of as potentialities, not as actualities. Wave function which
describes the state of quantum objects represents an ocean of possibilities
or potentialities; and these are interpreted as tendencies to occur.

      We conclude the present chapter, “An Overview of Quantum Physics”
with an excellent quotation from the renowned American philosopher and
psychologist William James on the status of potentiality or possibility in
relation to actuality in the totality of reality:

       It [quantum indeterminism] admits that possibilities may be in
       excess of actualities, and that things not yet revealed to our
       knowledge may really be ambiguous. Of two alternative
       futures which we conceive, both may now be really possible;
       and the one becomes impossible only at the very moment when
       the other excludes it by becoming real itself. Indeterminism
       thus denies the world to be one unbending unit and fact. . . . To
       that view, actualities seem to float in a wider sea of
       possibilities from out of which they are chosen; and,
       somewhere, indeterminism says, such possibilities exist, and
       form a part of truth. Determinism, on the contrary, says they
       exist nowhere, and that necessity on the one hand and
       impossibility on the other are the sole categories of the real.
       Possibilities that fail to be realized are, for determinism, pure
       illusions: they never were possibilities at all. There is nothing
       inchoate, it says about the universe of ours, all that was or is or
       shall be actual in it having been from eternity virtually there.
       248



Indeed, the quantum physics confers priority to potentiality over actuality
in contrast to classical mechanics which accords predominance to actuality
rather than to potentiality.
                        3
            THE QUANTUM FIELD THEORIES
       We may therefore regard matter as being constituted by the
       regions of space in which the field is extremely intense. . . .
       There is no place in this new kind of physics both for the field
       and matter, for the field is the only reality.

              –Albert Einstein, Quoted by Louis de Broglie,
                Nouvelles Perspectives en Microphysique

      The conceptual framework of quantum mechanics we have outlined
in the previous chapter is in fact incomplete just because it does not
incorporate one of the most important concepts of physics, namely,
gravitation. Contrariwise, Einstein's general theory of relativity which is a
theory of gravitation is also incomplete since it does not integrate the
domains of quantum world. Thus, the two great systems of contemporary
physics are unfinished for opposite reasons. Though these theories are
separately applicable to different realms of physical reality, each cannot be
individually applied to the totality of physical existence. In contrast, the
past systems of physics were complete and comprehensive. For instance,
Newton's mechanics was a complete theory in so far as it had explanation
for the whole range of physical reality. Newtonian theory was overthrown
and was replaced, not by one new theory, but by two, namely, the theory of
relativity and quantum physics; and both of these theories are in fact
incomplete. Hence, the revolution which began with the inventions of the
theory of relativity and quantum physics would be complete only with
their unification into a single theory that would give us a comprehensive
picture of the whole range of physical reality. Such is the rationale for
quantum field theories which endeavour to unify the theory of relativity
and quantum mechanics.

3.1. Towards Quantum Field Theories
     As an initial attempt at incorporating the two great systems of
physics, quantum physicists have invented a mathematical framework
called ‘relativistic quantum field theory.' This theory tries to integrate the
special theory of relativity with quantum physics. The fundamental
concept in quantum field theory is ‘field' itself. Although ‘field' is an old
concept in physics, it has undergone radical transformations at the hands
of great physicists; they have invented the idea of ‘quantum field.' This
field is supposedly the ‘mother' of all particles.

3.1.1. The Unification of Relativity and Quantum Theory

      The theory of relativity and quantum mechanics provide the basic
conceptual frameworks for investigating and comprehending physical
reality at the biggest and smallest dimensions.1 The general theory of
relativity investigates the megacosm, that is, regions of the universe which
are both gigantic in size and mass, like clusters of galaxies, galaxies and
different types of stars. It describes the largescale structures and behaviour
of the universe on scales as large as 93 billion light years which is
supposedly the diameter of the observable universe. The general theory is
employed in astronomy and cosmology with great success, even to explain
the origin of the universe. Quantum mechanics, on the other hand, deals
with the microcosm, that is, the domains of the universe, which are both
extremely small in size and mass, such as atoms and subatomic particles.
It explains the structure and behaviour of these quantum objects at
exceedingly minute scales, as small as one millionth of a millionth of
centimeter. Quantum theory has been extremely successful in the
prediction of quantum phenomena, and also applications in technology.
Thus, the general theory of relativity and quantum mechanics are
thoroughly proven to be unbeatable in their respective fields of
application. In the two extremely different dimensions of the universe,
namely, the biggest and the smallest, these theories are valid and are very
successful. Since the two theories are applicable to very different domains,
most situations require that only one of them be employed.

     However, there are dimensions of physical reality, such as Big Bang
regime and Planck magnitudes (Planck time of 10-43 sec, Planck length of
10-33 cm and Planck density of 1094 gm/cc), where the combined
application of both the theory of relativity and quantum mechanics is
called for. These are domains of space-time which are extremely minute,
yet with incredible gravity; the curvature of space-time would be
enormous, and quantum effects dominate the structure of space-time.
Hence, for a proper comprehension of physical reality at the Planck scales,
the simultaneous application of the general theory of relativity and
quantum mechanics is required. Unfortunately, these two theories are
inconsistent with each other. Nobel Laureate Steven Weinberg aptly says:

       Quantum mechanics without relativity would allow us to
       conceive of a great many possible physical systems. . . .
       However, when you put quantum mechanics together with
       relativity, you find that it is nearly impossible to conceive of
       any possible physical system at all. Nature somehow manages
       to be both relativistic and quantum-mechanical; but these two
       requirements restrict it so much that it has only a limited
       choice of how to be — hopefully a very limited choice.2

      In particular, the general theory of relativity and quantum mechanics
are apparently incompatible. The main difficulty is that the general theory
is a ‘classical' theory; that is to say, it describes a universe that is causally
determined. By contrast, at the heart of quantum physics lies Heisenberg's
uncertainty principle, that is, radical indeterminism. Greene says: “On
ultramicroscopic scales, the central feature of quantum mechanics — the
uncertainty principle — is in direct conflict with the central feature of
general relativity — the smooth geometrical model of space (and of
spacetime).”3 But without a unification of these two great theories of
physics, our knowledge of the universe would remain fragmentary. Greene
continues:

       And surely, regardless of how central this incompatibility is to
       their own research, most physicists find it hard to believe that,
       at rock bottom, our deepest theoretical understanding of the
       universe will be composed of a mathematically inconsistent
       patchwork of two powerful yet conflicting explanatory
       frameworks.4

     But the difficulty is, how to accomplish such unification between
these two great theories. Pagels says: “The problem is how to modify this
theory [general theory] in some way so that one does not lose the
successful predictions of large-scale gravity while at the same time
resolving the puzzles of short-distance quantum gravity and unifying
gravity with the other forces of nature.”5 In spite of the hard work of some
of the world's greatest physicists, the general theory and quantum physics
stubbornly resist to be integrated, but continue to co-exist in incompatible
ways. However, these two systems of physics have to be unified in order to
achieve a complete knowledge of the universe. Hawking tells us: “Now, if
you believe that the universe is not arbitrary, but is governed by definite
laws, you ultimately have to combine the partial theories into a complete
unified theory that will describe everything in the universe.”6 As an initial
attempt at such a unification of the two systems, physicists have invented
a highly mathematical framework called ‘relativistic quantum field
theory.' This theory endeavours to unify the special theory of relativity and
quantum physics. Smolin observes: “And because Einstein's special theory
of relativity is the right setting for electromagnetism, these theories can
also be seen as unifications of quantum theory with special relativity.”7

      Quantum physicists have found that the concept of field provides the
key to the unification of the theory of relativity and quantum mechanics.
The idea of field was proposed by James Clerk Maxwell in his theory of
electromagnetism, and by Einstein in his theory of relativity. Maxwell
invented equations describing electric and magnetic fields; and he also put
forward some ideas about field. However, Maxwell was ambivalent as to
whether the electric and magnetic fields needed the medium of ‘ether' that
was believed to pervade all space. But Einstein in his 1905 paper on the
special theory of relativity showed that ether was a superfluous concept.
Electromagnetic fields require no medium in which they propagate, and in
this sense fields are fundamental and irreducible entities. Major steps
towards the synthesis of quantum theory and the special theory of
relativity were taken by Max Born, Heisenberg, Pascual Jordan in the
years 1926-28, and by Eugene Wigner and Wolfgang Pauli in 1929-30.
Paul Dirac made important contributions towards the development of
quantum field theory in the 1930s. These physicists showed that each
different field, such as the electromagnetic field and gravitational field,
has an associated particle. Fields are the fundamental entities which are
manifested as particles. This is the basic idea of quantum field theory
which banished forever the old idea that particles and fields are separate
entities. According to quantum field theories, the universe is a vast arena
of interacting fields manifested as quantum particles interacting with each
other.

     Quantum field theories interpret the four fundamental forces of
nature (gravitational, electromagnetic, strong nuclear and weak nuclear
forces) as particle interactions mediated by virtual particles called ‘gauge
bosons' which are ‘exchange particles' or ‘forcecarriers.' Each of these
four interactions has an associated exchange particle. Thus, the gauge
boson associated with electromagnetic interaction is photon; the
electromagnetic interaction between two charged particles is mediated by
the exchange of photons. It is the first of such quantum field theories,
called ‘quantum electrodynamics' (QED). Then there is the electroweak
theory which is the unification of electromagnetism and weak interaction.
theory of strong nuclear interaction is called ‘quantum chromodynamics'
(QCD). Gravitational interaction does not enter into the picture for reasons
which will be explained later.8 Relativistic quantum field theories
represent the culmination of decades of scientific research, and so far they
have produced remarkable results. The fundamental laws of these theories
were in place by the 1930s. Some of their basic tenets have been
challenged, but never overthrown.9

3.1.2. The Quantum Field

    In the broadest sense of the term, field can be identified with space;
in this sense, field is where something exists, and through which
something moves. In general, field is something that exists throughout
space and time, as opposed to a particle that exists at only one point in
space at a time. “A field is something that exists at each point of space.”10
From a macroscopic perspective, it is the medium by which a force
communicates its influence. In the context of quantum field theories, field
refers to the influence of some unseen agent, considered as existing at all
points in space and exerting a force on objects placed at any point in
space. “Fields are nonmaterial regions of influence.”11 Electrical field,
gravitational field and magnetic field are some examples of fields in this
sense. Iron filings align themselves under magnetic field, like a herd of
sheep group themselves hearing the voice of a shepherd. Thus, field is the
means by which a force communicates its influence. It is the medium of
‘action-at-a-distance;' physical objects can affect each other through fields
even though they are not in physical contact. Fields are often contrasted
with material particles and their composites. Whereas these objects are
localized in some definite region of space, fields are considered to be
continuously distributed throughout space as a whole. A field, like a wave,
is spread out over a much larger area than a particle; in contrast, a particle
is restricted to one small region in space. Moreover, a field completely
fills a given space. Fields are known through their physical effects, even
though they cannot be usually detected directly by our senses.

     The most familiar examples of fields are gravitational and
electromagnetic fields which make their effects felt in our everyday life.
They are invisible, but they produce physical objects. The Earth's
gravitational field is all around us. We cannot perceive it; in that sense it is
not a material object, but it is nevertheless real. The gravitational field of
the Earth causes things to fall down to the ground. It is holding us down to
the Earth at this moment; without it we would be floating. The Moon
orbits the Earth because the Earth's gravitational field interacts with that
of the Moon. Massive bodies produce gravitational fields, that is, space-
time curvature, in the language of the general of theory relativity. Another
familiar field is electromagnetic field. In contrast to gravitational field,
electromagnetic fields are felt only by charged particles, and they give rise
to both attraction and repulsion among them. Electromagnetic fields are
integral to the organization of all material systems, such as atoms,
molecules and bigger physical objects, both organic and inorganic. All
around us there are countless electromagnetic fields which cannot be
perceived by our senses. The spatial structure of the field around an iron
magnet, for instance, is invisible in itself, but its effects are felt by
sprinkling iron filings around it. The electromagnetic field, like
gravitational field, is continuous, and unlike a material object, it cannot be
cut up into separate parts. If a magnet is cut into half, each half does not
merely possess half of the original field, abruptly ending where the
magnet is cut; rather, each piece becomes a whole magnet surrounded by a
complete magnetic field.

     The story of the concept of field in modern physics is fascinating
indeed. It starts with Newton's notion of field -- the gravitational field.
According to his mechanics, motions of physical objects are caused by the
force of gravity of these objects themselves. In this view, there is no
continuous field of gravity spread throughout space between objects.
Space is empty, and across this empty space, gravity influences
neighbouring objects as ‘action-at-a-distance.' The force of gravity is
transmitted instantaneously from one body to another. Thus in Newtonian
mechanics, mass or material body is separate from gravitational field. In
this view, fields and objects are completely independent of each other.
Gravitational field is believed to be produced in some unexplained way by
physical objects acting at a distance causing these objects to attract each
other. Newton thought of gravitation as a hidden force for which he had no
explanation.

     Modern field theories have transformed Newton's concept of
gravitational field profoundly resulting in a new idea of field, by the same
token changing also his notion of particle or physical object. Two such
archetypal concepts of fields are James Maxwell's electromagnetic field
and Einstein's metric field of gravitation to which we have already
referred. Philosopher Errol Harris summarizes the transformations of the
concept of field brought about by the electromagnetic theory of Maxwell
and Einstein's theory of relativity:

       The theory of relativity gave precedence to the concept of the
       field over that of the particle. This development also had been
       anticipated in the nineteenth century by the theory of
       electrodynamics due to J. Clerk Maxwell. The
       electromagnetic field in principle fills the whole of space, and
       in accordance with the general theory of relativity, space-time
       itself is regarded as the metrical field from which the
       measured quantities are inseparable, and in which the
       elements that have them are inextricably embedded. Energy
       and mass thus become degrees of curvature in space-time, and
       matter, having been equated with energy in Einstein's equation
       ‘E = mc2' is a singularity in space-time.12

     The modern concept of field was first invoked in physics by Michael
Faraday and James Maxwell in the nineteenth century in their
electromagnetic theory. In the 1840s, Faraday initially proposed an idea
similar to field, namely, ‘lines of force' as the means by which a force is
transmitted across space. In his investigations of electrical phenomena, he
employed this concept of ‘lines of force' around a charged particle in order
to explain how electrical force could be communicated through empty
space from one body to another. Harris explains: “A charged particle or a
magnet is surrounded by a configuration of lines of force along which a
free body subject to electrical or magnetic forces will be accelerated in the
direction in which the force acts. This configuration is known as the field
of force of the particle or the magnet.”13 The lines of force tend to pull
opposite charges together, and keep apart similarly charged particles.
Maxwell elaborated this concept into the first field theory in order to
describe electricity and magnetism. Central to Maxwell's theory was the
introduction of a profound and far-reaching new concept in physics -- the
field. Thus, Faraday and Maxwell replaced the classical concept of force
by that of field, and in doing so they went beyond Newtonian physics.
Instead of interpreting the interaction between positive and negative
electric charges as attraction/ repulsion between these two charges like the
gravitational attraction of two masses in Newtonian mechanics, Faraday
and Maxwell suggested that each charge creates a ‘disturbance' or a
‘condition' in the space around it; and the other charge, when it is present,
‘feels' a force. This condition in space is what is called a field. It is created
by a single charge, and it exists whether or not another charge is brought
in to feel its effect. This idea of field was the most profound
transformation in the conception of physical reality since the time of
classical physics. Thus, Faraday and Maxwell replaced the concept of
force like electromagnetic force by the much subtler notion of field.

     Maxwell thought of charged particles as being surrounded by their
electromagnetic fields like an invisible halo; their presence was quite
undetectable except when other charged particles were placed in their
fields. The action of the field was to exert a force upon charged particles.
Maxwell thus employed the concept of field for describing the forces
between electric charges. Electric fields are created by charged bodies and
their effects can only be felt by other charged bodies. An electric field
then is a condition in the space around a charged body, which produces a
force on other charged bodies in that space. When electric charges are set
in motion, they produce magnetic fields; that is, an electric current creates
a magnetic field. Conversely, a moving magnetic field generates electrical
forces, and produces a current flow if there is a circuit. Evidently, electric
and magnetic fields are not independent entities and are not separate.
Maxwell realized that these discoveries pointed to a unification of
electricity and magnetism. To fully unify them, he had to change his
equations. He did so simply by adding one term, and this unification
became the model for further unifications of other fields. Maxwell
combined together the results of experimental work already carried out by
Faraday and other physicists into a unified set of mathematical equations,
known as Maxwell's equations; these equations beautifully and correctly
described the interplay between the motion of charged particles and the
behaviour of electromagnetic field. Field was believed to be the means by
which electromagnetic forces were transmitted across space. The new
equations allowed electric and magnetic fields to turn into each other.

     Another classical field theory is Einstein's theory of relativity, in
particular, the general theory of relativity proposed in 1916. The general
theory is first and foremost a field theory. As we know, Einstein started his
project of unification with the concept of space-time in the special theory
of relativity; space is unified with time. However, in the special theory,
space-time and mass/energy remain separate entities. If all mass/energy in
the universe were removed, absolute space-time would remain as empty
background. Hence, space-time had to be unified with mass/energy.
Einstein accomplished this feat in his general theory of relativity which is
primarily a theory of gravity. According to Newton, gravitation is a force
of attraction between bodies acting at a distance. But in the general theory,
gravitation is neither a force nor an attraction; it is the ‘warping' or
‘curvature' of space-time in the presence of mass/energy. “Again, the
space around the object is ‘conditioned' in such a way that another object
will feel a force, but this time the conditioning affects the geometry, and
thus the very structure of space.”14 Gravitation is a geometrical property of
space-time. That is to say, the geometry of space-time is determined by the
distribution of mass/energy. Thus, the general theory equated gravitational
field with space-time curvature in the presence of matter. They are not
separate. Capra explains:

       Wherever there is a massive body, there will also be a
       gravitational field, and this field will manifest itself as the
       curvature of the space surrounding that body. We must not
       think, however, that the field fills the space and ‘curves' it.
       The two cannot be distinguished; the field is the curved space!
       In general relativity, the gravitational field and the structure
       — or geometry — of space are identical. They are represented
       in Einstein's field equations by one and the same
       mathematical quantity. In Einstein's theory, then, matter
       cannot be separated from its field of gravity, and the field of
       gravity cannot be separated from the curved space. Matter and
       space are thus seen to be inseparable and interdependent parts
       of a single whole.15

     Likewise, philosopher Milton Munitz observes :

       Apart from the field, then, there is nothing, and contrary even
       to special relativity, not even ‘empty space.' In this sense the
       field in Einstein's view replaces as unitary conception both
       matter (whether ponderable or imponderable) and space. It
       emerges as the true absolute, being indeed not only
       independent of any other physical existence, but in fact all-
       embracing. On the other hand, ‘space' must be seen as
       embedded in, and as an aspect of, the total structure of the
       field.16

      According to Bohm, with the coming of Einstein's theory of relativity,
“the notion of separate and independent particles as basic constituents of
the universe has to be given up. The basic notion was the idea of the field
that spread continuously through space. Out of this you had to construct
the notion of a particle.”17 Bohm suggests the analogy of the flow of a
fluid (field) such as water. Within such a fluid, there may be a constant
recurrent pattern. A vortex (particle) may be a convenient word to describe
that pattern. As Bohm points out, Einstein “proposed a set of continuous
fields pervading all space, in which particles would be treated as relatively
stable and independent structures in limited regions in which the field was
strong. Therefore each particle is explained as an abstraction of a
relatively independent and stable form, as with the vortex, spread out
through space with no breaks anywhere.”18 Later in his career, Einstein
was in search of a unified field theory which would encompass the whole
universe. Bohm articulates Einstein's vision of unified field:

       Einstein did in fact very seriously try to obtain such a
       description in terms of a unified field theory. He took the total
       field of the whole universe as a primary description. This field
       is continuous and indivisible. Particles are then to be regarded
       as certain kinds of abstraction from the total field,
       corresponding to regions of very intense field (called
       singularities). As the distance from the singularity [particle]
       increases, . . . the field gets weaker, until it merges
       imperceptibly with the fields of other singularities. But
       nowhere is there a break or a division.19

     The quantum field then is seen as the fundamental physical entity; it
is a continuous medium which is present everywhere in space. The
quantum world and indeed the whole universe could be viewed as a vast
domain of interacting fields. These fields obey the principles of special
theory and of quantum mechanics. It is meaningless to ask what fields are
composed of. Physics cannot explain fields in terms of anything more
fundamental. The current view among particle physicists is that fields are
irreducible; that they are not composed of parts. They are the simplest
entities in the universe. Quantum fields are the primary entities in terms of
which particles and everything else are to be explained. Quantum field is
“a fundamental entity that can exist in continuous form, as a field, and in
discontinuous form, as particles, different kinds of particles being
associated with different fields.”20 Hence, there is a fundamental
difference between classical and quantum fields. Babu Joseph clarifies:
“There is a subtle distinction between classical and quantum fields; while
the classical field is simply a continuum without (virtual) particles, the
quantum field does have them in it.”21

     Now, it is pertinent to ask, how are we to characterize fields like
electromagnetic and gravitational fields? What is their nature, and what
are their properties? Polkinghorne defines field as follows: “A field is an
entity taking values at every point of space and instant of time, a truly
extended physical system.”22 Smolin articulates further the nature of field:

       The idea is that a field is a quantity, like a number, one of
       which lives at each point in space. As you move through
       space, the value of the field changes continuously. The value
       of the field at a single point also evolves in time. The [field]
       theory gives us laws that tell us how the field changes as you
       move in space and through time. These laws tell us that the
       value of the field at a particular point is influenced by the
       value of the field at nearby points. The field at a point can also
       be influenced by a material body at the same point. Thus, a
       field can carry a force from one body to another. There is no
       need to believe in ghostly action at a distance.23

     With Capra, we conclude that quantum field theories effect a radical
transformation of the concepts of both matter and space. He says:

       The field theories of modern physics force us to abandon the
       classical distinction between material particles and the void.
       Einstein's field theory of gravity and quantum field theory
       both show that particles cannot be separated from the space
       surrounding them. On the one hand, they determine the
       structure of that space, while on the other hand they cannot be
       regarded as isolated entities, but have to be seen as
       condensations of a continuous field which is present
       throughout space. In quantum field theory, this field is seen as
       the basis of all particles and of their mutual interactions. 24

3.1.3. Quantum Fields and Particles

     Now, what is the relation between fields and particles? We have
already made some references above about this relationship. Before the
advent of quantum field theories, there was an ultimate dualism in
classical physics between matter and space -- between particles and field -
- with primacy given to matter. This is evident from the classical
definition of matter: “Matter = full space.”25 Although Maxwell and
Einstein introduced the concept of field (electromagnetic and gravitational
fields respectively), they nevertheless did not accord primacy to field over
particles. Maxwell presupposed dualism between electromagnetic field
and particles like electrons. Even Einstein in his general theory
distinguished between space-time curvature (gravitational field) and
mass/energy. Obviously, he would not say that mass/energy is the product
of spacetime warpage. In this sense, dualism between fields and particles
persisted in the minds of physicists. But with the introduction of the
concept of quantum field, this dualism was abolished -- in the late 1920s.
Quantum field theories accomplished a radical paradigm shift which
replaced the notion of particles as fundamental entities by the much
subtler notion of quantum fields. Capra sums up this scientific revolution:

       The classical mechanistic world-view was based on the notion
       of solid, indestructible particles moving in the void. Modern
       physics has brought about a radical revision of this picture. It
       has led not only to a completely new notion of ‘particles,' but
       has also transformed the classical concept of the void in a
       profound way. This transformation took place in the so-called
       field theories. It began with Einstein's idea of associating the
       gravitational field with the geometry of space and became
       even more pronounced when quantum theory and relativity
       theory were combined to describe the force fields of
       subatomic particles. In these ‘quantum field theories,' the
       distinction between particles and the space surrounding them
       loses its original sharpness, and the void [field] is recognized
       as a dynamic quantity of paramount importance.26

Steven Weinberg narrates the historical development of the paradigm shift
from particles to quantum fields:

       However, the world was still conceived to be composed of two
       very different ingredients -- particles and fields -- which were
       both to be described in terms of quantum mechanics, but in
       very different ways. Material particles, like electrons and
       protons, were conceived to be eternal. . . . It was not long
       before a way was found out of this distasteful dualism, toward
       a truly unified view of nature. The essential steps were taken
       in a 1928 paper of Jordan and Eugene Wigner, and then in a
       pair of long papers in 1929-30 by Heisenberg and Pauli. They
       showed that material particles could be understood as the
       quanta of various fields, in just the same way as the photon is
       the quantum of the electromagnetic field. There was supposed
       to be one field for each type of elementary particle. Thus, the
       inhabitants of the universe were conceived to be a set of fields
       -- an electron field, a proton field, an electromagnetic field —
       and particles were reduced to mere epiphenomena. In its
       essentials, this point of view has survived to the present day,
       and forms the central dogma of quantum field theory: the
       essential reality is a set of fields subject to the rules of special
       relativity and quantum mechanics; all else is derived as a
       consequence of the quantum dynamics of these fields.27

    With the invention of the concept of quantum field then the dualism
between field and particle was abolished, and field was taken to be the
fundamental reality. Field is not to be considered external to particle, and
so there is no dualism between the two. Quantum field theories also
reconcile the dualism between wave and particle. Polkinghorne writes:

       One can see that [the reconciliation of apparent opposites]
       most clearly in the way in which quantum field theory
       provides the formalism perfectly adapted to a duality of wave
       and particle. Because it concerns a field, it has the property of
       extension in space that goes with wavelike behaviour; because
       it is quantized, its energy and momentum come in the
       countable and indivisible packets (quanta) that go with
       particle behaviour.28

Harris describes the scenario in particle physics that has come about with
the introduction of the concept of field as fundamental reality:

       As the field fills the whole of space and time, no part of the
       physical universe is completely unaffected by it . . . . The
       introduction of the concept, therefore, indicative of the
       crumbling of the classical mechanics, gives rise to a more
       unified picture of the material world, in which every particle
       becomes, in a sense, all-pervasive and each becomes involved
       with every other in a complex of overlapping fields. For every
       particle is the centre at least of a gravitational field and may
       also have electrical and magnetic fields associated with it, the
       limits of none of which can be sharply drawn and which
       modify the physical environment of every other particle.29

Quantum field theories thus interpret field as the foundation of all
particles and their mutual interactions. The field is the continuum which is
present always and everywhere; it is the medium of all material
phenomena. In the Aristotelian terminology, we can say that quantum field
is the ‘substance' of the universe, and particles are the ‘accidents,' but they
are not separate.

   According to quantum field theories, matter-particles are the
momentary manifestations of interacting fields which are the fundamental
entities in the universe. Particles are merely local condensations of the
field; they are concentrations of energy which appear and disappear,
thereby losing their individual character and dissolving into the underlying
field. The intensity of a field at a point tells us the probability of finding
its associated particle. For instance, the intensity of electromagnetic field
at a point in space gives us the odds for finding a photon there, and so
photons are the manifestations of electromagnetic fields. This idea has
been extended to describe all subatomic particles and their interactions,
each type of particle corresponding to a different kind of field. Thus, Paul
Dirac who contributed much to the development of quantum field theory,
visualized a separate field for each type of particle. During his time, only
three kinds of subatomic particles (electron, proton and neutron) were
discovered, and so only three different kinds of fields were postulated. A
photon is a particle of electromagnetic field, an electron is a quantum of
electron-positron field, and a proton is a particle of proton-anti-proton
field, and so on. Thus, the fundamental postulate of relativistic quantum
field theory is that physical reality consists of a set of fields that specifies
the probabilities for finding their associated particles. The idea of matter
thus disappeared into that of field.

      Great quantum physicists, like Einstein, Heisenberg, Eugene Wigner,
Wolfgang Pauli, Pascual Jordan and Paul Dirac, have held that material
particles could be understood as quanta of various fields. They have thus
supported the primacy of quantum field over particles. We have strong
endorsements from Einstein himself. He comments about the invention of
the quantum field theory: “It needed great scientific imagination to realize
that it is not the charges nor the particles but the field in the space between
the charges and the particles which is essential for the description of
physical phenomena.”30 Einstein rejects the dualism between field and
particles saying that, “there is no sense in regarding matter and field as
two qualities quite different from each other . . . field being the only
reality.”31 Further, he affirms the primacy of field over particle: “The
material particle has no place as a fundamental concept in field theory.
Even Maxwell's electrodynamics are [sic] not complete for this reason.
Gravity as a field theory must also deny a preferred status to matter.”32
Einstein goes so far as to say that field is the only reality: “We may
therefore regard matter as being constituted by the regions of space in
which the field is extremely intense. . . . There is no place in this new kind
of physics both for the field and matter, for the field is the only reality.”33

     Other renowned physicists also affirm the primacy of quantum field
over matter. Weinberg agrees with Einstein when he says that “the
essential reality is a set of fields. . . . all else can be derived as a
consequence of the quantum dynamics of those fields.”34 Smolin makes a
similar remark: “If fields are not made from matter, perhaps fields are the
fundamental stuff. Matter must then be made from fields.”35 Likewise,
Capek says: “The ultimate material reality that physics can apprehend is
the ‘field' and in the aspect of the quantum field, it is both a continuum
and a discontinuum, the discontinuities being temporary condensations of
space-time where the field is unusually intense, giving rise to corpuscular
matter.”36 Bohm expresses a similar opinion: “In a quantum field theory,
what we perceive as particles are excitations of the quantum field itself.”37
The great mathematician, Hermann Weyl comments about the relation
between electromagnetic field and electron: “According to the [field
theory of matter] a material particle such as an electron is merely a small
domain of the electrical field within which the field strength assumes
enormously high values, indicating that a comparatively huge field energy
is concentrated in a very small space.”38

     We conclude the present narrative with Peat's observation about
quantum field theory: “Quantum field theory is a deeper way of dealing
with quantum mechanical systems than the quantum theory of Heisenberg
and Schrödinger.”39

3.2. Quantum Fields and Particle Interactions

      Indeed, the concept of field holds the key to the construction of
quantum field theories — the unification of special and general theories of
relativity with quantum mechanics. These field theories give new
interpretations to the four fundamental forces of nature in terms of particle
interactions. Relativistic quantum field theories deal with electromagnetic,
weak nuclear and strong nuclear forces, and the proposed quantum theory
of gravity concerns gravitational force.

3.2.1. The Fundamental Forces as Particle Interactions

      In classical mechanics, forces between particles are described in
terms of pushing and pulling action. For instance, in the case of
electromagnetic force, two negatively charged electrons are said to repel
each other because they push each other apart. They supposedly exert a
repulsive force on one another. But according to quantum field theories,
this is a very imprecise way of describing electromagnetic force. Neither
of the two electrons ‘feels' any force when they approach each other.
Physicists now prefer to speak about particle interactions rather than about
forces. Force is understood as an exchange of ‘virtual' particles between
interacting charged particles, such as electrons or protons. Quantum field
theory thus replaces the concept of force by the subtler notion of
interaction; and all interactions take place through the exchange of virtual
particles.40 Photons are the mediating particles of electromagnetism, weak
bosons are the force-carriers of electroweak force and gluons are the
exchange particles of strong force. The theory of particle interactions was
worked out in the 1940s by three physicists, namely, Richard Feynman, J.
Schwinger and S. Tomonaga.

     The complex interactions among electrons and other particles are
thus mediated by sets of virtual particles or gauge bosons.41 These are not
as real as other subatomic particles. For instance, the virtual photon that
mediates electromagnetic interaction is not as real as the photon which is a
particle of light. That is why it is called ‘virtual' photon. It has the nature
of a real particle, but it does not actually exist as a particle. A virtual
particle is a ‘shadow' particle, and has an extremely short duration; but a
real particle, like a photon or an electron, has a long life. Unlike real
particles, virtual particles cannot be observed in a bubble chamber because
of their extremely short life, but their existence is inferred mathematically.
But they are not merely imaginary particles because they produce
measurable effects, and also because they can turn into real ones under
appropriate conditions. Prediction about their existence has been
confirmed by experiments to a high degree of accuracy. The composition
and properties of virtual particles determine the range and types of
different interactions between particles. For example, virtual photons are
considered massless, and so electromagnetic interactions can take place at
indefinite distances. On the other hand, the vector bosons W± and Zo,
virtual particles associated with weak nuclear force are massive, and so
they give rise to the short-ranged weak force. Thus according to quantum
field theory, every field, like electromagnetism, has an associated virtual
particle which determines the nature of the field.

     For instance, let us consider the gravitational force which is believed
to be the force of attraction between bodies with masses. Thus we say that
two massive bodies -- the Sun and the Earth -attract each other through
their gravitational fields. But according to quantum field theory, the
gravitational field between the Sun and the Earth is not to be considered as
a force field between them. Rather, such a force of attraction is the result
of the exchange of ‘gravitons' which are the virtual particles of
gravitational field; this field is supposedly ‘quantized' into countless
gravitons. Thus, the Sun and the Earth are all the time exchanging these
gravitons; and these exchanges make up the gravitational field between
these two massive bodies. Stephen Hawking explains: “The gravitational
force between the sun and the earth is ascribed to the exchange of
gravitons between the particles that make up these two bodies. Although
the exchanged particles are virtual, they certainly do produce a measurable
effect - they make the earth orbit the sun!”42 Herbert states it differently:
“Thus the sun attracts the Earth (and vice versa) via the gravity field or via
an exchange of gravitons (the particle aspect of the gravity field).”43
Another example is how the electromagnetic interaction takes place in a
hydrogen atom which has an electron and a proton. The classical
explanation is that these two particles are bound together by an electrical
field. But according to quantum field theory, the two particles - proton and
electron - are exchanging a third particle, namely, the virtual photon. We
might think of proton and electron as two tennis players hitting a ball - the
photon - back and forth between them. The exchange of ball binds two
players together; the photon acts as a kind of glue that holds two
components of hydrogen atom together.44
      We shall illustrate the dynamics of particle interactions by examining
electromagnetic force/interactions in some detail. Take for example, an
electrically charged particle ‘repelling' another with similar electric
charge - say, two electrons. According to classical physics, each particle is
surrounded by an electric field — a ‘cloud' or ‘mist' of ‘electric-essence'
— and the force each particle ‘feels' arises from the repulsion between
their respective force fields. However, quantum field theory interprets
such repulsion as an interaction between charged particles mediated by
virtual photons. We can think of an electromagnetic field as consisting of a
swarm of photons; the interaction between two charged particles actually
arises from their ‘shooting' photons back and forth between themselves,
like ice-skaters throwing bowling balls at each other. One skater can affect
the fellow ice-skater's motion and also one's own motion by hurling a
barrage of bowling balls at the other. Similarly, two electrically charged
particles influence each other by exchanging virtual photons. However, in
this example, the exchange of bowling balls is always repulsive; always it
drives the skaters apart. But when two oppositely charged particles
interact through the exchange of photons, the resulting electromagnetic
force can be also attractive. Greene suggests that in an electromagnetic
interaction, a charged particle is conveying a message to the other particle
through virtual photon rather than imparting a force:

       It's as if the photon is not so much the transmitter of the force
       per se, but rather the transmitter of a message of how the
       recipient must respond to the force in question. For like-
       charged particles, the photon carries the message ‘move
       apart,' while for oppositely charged particles it carries the
       message ‘come together.' For this reason the photon is
       sometimes referred to as the messenger particle for the
       electromagnetic force. Similarly, the gluons and weak gauge
       bosons are the messenger particles for the strong and weak
       nuclear forces.45

     Thus, according to quantum field theory (which in the present case is
called ‘quantum electrodynamics'), two electrons repel one another when
virtual photons pass back and forth between them. The first electron emits
a virtual photon which is absorbed by the second. As a result of emitting
photon, the first electron recoils in response, and its direction of motion is
partially deflected. The photon also gives a small ‘kick' to the second
electron as it is absorbed, with its direction of motion likewise deflected.
These recoil and kick send them onto diverging paths. The net result is
that the two electrons are pushed away from one another, and they fly
apart from each other. Thus, if two electrons come close enough to each
other -close enough so that their virtual photon clouds overlap -- a virtual
photon that is emitted by one electron is absorbed by the other. This is the
case of an electron interaction - one electron interacting with another. Of
course, this is a two-way process with both electrons absorbing virtual
photons that are emitted by the other. The closer two electrons come, the
more virtual photons they exchange, and the more virtual photons they
exchange, the more sharply their paths are deflected. This is how electrons
are said to be repelling each other. Physicists call this process ‘scattering.'
(See Figure 14)

      However, if a second electron (or another charged particle) is not
present, the virtual photons have nowhere to go but to return back to the
‘mother' electron and then they are absorbed by it. “When an electron
