emits a virtual photon which is absorbed by another particle, the electron
is said to be ‘interacting' with another particle. However, when an electron
emits a virtual photon and then re-absorbs it, the electron is said to be
interacting with itself.”46 In this way, each electron acts on itself through
its own photon cloud. Such emitting and re-absorption of a virtual photon
by an electron is called ‘selfinteraction.' Hence, every electrically charged
particle continually emits and re-absorbs virtual photons, and/or
exchanges them with other charged particles. When two electrons (two
negative charges) exchange virtual photons, they ‘repel' each other. But if
a proton and an electron (a positive charge and a negative charge)
exchange virtual photons, they ‘attract' each other. The repulsive/attractive
force between these particles is simply the cumulative effect of the
exchanges of virtual photons; the number of these exchanges increases at
close range, and decreases with distance. Capra says: “The force is nothing
but the collective macroscopic effect of these multiple photon exchanges.
The concept of force is therefore no longer useful in subatomic physics. It
is a classical concept which we associate (even if only subconsciously)
with the Newtonian idea of a force being felt over a distance.”47 Hence,
according to quantum field theory, an electromagnetic force is in fact the
mutual exchange of virtual photons.




Figure 14. ‘Scattering' process of electromagnetic interaction
      Richard Feynman's space-time diagrams represent the notion of force
as exchange of particles. The diagram above shows the process of
exchanging a virtual photon; it represents the mutual repulsion of two
electrons by the exchanging of a virtual photon. One of them emits a
virtual photon at point A, and the other absorbs it at point B. When the
first electron emits the photon, it reverses its direction and changes its
velocity, and so does the second electron when it absorbs the photon. In the
end, the two electrons fly apart, having repelled each other through the
exchange of a virtual photon.

     Now, the question arises about the origin of virtual particles like
photons. How are they produced? Heisenberg's uncertainty principle and
quantum vacuum provide us the key to explain the creation of these
particles. As we have seen, although uncertainty principle is generally
stated in terms of position and momentum, it is also applied to the relation
between energy and time. If we know the energy of a particle exactly, then
we cannot predict with certainty the amount of time that it is likely to
remain in that state of energy. Conversely, if we know precisely the
duration of a particle in a state of energy, then we cannot predict with
precision its energy state. This relationship between energy and time has
important consequences. The uncertainty principle allows the violation of
the law of conservation of energy for an extremely short period of time. It
permits virtual particles to come into existence spontaneously from the
quantum vacuum for an exceedingly short time provided they disappear
within the time permitted by uncertainty principle. The extra energy for
creating these particles is ‘borrowed' from the quantum field (vacuum)
and it has to be ‘paid back' within the time limits stipulated by uncertainty
principle.48 This ‘borrowing' of energy and ‘paying it back' in the
interaction between two electrons, exchanging virtual particles (photons)
apparently have the following sequence: first, there are two electrons, then
one of them emits a virtual photon (borrowing energy), and the other
absorbs it (paying it back) within the time limits permitted by uncertainty
relation. If there is only one electron, then it emits a virtual photon
(borrowing energy), which is re-absorbed by the electron itself (paying it
back), within the stipulated time. This, we said above, is self-interaction.
Thus, the uncertainty relation between energy and time allows the emitting
and re-absorption of photons if it takes place within the limits of time
allowed by uncertainty principle -- that is, within about one thousand
trillionth (10-15) of a second. This explains the origin of virtual particles.

     A virtual particle like photon, we said, is a ‘shadow' particle, and is
not as real as the photon which is a particle of light. That is why it is
called ‘virtual' photon. It has the nature of a real photon, but it does not
actually exist as, nor have the reality of, a photon. Now the question is,
how does a virtual photon get promoted to the state of a real photon? The
answer is that if we provide the required energy for a virtual photon
without violating the conservation law of mass-energy, it would become a
real photon. Such a real photon is emitted by an excited electron. An
excited electron is an electron that is in an energy level higher than its
ground state. An electron's ground state is its lowest energy level where it
is as close to the nucleus of an atom as possible. The only photons which
electrons emit when they are in their ground state are virtual photons
which they re-absorb within the time permitted by uncertainty principle so
as not to violate the conservation law of mass-energy. An electron
considers the ground state as its ‘home.' It does not like to leave the home.
In fact, the only time it leaves its ground state is when it is literally pushed
out of it with extra energy supply. In that case, the electron's first concern
is to get back to its ground state. Since the ground state is a low-energy
state, the electron must lose its surplus energy before it can return to it.
Therefore, when the electron is at an energy level higher than its ground
state, it jettisons its excess energy in the form of a photon. The ejected
photon is one of the electron's virtual photons, that suddenly finds itself
with enough energy to become a real photon without violating the
conservation law of mass-energy. In other words, one of the electron's
virtual photons is suddenly ‘promoted' to be a real photon. The amount of
energy the promoted photon has, depends upon how much extra energy the
electron had to jettison.49

      We sum up our narrative on ‘Fundamental Forces as Particle
Interactions,' in the words of Paul Davies: “In this quantum description,
the existence of the electromagnetic interaction between two charged
particles is attributed to the exchange of another particle -- the photon.
The electromagnetic field of a charged particle may be envisaged as a
cloud of virtual photons surrounding the particle.”50 These remarks are
applicable to the other interactions of nature as well. To these, we now
turn.

3.2.2. The Four Basic Particle Interactions

     Quantum field theory, we saw, interprets the forces of nature as
particle interactions. There are four such fundamental forces, namely,
strong nuclear force, electromagnetism, gravity and weak nuclear force.
Of these, the first three are binding forces; the strong nuclear force holds
atomic nuclei together, the electromagnetic force binds atoms and
molecules, and the gravitational force holds planets, stars and galaxies.
The weak nuclear force does not bind anything, but manifests itself in
certain kinds of particle collisions and in particle decays, such as
radioactive decay. Before we explain these forces in terms of particle
interactions, it would be instructive to sum up how they are traditionally
understood.
      The most familiar fundamental force is gravity which is the force of
attraction between bodies that have masses. All phenomena of nature
described by physics are governed by gravity. This force is universal, that
is, every particle and every form of energy is affected by it. Except for the
first split-second after the origin of the universe in the Big Bang, it
dominates the universe at large throughout its evolution. The law of
gravitation, as is well known, states that gravitational force between two
bodies is proportional to the product of their masses, and inversely
proportional to the square of distance between them. Gravity is the
weakest of all forces of nature, but also the most dominant one. The
gravitational attraction of a single proton for an electron is about a billion
billion billion billion (1038) times weaker than the electromagnetic force
between them. Gravity can act over large distances and it is always
attractive. Its effects always add up rather than cancel. In the case of
bodies composed of enormous number of particles, such as planets or
stars, the tiny gravitational attraction of each particle adds up and grows
into a gigantic force.

     The second fundamental force of nature is electromagnetism; it
operates between subatomic particles which have electric charges. The
phenomena of nature described by chemistry are all manifestations of
electromagnetism. Chemical reactions involve the rearrangements of
electrons in the orbits around atomic nuclei, and electromagnetic force
governs these interactions. Protons have positive charge, and electrons
negative. Electromagnetic force between two positive charges is repulsive,
as is also the force between two negative charges; but the force is
attractive between positive and negative charges. Each atom has zero net
charge because the negative charge of its electrons is exactly balanced by
the positive charge of protons in its nucleus. There are almost equal
number of protons and electrons in an atom and so its total charge is zero.
A large body, like the Earth or the Sun, contains nearly equal number of
positively charged protons and negatively charged electrons. These
charges cancel each other out, and so there is very little net
electromagnetic force in large bodies. That is to say, the overall charge is
zero. Thus, most large quantities of matter are electrically neutral, and so
do not exhibit electromagnetic force. Hence, the most significant effects
of electromagnetism are observed only at the levels of atoms. In
individual atoms, electrons have associated electromagnetic field which
holds them in their orbits around the nucleus.

     The third fundamental force of nature is weak nuclear force. Unlike
gravitational and electromagnetic forces which have long range, the weak
force has extremely short range. It is much weaker than strong nuclear
force. The weak force accounts for radioactivity or disintegration of atoms
like uranium. In spite of the fact that there are many subatomic particles,
only a small number of them, namely, electrons, photons, protons and
neutrinos, are stable; that is, if left to themselves, these do not
disintegrate. Other particles are unstable, and will disintegrate rather
rapidly into stable ones; and as they disintegrate, they emit particles which
are detected as radioactivity. For example, isolated neutrons are unstable,
and will decay after a period of time into a proton, an electron and an anti-
neutrino. It is the weak force that governs the disintegration of particles
like neutron.

     The last of the fundamental forces of nature is strong nuclear force
which is the strongest of the four forces with shortest range of them all. It
accounts for the stability of nuclei of atoms. The nuclei of atoms except
those of hydrogen contain a number of protons (with positive charge) and
neutrons (with no charge) packed closely together. These nuclear particles
themselves are composite objects, each made up of smaller units called
‘quarks.' The strong force holds quarks together within protons and
neutrons in atomic nuclei. Hence, the strong force is also called ‘quark-
binding' force. It acts attractively among protons and neutrons in order to
hold atomic nuclei together; it is strong enough to counteract the repulsive
force of protons in the nucleus. This force has an extremely short range,
dropping sharply to zero beyond a distance of about a ten-trillionth of a
centimeter. At normal energies, the strong force operates by binding
protons and neutrons of atomic nuclei even though the electromagnetic
force of repulsion between protons tries to blow nuclei apart. However, at
high energies and enormous temperatures, the strong force becomes much
weaker, unable to hold protons and neutrons together, and then the nuclei
break apart.51
     From the foregoing descriptions of gravitational, electromagnetic,
weak and strong forces, it appears that they are quite different and have
nothing in common. But according to quantum field theories, the
differences among the four forces are merely superficial. This is borne out
by the fact that these theories interpret all the four forces as interactions
mediated by ‘gauge bosons,' also called ‘force-carriers,' ‘exchange
particles' or ‘messenger particles.' Each force interacts between particles
through some property — ‘charge' for electromagnetism, ‘flavour' for
weak force and ‘colour' for strong force. The virtual photon is the
exchange particle of electromagnetic interaction; the exchange of virtual
photon mediates the interaction between charged particles.
Electromagnetic and weak forces are unified into electroweak field theory;
its exchange particles are ‘vector bosons' and virtual photons. The virtual
particles of the strong, quark-binding force consist of a set of eight
‘coloured gluons.' The force-carriers of gravitational interaction are
supposedly gravitons; gravitation is believed to take place by the exchange
of gravitons. Thus in all, there are twelve virtual, exchange particles which
mediate the four fundamental forces or interactions of nature.

      The initial attempts at formulating quantum field theory started with
the unification of electromagnetic interaction and special theory of
relativity. This theory, we have seen, is called ‘quantum electrodynamics'
(QED) which is the first relativistic quantum field theory invented. “It was
‘relativistic' because it incorporated the principle of Einstein's special
relativity theory; it was ‘quantum' because it embraced the idea of the new
quantum mechanics; and it was a ‘field theory' because the primary
objects of its investigation were fields like electric and magnetic field.”52
Capra explains the important characteristics of this theory:

       The striking new feature of quantum electrodynamics arises
       from the combination of two concepts: that of the
       electromagnetic field, and that of photons as the particle
       manifestations of electromagnetic waves. Since photons are
       also electromagnetic waves, and since these waves are
       vibrating fields, the photons must be manifestations of
       electromagnetic fields. Hence the concept of a ‘quantum
       field': that is, of a field which can take the form of quanta, or
       particles.53

     According to QED, charged particles, such as electrons and protons,
interact with one another by exchanging virtual photons which are the
force-carriers of electromagnetic field.54 It was invented in the 1920s by
Heisenberg, Wolfgang Pauli, Pascual Jordan, and Paul Dirac. The final
version of quantum electrodynamics was developed by Richard Feynman,
Julian Schwinger, and S. Tomonaga in the late 1940s. It is the first model
of relativistic quantum field theory and even now the most successful. The
agreement between QED and experiments is absolutely stunning. Some of
the predictions have been tested in experiments to an extraordinary degree
of accuracy, and in some cases better than one part in a trillion. Such
extremely close agreement gives physicists great confidence that the
‘photon exchange' model of quantum electrodynamics is absolutely
correct. Quantum electrodynamics was so successful that it became the
paradigm for the interpretation of other forces in terms of particle
interaction.

     The success of quantum electrodynamics inspired particle physicists
in the 1960s and 1970s to employ similar methods for developing quantum
mechanical interpretations of weak, strong, and gravitational forces. Now,
the electromagnetic and weak nuclear interactions are ordinarily
considered as separate. But in the 1960s, quantum physicists, Sheldon
Glashow, Steven Weinberg and Abdus Salam found that these interactions
can be combined in a quantum field description even though their
manifestations are quite distinct in the world around us. They invented a
quantum field theory which unifies quantum electrodynamics and weak
interaction into a mathematical formalism, called ‘electroweak theory.'
The unified electroweak interaction is mediated by a set of four particles -
- a photon and three particles of weak interaction called ‘intermediate
vector bosons.' These are denoted by W+ (pronounced W plus) and W-
(pronounced W minus) and a neutral one Zo (pronounced Z naught). The
key to the unification of electromagnetism and weak interaction into
electroweak theory is a principle which physicists had long known: that
the range of a force is directly related to the mass of the virtual particle
that is exchanged; that is, the bigger the mass, shorter the range. The
photon is massless, and so electromagnetism has unlimited range. The fact
that the weak force has very short range implies that its exchange particles
have very high mass. If somehow the mass of these particles could vanish,
then electromagnetism and weak nuclear force would actually be quite
similar in their properties.

     Precisely this happens at very high energies and temperatures where
the properties of virtual particles of electromagnetism and weak
interaction are similar and indistinguishable. Thus photon, the virtual
particle of electromagnetic interaction, and the three vector bosons, the
exchange particles of weak interaction, behave in a similar way at higher
energies where the distinction between electromagnetic and weak forces
disappears; but they become distinct at low energies, as in the present
universe. Hawking explains:

       In the Weinberg-Salam theory, at energies much greater than
       100 GeV, the three new particles and the photon would all
       behave in a similar manner. But at the lower particle energies
       that occur in most normal situations, this symmetry between
       the particles would be broken. W+, W-, and Zo, would acquire
       large masses, making the forces they carry have a very short
       range.55

Crucially, such a project works out only if we assume that the virtual
particles W+, W- and Zo are really massless - that they have no intrinsic
mass; instead, they acquire their high masses by a new type of physical
mechanism called ‘Higgs mechanism.'56 These virtual particles were
discovered in 1983 at CERN, Geneva, with exactly the properties predicted
by the theory, and electroweak theory was thus confirmed.57

     Next, in sequence was the invention of the quantum field theory of
strong nuclear force; it is called ‘quantum chromodynamics' or QCD for
short. It was proposed by David Gross, David Politzer and Frank Wilczek
in 1973. The strong force acts on quarks which are bound together in
protons and neutrons of atomic nuclei. QCD was constructed in analogy
with quantum electrodynamics. Following the lead given by QED,
quantum chromodynamics predicts the existence of virtual particles which
mediate strong interaction between quarks with the property of ‘colour.'
QCD postulates three different types of colour charges, called ‘red,'
‘green' and ‘blue.' The effect of strong force is limited to the behaviour of
quarks, and of composite particles built from quarks, such as the familiar
protons and neutrons which make up atomic nuclei. Whereas QED
requires just one kind of photon, and electroweak theory employs three
types of vector bosons, QCD postulates eight distinct ‘gluons' in order to
effect all the interactions between three colours of quarks - red, blue,
green; this renders the theory much more complex and cumbersome. QCD
has not been quite successful in describing the processes involving strong
interactions. In spite of many great efforts, physicists have not been able
to apply QCD beyond a narrow range of phenomena.58

     Now, it is significant that the range of each interaction depends on the
mass of its mediating particle. The more massive the particle is, the
shorter its range, and the less massive the particle, the longer its range.
The effects of less massive particles are observed in our ordinary life
conditions. For instance, the electromagnetic interaction is mediated by
the exchange of massless photons and so its range is indefinitely long. The
effects of electric and magnetic forces are encountered in the everyday
world. The gravitational force is believed to be mediated by massless
gravitons, and so its range is infinite. As in the case of electromagnetic
force, the effects of gravitational force are also experienced in the world
around us. Further, strong interaction exchanges massive particles - gluons
-- and so it has a very short range. It extends only about the size of a
proton and so cannot build up a macroscopic force. Strong interaction is
thus not observed in everyday world. Similarly, the weak interaction
exchanges heavy particles -- the vector bosons W± and Zo -- and so it has
also an extremely short range like the strong force. Thus, electromagnetic
and gravitational forces have massless mediating particles, and their range
is infinite. In contrast, the strong and weak nuclear forces have exchange
particles which are heavy and their ranges are very short.
     We summarize the dynamics of the three particle interactions - of
electromagnetism, weak and strong nuclear forces -- in the words of
Greene: “The photons, gluons and weak gauge bosons provide the
microscopic mechanism for transmitting the forces they constitute.”59

3.3. Quantum Gravity and Quantum Vacuum

      In unifying the theory of relativity and quantum mechanics, the first
stage is the integration of the special theory with quantum physics. The
relativistic quantum field theories of quantum electrodynamics,
electroweak theory and quantum chromodynamics accomplish this task.
The next step is to unify the general theory of relativity with quantum
mechanics. This is achieved by constructing a quantum field theory of
gravity or a quantum theory of gravity. The general theory, we know, is
first and foremost a theory of gravity and so the task before physicists is
the quantization of gravity. This implies the quantum mechanical
treatment of space-time fabric since according to the general theory,
gravity is space-time curvature. A quantum theory of gravity takes us to
the quantum vacuum - the ultimate space-time fabric.

3.3.1. Towards Quantum Theories of Gravity

     Our discourse on quantum theory of gravity starts with some
conceptual paradoxes in Einstein's general theory of relativity and in
quantum physics. That is, when these theories are applied to certain
domains of physical reality, the equations produce infinities. Appearance
of infinities in the equations of a physical theory signals the break down of
the theory in question.

3.3.1.1. Infinities Galore

    The relativistic quantum field theories were invented by great
physicists with relative ease. The three non-gravitational forces of nature
were interpreted in terms of particle interactions. However, even after
decades of strenuous efforts, the finest physicists in the world have not
been able to construct a satisfactory quantum field theory of gravity or a
quantum theory of gravity. The question is, why does gravitation so
stubbornly resist quantum mechanical treatment? In order to get a clue to
answer this question, we propose to examine the fate of the concept of
gravity in modern physics; that is, how it came to break down with
infinities. We know, it was the great Isaac Newton who for the first time in
the history of physics proposed a theory of gravity in a systematic way. He
formulated the law of universal gravitation as a force of attraction between
bodies that have masses. These bodies interact with each other under of
the influence of gravitation in space and time. However, gravity in no way
interacts with space and time which do not influence it either. Space and
time function merely as the passive background of all motions of bodies
caused by gravitational force. Although Newton proposed the theory of
gravitation, he had no explanation for it. He is famously quoted to have
said: “I have not as yet been able to discover the reason for these
properties of gravity from phenomena, and I do not feign hypotheses.”60

      Einstein's general theory of relativity radically modified Newton's
concept of gravity. The special theory unified Newtonian space and time
into space-time, and the general theory incorporated gravity with space-
time. Gravitation is not strictly speaking a force separate from space-time,
but it is a warping of space-time. It is the curvature of space-time in the
presence of mass/energy. This means that wherever there is mass/energy, it
warps space-time continuum. The larger and denser is mass/energy, the
more pronounced is the curvature of space-time. John Polkinghorne says:
“Albert Einstein's successive great discoveries of special and general
relativity showed that space, time, and matter are intimately related in a
single package deal. Matter curves the geometry of spacetime and
curvature, in turn, deflects the paths of matter.”61 By the same token, space
and time become active ‘players' in the cosmic drama whereas they are
passive ‘onlookers' in Newtonian mechanics. Hawking says: “Space and
time are now dynamic quantities: when a body moves, or a force acts, it
affects the curvature of space and time — and in turn the structure of
space-time affects the way in which bodies move and forces act. Space and
time not only affect but also are affected by everything that happens in the
Universe.”62
      Now, precisely this dynamic nature of space-time resulted in its
infinite curvature (gravity) and the consequent downfall of the general
theory of relativity. Such is the implication of ‘singularity theorems'
invented by Stephen Hawking and Roger Penrose in the year 1964. They
demonstrated that along with some general conditions of matter/energy in
the universe, every solution to Einstein's general theory of relativity
eventually develops a state of singularity at which a star collapses to a
point of infinite spacetime curvature (gravity), infinite density and infinite
temperature. Hawking explains: “This means that all the matter in the
[collapsing] star will be compressed into a region of zero volume, so the
density of matter and the curvature of space-time become infinite. In other
words, one has a singularity contained within a region of space-time
known as a black hole.”63 Moreover, Hawking and Penrose found that the
standard model of the Big Bang theory satisfies the requirements of
singularity theorem. In 1970, they proved that “our Universe must have
had a spacetime singularity at the beginning of its big bang expansion, and
if it one day recollapses, it must produce a singularity in its big crunch.”64
Paul Davies observes: “Neither space nor time can be extended back
through the initial singularity.”65 For, at the Big Bang singularity certain
parameters, like space-time warpage (gravity), density and temperature,
become infinite.

     When a quantity that the equations of physics describe becomes
infinite, it means that they cease to be meaningful. It implies that the
theory in question is employed in a domain that is beyond the bounds of
its applicability. Hence, the general theory of relativity breaks down at
singularity on account of the appearance of infinite quantities. Davies
remarks: “The whole mathematical edifice on which the [general] theory
of relativity is based becomes quite meaningless under such conditions of
infinite density, showing that the theory is falling apart at the seams.”66
Likewise, Clifford Will observes:

       The idea that there should exist singularities of spacetime,
       places where general relativity and all the other laws of
       physics should break down, is very disturbing. Physicists are
       uncomfortable with the possibility of being unable to predict
       the future from some given initial data, but this was precisely
       what a singularity would do, because the physics that emerges
       from a singularity is not under any control.67

Physicist Dennis Sciama caustically comments that the general theory
“contains within itself the seeds of its own destruction.”68

     The point here is that according to the general theory, as we saw, the
density distribution of mass/energy in the universe determines the
curvature of space-time or gravity. Now, if this theory is applied
uncritically all ‘the way down' to the origin of the universe, it predicts that
the space-time curvature/gravity would become infinite. Such a state of an
infinite curvature of space-time is Big Bang singularity. In fact, even
before singularity is reached, the field equations of the general theory
break down and space-time curvature becomes infinite - at the Planck
scales: at the Planck time of 10-43 sec and Planck length of 10-33 cm, with
density of matter at 1093 gm/cc, temperatures above 1032 K, and energies in
excess of 1019 GeV. At these Planck domains of physical reality, gravity
displays uncontrolled behaviour, and the general theory of relativity,
Einstein's theory of gravitation, breaks down spewing out infinities.69

     Quantum theory, in its turn, too had its own bundle of troubles with
infinities in quantum field theories. Although Dirac's quantization of
electromagnetic field solved some problems, it raised many more. Briefly,
these difficulties relate to the frequent occurrence of infinities in the
theory. Infinities began to appear in mathematical formalisms when
physicists attempted to employ quantum mechanics to describe fields like
electromagnetic field. Smolin explains:

       The problem is that the electric and magnetic fields have
       values at every point in space. This means that there are an
       infinite number of variables (even in a finite volume there are
       an infinite number of points, hence an infinite number of
       variables). In quantum theory, there are uncontrollable
       fluctuations in the values of every quantum variable. An
       infinite number of variables, fluctuating uncontrollably, can
       lead to equations that get out of hand and predict infinite
       numbers when you ask questions about the probability of
       some event happening, or the strength of some force.70

Such wild behaviour of equations is mathematically repugnant and
suggests that the theoretical framework has broken down somewhere along
the way. Nevertheless, the seemingly absurd infinities that occur in
quantum field theories are handled in most cases by a mathematical
technique called ‘renormalization.' Ingenious physicists, like Freeman
Dyson, Richard Feynman and Julian Schwinger, invented this method of
renormalization in order to ‘tame' infinities by cleverly cancelling them
out with other infinities. The resulting formalism provides meaningful and
remarkably successful predictions. Hawking comments: “Although this
technique is rather dubious mathematically, it does seem to work in
practice, and has been used with these [quantum field] theories to make
predictions that agree with observations to an extraordinary degree of
accuracy.”71

     However, unlike the three non-gravitational interactions, gravitation
resists the technique of renormalization. According to quantum field
theories, as we saw, each of the three interactions -electromagnetic, weak
and strong nuclear - has virtual, exchange particles. Gravitation is also
believed to be mediated by such an exchange particle, namely, graviton; it
purportedly plays the role equivalent to photon in quantum
electrodynamics. A graviton, though it exists in theory, interacts extremely
weakly with matter (far more weakly than neutrinos), and so even vast
improvement on today's technology would not produce machines that
would detect it.72 That is to say, since gravity is exceedingly weak, there is
no physically realistic way to conduct an experiment at energy levels at
which its quantum mechanical effects would be manifest; it would take a
particle accelerator of galactic size even to approach the required energies
- energies in excess of 1019 GeV at the Planck time of 10-43 sec. It is
suggested that it would take 100 years of experimentation by a particle
collider as heavy as Jupiter to detect one graviton.
     Nevertheless, theoretical physicists can mathematically calculate
graviton's interactions with matter; but when they do so, they encounter
infinities in their equations. Of course, infinities arising in the equations
of quantum electrodynamics can be tamed by the mathematical technique
of renormalization. However, that is not possible with infinities generated
by graviton. Pagels attests: “But the infinities encountered in the
graviton's interactions could not be ‘renormalized' away; they were far
worse.”73 They cannot be accommodated by means of renormalization.
Greene expresses it differently: “The infinities arising from attempts to
merge general relativity and quantum mechanics are far more severe and
are not amenable to the normalization cure.”74 John Bell makes an
interesting remark about such conceptual problems in quantum mechanics:
“But to an unusual extent its [of quantum physics] ultimate fate is
apparent in its internal structure. It carries in itself the seeds of its own
destruction.”75 Incidentally, we recall that Sciama has made an identical
remark about the break down of the general theory of relativity: that it
“contains within itself the seeds of its own destruction.”76

     Nonetheless, what is most intriguing about the occurrence of these
infinities in the general theory of relativity and quantum field theories is
that we actually observe nothing of the sort in nature. That is the point of
Smolin's scathing remark: “In nature, we have yet to encounter anything
measurable that has an infinite value. But in both quantum theory and
general relativity, we encounter predictions of physically sensible
quantities becoming infinite. This is likely the way that nature punishes
impudent theorists who dare to break her unity.”77 Hawking makes a
similar observation about theories that churn out infinities: “One therefore
has a theory that seems to predict that certain quantities, such as the
curvature of space-time, are really infinite, yet these quantities can be
observed and measured to be perfectly finite!”78 However, in a truly
unified theory, there would be no infinities at all. Smolin assures us: “If
infinities are signs of missing unification, a unified theory will have none.
It will be what we call a finite theory, a theory that answers every question
in terms of sensible, finite numbers.”79 The first step towards such a
unified theory is the invention of a quantum theory of gravity.
3.3.1.2. Quantum Theories of Gravity

     The foregoing discussions on the break down of both the general
theory of relativity as well as quantum field theories spewing out infinities
confirm that Planck magnitudes represent a watershed in our ability to
describe space-time domain. At the Planck time of 10-43 sec, the Planck
length of 10-33 cm, with the Planck density of 1094 gm/cc, space-time
warpage/gravitational field becomes enormous and undergoes radical
modification; and quantum effects begin to be felt on account of
uncertainty principle. John Gribbin, Arthur Eddington and John Boslough
have made some significant comments on the oddity of the Planck regime.
Gribbin refers to the uniqueness of Planck dimensions:

       One way of interpreting the Planck time is that it defines both
       a time scale and a distance scale where the quantum effects of
       gravity dominate the structure of space-time. The distance
       scale of the Planck units is the distance scale at which gravity
       becomes the equal of the other forces of nature, corresponding
       to temperatures above 10-32 K energies in excess of 1019 GeV.80

Similarly, the great physicist Eddington remarks about the peculiar
character of the Planck length of 10-33 cm:

       It seems to be inevitable that this [Planck] length must play
       some role in any complete interpretation of gravitation. . . . In
       recent years great progress has been made in knowledge of the
       excessively minute; but until we can appreciate details of
       structure down to the quadrillionth of quintillionth of a
       centimetre, the most sublime of all the forces of Nature
       [gravity] remains outside the purview of the theories of
       physics.81

John Boslough speaks about the scenario at the ‘wall of Planck time:' “The
wall of Planck time comes at 10-43 second. At this point there is a
fundamental breakdown in the ability of physicists to describe space, time,
or matter. It is presumed that gravity has just broken its bond with the
single unified force that existed at the instant of the Big Bang.”82 Such
Planck regime is purportedly the domain of quantum gravity. Steven
Winstein explains:

       It is almost Gospel that quantum gravity is what happens
       when you reach the Planck scale. The standard refrain is that
       ‘something peculiar' happens to our concepts of space, time,
       and causality at such scales requiring radical revisions that
       must be described by the quantum theory of gravity. This scale
       is so remote from current experimental capabilities that the
       empirical testing of quantum gravity proposals along standard
       lines is rendered near-impossible.83

      Now the question is, what is this ‘something peculiar' happening at
the Planck scales such that it requires radical revisions of
Newtonian/Einsteinian concepts of space and time? There is also the
related question, why does space-time warpage become so enormous -
really infinite - so that the field equations of the general theory of
relativity break down at these dimensions? The answer is that while
Einstein transformed Newton's concepts of space and time into space-
time, he nevertheless continued to assume that spacetime is continuous
and smooth; that it is a continuum.84 David Bohm says: “Similarly, the
special theory of relativity requires the strict continuity of space-time; and
the general theory of relativity assumes that its basic components space-
time and matter fields be continuous and smooth.”85 That is to say,
according to the theory of relativity, the properties of space remain
homogeneous and unchanged even in smaller and smaller scales as one
‘descends' to Planck dimensions. As Capek rightly points out, the
Newtonian conception of the homogeneity of space assumes that the
structure of space must have identical quality “no matter what scale of
magnitude we consider.”86

     However, this assumption is no longer valid at the extremely minute
Planck magnitudes because quantum effects begin to affect space-time
fabric on account of uncertainty principle. This means that at the Planck
scales, the general theory of relativity can no longer be employed because
it assumes that space-time is a smooth continuum. Under such domains,
space-time is not continuous and smooth, and gravity itself needs quantum
mechanical treatment. William Stoeger says: “A rather different theory of
gravity and space-time at these extremely high temperatures and energies
is certainly needed, but that does not necessarily imply that such
superunification [of all forces] must follow.”87 This implies that the very
structure of space-time -the bedrock of physical reality -- has to be
quantized at the Planck dimensions. Peat comments: “Now this is a very
curious result, for it implies that, at the subatomic level, space-time
curvature has to be explained in terms of quantum mechanical gravitons.
Somehow the structure of space-time itself demands a quantum
mechanical explanation.”88 Stoeger shares a similar view: “This almost
certainly requires quantization of the gravitational field itself, and
therefore, in some way, a quantum description of space and time along
with massenergy at extreme energies.”89 Stoeger further suggests: “It is
with good reason, therefore, that most physicists see the predicted initial
singularity as an indication of a regime within which the classical
continuum model of space, time, and gravity does not apply, and needs to
be replaced with a quantum description of gravity.”90 This is the domain
where gravity is extremely strong, and where quantum effects begin to
dominate the structure of space-time. Quantum theories of gravity
describe the quantum behaviour of gravitational field, that is, quantized
space-time framework. These theories seek to describe gravitation at the
Planck magnitudes according to the principles of quantum mechanics.
Winstein opines: “Quantum gravity, broadly construed, is a physical
theory incorporating both the principles of general relativity and quantum
theory. Such a theory is expected to be able to provide a satisfactory
description of the microstructure of spacetime at the Planck scale.”91

      The foregoing discussions imply that it is difficult, if not impossible,
to identify the space-time realm at the Planck dimensions with the space
and time of Newtonian mechanics, or the space-time of the theory of
relativity. Physicists suggest that the Newtonian and relativistic concepts
of space and time would lose their smooth and continuous nature at these
magnitudes. Space-time would be quantized, that is, rendered discrete on
account of Heisenberg's uncertainty principle. But there is a catch. The
uncertainty relation was actually formulated to describe the quantum
world at the scale of atoms and elementary particles. To use it to talk about
spacetime domain at the Planck scales requires an extrapolation that is as
great as the change of scale between our own bodies and elementary
particles. Nevertheless, most physicists are confident to apply uncertainty
principle right down to the Planck length of 10-33 cm, and at such small
distances, it is clearly necessary to abandon the smooth and continuous
character of space-time of classical physics.

     However, in spite of the hard work of some of the most brilliant
particle physicists in the world, a satisfactory theory of spacetime
framework at the Planck domains, and a quantum theory of gravity have
not emerged. Although there is no complete idea of what a quantum theory
of gravity might involve, some promising hypotheses are put forward.
Thus, there is an agreement among physicists that the space-time of the
general theory of relativity must be quantized - rendered discrete -- in
quantum theories of gravity. This suggests that although the Newtonian
and Einsteinian spacetime appears continuous and smooth, at the Planck
magnitudes space-time would be much more lumpy and complicated,
perhaps consisting of ‘foam-like' topology of ‘bubbles' that are continually
forming and ‘de-forming.' Renowned quantum physicists Stephen
Hawking, James Hartle and John Wheeler have dared to make some
speculations regarding the topology of space-time regime prevailing at the
Planck scales. According to them, under extreme conditions of the Planck
magnitudes, the smooth space-time of general theory of relativity would
have been radically modified since it is subject to quantum fluctuations on
account of uncertainty principle.

     According to Hawking and Hartle, at the Planck time of 10-43 sec,
quantum uncertainties associated with uncertainty principle would have
begun to erase the difference between space and time. At this moment, the
distinction between space and time becomes blurred, and concepts, such as
‘before' and ‘after' lose their meaning. Time begins to ‘turn' into space;
time becomes ‘spatialized.' In Hawking's terminology, time is ‘smeared'
into space and becomes ‘imaginary.'92 He says: “That is, time is imaginary
and is indistinguishable from directions in space.”93 Imaginary time
provides a way of looking at time dimension as if it were a dimension of
space, so that it is possible to move forwards and backwards along time,
just as one can move right and left, or up and down in space. Hence, at the
Planck scales, there is no longer a time-dimension, but only four space-
like dimensions; and then it is more accurate to talk, not of space-time, but
of four-dimensional space. Hawking calls it ‘Euclidean spacetime.' He
explains:

       In Euclidean space-time there is no difference between the
       time direction and directions in space. On the other hand, in
       real space-time, in which events are labeled by real values of
       the time coordinate, it is easy to tell the difference. . . . One
       can regard the use of imaginary time as merely a
       mathematical device - or trick - to calculate answers about
       real space-time. However, there may be more to it than that. It
       may be that Euclidean space-time is the fundamental concept
       and what we think of as real space-time is just a figment of
       our imagination.94

      Likewise, John Wheeler has attempted to unify the laws of general
theory of relativity and those of quantum mechanics, and has come up
with still more revolutionary concept of space-time. Wheeler and many
physicists believe that at the Planck magnitudes, quantum disturbance on
account of uncertainty principle becomes so severe that the topology of
space-time would be completely modified.95 It is a domain of physical
reality which is governed by gravity as well as by uncertainty principle; it
is the regime of quantum gravity which radically transforms the geometry
of spacetime. Space-time dimension at the Planck magnitudes is believed
to be, not as a smooth, flat sheet of paper, but as a structure in which loops
and bridges between one region in space and another appear and vanish
frenetically. The geometry of space at these scales is highly curved and
warped, subject to energetic, random fluctuations. On account of the
extreme uncertainty of this regime, it can even be said that at this level,
space cannot ‘make up its mind' which of the infinite number of
topological possibilities it would like to manifest. Space-time, thus, is not
a static stage upon which nature dances, but it reverberates between a
multitude of alternate geometries.96

     Wheeler suggests that at the size of the Planck length of 10- 33 cm or
smaller, quantum fluctuations would be so enormous that space begins to
‘boil;' he uses the term ‘quantum foam' in order to describe this state of
the frenzy of space-time. ‘Quantum foam' refers to the frothy, writhing,
violent character of space-time fabric at the Planck scales. Thus, in
quantum theory of gravity, the smooth structure of relativistic space-time
takes on the topology of quantum foam. Nobel Laureate Kip Thorne
explains:

       Quantum gravity then radically changes the character of
       spacetime: It ruptures the unification of space and time into
       spacetime. It unglues space and time from each other, and
       then destroys time as a concept and destroys the definiteness
       of space. Time ceases to exist; no longer can we say that ‘this
       thing happens before that one,' because without time, there is
       no concept of ‘before' or ‘after.' Space, the sole remaining
       remnant of what was once a unified spacetime, becomes a
       random, probabilistic froth, like soapsuds.97




             Figure 15. Quantum space-time ‘foam'
      Space-time would break up and take on features more akin to those of
turbulent ‘foam' than of smooth continuum. Instead of the continuous, flat
space-time of general relativity, it would display a foam-like structure, and
would assume frothing, turbulent, twisted forms. (See Figure 15) Thorne
illustrates the nature of ‘space-time foam' with the example of a piece of
wood cloaked in water being burned out by fire:

       Before its rupture (that is, outside the singularity and Planck
       magnitudes), spacetime is like a piece of wood impregnated
       with water. In this analogy, wood represents space, the water
       represents time, and the two (wood and water; space and time)
       are tightly interwoven and unified. The singularity and the
       laws of quantum gravity that rule it are like fire into which the
       water-impregnated wood is thrown. The fire boils the water
       out of the wood, leaving the wood alone and vulnerable; in the
       singularity, the laws of quantum gravity destroy time, leaving
       space alone and vulnerable. The fire then converts the wood
       into a froth of flakes and ashes; the laws of quantum gravity
       then convert space into a random, probabilistic froth.98

Physicists have given different names to quantum space-time foam.
Davies calls it ‘superspace.'99 Gribbin's designation for it is
‘superspacetime.' Willem B. Drees names it, ‘Mother Spacetime.' At its
deepest level, super-spacetime is foamy or granular rather than smooth,
like the structure of ordinary space-time. Super-spacetime contains no
enduring quantum particles like electrons and protons.100 It is indeed the
quantum vacuum.101

3.3.2. The Quantum Vacuum

     We have reached the concept of quantum vacuum via quantum theory
of gravity. However, the concept of quantum vacuum - empty space -- has
a story of its own. It is in fact the appalling saga of Newtonian empty
space transformed into the ‘plenum' or fullness -the vacuum. The essential
characteristic of vacuum is that it is ever ‘fluctuating' - creating and
annihilating particles and anti-particles.
3.3.2.1. Empty Space as Quantum Vacuum

     Newtonian space is empty in the literal sense of the term - the
emptiest possible entity.102 Space was regarded as the homogeneous
medium existing objectively and independently of its physical content,
and its rigid and timeless structure was described by the axioms and
theorems of Euclidean geometry. This self-sufficiency of space and its
independence from matter were clearly formulated by Newton in his
Principia Mathematica: “Absolute space, in its own nature, without regard
to anything external, remains always similar and immovable.”103 Space,
according to Newtonian mechanism, is the passive container of all matter;
it affects matter in no way, nor is it affected by matter. It is pure and
empty, and is characterized by the property of homogeneity. This means
that space has the same quality always and everywhere. From
homogeneity follows the causal inertness or passivity of space. That is to
say, space is completely indifferent to all changes in the universe; space
does not initiate any change, nor do changes influence it. Such
homogeneous space is smooth and continuous. Space is correlated with
another absolute -the Newtonian time -- with most of the properties of
space, except that time is succession whereas space is side-by-sidedness.
Space and time are absolute and they constitute the background of all
changes in the universe.

     Einstein's theory of relativity completely transformed Newtonian
concepts of space (and time) in two steps. First, the special theory unified
space and time into space-time, thus rendering space and time relative to
each other, and stripping them of their independent status. However, like
Newton's space and time, spacetime of special relativity constitutes the
background of all events in the universe. Space-time and physical bodies
of the universe are independent of each other. Second, in the general
theory, spacetime is rendered relative to mass/energy. There is no
independent space-time apart from mass/energy. Physical reality is space-
time-mass/energy. Whereas Newtonian space was independent of matter,
according to the general theory, there is an intimate relation between
space-time and mass/energy. Capra beautifully summarizes the
transformations that have taken place in the concepts of space and time
and mass/energy in the theory of relativity:

       In the general theory of relativity, the classical concepts of
       space and time as absolute and independent entities are
       completely abolished. Not only are all measurements
       involving space and time relative, depending on the state of
       motion of the observer, but the whole structure of space-time
       is inextricably linked to the distribution of matter. Space is
       curved to different degrees, and time flows at different rates
       in different parts of the universe. We have thus come to
       apprehend that our notions of a threedimensional Euclidean
       space and of linear flowing time are limited to our ordinary
       experience of the physical world and have to be completely
       abandoned when we extend this experience.104

      John Wheeler's famous statement expresses the intimate relation
between space-time and mass/energy: “Space-time tells matter how to
move; matter tells space-time how to curve.” Evidently, the theory of
relativity rendered Newtonian, inert space dynamic, first by unifying it
with time in the special theory, and then by incorporating spacetime with
mass/energy in the general theory. However, in spite of such radical
transformations of Newtonian concept of space (and of time), the theory of
relativity carried over certain of its suppositions, namely, its smooth and
continuous character. Peat remarks: “While Einstein revolutionized the
Newtonian concept of space and time, he nevertheless continued to assume
that space is continuous. That is, the properties of space continue
unchanged to smaller and smaller scales.”105

      The advent of quantum mechanics, and in particular quantum field
theories, heralded a radical revolution in the conception of space.
Newtonian empty space and Einsteinian dynamic space-time were
transformed beyond all recognition. It is now called ‘quantum vacuum
state' or simply ‘quantum vacuum.' The vacuum is usually thought of as a
region of space from which everything has been removed -- all particles,
all waves, all fields. According to quantum field theories, even if a region
of space can in principle be emptied of all matter, this vacuum is neither
empty nor quiescent. Smolin tells us the reason:

       If something is exactly still, it has a definite position and
       momentum, and this contradicts the uncertainty principle,
       which says that you cannot know both these things about a
       particle. A consequence is that even when the temperature is
       zero, things keep moving. There is a small residual energy
       associated with any particle and any degree of freedom, even
       at zero temperature. This is called vacuum, or ground-state,
       energy.106 Consider, for instance, a volume of space in a small
       box; empty it of all matter, and remove all photons or
       elementary particles within it. We might think of it as pure,
       empty space. However, Heisenberg's uncertainty principle
       does not allow empty space to be featureless. Quantum
       physicists believe that such vacuum contains a residual energy
       that persists even when all matter and fields have been
       experimentally eliminated. Calculations show that this
       residual energy is enormous. “In one cubic centimetre of
       empty space the amount of energy is much greater than the
       total amount of energy of all the matter in the known
       universe!”107 Since there is no known means of removing it, it
       would be truly the energy of empty space, of vacuum itself.
       This energy density is not confined to regions of pure vacuum;
       but it is present throughout the whole of space, including
       regions where matter and other forms of energy are present. In
       this sense, the energy of the vacuum appears to be a vast
       omnipresent ocean of infinite energy; and all other forms of
       energy — thermal and gravitational, for instance -- appear as
       the thinnest of films upon the surface of this fathomless sea.
       Such energy appears to be an inherent property of space itself.

     In this way, quantum physicists arrived at a radically revolutionized
concept of space -- the vacuum -- that is not empty; rather, it is plenum -
fullness -- of all forces and fields. Hence, the classical, Newtonian empty
space free of all matter and fields is not physically realizable. Pagels
speaks about quantum vacuum in different ways: “Instead of ‘Nature
abhors a vacuum' the view of the new physics suggests, ‘The vacuum is all
of physics.' Everything that ever existed or can exist is already potentially
there in the nothingness of space.”108 Then he states: “Almost everything is
vacuum.”109 Expressing it ironically, Pagels says that “it [vacuum] is not
empty; it is a plenum.”110 In quantum field theories, the quantum vacuum
is defined as the quantum state of lowest possible energy. Generally, it
includes no physical particles although it still contains quantum fields.
Indeed, at the quantum level of description, vacuum is the dominant
structure. What we call particles are only minor disturbances bubbling up
in this sea of vacuum.

      In order to get an idea of the physical nature of quantum vacuum, we
zoom in sequentially, and magnify ever smaller regions of the spatial
fabric with a hypothetical super-microscope; thereby we examine space
and its properties at microscopic dimensions. (See Figure 16) We start
zooming from the scale of our bodies (hundreds of cms) which is the first
level. The second level is the size of bacteria (10-5 to 2 10 5 cm). The third
is the magnitude of an atom (10-8 cm). The fourth level is the scale of an
atomic nucleus (10-13 cm). Further zooming takes us to the fifth level, to
10-33 cm which is twenty powers of 10 less than atomic nucleus. As we
zoom in, at the primary levels of magnification -- the first, second, third
and fourth levels -- the structure of space retains the same basic form. At
these levels, space would appear completely continuous and smooth with
tiny amounts of curvature. This would be Newtonian-Einsteinian flat space
- the continuum. From the classical point of view, we would expect this
flat image of space to persist all the way to the smallest length. However,
as the microscopic zooming nears, and then passes 10-32 cm, one would
observe space beginning to writhe, ever so slightly at first, and then more
and more violently until, at a region just 10-33 cm in size, space would turn
seething with enormous energy. This fifth level is indeed the Planck length
of 10-33 cm. Such a spatial dimension is small beyond our wildest
imagination: it is a millionth of a billionth of a billionth of a billionth of a
centimeter -magnitudes at the level of quantum vacuum. To get a sense of
this size, we magnify an atom to the size of the known universe; then the
Planck length would barely be the height of an average tree.
     However, as we recede from the level of Planck size back to more
ordinary distances in reverse direction, the random, violent small scale
undulations cancel each other out and the smooth space of the fabric of the
universe once again becomes apparent. It is like looking at a dot-matrix
picture. From far away, the dots which compose the picture blend together
and create the impression of a smooth image. But when we inspect the
picture on finer distance scales, we realize that it markedly differs from its
smooth, longdistance appearance. It is nothing but a collection of discrete
dots, each quite separate from the others. We become aware of the discrete
nature of the picture only when we examine it on smallest scales; but seen
from a distance, it looks smooth and continuous. Similarly, the fabric of
space-time appears smooth except when examined on ultra-microscopic
scales. This is why the general theory of relativity works on large enough
distance (and time) scales — the scales relevant for many typical
astronomical phenomena — but it is not applicable on shorter distance
scales and shorter intervals of time. Greene says: “The central tenet of a
smooth and gently curving geometry is justified in the large but breaks
down due to quantum fluctuations when pushed to the small.”111 Such is
the regime of quantum vacuum.112
   Figure 16. Sequential magnification of a region of space
    [Courtesy: Brian Greene, The Elegant Universe (figure
                         modified)]
      Incidentally, there is a very interesting subplot to the story of
quantum vacuum. In a strange twist in the history of physics, particle
physicists found that quantum vacuum has some affinity with the cosmic
repulsive force represented by Einstein's cosmological constant. His
cosmological model of the universe required a constant which represented
such a repulsive force. Like the vacuum, this force is also the energy of
empty space. When Einstein applied his general theory of relativity to the
universe, he found that solutions to the equations suggested that space was
either expanding or contracting, but not static. In order to make his model
of the universe static, Einstein proposed an ‘anti-gravity' or cosmic
repulsive force that would exactly balance the attractive force of gravity of
all matter in the universe. The cosmological constant inserted into the
equations represents this repulsive force. It is designated by the Greek
letter ‘A' ‘lambda.' Hawking comments:

       Einstein introduced the new ‘antigravity' force, which, unlike
       other forces, did not come from any particular source, but was
       built into the very fabric of space-time. He claimed that
       spacetime has an inbuilt tendency to expand, and this could be
       made to balance exactly the attraction of all matter in the
       universe, so that a static universe would result.113

The cosmological constant describes the strength of repulsive force. It
would be zero on small scales, but would increase as a function of
distance. Such a force is extremely small on the scale of the solar system,
but it produces a huge cosmic repulsion on a large scale.114

     However, from the perspective of quantum physics, the cosmic
repulsive force can be produced by the energy density of quantum vacuum.
Paul Davies says: “The key to understanding the re-discovered cosmic
repulsion is the nature of the quantum vacuum.”115 Although a region of
space can in principle be emptied of all matter, as we saw, this vacuum
contains a residual energy. For, Heisenberg's uncertainty principle does not
permit empty space -- the vacuum -- to be totally quiescent. Rather, it is
believed to be a seething sea of ‘vacuum fluctuations.' The various fields
that inhabit the universe are subject to continuous quantum fluctuations
that give an energy density even to nominally empty space. The vacuum
energy of empty space is thus a direct function of all fields and particles in
the universe. The vacuum energy density is assigned a value proportional
to its strength; this value represents the cosmological constant from the
point of view of quantum physics. It is not a simple quantity, but it is a
composite value that contains a very large number of contributing factors.
In this way, the general theory of relativity and quantum physics admit the
energy of empty space — of the quantum vacuum.116

      Now comes the greatest twist in the story of quantum vacuum. Unlike
the cosmic repulsive force, the energy of vacuum is enormously gigantic!
Davies says: “The quantum vacuum behaves exactly like the previously
hypothetical medium which produces cosmic repulsion, only this time the
numbers are so big that the strength of the repulsive force is 10120 times
greater than Einstein needed to prop up a static universe.”117 Similarly,
Weinberg remarks: “But, with any reasonable guess . . . , the vacuum
energy per volume comes out to be enormously large: it is about trillion
trillion trillion trillion trillion trillion trillion trillion trillion trillion times
larger than is allowed by the observed rate of expansion of the
universe.”118 All studies on the behaviour of elementary particles and
energy fields existing in the universe predict that the value of
cosmological constant is extremely high — about 10120 times bigger than
its actual value based on the data of the expansion of the universe. In other
words, astronomical observations of the rate of expansion of the universe
show that the cosmological constant is amazingly small — smaller by a
factor of 10-120 than the predicted energy of the quantum vacuum.119
Smolin opines: “Quantum theory - in particular, the uncertainty principle -
appeared to require a huge cosmological constant.”120 Although there is
such an enormous disparity between the values of cosmological constant
and the quantum vacuum, physicists agree “that quantum theory had
something to say about the cosmological constant.”121 Davies concludes:
“Both relativity theory and quantum theory, for different reasons and in
different ways, unequivocally declare that empty space, or the vacuum,
has structure.”122 Truly, these theories do agree on the energy of the empty
space, that is, of quantum vacuum.

     Indeed, the metamorphosis of Newtonian empty space into the
fullness of quantum vacuum is far beyond physicists' wildest dreams:
from empty space through space-time warpage to quantum vacuum. We
conclude with astronomer J. McKim Malville:

       We have progressed an awesome distance from that passive
       and flat emptiness [Newtonian space] which used to lie
       between the atomic things of the world. The things are no
       longer things and the void is no longer empty. That inter-
       atomic void, free of matter and energy, once looked as smooth
       and featureless as the unblemished surface of a quiet pond.
       But now it appears that below its tranquil surface there
       simmers all the violence of the larger world, reduced in scale
       and increased in frequency.123

3.3.2.2. Quantum Vacuum Fluctuation

      The essential property of the quantum vacuum is its fluctuation -
quantum fluctuation. The vacuum is nothing if it does not fluctuate. It is
not a passive entity like Newton's empty space; the latter is eternally at
rest. However, in quantum mechanics, Heisenberg's uncertainty principle
does not permit empty space -- the vacuum -- to be at rest; it fluctuates -
even violently at shorter distances and smaller time intervals. It is
regarded as a turbulent whole of fluctuations and a ferment of quantum
activity. John Wheeler says: “No point is more central than this, that
empty space is not empty. It is the seat of the most violent physics.”124
Such restless vacuum activity goes on all around us, including in the space
between atoms of ordinary matter. Apparently, inert vacuum is a sea of
frenetic activity, full of ghostly particles which appear, interact and
vanish.
      We obtain a preliminary notion of vacuum fluctuation by comparing
it with Newtonian dynamics. Consider, for instance, a very elementary
physical system -- the simple pendulum. According to Newton's
mechanics, its vacuum state or its lowest energy state would be just that in
which the pendulum is at rest and at bottom. (Newtonian law of inertia
allows it). But if it is a ‘quantum pendulum' (empty space or vacuum in
the present context), Heisenberg's uncertainty principle does not permit it
to be at rest; for then we would both know where the quantum pendulum is
(at bottom), and what it is doing (it is at rest). If empty space is exactly
still, it has a definite position and momentum; and this violates the
uncertainty principle which states that we cannot know both these
quantities about a quantum state at the same time. Hence, even in the most
quiescent setting imaginable, there would be a tremendous amount of
activity going on in an empty region of space or vacuum; and this activity
gets increasingly violent and agitated at ever smaller distance and time
scales. This is called ‘quantum fluctuation' which is a temporary change in
the state of vacuum, allowed by uncertainty principle; and in fluctuating,
the vacuum ‘officiates' at the creation of particles and anti-particles, and
their mutual annihilation. “A vacuum fluctuation consists of a particle and
its anti-particle springing into virtual existence at a point in space and then
immediately annihilating each other.”125 In vacuum fluctuation, particles
and anti-particles are created from the energy of the vacuum; and in an
extremely short period of time, they mutually collide and convert
themselves into energy according to Einstein's equation of mass/energy
conversion E = mc2. Capra writes: “The relation between the virtual
particles and the vacuum is an essentially dynamic relation; the vacuum is
truly a ‘living Void,' pulsating in endless rhythms of creation and
destruction.”126

     In fact, one of the first successes of relativistic quantum field theory
was the prediction of the creation and annihilation of particles and anti-
particles. David Bohm says: “A quantum field theory can account for the
observed, well-studied creation and annihilation of particles and [anti-
particles], processes that do not exist in quantum mechanics.”127 The
existence of anti-particles was predicted by the great quantum physicist
Paul Dirac. He invented a relativistic wave equation (which now bears his
name) that is obeyed by the electron. When Dirac solved his equation, he
found that besides describing an electron, the equation had additional
solutions that described another particle with an electric charge opposite
to that of electron. At the time Dirac made this observation, the only
known particle with this property was proton. Since he did not want to
proliferate the number of particles, Dirac suggested that the additional
solutions to his equation referred to proton. But after more careful
investigation, it was found that the particle in question has precisely the
same mass as that of electron; this ruled out proton which has a mass of
about 1,836 times the electron's mass. Hence, the particle had to be a
completely new one with the same mass as that of electron, but with
opposite charge — an anti-electron. This particle, now called ‘positron,'
was experimentally discovered by Carl Anderson in 1932 in cosmic rays
raining down on the Earth. Pagels comments: “Soon it was realized by
physicists that the Dirac equation, in implying the existence of the anti-
electron, [positron] was offering but one specific example of the general
consequence of combining quantum theory with the principle of relativity
-- namely, the existence of antimatter.”128

     In general, quantum vacuum fluctuation consists in the creation of
particles and anti-particles out of the vacuum and their annihilation. The
key to this interaction is Heisenberg's uncertainty principle. Malville
explains:

       Even a perfect vacuum, free of all matter and energy, contains
       fluctuations of fields and particles. Quantum uncertainty
       allows small amounts of energy to appear out of the vacuum,
       provided they disappear in a very short time. This energy may
       take the form of short-lived pairs of particles and anti-
       particles, for example an electron-positron pair. Out of the
       vacuum electrons, positrons, and photons and their anti-
       particles emerge spontaneously, existing for an extremely
       short time before they annihilate each other.129

Similarly, Greene says:
       Quantum uncertainty implies that the energy in the field -even
       in an empty region of space - fluctuates up and down, with the
       size of fluctuations getting larger as the distance and time
       scales on which the region is examined gets smaller. The
       energy embodied in such momentary field fluctuations can
       then, through E = mc2, be converted into momentary creation
       of pairs of particles and their antiparticles, which annihilate
       each other in great haste, to keep the energy from changing,
       on average.130

     Now in the case of quantum fluctuation, the uncertainty principle
concerns the mass of a virtual particle and the length of time that it can
remain in existence. Heavy particles which need more energy for their
creation exist only for an extremely short period of time, whereas lighter
particles requiring less energy for their production survive for a longer
time. For example, lighter virtual electronpositron pair will survive for
about 10-21 sec before the two particles disappear in mutual destruction.
Heavier virtual proton and virtual anti-proton, on the other hand, will
vanish after 10-24 sec.131 Creations and annihilations of particles and anti-
particles take place in the case of all forms of fluctuations - of
electromagnetic, weak and strong force-field fluctuations. Such
fluctuations have been confirmed by countless experiments. Physicists
have observed such quantum fluctuations in laboratory down to distances
of about 10-16 cm and over times of about 10-26 sec.




        Figure 17. Annihilation of electron-positron pair
      The figure above is a Feynman diagram of the collision of an electron
and a positron. An electron on left side approaches a positron which is
coming from right side. At their point of contact at the center, they
mutually annihilate each other, and two photons (marked by broken lines)
are created which depart in opposite directions at the speed of light. In
fact, this unceasing dance of creation and annihilation ever goes on among
subatomic particles.

      However, this process of quantum fluctuation involving the creation
and annihilation of particles and anti-particles apparently violates one of
the most fundamental laws of classical physics, namely, the law of the
conservation of energy. This law states in effect that the quantity of energy
in the universe remains constant; and that the total amount of energy is
preserved even though its spatio-temporal distribution changes. This
means that “no quantity of energy in the world can arise out of nothing,
but only from the transformation of a previous energetic equivalent” and
that “no particular quantity of energy can disappear without being
transformed into its equivalent.”132 In other words, energy cannot be
created or destroyed. Now, let us imagine that this law of energy
conservation is checked by an energy accountant who keeps track of the
total energy of physical interactions in the universe. The accountant's
ledger records energy incomes and energy expenses in two columns, and
both columns have to balance exactly. For example, the chemical energy
stored in petroleum can be transformed into heat and motion of cars. There
should be an exact balancing of energy on both sides in this process. In
this way, an energy balance sheet would account for all the
transformations of chemical energy and all other forms of energy. That is
the requirement of the law of conservation of energy. Thus according to
this law, energy is neither created nor destroyed but rigorously conserved,
merely being converted from one form to another.

     Now, if according to the law of energy conservation, energy is neither
created nor destroyed, how then can we explain the creation and
annihilation of particles and anti-particles in quantum vacuum
fluctuation? Heisenberg's uncertainty principle provides us the answer.
This principle can suspend the law of conservation of energy for an
extremely short period of time; and this is the consequence of uncertainty
relation between energy and time. Accordingly, there is an uncertainty in
the measurement of the conjugate quantities of energy and time. If we
know the energy state of a particle with precision, then we are not certain
about the amount of time it is likely to remain in that state. Conversely, if
we know precisely the length of time a particle has been in that state, we
would be uncertain about the energy needed to remain in that state. Hence,
there is a limit to the accuracy with which we can measure energy of the
quantum state of a particle within a given interval of time. Such
uncertainty between energy and time implies that the law of the
conservation of energy can be suspended for a very short period of time;
and the uncertainty of time interval can be ‘exploited' for different energy
needs. During this brief duration, energy can be ‘borrowed' for different
purposes, for instance, to create particles and anti-particles. They emerge
by borrowing energy from the vacuum, and within an extremely short
time, they ‘pay the debt back' by mutual annihilation and disappear. That
is to say, the uncertainty between energy and time allows a system's
energy to go into ‘debt,' provided the debt is rapidly ‘paid back' within the
limits of time permitted by uncertainty principle. Thus, energy can be
borrowed for creating particles and anti-particles if it is paid back by their
mutual annihilation within an extremely short period of time. Particles and
anti-particles so produced will be short-lived because the energy tied up in
them has to be ‘repaid' after a very minute fraction of a second. Since
these particles do not have a permanent existence, they are called ‘virtual'
particles.

     The quantum process of creation and annihilation of particles and
anti-particles can be illustrated by the example of a compulsive borrower
who goes from friend to friend asking for money. The shorter the time for
which a friend can lend him/her money, the larger the loan s/he seeks.
Borrow and return, borrow and return; over and over again with untiring
intensity, s/he takes in money only to give it back in shorter and shorter
intervals as the amount of money s/he seeks gets bigger and bigger. Like
wild fluctuations of stock prices in share market on a roller-coaster day,
the amount of money the compulsive borrower possesses at any given
moment goes through extreme fluctuations. But when all his/her
borrowing and paying back is over, an accounting of his/her finances
shows that s/he is no better off than when s/he began. The borrower's
financial condition is safe even if s/he had borrowed a big sum since s/he
had paid back within the briefest period of time. Similar wild fluctuations
are occurring constantly at microscopic distances and time intervals. The
uncertainty principle permits energy to be borrowed from the quantum
vacuum provided the debt is rapidly paid back. Such is the vacuum
fluctuation of creation and annihilation of particles and anti-particles.
Richard Feynman once jested about this frantic process of quantum
fluctuation: “Created and annihilated, created and annihilated — what a
waste of time.”133

     Since the creation and destruction of particles on the average cancel
each other out, an empty region of space looks calm and placid when
examined except on microscopic scale. Further, in our everyday
experience, space appears empty only because vacuum fluctuations take
place over extremely short time and distance scales. Over longer distances
and times, the vacuum appears serene and smooth — like an ocean which
appears quite smooth when we fly high above it in an aircraft. But if we
travel in a small boat on the surface of the ocean observing it at close
range, we see big waves, high and fluctuating. Malville writes:

       Thus, superimposed upon the large scale geometry of space of
       familiar experience are random quantum fluctuations like
       small ripples on large waves. In everyday circumstances, the
       fluctuations in the geometry of space and time are not
       noticeable; only at a submicroscopic scale do these
       fluctuations become significant.134

Thus, the vacuum fluctuation of creation and destruction of quantum
