particles is observable only at extremely short distances and times. Even
at the level of atoms, these fluctuations are exceedingly small, though
observable. The electromagnetic vacuum fluctuations are well understood
and are confirmed by experiments.
      However, gravitational vacuum fluctuations have not yet been
observed experimentally. Because it is an extremely weak force -by far the
weakest of the four forces of nature — gravity does not manifest its
quantum nature at macroscopic domains, or at the scale of atoms or even
of atomic nuclei where quantum properties of other forces are apparent.
Gravitational fluctuations become manifest only at the Planck length of
10-33 cm and the Planck time of 10-43 sec. These fluctuations involve the
quantization of gravitational field since at these Planck scales quantum
effects begin to be felt on account of Heisenberg's uncertainty relation.
The quantization of gravity means the quantum mechanical treatment of
space-time regime because the general theory of relativity describes
gravitation as the curvature of space-time. Greene speaks about the severe
effects on space at Planck scales wrought about by the fluctuations of
spacetime fabric: “The notion of a smooth spatial geometry, the central
principle of general relativity, is destroyed by the violent fluctuations of
the quantum world on short distance scales.”135 Such vacuum fluctuations
of space-time fabric take on frothing, turbulent, twisted forms in which
millions of tiny ‘bubbles' with different shapes are continually ‘bursting'
as new ones are formed. At these extremely small Planck dimensions, the
geometry of space is not well-defined; instead, it has probability for any
conceivable form. Indeed, this is the regime of quantum gravity.

     As we come to the end of the present chapter, “The Quantum Field
Theories,” we make some observations on the immutability/ mutability of
matter occasioned by these theories and Dirac's discovery of the creation
and annihilation of particles and antiparticles. Throughout the centuries
and even in modern times, matter was thought to be unchangeable and
immutable. Molecules could be altered, and atoms could decay by
radioactive processes, but the fundamental particles were believed to be
unchanging. Before the invention of relativistic quantum field theories, the
fundamental constituents of matter were considered to be elementary units
which were indestructible and unchangeable. The basic question was
whether one could divide matter again and again indefinitely, or whether
one would finally arrive at some smallest indivisible units. This scenario
is bound to remain paradoxical as long as one adopts the static view of
composite objects as consisting of basic building blocks. Only when seen
from the dynamic view of relativistic quantum field theories does the
paradox disappear.

     With the advent of these theories and with Dirac's discovery of the
creation and annihilation of particles and anti-particles, the whole question
of the division of matter into smaller particles appears in a new light. The
only way to divide subatomic particles further is to smash them together
in collision processes employing high energy particle accelerators. In this
way, we can divide particles again and again, but we never obtain smaller
pieces because we just create particles out of the energy involved in the
process. Thus, when two particles collide with high energies, they
generally break into pieces, but these pieces are not smaller than original
particles. They are created out of the energy of motion (kinetic energy)
involved in collision, and also are of the same kind as original particles. In
such a scenario, the energy of two colliding particles is redistributed to
form a new pattern, and if there is sufficient amount of kinetic energy, this
new pattern may produce additional particles. Most of the particles created
in these collisions exist for only an extremely short time after which they
disintegrate again. In spite of their exceedingly short life time, these
particles can be detected in cloud chambers and their properties measured;
they actually leave their tracks which can be photographed.

     When, for instance, highly energetic cosmic rays hit the atmosphere
of the Earth, they collide with the nuclei of air molecules and produce a
great variety of secondary particles which either decay or undergo further
collisions, thus creating more particles which collide and decay again until
the last of them reach the Earth. The same phenomena can be observed in
the collision experiments of high energy physics. The high energy
scattering experiments of the past decades have shown us the dynamic and
ever-changing nature of the particle world in the most striking way. Capra
rightly remarks: “Matter has appeared in these experiments as completely
mutable. All particles can be transmuted into other particles; they can be
created from energy and can vanish into energy.”136 Indeed, quantum
physics has taught us the great truth about the mutability of matter: that
subatomic particles are fundamentally mutable. Pagels aptly says: “The
mutability of matter became the cornerstone of the new particle
physics.”137 The whole problem of dividing matter is thus resolved in an
unexpected manner.

    We conclude the present chapter with a quote from the great
Heisenberg who beautifully summarizes the paradigm shift in physics
from immutability of matter to its mutability:

       I believe that the discovery of particles and antiparticles by
       Dirac has changed our whole outlook on atomic physics. . . .
       Up to that time I think every physicist had thought of the
       elementary particles along the lines of the philosophy of
       Democritus, namely by considering these elementary particles
       as unchangeable units which are just given in nature and are
       always the same thing, they never change, they never can be
       transmuted into anything else. They are not dynamical
       systems, they just exist in themselves. After Dirac's discovery
       everything looked different, because one could ask, why
       should a proton not sometimes be a proton plus a pair of
       electron and positron and so on? . . . Thereby the problem of
       dividing matter had come into a different light.138
                                      4
 THE STANDARD MODEL OF PARTICLE PHYSICS
       If paradise is the state of ultimate and perfect symmetry, the
       history of the ‘big bang' resembles that of ‘paradise lost' . . .
       The result is the varied universe of broken symmetry that now
       surrounds us.

                       –John Barrow and Joseph Silk,
                         The Left hand of Creation

     The quantum field theories discussed in the previous chapter --
quantum       electrodynamics,      electroweak      theory    and    quantum
chromodynamics - attempt to unify the special theory of relativity with
quantum physics; and the quantum theory of gravity proposes to integrate
the general theory with quantum mechanics. These theories interpret the
forces of nature in terms of particle interactions. These particles include
exchange particles or force-carriers corresponding to every field -- virtual
photons, vector bosons, coloured gluons and supposed gravitons. These are
all virtual particles. Then there are the real, matter particles and their
composites at different levels of physical reality -- atoms, nuclei, hadrons,
right down to quarks and leptons. The quantum world is indeed a particle
zoo. In order to unify these myriad particles and diverse interactions, the
finest quantum physicists in the world had been working hard for decades.
Although they have not yet achieved the final goal of complete unification,
they have proposed a comprehensive model which maps all particles and
their interactions except gravity. It is called ‘the Standard Model of Particle
Physics.' Its present configuration is consistent with, and is confirmed by,
experiments.

4.1. Towards the Standard Model

    The phenomena of nature are distributed over an extremely wide range
of magnitudes — clusters of galaxies, individual galaxies, stars, planets,
living organisms, cells, molecules, atoms and subatomic particles. These
enormous varieties of physical objects are all interrelated and inter-
connected in numerous ways. Physicists tell us that they are constituted by
four elementary particles and four fundamental forces. Our universe and
the cosmic objects therein, from galaxies to atoms, are all given their basic
structure and shape by these subatomic particles which interact with each
other through a different mix of basic forces. These particles are protons,
neutrons, electrons and neutrinos, and the forces are gravitational,
electromagnetic, weak and strong nuclear forces.1 The incredible variety of
the phenomena of nature is apparently the magnificent outcome of the
workmanship of these particles and forces. Now, we may ask, whether the
four forces are just manifestations of a ‘super-force'? Could it be that the
manifold elementary particles and their composite atoms are all
expressions of a ‘super-particle'? Intriguingly, may the super-force and the
super-particle be ultimately unified into a grand totality? Such dreams of
particle physicists to discover ultimate unity in the diversity of
fundamental particles and their interactions have been the source of much
progress in contemporary physics; and it is currently the final goal of their
research. The greatest outcome of this dream is the Standard Model of
particle physics. It represents a great leap forward in our understanding and
comprehension of physical reality. This model succeeds, at least partly, in
unifying and integrating various particles and forces of nature.

     The Standard Model is a remarkably simple and an exceptionally
successful conceptual framework in particle physics. It consists of complex
equations that allow theorists to make very precise predictions. The
predicted value of nearly every quantity of the Model has been confirmed
with stunning accuracy. It is thus the most rigorous theory in contemporary
physics, and it is astonishingly precise and accurate in its predictions. The
Standard Model is the end result of arduous and often frustrating,
theoretical and experimental work of hundreds of renowned particle
physicists around the world. The current formulation was finalized in the
mid-1970s when the existence of quarks was experimentally confirmed.
The history of the Standard Model actually begins in the 1930s with the
development of the physics of elementary particles, and with the discovery
that the different kinds of atomic nuclei are composed of protons and
neutrons. As larger particle accelerators were built and experiments
conducted over the next twenty years, many more fundamental particles
were discovered, each apparently as elementary as protons and neutrons
themselves. By the late 1950s, their count was in the hundreds. In the
1960s, physicists came to believe that most of these particles, including
protons and neutrons, are not elementary. Rather, they are composed of still
more fundamental entities, called ‘quarks.' The triumph of quark theory
was part of a revolution in particle physics which has taken place in the
early 1970s in the understanding of the forces that are at work inside
atomic nucleus. The confirmation of the existence of top quark (1995), of
tau neutrino (2000), and of the Higgs boson (2013) has rendered the
Standard Model more credible and acceptable. It has made intelligible a
great many different pieces of experimental data that had been
accumulating for several decades. In addition to describing and mapping
the particles and forces already discovered, this Model predicted with great
accuracy the properties of the W± and Zo bosons of electroweak theory, and
also the existence of the Higgs boson.

     The Standard Model is actually a set of mathematical formulations
and measurements describing and classifying elementary particles and
interactions between them. It is similar to the way the periodic table of
elements describes atoms, categorizing them on the basis of their
properties. The basic particles of matter are fermions which interact with
each other by exchanging other particles associated with the four
fundamental forces; these particles are the force-carriers, called ‘gauge
bosons.' Thus, the Standard Model classifies elementary particles into
fermions (matter particles) and bosons (force-carriers). Fermions come in
two families, namely, quarks and leptons. The quarks family contains six
quarks (up, down, top, bottom, charm and strange). The leptons family has
six members (electron, muon, tau, and each with their neutrinos), with
electron being the best known of them. The twelve fermions are the
building blocks of matter, and they interact with each other through
fundamental forces. Each force is mediated by one or more force-carriers.
There are four such force-carrying particles (virtual photon, W± and Zo
bosons and gluons); there is also the Higgs boson which confers mass on
particles. (See Table 1) The Standard Model classifies and describes the
properties of these elementary particles.

     The Standard Model of Particle Physics




                    Table 1. Fermions and Bosons
     Further, the Standard Model includes relativistic quantum field
theories which interpret electromagnetic, weak and strong nuclear forces in
terms of particle interactions. In accomplishing this feat, particle physicists
have invented electroweak theory and quantum chromodynamics which
unify the special theory of relativity and quantum mechanics. Pagels says:
“The electro-weak model when taken together (although not unified) with
quantum chromodynamics, the theory of the strong force, make up what
became known as ‘the standard model' of interacting quarks, leptons and
gluons.”2 The first is the Weinberg-Salam electroweak theory which is the
unification of electromagnetic and weak interactions. The second is
quantum chromodynamics which deals with strong quark-binding
interaction; and it explains how quarks bind themselves together to make
protons, neutrons and many other particles. Combined together, these two
quantum field theories can, in principle, account for everything we observe
in the physical world except those caused by gravity.
     The Standard Model is currently the most accurate theory about the
foundations of particle physics. It exhibits a wide range of fundamental
mechanisms of the quantum world, such as gauge symmetry and
spontaneous symmetry breaking. This Model is used as the basis for
constructing more exotic models, like the Grand Unified Theory and the
Theory of Everything which employ hypothetical particles, extra
dimensions and elaborate gauge symmetries. The Standard Model is
believed to be theoretically self-consistent; it has been confirmed by
experiments with stunning degree of accuracy, and has made experimental
predictions with great success. However, it leaves some quantum
phenomena unexplained and falls short of being a complete theory of
fundamental interactions. Hence, it cannot be considered a final theory (a
Theory of Everything) of particle physics. The Standard Model is
incomplete in three respects. First, it does not incorporate gravity and the
principles of the general theory of relativity. Second, the unification of
fields (even without gravity) in the Standard Model itself is incomplete.
Although electromagnetic and weak interactions are unified in electroweak
theory, the strong interaction is not unified with these. As we shall see, a
Grand Unified Theory (GUT) integrating these three interactions has yet to
be successful. Thirdly, the Standard Model employs many free parameters
and constants. No theory with many such parameters which can be freely
chosen can be considered a fundamental theory of nature.3

    We close ‘Towards the Standard Model,' with a memorable quote from
Smolin on the Standard Model of Particle Physics:

       The standard model of elementary particle physics stands as a
       monument to more than a century of continual discovery. In it
       is captured all of our knowledge of the very small,
       accumulated from the invention of electric and magnetic fields
       in the 1840s to the discovery of W and Z particles in the 1980s.
       If it has been difficult to improve on it, this is because it
       represents such a total triumph. In its imperfections, however,
       the standard model also forces us to confront all that was not
       understood during this long period.4
4.2. The Ingredients of the Standard Model

     The Standard Model, we said, deals with the classification and
properties of elementary particles and the dynamics of four interactions.
During the first decades of the 20th century, physicists have discovered five
levels of matter particles, specifically, the molecular, atomic, nuclear,
hadronic, and quark levels, and also leptons like electrons. Molecules are
composed of atoms, atoms made up of nuclei and electrons, nuclei consist
of hadrons, and finally hadrons are constituted by quarks. In general, these
are called ‘fermions.' Quantum field theories brought in also a bundle of
exchange particles or force-carriers which are called ‘gauge bosons.' This
classification of quantum particles into fermions and bosons is based on the
property of their spins. 5

4.2.1. Through Particle Spin to Particle Classification

     Fermions include all elementary particles, such as quarks and leptons
as well as all composite particles like baryons (for example, protons and
neutrons). Gauge bosons consist of exchange particles (photons, W± and Zo
vector bosons, gluons and hypothetical gravitons), and the Higgs boson.
Some composite particles, like mesons and stable nuclei of even mass
number, such as deuterium 2 and helium-4 are also considered bosons. This
classification of quantum particles into fermions and gauge bosons is based
on their spins. Spin is one of the three properties of a quantum particle like
an electron; others are its mass and charge. Spin is the intrinsic form of
angular momentum carried by elementary particles (quarks and leptons),
composite particles (hadrons like protons and neutrons) and atomic nuclei
(like deuterium and helium). Spins of these particles are analogous to the
spins of a macroscopic body, like a tennis ball. But the classical image of a
spinning tennis ball is not fully adequate to describe a spinning subatomic
particle. In some sense, the particle spin is a rotation about the particle's
own axis, but as always in quantum physics, this classical concept has a
limited sense. For, a quantum particle cannot generally be said to spin
about a definite axis; it merely has a certain tendency or potentiality to do
so, and it is the act of measurement that gives the particle a definite axis of
rotation. Further, in the case of an electron, the amount of spin is always
the same, but the electron can spin in one or the other direction, clockwise
or anti-clockwise, for a given axis of rotation. If the electron spins
clockwise on its axis, it is ‘spin-up;' and if it spins counter-clockwise, it is
‘spin-down.' Physicists thus denote these two values of spin by ‘up' and
‘down.'

     The spin of a quantum particle, in special units, takes only discrete
values of 0, 1, 3/2, 2, 5/2, 3 . . . That is, either an integer or a half-integer
value.6 Spin zero means that the top does not spin. Spin indicates a specific
amount of spin, and spin 1 signifies double of this amount and so on. Now,
the division of subatomic particles into fermions and bosons is based on
their spin number. All elementary particles of matter, such as quarks and
leptons, are fermions which possess half-integer spin, like 3/2, 5/2. All the
force-carrying particles (gauge bosons) have integer spins which are either
zero or a whole number - 0, 1, 2, 3, and so on. This is an important
distinction among elementary particles because each set of spinning
particles interacts with other particles very differently. The spin of the
particles deeply affects their properties, especially in the way these
particles behave collectively. For example, the total number of fermions
entering a reaction has to be equal to the total number leaving it; that is,
fermions are conserved. But no such conservation law applies to bosons.

      Further, particle spin is intimately related to the Pauli Exclusion
Principle. The electron spin, for example, can be inferred theoretically
from this principle. The Exclusion Principle was formulated for electrons
in 1925 by the great Austrian physicist Wolfgang Pauli, and later extended
it to all fermions in 1940. In general, this Principle states that two or more
identical fermions cannot occupy the same quantum state within a quantum
system simultaneously. Particles with half-integer spin like fermions obey
this principle. The Exclusion Principle is displayed by electrons which, as
they orbit nucleus, cannot all occupy the same energy state. Thus, not all
electrons in an atom remain in the lowest orbit; once an electron is in a
particular orbit, or in a quantum state, another electron cannot occupy the
same orbit or state. The exclusion of electrons results in mutual repulsions,
and explains why atoms do not collapse if squeezed together. However,
particles with an integer spin, like gauge bosons, are not subject to the
Exclusion Principle. Bosons differ significantly from fermions in so far as
any number of them can occupy the same quantum state. In a laser beam,
for example, countless photons occupy the same quantum state. There is no
limit to the number of photons which can occupy the same quantum state at
the same time.7

     The Standard Model presents the current classification of all
elementary particles: they are the grains of matter (fermions with half-
integer values of spin) and the force-carriers (bosons with integer values).
Fermions are further classified into quarks and leptons. There are six types
of quarks and six types of leptons. This balance “between the two families
is regarded as a significant confirmation of the validity of the standard
model.”8 Quarks combine to form the familiar protons and neutrons. A
proton, for example, is composed of one ‘down quark' and two ‘up quarks'
which are glued together by strong nuclear force. But the strong force does
not influence the second class of fermions, namely, leptons. Leptons
include electrons which hover round nuclei of atoms; other leptons are
electron-like particles, such as taus and muons, and extremely small
particles, namely, neutrinos. Quarks and leptons appear to be fundamental
point-like particles. Leptons, like electrons, can be directly detected, while
quarks exist only as bound up together in particles, like protons and
neutrons.

     According to the Standard Model, bosons constitute another class of
elementary particles which are not composed of smaller particles. These
include gauge bosons, the virtual particles which mediate the fundamental
forces. The Standard Model describes electromagnetic, weak and strong
interactions in terms of mediating gauge bosons. It recognizes four kinds of
gauge bosons: (virtual) photons, W± and Zo vector bosons and gluons.
Photon is the force-carrier of electromagnetic interaction. W bosons
(positive and negative charge) and Zo are gauge bosons mediating weak
nuclear force, and gluons are exchange particles of strong nuclear force.
Then there is the Higgs Boson, the particle that confers mass on quantum
particles. The force-carrier for gravity is supposedly graviton which is
predicted to have a spin value of 2. Moreover, if two quarks which are
fermions with half-integer spin value are bonded together, then the spin
value of the resulting particle (which is the sum of individual spins) would
be an integer, making it a boson. Some fermions, like mesons (for example,
pions and koans) are thus formed, and are also categorized as bosons.
Atoms, like helium-4 which contains 2 protons, 2 neutrons and 2 electrons
also have an integer spin. Hence, they are also considered as bosons. The
Standard Model gained widespread acceptance in the mid-1970s after the
experimental confirmation of quarks.9

4.2.2. The Particle Zoo

     Now, we go in for a detailed description of fermions and gauge bosons
which are basically different in their properties; and so they behave also
differently. Fermions, we said, are matter particles whereas gauge bosons
are exchange particles or force-carrying particles. The Higgs bosons confer
mass on all these particles.

4.2.2.1. Fermions

     Truly, fermions, like quarks and leptons, and their composites are all
that there is in the universe — the ultimate material ‘stuff' from which all
the complex physical objects emerge. They constitute atoms which have
two basic components -- nucleus at the center and the cloud of electrons
around it. While some particle physicists were interested in studying the
electronic properties of atoms, others turned their attention to the nucleus,
the heart of the atom. At first, nucleus, the primary component of atom was
a great puzzle to them. Physicists knew that the tiny core accounted for
most of the mass of atoms, and that it was the location of enormous energy
transformation, as evidenced by radioactivity, that is, the emission of
particles from nucleus. The discovery of proton as a component of nucleus
is credited to the New Zealand physicist Ernest Rutherford. In the year
1917, he proved that the nucleus of hydrogen atom -a proton -- is present in
the nuclei of all other atoms. Then James Chadwick, an English physicist in
1932, discovered another particle; he called it ‘neutron' which was similar
to proton in its properties except that it had no electric charge. With this
breakthrough, it was clear that atomic nuclei were composed of two major
particles — protons and neutrons -- which were bound together into a tiny
region by immense nuclear forces.

      By probing the nucleus with high energy beams of particles, physicists
discovered that protons and neutrons in the nucleus have a definite
structure. These particles arrange themselves into shells, and they jump
from shell to shell just as electrons jump from orbit to orbit around the
nucleus, and release energy. The shifting of positions by protons and
neutrons inside each nucleus signals transitions from one energy level to
another. The observed energy levels of nuclei were hundreds of times
greater than those of electrons swarming around the nucleus. This meant
that the force which binds protons and neutrons together into a tight little
nucleus is many times stronger than the electromagnetic force that binds
electrons to nucleus. In the year 1935, Hideki Yukawa, a Japanese physicist,
discovered this strong nuclear force. He reasoned that just as the
electromagnetic force which binds electrons to the nucleus has an
associated quantum particle (photon), so the strong force also should have
an associated particle; its interaction with protons and neutrons has to be
hundreds of times stronger than photon's interaction with electrons.
Further, the nuclear force associated with this new particle has to act
strongly over only a very short distance because the nucleus is so tiny.
Yukawa concluded that the particle associated with strong nuclear force
was also massive (about 200 times more massive than electron). He called
this particle a ‘meson.' Meson's existence was verified in later experiments.

     Now, just as the electromagnetic force holds together molecules, so
strong nuclear force holds tight hadrons which are subatomic particles
composed of two or more quarks.10 Most of the mass of ordinary matter
comes from two hadrons, namely, proton and neutron in the nucleus of
atoms. These particles are relatively stable in comparison with other
hadrons. Protons and neutrons bind together to build up all atomic nuclei in
the visible universe. Experiments in particle accelerators conducted in the
1960s employing high energy beams of protons to bombard other protons
revealed the existence of hadrons. When a proton beam collides with other
target protons, energy of protons in the beam creates new forms of matter,
namely, hadrons. Neutron and pion were the first hadrons which were thus
discovered. Physicists believe that there is an infinite number of hadrons,
most of them highly unstable, disintegrating in less than a billion billionth
of a second into more stable hadrons. Smashing protons with high energy
proton beam, as was done at particle accelerator at CERN, Geneva, did not
reveal a simpler structure. Instead, it produced still more hadrons. It was
evident from these experiments that hadrons have a definite extension in
space unlike electrons which seem to behave like point-particles. Hadrons,
like protons, were visualized as little balls of bound energy. The hadron-
balls had properties like spin and electric charge, but their interior region
was an unknown territory. The bag model of hadrons successfully accounts
for many observed features of hadrons. It gives an intuitive picture of
hadron structure.

     Now, what is the ‘stuff' of hadrons? “The answer to the riddle of the
hadrons is that the hadrons are quark ‘molecules.'”11 Murray Gell-Mann,
and independently George Zweig, proposed that hadrons were composed of
more fundamental particles which Gell-Mann called ‘quarks.' Quarks are
elementary particles which combine to form composite particles called
‘hadrons;' the most stable hadrons are protons and neutrons, the
components of atomic nuclei. Hadrons are visualized as tiny bags with
point-like quarks, trapped permanently inside. Each hadron can be viewed
as made up of a few quarks orbiting around each other in a specific
configuration. Since quarks can orbit in an infinite variety of different
configurations, there is actually an infinite number of hadrons. Thus,
quarks are the constituents of hadrons, and they interact through strong
force. They are point-particles similar to electrons with the same spin
value. However, they have only a fractional electric charge compared to
electron's one unit of charge, and unlike an electron, no one has ever
observed a quark. There are six types of quarks, as we have mentioned, and
these types are known as ‘flavours' of quarks. The flavours are up, down,
strange, charm, top and bottom. (See Table 2) There are also anti-particles
of quarks -- anti-quarks. Quantum chromodynamics mathematically
describes how quarks bind together so tightly that they become
permanently confined to hadrons.

                    units of the electron's        units of proton charge
                    mass
Up
quark          u                   2                          2/3
Down
quark          d                   6                          -1/3

quark          c                3000                          2/3

Top
quark          t                   ?                          2/3

                            Table 2. Quarks
     Physicists first detected the presence of quarks probing the interiors of
hadrons, like protons and neutrons, employing high energy accelerators.
However, quarks have never been observed as free particles; they seem to
exist only when they are bound together in the form of hadrons. Physicists
have looked for quarks in laboratory experiments, cosmic rays and other
physical states, but without success. If they try to break a hadron apart into
its quarks, like splitting a molecule into atoms, they do not get quarks, but
just more hadrons. The quark model is actually a mathematical ‘fiction'
that somehow works. Most physicists today believe that quarks will never
be directly observed, and that they are permanently trapped inside hadrons.
Quarks do exist, but they always seem to be bound together with other
quarks so tightly that one cannot pull them apart. Sheldon Glashow makes
the following observation about the scientific search for the ultimate
structure of matter:

       If this interpretation of quark confinement is correct, it
       suggests an ingenious way to terminate the apparently infinite
       regression of finer structure in matter. Atoms can be analyzed
       into electrons and nuclei, nuclei into protons and neutrons, and
       protons and neutrons into quarks, but the theory of quark
       confinement suggests the series stops here.12

All scientific evidence supports the view that with quarks, we have reached
the rock bottom of matter.

     Apart from quarks, there is also another class of fermions, namely,
leptons.13 Physicists knew about leptons and their properties for a long
time. In many ways, quarks resemble leptons. They too are point-particles
without a structure, and with one-half spin. But there are significant
differences as to how quarks and leptons appear in nature. Unlike quarks,
leptons like electrons can be directly detected. We can make electron
beams, but not quark beams. Like six kinds of quarks, there are also six
types of leptons: electron, electron neutrino, muon, muon neutrino, tau and
tau neutrino.

      The first family of leptons, the ‘electron family,' consists of electron
and its associated neutrino. These have their anti-particles, namely,
positron and anti-electron neutrino. Electrons were discovered long ago in
1897. Of all elementary particles, they are the best understood. Electrons
are easily freed from their bonds to nuclei, and they have the lightest mass
of all electrically charged particles. They are absolutely stable and do not
decay into other, lighter particles. Electron's anti-particle ‘positron' was
predicted by Dirac equation. The swarm of electrons surrounding atomic
nuclei is responsible for the chemical properties of atoms. Because
electrons are so plentiful in nature and are easily liberated from their bonds
to nuclei, they have been extensively studied and their properties precisely
determined. An electron neutrino, electron's family partner, is extremely
light; it may have zero mass. Because neutrinos have no electric charge,
they do not interact directly with electromagnetic field. They have only
very weak interactions with other forms of matter, and consequently they
fly right through our bodies, the Earth and anything else that gets in their
way.
      The second lepton, the muon, was discovered in 1937. Muons are the
major components of cosmic radiation that swarm through the surface of
the Earth. At this moment, numerous muons are flying around us and
through our bodies. Muon's properties are identical with those of electron
except that its mass is about 207 times greater, and it is negatively charged;
it is actually a ‘fat' electron with negative charge. It is also described by the
Dirac equation, and its interactions are specified with great accuracy by
quantum electrodynamics. The muon's family partner, the muon neutrino,
may have no mass at all. Muon and its neutrino, like the electron family,
have anti-particle partners; the anti-muon has positive charge since muon is
negatively charged. Muons, however, are unstable and are not found
everywhere like electrons. For a long time, physicists thought that electron,
muon, and their associated two neutrinos were the only leptons. As recently
as the year 1977, yet another lepton was discovered. This electrically
charged member is called ‘tau'; it has also an associated neutrino. A tau is
3,491 times more massive than an electron. Presumably, tau also has a
neutrino associated with it, although there is little direct evidence for this.
Because tau is so massive, it can decay into lots of other lighter particles.
The main difference between electron, muon and tau seems to consist in
their masses. If muon is a ‘heavy' electron, then tau is a ‘heavy' muon.

     Finally, there is another family of leptons, namely, neutrinos. In the
1930s, physicists who were studying radioactive decays of nuclei came
across something unexplainable: they found that there was more energy
before the disintegration of a nucleus than after, which is a violation of the
law of mass-energy conservation. Eminent physicist Wolfgang Pauli
suggested that a new elusive particle was carrying off the undetected
energy. Eventually, these particles were discovered. Unlike electron and
muon, these particles have no electric charge. Renowned physicist Enrico
Fermi named them ‘little neutral ones' or ‘neutrinos.' Neutrinos are truly
elusive leptons. They are lighter in mass than electrons; in fact, it is not
clear if they have any mass at all. Their interactions with the rest of matter
are extremely weak. Neutrinos are often produced in the decay remnants of
other particles. For example, muon decays into an electron, a neutrino and
an anti-neutrino. Because it has only very weak interactions with matter, it
is hard to stop neutrinos once they are produced; it takes about eight solid
light years of lead to stop half of the neutrinos emitted in a typical nuclear
decay. Since neutrinos are massless, they travel at the speed of light. As we
saw, there are three kinds of neutrinos: one associated with electron,
another with muon and still another with tau. They are called electron
neutrino, muon neutrino and tau neutrino. (See Table 3)

     The discovery of tau particles seems to be the end of the lepton story
for now. But many physicists would wager that there are heavier leptons
waiting to be discovered, once machines with energy capable of creating
them are built. But the interesting question is, why do leptons beyond
electrons and its neutrinos exist? For, only electrons are needed to build
atoms. Yet, muons and taus appear to be just as fundamental as electrons.
So far nobody knows why they exist, and why they have the masses they
actually have. However, recently particle physicists seem to think that
muons could lead to new discoveries in the quantum world. The recent
(announced on April 7th 2021) Muon g-2 experiment results from Fermilab
(Fermi National Accelerator Laboratory at Chicago) regarding the
behaviour of muons have in fact created excitement among physicists.
These experiments reported a tiny difference between the predictions of the
behaviour of muons in the Standard Model, and their actual behaviour in
the giant particle accelerator. Physicists surmise that such a deviation could
be influenced by their interactions with as yet undiscovered particles or
forces. They wonder whether muons' behaviour would lead to the discovery
of a ‘fifth' force.

     With quarks and leptons, we have reached the foundations of the world
of matter. Quarks are real particles trapped inside hadrons, while leptons
exist independently as real particles. But how do quarks interact among
themselves, and so also how do leptons interact? The interactions among
these particles are mediated by yet another set of particles - the gauge
bosons.14

4.2.2.2. Gauge Bosons

      Gauge bosons comprise a class of subatomic particles which are force-
carriers. Interestingly, from the point of view of quantum field theories,
they exist because of gauge symmetry.15 Unlike fermions, two or more
bosons can occupy the same quantum state at the same time. Hence, they
have a spin of 0, 1, or 2. Gauge bosons, in their role as mediators of
interactions between quarks and leptons, can be thought of as quantum
particles which are exchanged between two other quantum particles like a
ball thrown between two ball-players. These bosons have characteristic
coupling strengths in relation to quarks and leptons, which measure their
‘stickiness' -- how strongly they latch on to other particles; that is, the
stickiness of bosons is a measure of the strength of the interaction. That
coupling strength of bosons is proportional to the different charges -
generalizations of the idea of electric charge -- which quarks and leptons
possess. For example, the virtual photon couples to the electric charge of
other particles with the strength proportional to that charge. This means
that the strength of the quantum interactions of bosons depends on the
energy of interacting particles. Thus, quarks and leptons interacting at
relatively low energies available at present day laboratories, experience the
four distinct interactions - gravitational, weak, electromagnetic and strong
interactions; and so the strengths of the four interactions - the stickiness of
their bosons -- are different and so distinct. However, at much higher
energy levels, as at the early stages of the universe, the strengths of four
interactions -- the stickiness of their gauge bosons -- might all become
equal, and the distinctions between them vanish. “The four interactions
might really be the manifestation of but one universal interaction! This
possibility is the basis for a unified theory which has long been the dream
of particle physicists.”16

     As we have seen, according to the Standard Model, each of the four
interactions is mediated by a set of gauge bosons which are exchange
particles or force-carriers.17 The current Model deals with three interactions
except gravity. These interactions are mediated by four types of gauge
bosons - virtual photon, W± and Zo vector bosons and gluons. Virtual
photon is the gauge boson associated with electromagnetic interaction
between charged particles. Elementary particles, like electrons and protons,
interact with one another by exchanging virtual photons. According to this
theory, two electrons repel one another because virtual photons pass back
and forth between them. The outcome of this interaction is that the two
electrons are pushed away from one another. The weak interaction plays a
role in the disintegration of elementary particles. Only a small number of
subatomic particles, such as photon, electron, proton and neutrino, are
stable. Other particles, like muons, neutrons and other hadrons, disintegrate
rather rapidly into stable ones. Such particle decay is governed by weak
interaction. Unlike electromagnetic and gravitational interactions which
can have long-range effects, the weak interaction has extremely short
range. The electromagnetic and weak interactions used to be considered
separate. But at present they are interpreted as unified electroweak
interaction mediated by a set of gauge bosons, consisting of a photon which
is the exchange particle of electromagnetic field, and three weak vector
bosons -electrically charged, W+ and W- and a neutral one, Z°. These are
the electroweak gauge bosons.

                                Gravity; binds planets to the Sun, and
 Graviton          Mass                   stars to the galaxy
                  Electric         Electromagnetic waves; binds
  Photon          charge                 electrons to nucleus
  Weak Weak, flavour                Radioactive decay processes of

                         Table 4. Gauge bosons
     Quarks are bound together in hadrons by a set of gauge bosons which
are so strong that quarks can never come apart. This is the domain of strong
nuclear force. The quantum field theory that deals with quark-binding
bosons, we saw, is quantum chromodynamics. According to this theory,
quarks (up and down, top and bottom, strange and charm) come in three
primary ‘colours'-- red, blue or green. The ‘colour' of quarks here refers to
a new set of charges. For example, there are red up quarks, blue up quarks
and green up quarks. Then, there is a set of eight ‘coloured gluons' that act
as strong quark-binding forces. Quarks interact with each other through
these coloured gluons. That is, these particles interact and exchange colour
charges among quarks; they also interact among themselves exchanging
their colour charges. According to quantum chromodynamics, coloured
gluons bind quarks together into little bound systems that can be identified
with observed hadrons.18 In fact, the overwhelming majority of particles are
strongly interacting particles. Only photon and four leptons (and their
antiparticles) do not participate in the strong interactions.

4.2.2.3. The Higgs Boson

     The Higgs boson, like other gauge bosons, is a particle associated with
a field called the ‘Higgs field.' However its function and interaction with
particles are different from those of other gauge bosons (photons, W± and
Zo bosons and gluons). It is not a force-carrying particle, but rather, it is a
‘mass-carrying' particle; it confers mass on particles. Further, the Higgs
boson is different from other gauge bosons in so far as the Higgs
mechanism does not result in anything resembling a force, like the
electromagnetic, strong and weak forces. Particle physicists believe that all
of space is filled with ‘Higgs field' which is a background sea of virtual
Higgs bosons that pop in and out of existence. Quarks, leptons and W± and
Zo bosons moving in space interact with this field, and acquire their
masses. Photons and gluons do not interact with Higgs field, and so they do
not have mass. Even the Higgs boson itself interacts with Higgs field and
confers mass on itself.

     The need for Higgs mechanism arose in connection with the invention
of electroweak theory. By around 1960, all attempts to invent a field theory
for weak nuclear force (more precisely an electroweak theory) had
consistently failed. The existent theories predicted that gauge bosons of
weak force (W± and Zo) should have zero mass. But experiments confirmed
that they have nonzero mass. This meant that something unknown was
giving these particles their masses. In 1964 along with five physicists,
Peter Higgs published papers showing that the electroweak symmetry
would be ‘broken' if an unusual type of field existed throughout the
universe; and this field would generate mass for subatomic particles. The
hypothetical field was called the ‘Higgs field,' and the method by which it
achieves symmetry breaking, was designated the ‘Higgs mechanism.' This
mechanism required that a spinless boson should exist with properties as
described by the Higgs mechanism theory. This particle was called the
‘Higgs boson.'19 It is associated with the Higgs field. Just as the
electromagnetic field has a quantum particle associated with it, namely,
virtual photon, so the Higgs boson is the particle allied with the Higgs
field. It is predicted to have an enormous mass, much greater than proton
mass. The Higgs boson acquires its own sizeable mass by interacting with
its own field. It is predicted to have zero spin value, and so it is classified
as a boson.

     A key feature of the Higgs field is that it would take less energy for it
to have a non-zero value than a zero value; and so unlike all other known
fields, the Higgs field has a non-zero value everywhere. This non-zero
value would break electroweak symmetry, thereby generating mass for
vector bosons, W± and Zo. Thus, the Higgs theory demonstrated how gauge
bosons of weak interaction could have mass. Accordingly, the Standard
Model requires the Higgs field in order to break electroweak symmetry and
to confer on vector bosons their appropriate masses. In general, the Higgs
mechanism explains how subatomic particles, such as electrons and quarks,
acquire their masses. Strictly speaking, the Higgs boson does not confer
mass to other particles. Rather, the Higgs boson is a quantized
manifestation of the Higgs field that generates mass through its interaction
with other particles. The amount of mass these particles obtain depends on
how strongly they couple to the Higgs field. A photon does not interact
with the Higgs field at all, and so it has no mass. In contrast, the W± and Zo
particles acquire their very considerable mass by coupling to the Higgs
field. Quarks couple to the Higgs field much more strongly than electrons;
the top quark ‘feels' it most, and attain a mass hundreds of thousands of
times greater than an electron.

      Although the existence of Higgs field was predicted in 1964, it was far
from easy to confirm it. It can be proved to exist by detecting its excitation
which is manifested as the Higgs boson. But it is extremely difficult to
produce and detect the Higgs boson on account of the enormous amount of
energy required to create it. Hence, it took more than 30 years (1980-2010)
to develop particle colliders, detectors and computers capable of searching
for the Higgs boson. It involved the construction of one of the world's most
expensive and most complex experimental facilities to date, CERN's Large
Hadron Collider (LHC), in order to produce Higgs bosons for observation
and study. On 14th March 2013, physicists working at the LHC announced
that they have discovered particles with the predicted properties of the
Higgs boson and also the associated Higgs field. Thus, the concept of some
type of Higgs field spread throughout space was experimentally confirmed.
However, more investigations are needed to verify with higher precision
that the discovered particle has predicted properties.

     Now, how does the Higgs mechanism actually operate? How does, for
instance, W± and Zo bosons acquire mass? Measurements show that these
gauge bosons do have relatively large masses. In the extremely hot early
stage of the universe, when the temperatures were above the electroweak
phase transition (1015 K), the Higgs field averages to zero. With a zero
average Higgs field, the particle masses also are zero. The W± and Zo
particles too are massless, just like photons, implying that both weak and
electromagnetic forces have long range. When the temperature drops, the
Higgs field accomplishes a trade-off between symmetry and stability. A
zero Higgs field is symmetric but unstable; a non-zero Higgs field is stable
but breaks symmetry. The Higgs field spontaneously breaks symmetry, and
as a result its strength jumps from an average zero value to a very large
non-zero value, triggering the Higgs mechanism. In other words, below the
electroweak temperature of 1015 K, the Higgs field spontaneously breaks
symmetry during interactions. This symmetry breaking triggers the Higgs
mechanism, and the W± and Zo bosons which interact with the Higgs field
acquire their masses. When these bosons thus obtain mass, they are limited
to very small distances. Furthermore, it was later found that the same Higgs
field would also explain, in a different way, how other matter particles, like
electrons and quarks, acquire mass. Remarkably, the observed masses of
the W± and Zo particles accurately agreed with the predictions of
electroweak theory.

     We conclude our visit to the particle zoo with the following remarks.
In the depths of matter, we reached atoms which have two primary
components, namely, nuclei and electrons; we then journeyed on to the
nucleus which is composed of protons and neutrons. These two particles
turned out to be two hadrons out of an infinite number. At the bottom of the
riddle of hadrons lay quarks; these are point-like particles which are bound
together by strong nuclear force into an infinite number of different
configurations; these are hadrons. Our voyage into atomic nucleus thus
ends up with quarks. The other primary component of atom is the swarm of
electrons surrounding the nucleus. The electron is the first member of a
class of particles with spin value, which are called leptons. The other
leptons are muons, taus and elusive neutrinos. Unlike quarks, leptons can
exist in a free state. For example, the electron which is bound to the
nucleus of atom by electromagnetic force can easily be freed. Leptons, like
electrons, really exist out there in the world. Then there are also gauge
bosons - virtual photons, W± and Zo vector bosons, and gluons -- which are
force-carrying particles. The classification of particles ends up with the
Higgs boson.20

4.3. The Mechanisms of the Standard Model
     Related to the Higgs field and the Higgs boson are two key concepts of
the Standard Model of particle physics: they are gauge principle and
symmetry breaking. In fact, the unification of electromagnetic and weak
nuclear interactions is the accomplishment of these two postulates. We
observe breaking of gauge symmetry in the evolution of the universe.

4.3.1. Gauge Symmetry and Symmetry Breaking

     In our discussions on the Higgs boson, we had occasion to refer to the
concepts of symmetry and symmetry breaking. The Standard Model
employs these two key mechanisms in order to unify the diversity of
particles and forces of nature. Smolin explains:

       But the first thing that must be understood about the Standard
       Model is how it resolves the tension between unity and
       diversity. How is it that phenomena as different as
       electromagnetism and the weak and strong nuclear interactions
       can be encompassed within a single theory? The theory
       succeeds in doing this because it is based on two simple ideas,
       which are called the gauge principle and spontaneous
       symmetry breaking.21

The first of these mechanisms, the gauge principle, accomplishes the
unification of forces; and the second, the spontaneous symmetry breaking,
explains why, although unified, the forces are so different. “These two
principles together form the cornerstone of the Standard Model of particle
physics.”22 John Barrow suggests: “The Platonic faith in symmetry and the
implementation of those symmetries as the basis for gauge theories is the
foundation of our knowledge of elementary particle interactions.”23

     Literally, the word ‘gauge' means a measurement, a thickness, such as
the distance between two railway tracks. There are different modes of
measurements or gauges between tracks, like broad gauge and meter gauge.
Though there are different kinds of tracks, there is a law governing them:
they are all parallel. This is the law of symmetry controlling different
varieties of gauges of railway lines. The gauge principle in physics can be
best understood in terms of such symmetry. Barrow says: “Gauge theories,
by their very nature, are built upon symmetry. These symmetries are built
up by operation of a finite number of variations upon a single theme.”24
Pagels expresses this idea differently. “A symmetry has to do with how
some objects remain unchanged or invariant if we transform them.”25 This
means that the gauge principle involves a variety of factors around a single
pattern. It is not merely a law; a law in fact states invariance among
different physical phenomena. For example, the law for calculating force: F
= m x a. It can be used to calculate the forces generated by different kinds
of physical objects in different circumstances, such as the force of a water
fall, of a rock rolling down the hill, or of a running car. It has an invariance
in so far as the same law is applied to different instances of generating
force. However, this invariance is different from the invariance involved in
gauge principle and symmetry. These state an invariance among different
laws; as such, it can be called a ‘super-law.' In that sense, gauge principle
states a proportion, a symmetry or a pattern between different laws. Take
for instance, the simple proportion: 3 x 2 : : 6 x 4. This proportion
comprises both sides of the proportion sign (: :). Whereas the
multiplication sign (x) controls each side of the proportion sign separately,
the proportion sign controls the whole proportion or symmetry. We may say
that a (gauge) field encompasses both sides of the proportion. Here,
proportion or symmetry functions as an invariance. The whole proportion
can be ‘re-gauged' with different statements, but still keeping the
proportion or symmetry, thus: 5 x 4 : : 10 x 8. However, in these ordinary
cases, symmetries are not hidden, but manifest; and also symmetries are
not broken, but they are sustained.

     In the present context, symmetries are to be understood together with
symmetry breaking. Consider, for example, a sharp pencil poised vertically
with its tip on a horizontal surface. It is in a symmetrical state because,
while it is balanced on its point, one direction is as good as any another.
The pencil looks the same and is symmetrical even if it is rotated about a
vertical axis. (See Figure 18) In this state, the pencil manifests the
underlying symmetry of the gravitational field, which is indifferent to
horizontal directions. However, this symmetric state is unstable, and the
pencil will topple down. When the pencil falls, as it inevitably must, it will
fall randomly, in one direction or another, breaking the symmetry. (See
Figure 19). In the horizontal state, the pencil is stable, but its symmetry is
broken; it no longer manifests its symmetry. In other words, the symmetry
of the vertically poised pencil is unstable; the pencil breaks symmetry by
falling to the horizontal state which is stable, but it does not manifest
symmetry. Davies explains: “So the pencil trades symmetry for stability. . .
. When there is such a tradeoff between symmetry and stability like this, it
is called spontaneous symmetry breaking, because the system itself chooses
(arbitrarily) how to break the symmetry -- it is not imposed by some
external influence.”26




                      Figure 18. Rotating pencil
                      Figure 19. Fallen pencil
     Let us take another example -- of a ferromagnet.27 A bar of
ferromagnet consists of numerous micro-magnets. The domains of these
micro-magnets are aligned, thereby combining their magnetic fields in an
orderly way. (See Figure 20) But if it is heated, it will




                   Figure 20. Ordinary magnet




                    Figure. 21. Heated magnet
     soon lose its magnetism: its magnetic domains become disoriented
and start positioning in random directions. The magnetic field of the bar
gets weaker. Eventually when a critical temperature, called the ‘Curie
temperature' is reached, all mini-magnets become independent, and the
magnetic field of the bar vanishes; micromagnets no longer align in a
preferred direction.28 (See Figure 21) Under these circumstances, their
orientations are random: there is no systematic line-up in any particular
direction. The original rotational symmetry for which there is no preferred
direction has been restored. Although the hot iron is made of micro-
magnets, the net magnetic field averages to zero.

     However, if the hot iron bar is now slowly cooled down through the
Curie temperature, the magnetic field returns. At this critical temperature,
the disruptive effect of thermal agitation is suddenly overcome, and all
micro-magnets spontaneously organize themselves into a coherent pattern.
The magnetic fields of component micromagnets add together to produce a
large-scale field. They pick out a particular direction in space and thereby
break the perfect rotational symmetry of space, in which one direction is
equivalent to any other. The basic law of magnetism is symmetric; that is,
the mini-magnets which comprise the bar of magnet are indifferent to
directions in space. The magnetic state of hot iron bar conforms to this
symmetry, and so it has no magnetic field. But below the Curie
temperature, magnetic field spontaneously sets in as each mini-magnet
locks into a particular direction. That is to say, a given magnet breaks
symmetry by picking out a particular direction. The ferromagnet illustrates
the basic ideas of spontaneous symmetry breaking. The symmetrical state
is unstable, but the state of broken symmetry is stable. The general rule
regarding symmetry breaking is the following: “High-temperature systems
manifest more symmetry than low-temperature systems. When the
temperature falls, symmetries get broken.”29 Physicists call this kind of
transformation ‘phase transitions.'30

4.3.2. The       Gauge       Symmetry        (Breaking)      in    Particle
Interactions

     There are many kinds of symmetries in physics. Positive and negative
electrical charges are simple examples of symmetries. They stand in the
same relation as left-right symmetry; if all positive and negative charges in
the universe were magically transposed, the laws of electromagnetism
would be ‘unaware' of the changeover. There is also another kind of
symmetry involved in gauge forces. Smolin writes:

       But there are special situations in which the symmetries
       completely determine the forces. This turns out to be the case
       for a class of forces called gauge forces. . . . But the fact that
       all the properties of a force can be determined by knowing the
       symmetries is one of the most important discoveries of
       twentieth-century physics. This idea is what is meant by the
       gauge principle.31

As physicists came to understand the mathematical symmetries of field
theory, they discovered that these symmetries actually required the very
interactions they had observed among the fields and their associated
particles. Such symmetries are called ‘gauge symmetries.' Gauge
symmetry, Greene says, is “the symmetry principle underlying the
quantum-mechanical description of the three non-gravitational forces; the
symmetry involves the invariance of a physical system under various shifts
in the values of force charges, shifts that can change from place to place
and from moment to moment.”32

     Gauge symmetries are crucial to our understanding of the fundamental
laws of physics at the subatomic level, and they provide us keys to the
unification of the four fundamental forces of nature. Davies comments:
“Hidden symmetry, however, plays an even more important role in physics.
Indeed, much of our present understanding of the fundamental forces of
nature draws heavily on the concept of abstract symmetries that are not
obvious on casual inspection.”33 Likewise, Barrow remarks about the
important role of symmetry in particle interactions:

       Symmetry has become the dominant theme in particle physics.

       . . . Thus, a blind faith in symmetry provides an efficient recipe
       for generating candidate theories of elementary-particle
       interactions. No such machinery exists to generate candidate
       theories to explain the workings of less basic entities like
       economies or weather systems. The stronghold of symmetry is
       the unseen world of the smallest things.34

The best theories about the forces of nature are gauge theories. Most
physicists believe that the unification of three/four interactions would be
achieved only by a gauge theory. Smolin suggests: “The discovery that all
three forces are expressions of a single unifying principle — the gauge
principle — is the deepest accomplishment of theoretical particle physics
to date.”35 Thus, gauge principle has turned out to be one of the most
important insights in contemporary physics and the key to unifying the
forces and particles of nature.

     The mechanisms of gauge symmetry and of symmetry breaking were
most successfully employed in inventing the electroweak theory by Steven
Weinberg, Sheldon Glashow and Abdus Salam. They were investigating
how it would be possible to interpret weak interaction as a gauge field. But
surprisingly, these physicists found that they could not use a single gauge
field for weak interaction alone, but needed a sort of unified field which
describes, not simply the weak interaction but the weak and
electromagnetic interactions combined. This, however, means that two of
the fundamental forces of nature — electromagnetic and weak nuclear
forces — must now be treated in a unified way. Peat describes the scenario:

       Physics had hitherto viewed these forces as quite distinct --
       after all, the weak nuclear force has to do with elementary
       particles, while electromagnetism is an infinitely ranged force
       at our own scale of things -- but now nature was forcing
       physicists to treat these forces as one and the same. The result
       was a single gauge field to describe a new unified electroweak
       force. It turned out that this field has four components, and
       therefore four massless vector bosons, which carry the force.
       Two of these gauge particles have electrical charges, two do
       not. One of the massless vector bosons looks very like our old
       friend the photon.36

Now, what does this discovery of Weinberg, Glashow and Salam mean? It
means that nature “knows only a single gauge field which embraces both
the weak and electromagnetic interactions.”37 However, a host of
experiments showed very clearly that the weak force is different from the
electromagnetic force. It has a strength and range different from
electromagnetism. Peat wryly remarks: “At first sight, the cost of unifying
two of the forces seemed too high. Putting the weak force on an equal
footing with the electromagnetic introduced too much symmetry into the
theory. Nature simply does not look that way.”38

     It was at this point that another powerful idea struck particle
physicists -- that of symmetry breaking. Peat explains: “In essence, this
suggests that while the laws of nature have a high degree of symmetry, their
particular solutions — in other words, the objects we observe in the world
— can have a much lower degree of symmetry.”39 The mechanism to
accomplish this feat is symmetry breaking which is the second key to the
Standard Model. Smolin confirms: “Spontaneous symmetry breaking is the
way that nature contrives to resolve the dilemma of unification and
variety.”40 This is precisely what physicists Weinberg, Glashow and Salam
discovered in inventing the electroweak theory. They found that the
mechanisms of gauge symmetry and spontaneous symmetry breaking can
explain the unity of, and differences between, electromagnetic and weak
interactions. “The basic idea of this remarkable gauge field [electroweak]
theory is that a spontaneous symmetry breaking gives rise to the difference
between the weak and the electromagnetic interactions.”41 Pagels
summarizes the process of symmetry breaking in electroweak interactions:

       But in the case of the weak interactions, broken symmetry is
       just what is wanted to distinguish the electromagnetic from the
       weak interactions. The W and Z fields become very massive
       because of this symmetry-breaking ‘Higgs mechanism,' while
       the photon, which retains a remnant of the original exact
       symmetry, remains massless.42

     As we saw, symmetry breaking takes place when a hot magnet is
gradually cooled, attaining its low energy state; its micro-magnets then
pick out a particular direction in space, and they form a coherent magnetic
field. Thus, the rotational symmetry of the magnet's hot state where its
micro-magnets have no particular directions in space is broken. Similar
dynamics of symmetry breaking is operative in electroweak theory. Peat
explains:

       Physicists speculated that exactly the same thing [gaining and
       losing magnetic field] happens with the weak-electromagnetic
       force. According to the gauge field approach, the weak-
       electromagnetic force is already unified and carried by a
       massless gauge field of infinite range associated with four
       massless vector bosons. However, in this force's lowest energy
       states, its basic symmetry or unity is broken. The weak and the
       electromagnetic forces now assume different strengths, three
       of the four gauge particles acquire mass, while the third, the
       photon, remains massless. It turns out that the two field vector
       bosons W+ and W-, as well as the neutral vector boson Z°,
       assume quite large masses. The result is that, rather than
       having an infinite range, the weak interaction is now of
       extremely short range. However, at very high energies found
       during the first instants of the creation of the universe, these
       two forces of nature appear identical.43

     The essential idea about symmetry breaking is that a general
symmetry is broken or hidden in the lowest energy states, but its full
symmetry can still be manifested at high enough temperatures or energies.
Such high energy has to do with extreme energies far beyond those of our
ordinary experience, comparable to the energies involved during the first
instants of the creation of the universe. At these high energy states, the four
interactions of nature are symmetrical and indistinguishable, but at lower
energy states the symmetry is broken, and they become distinct as in the
present universe. Hence, the dynamics of symmetry breaking involves
different energy states. According to the electroweak gauge field theory, at
high energies, electromagnetic and weak forces are unified and carried by a
massless gauge field of infinite range associated with four massless vector
bosons. These two forces appeared identical at temperatures above 1015 K
at the beginning of the universe, and the underlying symmetry was
manifest. The weak force had a long range like electromagnetic force. In
other words, the two interactions merge their identities at temperatures
above 1015 K.

     Now the question is, how would such a symmetrical state of
electromagnetic and weak interactions come about? How would weak force
acquire infinite range like electromagnetic force? The weak force, we
know, has actually very short range because it is carried by very heavy
bosons -- W± and Zo. Electromagnetism, on the other hand, is carried by
photons which have zero mass. A zero mass virtual particle can live
forever, and so the range of electromagnetic force is, in principle, infinite
whereas heavy particles, like W± and Zo can remain in existence only for an
extremely short time permitted by Heisenberg's uncertainty principle. The
duration of these virtual particles depends on their masses: the more
massive they are, the less time they are allowed to exist; conversely, the
less massive the particles are, the more time they can exist. They have to be
created, exchanged and have to disappear within the time allowed by the
uncertainty relation.

     However, when the universe had enormous energy in its primordial
stages, there would have been many W± and Zo particles which exist
independently by themselves, and would not be restricted by the limits of
uncertainty relation. Gribbin describes this scenario:

       In the very early universe, when there was plenty of energy
       about, there would also have been many W and Z particles, for
       example, existing in their own right. If the energy density of
       the Universe were higher than the energy needed to maintain a
       sea of W and Z particles, then they would not be subjected to
       the constraints imposed today by the uncertainty principle.
       Like photons, they would be massless, and would have infinite
       range, and there would be no way to distinguish between the
       electromagnetic and weak forces.44

Thus, above the electroweak energy of 1015 K, electromagnetic and weak
interactions would become symmetrical; both would have infinite range,
and the vector bosons of the weak force - W"± and Zo particles -- would be
massless like photons.
     But as the temperature fell below 1015 K, there was a phase transition
at which the electroweak gauge symmetry was spontaneously broken.
Consequently, the weak force abruptly became considerably weaker than
the electromagnetic force; it also became very short-ranged, and restricted
to the subatomic domain as at present. In other words, after the electroweak
symmetry was broken, the electromagnetic and weak forces assume
different strengths and became distinct in their properties. Only one of the
four particles -- the photon of electromagnetic interaction -- remained
massless, and the other three particles acquired huge masses (hundreds of
times more than proton mass). They are the gauge bosons of weak
interaction -- W+ and W- and Z° particles. The differing masses of the four
originally massless gauge bosons reflect broken symmetry. Rather than
having an infinite range, the weak interaction has now an extremely short
range. Electromagnetism, on the other hand, has infinite range and is
carried by photons which have zero mass.

     However, the gauge bosons cannot by themselves spontaneously break
gauge symmetry and confer on themselves different masses. These
particles acquire their masses, we have seen, by interacting with the all-
pervading Higgs field which is also responsible for breaking the gauge
symmetry of the electroweak force. The Weinberg-Salam electroweak
model requires that the Higgs field exist with the associated particle -- the
Higgs boson. At temperatures above that of electroweak phase transition
(1015 K), such as prevailed in the hot early state of the universe, the value
of the Higgs field averages to zero. With a zero Higgs field, all the particle
masses are in effect zero. The W± and Zo particles are massless too, just
like photon, and consequently both weak and electromagnetic forces have
long range. When the temperature drops, the Higgs field faces the same
conundrum as the pencil standing on its head: a trade-off between
symmetry and stability. That is to say, a zero Higgs field is symmetric but
unstable, whereas a non-zero Higgs field is stable but breaks symmetry.

     Like the toppling pencil, the Higgs field spontaneously breaks
symmetry. Some of the ‘toppling energy' thus liberated gets used to ‘pay'
for the masses that the Higgs field bestows on the participating particles.
The strength of the Higgs field jumps from a zero average value to a very
large non-zero value; and the W± and Zo bosons which interact with the
Higgs field acquire their masses. When these particles attain large masses,
the weak interaction becomes short-ranged, and limited to a very small
distance. Thus, the gauge field has unified two of the fundamental forces of
nature, whose symmetry is manifest at high energies, and broken at low
energies. In the words of Smolin: “In the weak and electromagnetic
interactions gauge theory acts in a still more interesting way, because it
succeeds in unifying things that are, apparently, quite different. The range
of the electromagnetic force is infinite, while the range of the weak nuclear
force is no more than the diameter of an atomic nucleus.”45 The great
triumph of electroweak theory is the eventual discovery of the gauge field
vector bosons themselves. The first experimental indications of a W boson
were found in 1982, and the Z particle was discovered on 4th May 1983.

     With the unification of electromagnetic and weak interactions
employing gauge symmetry, particle physicists attempted to apply it to
strong interaction acting between quarks. One of great problems of the
1950s with regard to the strong force was now seen in an entirely new light.
So far physicists were trying to account for the strong force in terms of the
exchange of mesons between protons and neutrons; but in the context of
quantum field theories, they began to view it in terms of gluons -- the
virtual particles of the strong force -- being exchanged between quarks.
Experimental physicists had already confirmed that hadrons are made up of
quarks. But what binds quarks together inside hadrons? Physicists reasoned
that these quarks would be bound together by a new set of gauge bosons
whose existence was required by a gauge symmetry. This insight led to the
creation of the quantum field theory of strong interaction - the quantum
chromodynamics. According to this theory, each quark has a new kind of
charge, a ‘colour' charge. Quarks are assigned three charges in terms of
three primary colours - red, blue and green. Thus, instead of just a single up
quark, there are now a red up quark, a blue up quark and a green up quark.
Introducing these three extra charges allows physicists to postulate a new
symmetry among quarks. This symmetry is similar to the rotational
symmetry of a sphere in three-dimensional space; each of the three spatial
directions now corresponds to one of the three colours, red, blue and green.
If the sphere is rotated, different colours are mixed; and then perfect
symmetry means that the three primary colours have to be equally mixed.
An equal mixture of the three colours produces white which is not a colour
at all. Requiring such colour invariance thus implies that only those
combinations of coloured quarks are allowed which when mixed results in
no colour at all. These colourless combinations of coloured quarks
correspond precisely to observed hadrons like protons and neutrons.

      The colour symmetry of quarks is postulated as a gauge symmetry
which requires a new set of gauge bosons or virtual/ exchange particles;
accordingly, there are eight coloured gluons which couple to the coloured
charges of quarks. But unlike photon which does not couple to itself,
because it has no electric charge, the eight coloured gluons do interact
among themselves. The coloured gluons, not only stick to quarks, but they
also stick to each other. The coloured gluons are the real source of strong
interaction. According to quantum chromodynamics, Pagels says, “the
color charges of the quarks coupling to the eight colored gluons is [sic] the
totality of strong interaction physics — all the complexity of hadrons is to
be accounted for by this single idea of a gauge symmetry.”46 The coloured
gluons provide the binding force that traps quarks inside hadrons. Only
those hadrons which are colourless combinations of coloured quarks and
gluons can exist as free particles, and this is just what we observe in the
real world. That is, all the coloured quarks and coloured gluons bind
themselves permanently together into colourless hadrons. This is the gauge
theory of quantum chromodynamics. “Technically, it is a gauge field theory
describing the interactions of quarks and gluons.”47 This theory is
considered a great triumph of the twentieth century particle physics.

     As we conclude our discourse on ‘The Mechanisms of the Standard
Model,' we make some observations on the paramount importance of gauge
principle in quantum physics. Particle physicists are most impressed about
the profound role of gauge symmetry and symmetry breaking in the
dynamics of quantum field theories. They have discovered that these
symmetries apparently determine the nature and properties of the four
interactions and their associated particles. Indeed, the gauge field theory is
a great revolution in particle physics that has given physicists a deep
insight into the structure of physical reality. The great and most successful
fundamental theories of physics - the general theory of relativity,
electroweak theory and quantum chromodynamics -- are all gauge field
theories. Steven Weinberg remarks about the importance of symmetry in
the theories of particle physics: “Even if a theory postulates a high degree
of symmetry, it is not necessary for . . . the states of the particles to exhibit
the symmetry. . . . Nothing in physics seems so hopeful to me as the idea
that it is possible for a theory to have a high degree of symmetry which is
hidden from us in ordinary life.”48 Barrow makes a beautiful observation
about hidden/broken symmetries in the laws of nature:

       We observe the broken symmetries in the particulars of the
       world and from them we must deduce the hidden symmetries
       that characterize the laws of Nature. There is an elegant
       tapestry of Nature of harmonious weave, but we are seeing the
       back of it. From the loose threads we must create a picture of
       the hidden pattern behind it.49

4.3.3. Symmetry Breaking in the Early Universe

     We find the mechanisms of gauge symmetry and symmetry breaking
operative in the evolution of the early stages of the universe. According to
