the Standard Model of cosmology, the universe had its origin in the
purported Big Bang singularity. At time zero, the universe supposedly had
infinite energy density, infinite curvature and infinite temperature - a state
known as singularity. If we go back to the first moments of the creation of
the universe, its energies and temperatures were extremely high. In such a
regime, the four interactions were unified as one highly symmetrical super-
force; the four forces of nature were indistinguishable and equivalent to
one another. Pagels tells us:

       It is held today that that all the four interactions become
       unified as one universal interaction at ultra-high energies. The
       reason we see such different interactions is that the familiar
       world we know has its physical processes occurring at low
       energy, and there the strengths of the interactions can be very
       different. The symmetry and simplicity of physics will be
       revealed only at ultrahigh energies. Unified field theories —
       and many have been constructed in the last several years —
       incorporate these features.50

     However, as the universe expanded and temperatures dropped, this
perfect symmetry began to break; and the four interactions split off at
appropriate temperatures and limited themselves to the strengths they have
at present in our universe. According to the proposed unified theories, all
the interactions we observe in the universe are the asymmetrical remnants
of a perfectly symmetrical super-force at ultra-high energies of the Big
Bang regime. “The universe from its very beginning to the present may be
viewed as a hierarchy of successively broken symmetries — a transition
from a simple perfect symmetry at the beginning of time to the complex
patterns of broken symmetries we observe today.”51 Barrow suggests: “Of
necessity, we live in an era when the deep symmetries of Nature have long
since broken and disguised the true simplicity behind things. Moreover,
many of the things that we witness in the Universe may have been
predominantly determined by the random way in which symmetry breaking
just happened to fall out.”52 The history of the universe can therefore be
interpreted as a succession of symmetry breaking as temperature dropped.
The universe started with perfect unity and symmetry, but as it expanded,
more and more symmetries were broken, and more and more structures and
complexities emerged, much of it random and spontaneous.

     Just after the Big Bang itself, physicists believe that there was an era,
called the ‘Planck epoch.' During this period, there was only one ‘super-
force' in which gravity was unified with other three interactions. At the
Planck time scale of 10-43 sec, distance scale of 10-33 cm, temperatures
above 1032 K and energies in excess of 1019 GeV, the four fundamental
forces were unified and gravity was equal to the other forces of nature.
From the Planck time of 10-43 sec, the early universe is believed to have
undergone a series of phase transitions, and passed through a number of
spontaneous symmetry breaking phases; and the four forces were separated.
First, the ‘super symmetry' between gravitational force and other forces
was broken. In the ‘GUT era' -- from 10-43 sec after the Big Bang
singularity to 10-35 sec at the temperature of 1032 K -- the gravitational force
became separated from the strong and electroweak forces. As the universe
began to expand smoothly, and the temperature fell, the other three forces
were still unified. Hawking speaks about the dynamics of these forces at
this stage of the expansion of the universe. He says that “the strong nuclear
force gets weaker at high energies. On the other hand, the electromagnetic
and weak forces . . . get stronger at high energies. At some very high
energy, called the grand unification energy, these three forces would all
have the same strength and so could just be different aspects of a single
force.”53 During the ‘electroweak era' -- from 10-35 sec to 10-10 sec at the
temperature of 1026 K - the strong force was separated from electroweak
force, and it became restricted to the distances we know today in the nuclei
of atoms. Still the electromagnetic and weak nuclear interactions were
united as a single, undifferentiated electroweak force.

     At temperatures above 1015 K, the electromagnetic and weak forces
were symmetrical, and the W± and Zo weak bosons were effectively
massless like photons. But as the temperature dropped below 1015 K, the
electroweak symmetry was broken and the distinction between these two
interactions became manifest. The weak bosons W± and Zo acquired masses
while photons continued to exist as massless. During the ‘free quark era' --
from 10-10 to 10-4 sec with still temperatures falling, the electromagnetic
force became separated from weak force. When this occurred, the
temperature of the universe was equivalent to the highest energies yet
achieved in particle accelerators on Earth. This is one of the reasons why
the electroweak theory has been much more decisively confirmed by
experiments than quantum chromodynamics. Each of these separations is
the breaking of a gauge symmetry of a unified regime of two or more
interactions; and each of the symmetries is broken in a random way.

     After the electroweak symmetry was broken, the universe consisted of
a gas of approximately equal numbers of leptons, quarks, their anti-
particles, coloured gluons and photons which were continuously created
and destroyed. Effectively, the quarks were freely flying about and
interacting with other particles. At high energies, quarks and coloured
gluons behave as if they were free and non-interacting. This property of
these particles is called ‘asymptotic freedom.' At high energy, the strength
which couples coloured gluons becomes weaker; that is, the ‘stickiness' of
gluons decreases. Hence, at temperatures beyond 1014 K, the coupling
strength of strong interaction decreases, and so it becomes weak; the
hadrons literally become unglued and the quarks are freed. However, as the
universe continued to expand and the temperature dropped below 1014 K,
little bags we call ‘hadrons' were formed containing quarks, ‘imprisoning'
them for ever; quarks were trapped in hadrons. This process is called
‘hadronization.' It marks the beginning of the hadron era, with temperatures
from 1014 K to 1012 K. All the hadrons now became part of particles of the
universe, and free quarks and coloured gluons have completely
disappeared. Pagels suggests: “It is as if a color movie (the colored quarks
and gluons) all of a sudden became black-and-white (the hadrons).”54 The
universe today with its relatively low temperature is then the frozen fossils
of the Big Bang regime. These are the grand structures of the universe:
galaxies, stars and the greatest of them all -- life itself.

    We conclude with an interesting quote from Pagels on the fascinating
dynamics of symmetries and symmetry breaking in the universe:
symmetries are restored as we go backward to the beginning of the
universe, and they are broken as the universe evolves to the present time:

       Here, for the first time, we see a remarkable feature of the
       modern theory of the origin of the universe: the further back in
       time we go, the hotter the universe becomes, and broken
       symmetries are restored. The universe and all its particle
       interactions are becoming more and more symmetrical as we
       descend deeper into the big bang. . . . Conversely, were we to
       progress forward in time, we would see that as the temperature
       falls, those perfect symmetries are broken. Now the physical
       differences between the various interactions — strong, weak
       and electromagnetic — become apparent.55

4.4. Beyond the Standard Model

    The Standard Model currently provides the best picture of the
behaviour of elementary particles and fundamental forces. It makes precise
predictions of their properties which have been confirmed by numerous
experiments since it was proposed in the 1960s. However, new discoveries
in particle physics pose challenges to the Model. Further, the present
design of the Standard Model is in fact incomplete. It has yet to unify
electroweak model and quantum chromodynamics into a Grand unified
Theory (GUT). Finally, the Model has to integrate all the forces of nature
including gravitation into a Theory of Everything (TOE).

4.4.1. The Challenges to the Standard Model

     Some unsolved problems in quantum physics and cosmology as well
as new discoveries about the behaviour of quantum particles do pose
challenges to the validity of the Standard Model. This Model has yet to
confront certain important questions in quantum mechanics itself. As we
have seen, it applies only to the three non-gravitational interactions of
nature, but does not include gravity. In order to incorporate gravity with
quantum mechanics, a successful quantum theory of gravity has to be
invented. But in spite of the strenuous efforts of many renowned particle
physicists, the prospects of such a theory at present appear bleak. Further,
the Standard Model contains at least 19 arbitrary parameters. The values of
these parameters cannot be deduced from the Model itself, but have to be
determined through experiments. Moreover, for the most part, the Standard
Model is applicable only to the universe at low energies, that is, the
universe as we ordinarily experience. That is one of the reasons why low
energy theories, like electroweak model, are readily confirmed by
experiments. But the Model is not applicable to the state of the universe at
ultra-high energies, for instance, the Planck regime.

     Another limitation of the Standard Model is that although it is a theory
of fundamental particles and forces of the universe, it fails to explain the
wide range of the masses of elementary particles and huge differences in
the strengths of the four forces. For example, an electron is about 200 times
lighter than a muon and 3491 times lighter than a tau. Similar disparity is
observed regarding the mass of quarks: the top quark is supposedly 75,000
times heavier than the up quark. The differences in the strengths of the four
forces are still more appalling. At low energies as in the present universe,
they are distributed across enormous ranges. These forces have strengths so
varied that the strongest of them (strong nuclear force) is some trillion
trillion quadrillion times stronger than the weakest (gravity). In units in
which the strength of the strong force is 1, that of electromagnetic force is
10-2 (1% of the strength of strong force), and the strength of weak force is
10-6 (one-millionth of the strength of strong force), the strength of gravity
is a mere 10-40.

     Moreover, the Standard Model of particle physics has to explain also
some important problems in cosmology. It accounts only for the observable
regions of the universe, and it has no explanation for the totality of
matter/energy content of the universe. Cosmological theories inform us
that galaxies, stars and inter-stellar material comprise only about 4.9% of
the total contents of the universe. The dark matter constitutes about 26.8%
of the stuff; it is believed to interact only weakly (if at all) with ordinary
matter and quantum fields. The Standard Model does not propose any
elementary particle to explain dark matter. Further, the dark energy
supposedly includes the remaining 68.3% of the contents of the universe; it
is believed to be the energy density of quantum vacuum. However, the
attempts to explain dark energy in terms of the energy of vacuum have
ended up in utter failure. Calculations based on the behaviour of
elementary particles and energy fields of the universe predict that the value
of the vacuum is extremely high — about 10120 times bigger than its actual
value based on the data of the expansion of the universe. The Standard
Model has no adequate explanation for this enormous disparity.
Furthermore, the Model does not account for the preponderance of matter
over anti-matter in the present universe. Like particles, anti-particles are
constituent components of physical reality. The Big Bang model predicts
that matter and anti-matter were created in almost equal amounts at the
beginning of the universe. But our observable universe is composed mostly
only of matter. However, there could be galaxies and stars entirely made up
of anti-matter. Yet, there is no mechanism in the Standard Model to
sufficiently explain the present predominance of matter over anti-matter in
the present universe. Hence, it is generally agreed by physicists that these
fundamental problems in particle physics and cosmology demand a new
physics beyond the Standard Model.
     Apart from these theoretical problems, the Standard Model has to face
challenges from the experimental work in particle physics. The latest
results coming from experiments conducted in different particle
accelerators around the world show that all is not well with the Standard
Model. Increasing number of experiments show that not all measurements
of elementary particles can be explained in the framework of this Model.
The new results from CERN's LHCb (Large Hadron Collider beauty) and
Fermilab (Fermi National Accelerator Laboratory, Chicago) suggest that
muons do not behave according to the principles of the Standard Model.
LHCb results focus on the violations of the concept of ‘lepton flavour
universality.'56 The Standard Model predicts that decays involving different
flavours of leptons should occur with the same probability; this symmetry
is called ‘lepton flavour universality.' Thus, the three charged elementary
particles — electrons, muons, and taus — can be treated identically,
provided their mass differences are also taken into consideration. In other
words, they would interact in the same way with other particles, as long as
differences in their masses are taken into account. Consequently, the
different lepton types would be created equally in particle transformations
or decays. This concept of ‘flavour universality' is deeply ingrained in the
symmetry structure of the Standard Model. Previous measurements have
shown that a wide range of particle decays are consistent with this
principle.

     However, the new results of the measurements made by the LHCb
Collaboration report a violation of this principle of flavour universality.
These measurements compare two types of decays. The first decay involves
the electron, and the second, the muon which is a lepton 200 times heavier
than electron. The decays involving lepton flavour universality are usually
measured by the ratio between decay probabilities. According to the
Standard Model, this ratio should be very close to one. But the new results
indicate hints of a deviation from one. Thus, in the measurements recently
reported, B mesons seemed to decay into electrons more often than to
muons, which would violate the principle of lepton universality.57 These
results were announced on 23rd March 2021 by scientists working at the
LHCb experiment; they have in fact observed the violation of lepton
universality. The deviation observed by them is consistent with a pattern of
anomalies measured in similar processes by LHCb and other experiments
world wide. The new results determine the ratio between the decay
probabilities with greater precision than previous measurements and use all
the data collected so far by the LHCb detector. The confirmation of these
results could indicate the existence of new particles and/or interactions;
and it could have profound implications for the validity of the Standard
Model, and for our understanding of the quantum world.

     Scientists associated with LHCb experiments are very much excited
about the new discoveries. They point out that the new results may indicate
a new physics beyond the Standard Model. Paula Alvarez Cartelle of the
LHCb Collaboration team observes: “These new results offer tantalizing
hints of the presence of a new fundamental particle or force that interacts
differently with these different types of particles.”58 Similarly, Chris
Parkes, spokesperson of the LHCb team says: “If a violation of lepton
flavour universality were to be confirmed, it would require a new physical
process, such as the existence of new fundamental particles or
interactions.”59 Likewise, Konstantinos Petridis also a member of the LHCb
Collaboration, suggests: “The discovery of a new fundamental force or
particle, as hinted at by the evidence of differences in these measurements
could provide the breakthrough required to start to answer these
fundamental questions.”60 Eluned Smith who coordinated the analysis of
LHCb results remarks: “There are cracks in the theory of the symmetry
between electrons and muons! If we are able to confirm the measurements
with further data, this would strongly hint at a new physics beyond the
standard model.”61

     Similar anomalous behaviour of muons is reported by Muon g-2
experiment results announced on April 7th 2021 by Fermilab. Muons occur
naturally when cosmic rays strike Earth's atmosphere; and particle
accelerators also can produce them in large numbers. Like electron, a muon
acts as a spinning magnet, as if it has a tiny internal magnet. In a strong
magnetic field, the direction of the muon's magnet precesses or wobbles,
much like the axis of a spinning top.62 The strength of the internal magnet
determines the rate at which the muon wobbles in an external magnetic
field; and it is described by a number that physicists call the ‘g-factor.'63
This parameter indicates how strong the internal magnet is and the rate of
its gyration. The accepted theoretical value of g for a charged lepton
(electron, muon, or tau) is slightly larger than 2. The difference from 2 (the
‘anomalous' part) depends on the lepton, and can be computed quite
precisely based on the Standard Model of particle physics. This difference
from 2 is produced by higher-order contributions from quantum fields.
Measurements of the electron about the difference from 2 are in excellent
agreement with this computation.

     However, that is not the case with regard to muons. As muons
circulate in Muon g-2 magnet, they also interact with the quantum ‘foam'
of subatomic particles popping in and out of existence. Interactions with
these short-lived particles affect the value of the g-factor, influencing the
muons' precession to speed up or slow down very slightly. But if the
quantum foam contains additional forces or particles which are not
accounted for by the Standard Model, that would tweak the muon g-factor
further. In measuring g-2 with high precision and comparing its value with
theoretical prediction, physicists can discover whether the experiment
agrees with theory. Any deviation would point to as yet undiscovered
particles/ forces in nature. The results of Fermilab experiments have in fact
reported such a deviation: there is a tiny difference between how muons
should behave according to the Standard Model, and how they actually
behave inside the giant Fermilab accelerator. In other words, they behave in
the accelerator slightly differently from the way their behaviour is
predicted by the Standard Model.

     The predecessor experiment at Brookhaven National Laboratory,
which concluded in 2001, had already hinted that the muon's behaviour
disagreed with the predictions of the Standard Model. The new
measurement from the Muon g-2 experiment at Fermilab strongly agrees
with the value observed at Brookhaven. Together, the two results provide
strong evidence for the fact that the measurements of muons' g-factor
diverge from the Standard Model's prediction. Renee Fatemi, a physicist at
the University of Kentucky and the simulations manager for the Muon g-2
experiment says:
       This quantity we measure reflects the interactions of the muon
       with everything else in the universe. But when the theorists
       calculate the same quantity, using all of the known forces and
       particles in the Standard Model, we don't get the same answer.
       This is strong evidence that the muon is sensitive to something
       that is not in our best theory.64

     Allena Opper, a program director in National Science Foundation's
Division of Physics concurs: “The Standard Model is very, very good at
predicting our observations of the subatomic world. This [Fermilab] result
is so exciting because it deviates so clearly from the Standard Model
prediction of the muon's g-factor; there has to be some new particle or
interaction that affects muons.”65

     Potentially, the new results are ground-breaking indications of the
existence of processes that cannot be described within the conceptual
framework of the Standard Model of particle physics. These new outcomes
from experiments at LHCb and Fermilab strengthen the case for missing
fundamental particles and/or interactions which are not accounted for
within the structure of the Standard Model. It is now up to the scientists at
the LHCb and Fermilab to further verify their results by collating and
analyzing more data. If these data are strengthened by further
measurements, it would suggest cracks in the framework of the Standard
Model. This in turn would lead to the invention of a ‘fifth' force of nature,
such as a new fundamental interaction between quarks and leptons; and
further, it would take us to a new physics beyond the present Standard
Model. Indeed, physicists believe that muons could be interacting with as
yet undiscovered particles or forces, and thus they act as windows to the
subatomic world.66

4.4.2. The Grand Unified Theory (GUT)

     The new results from LHCb at CERN about the violations of lepton
flavour universality, and the measurements of muon g-2 factor by Fermilab,
we saw, have inspired particle physicists to look beyond the Standard
Model. There is yet another reason to search for a better Model: the
incompleteness of the Standard Model itself. The current Model has not
achieved complete unification of all the four interactions; it has unified
only electromagnetism and weak nuclear force. The unfinished task is to
bring strong nuclear and gravitational forces into the fold, and achieve a
final unification. Such was the dream of Einstein who took the initiatives
towards the formulation of a unified theory in the 1920s and 1930s. Though
his efforts to construct a final theory were not successful, he inspired many
physicists by the suggestion that the different interactions of nature might
be manifestations of a single unified field. This possibility is the basis of a
unified field theory which has long been the dream of particle physicists. In
particular, it is suggested that the four fundamental forces of nature might
really be the expressions of one universal force at the beginning of the
universe. By the last quarter of the 20th century, particle physicists have
partially realized this dream of unifying different interactions. There are
now theories which attempt to unify electromagnetic, weak and strong
forces into one force. These are the unified field theories which are the
results of many years of determined efforts by reputed particle physicists;
while they may argue over the details of a unified theory, the principle of
unification is firmly established.

     The first step towards a unified field theory is the electroweak theory
in which two distinct interactions -- electromagnetic and weak -- are found
to be manifestations of underlying field symmetries. This theory serves as
the inspiration for subsequent attempts at further unifications. The next
step is the integration of electroweak model and quantum chromodynamics
into a Grand unified Theory (GUT). Even without including gravity, such a
unification of the three interactions into a magnificent theory is often
referred to as the Grand Unified Theory. Hawking comments on the
impropriety of such a designation:

       This title is rather an exaggeration: the resultant theories are
       not all that grand, nor are they fully unified, as they do not
       include gravity. Nor are they really complete theories, because
       they contain a number of parameters whose values cannot be
       predicted from the theory but have to be chosen to fit in with
       experiment. Nevertheless, they may be a step toward a
       complete, fully unified theory.67

But the title ‘Theory of Everything' or TOE is reserved for the final theory
which would include gravity as well.

     With the introduction of the concepts of gauge principle and
symmetry breaking, the programme of unification took on a new direction.
These concepts were successfully employed in the integration of
electroweak theory. Referring to the unification of electroweak and strong
interactions, Pagels says: “The problem of unifying these interactions is
thus the problem of finding a single (rather than a multiple) symmetry that
upon spontaneous symmetry breaking results in the smaller subsymmetries
that correspond to the strong and electroweak interactions.”68 Viewed in
this way, electromagnetic, weak and strong interactions owe their parentage
to a master GUT symmetry which is spontaneously broken. In other words,
in inventing the GUT, particle physicists attempt to construct a single
gauge symmetry which incorporates both electroweak theory and quantum
chromodynamics, and which spontaneously breaks at appropriate times
during the evolution of the universe. The unification of electromagnetic
and weak forces into one electroweak model is thus part of much bigger
schemes, the GUTs which claim to include the strong force as well. Only
the electroweak part of these theories has been tested; it predicts the
masses of the W± and Zo particles (about 100 times the mass of the proton),
and these have been measured at CERN. Although there is no experimental
support for grand unified theories, several projects have been proposed,
that unify the three interactions into a common framework leaving out
gravity. One such GUT was proposed by two Harvard physicists, Howard
Georgi and Sheldon Glashow in 1977; it is based on a single gauge field.
The crucial idea of their theory is to treat quarks and leptons on an equal
footing before the single symmetry is spontaneously broken. The gauge
symmetry they postulated requires a set of 24 exchange particles which
interact with quarks and leptons in a symmetrical way. But no particle
accelerator on Earth can produce enough energy to confirm the predictions
of such a Grand Unified Theory.
     One way to comprehend the unification of the three interactions is to
observe the evolution of the universe. According to Grand Unified
Theories, there was supposedly a GUT era in the very early universe in
which electroweak and strong interactions were perfectly symmetrical, and
not yet distinct. GUT models predict that at very high energies (at 1026 K),
there was no distinction between the weak, electromagnetic and strong
interactions; they were symmetrical and would be unified into a single
electronuclear interaction. This unified interaction was characterized by
one larger gauge symmetry, and had several force-carrying particles. But as
the universe expanded and the temperature dropped, the symmetry among
these interactions was spontaneously broken. With the breaking of
symmetry, various interactions became manifest; first, the super-heavy
gluons were distinguished from weak vector bosons which were then
differentiated from massless photons. Unifying gravity with the
electronuclear interaction would result in the most comprehensive theory -
the Theory of Everything (TOE). Thus, GUTs are often considered
intermediate steps towards a TOE. They are indeed half-way houses.

      Incidentally, particle physicists have noticed a curious display of small
distance scales in connection with the GUT scale in the early stages of the
universe. Apparently, there is a hierarchy of distance scales which are
milestones to ever shorter distances en route to the origin of the universe.
The relatively low energies of today's particle accelerators enable
physicists to explore down to the distance scale of the Standard Model --
10-16 cm (the electroweak scale). In this domain, electromagnetic and weak
interactions are symmetrical and unified. Physicists have noted that as the
distance scale is further decreased from the electroweak scale of 10-16 cm to
the GUT scale of 10-29 cm, no important event happens; and no new
particles appear. Glashow has appropriately dubbed this vast microscopic
region ‘the desert' because it is empty of any significant quantum event. At
the GUT scale of 10-29 cm, the GUT interactions (electroweak and strong
interactions) are perfectly symmetrical and unified. Finally, at the
incredible Planck distance scale of 10-33 cm, the effects of quantum gravity
(which have not been included in the GUTs) are manifest. Because the
Planck scale is about one ten-thousandth the length of even the incredibly
short GUT scale, most physicists prefer to ignore the effects of quantum
gravity in their speculations about GUTs. At the higher energies of the early
universe, physicists have calculated a hierarchy of extremely short
distances as follows:

The         = 10 -
electroweak 16 cm
scale
The GUT     = 10 -
scale       29
               cm
The Planck = 10 -
scale the foregoing
Between
            33
               cm three distance scales, physicists have noted large
pure numbers 10-13 (between 10-16 cm and 10-29 cm) and 10-17 (between 10-16
cm and 10-33 cm). Many of them are especially uneasy about the GUT
extrapolation of distance scales from the electroweak scale of 10-16 cm to
the GUT scale of 10-29 cm. This extrapolation is 13 powers of ten; it is
approximately similar to the thickness of one of our fingers compared to
the distance between the Earth and the Sun. If the GUT idea is correct, then
nature has a ‘micro-desert' covering an immense subatomic region.
Physicists have observed that from the macroscopic scale of super-clusters
of galaxies to the microscopic electroweak scale of 10-16 cm, nature reveals
new physical structures as the distance scale changes. Philip Morrison in
his book Powers of Ten illustrates the richness and variety of nature's
creations every time the distance scale changes by a factor of ten. Is there
some reason for this richness to suddenly cease as we probe beyond
distances corresponding to the electroweak scale of 10-16 cm, and then only
to become significant again at the GUT scale of 10-29 cm —13 powers of
ten smaller? No one knows the answer to this question. The only way to
find the answer is to continue conducting accelerator experiments at still
higher energies.

     Yet, although Grand Unified Theories are exciting, they have no direct
experimental support unlike their predecessor, the electroweak theory.
GUTs are examples of new ideas that are consistent with general principles
of physics, but as yet have no direct supporting evidence. Nevertheless,
they are at the forefront of researches in particle physics today. Physicists
are encouraged by the fact that GUTs enable them to unify the three forces
of nature at least in theory. Currently, such unification is on the mainstream
agenda of particle physics. GUTs fall short of total field unification because
they do not include gravity, the weakest of the four forces. Theoretical
physicists exploring GUTs believe that they have profound implications for
the nature and evolution of the very early universe. GUTs also predict the
existence of new properties of quantum particles, such as proton decay as
well as the existence of a whole new class of elementary particles, like
magnetic monopoles.69 We conclude with Peat that the goal of grand
unification is “nothing less than a combination of the standard model of the
elementary particles — six basic quarks and six leptons — with a universal
force.”70

4.4.3. The Theory of Everything (TOE)

     The Grand Unified Theory is in fact the stepping stone to the Theory
of Everything (TOE). Whereas the GUT theories claim to accomplish a
unification of the three non-gravitational interactions (electroweak and
strong), the TOE proposes to achieve a truly grand unification of all forces
of nature including gravity. Briggs and Peat explain the grand objective of
the Theory of Everything:

       The object of the grand unification theory [the Theory of
       Everything] is to provide a way of mathematically translating
       these four forces into each other so it can be shown how they
       all evolved from, and are expressions of, a single force. This
       would mean physicists had discovered a single unified field of
       force which accounted for everything -- the very field Einstein
       hunted for in his later years. The grand unification theory
       assumes, however, that everything, including the four forces, is
       fundamentally quantum in nature and obeys the statistical laws
       of quantum mechanics.71

The Theory of Everything is the ultimate theory, or master theory which is
supposedly a single, all-encompassing, coherent, theoretical framework of
particle physics that would fully explain and link together, all the physical
phenomena of the universe. Nobel Laureate Leon Lederman observes: “We
hope to explain the entire universe in a single, simple formula that you can
wear on your T-shirt.”72

     The Theory of Everything is the contemporary expression of the
inherent drive of the human mind to attain the ultimate unity of truth in a
final theory. Traditionally, philosophy was the model of such a
comprehensive system. According to the Greek philosopher Plato, it is
‘eros' that thrusts our mind towards ultimate simplicity and unity of truth
to be attained in philosophy. It drives constantly the philosopher as well as
the scientist/physicist to go beyond the frontiers of empirically verifiable
data and to think about a state of comprehensive unity and simplicity. For
physicists, the Theory of Everything is such an ultimate theory about
physical reality. Hawking says: “The eventual goal of science is to provide
a single theory that describes the whole universe.”73 Likewise, Barrow
suggests:

       Modern physicists believe they have stumbled upon a key
       which leads to the mathematical secret at the heart of the
       Universe - a discovery that points towards a ‘Theory of
       Everything', a single all-embracing picture of all the laws of
       Nature from which the inevitability of all things seen must
       follow with unimpeachable logic. With possession of this
       cosmic Rosetta Stone, we could read the book of Nature in all
       tenses: we could understand all that was, is, and is to come.74

Unlike other theories, like the theory of gravitation or of electromagnetism,
which deal with particular phenomena of nature, the range of TOE is much
more comprehensive and much deeper, and it relates to the whole of range
of physical reality. Barrow continues:

       Today, the real goal of the search for a Theory of Everything is
       not just to understand the structure of all the forms of matter
       that we find around us but to understand why there is any
       matter at all, to attempt to show that both the existence and the
       particular structure of the physical Universe can be understood,
       to discover whether, in Einstein's words, ‘God could have made
       the Universe in a different way; that is, whether the necessity
       of logical simplicity leaves any freedom at all'.75

     In fact, scientists have always longed for a Theory of Everything.
According to the Newtonian ideal of scientific knowledge, “from a total
description of the state of the world at one time, and a specification of all
of the laws of nature, a total description of the world at any other time can
be derived by a purely logical, deductive, inference.”76 Proposing his
version of the Theory of Everything, the great mathematician Pierre Simon
de Laplace (1749-1827) refers to an all-knowing intellect that comprehends
in one formula the motions of all physical objects in the universe:

       An intellect which at a given instant knew all the forces acting
       in nature, and the position of all things of which the world
       consists — supposing the said intellect were vast enough to
       subject these data to analysis — would embrace in the same
       formula the motions of the greatest bodies in the universe and
       those of the slightest atoms; nothing would be uncertain for it,
       and the future, like the past, would be present to its eyes.77

During the last forty years of his life, Einstein was in search of a Theory of
Everything, a unified field theory, capable of describing natures's laws and
forces in a single, all-encompassing and coherent framework. His most
cherished dream was to construct a truly Grand Unified Theory, unifying
the theory of relativity and quantum mechanics. Briggs and Peat remark on
Einstein's conception of the unified field:

       Ultimately, Einstein hoped to take relativity further by
       eliminating the ideas of other forces such as electricity and
       magnetism along with matter and replacing them all with a
       geometry of spacetime. He envisioned a ‘unified field theory'
       in which matter would appear as concentrations or humps in a
       universal field. . . . Einstein believed [sic] the universe is
       whole, that everything is ultimately equivalent to and
       transforms into everything else (depending on the relative
       motion of the observer): matter is equivalent to energy, gravity
       to acceleration, space to time. It is all one unified field.78
Hawking in his inaugural lecture at the University of Cambridge expressed
the hope that a final Theory of Everything is indeed possible; accordingly,
the title of his lecture read, “Is the End in Sight for Theoretical Physics?” It
implies that with the invention of the TOE, theoretical physics will come to
an end because, as its name suggests, it would explain everything.

     Now, the method of constructing the Theory of Everything consists in
the progressive unification of all partial theories, in particular the quantum
field theories. Hawking opines that “it would be very difficult to construct
a complete unified theory of everything in the universe all at one go. So
instead we have made progress by finding partial theories that describe a
limited range of happenings.”79 To start with, the special theory of relativity
has to be unified with quantum physics. Accordingly, particle physicists
have invented relativistic quantum field theories, such as quantum
electrodynamics, electroweak theory and quantum chromodynamics. As the
project of unification progresses, apparently different and independent field
theories are found to be linked at deeper conceptual levels. The leading
concepts in these projects are the two magnificent principles of
contemporary particle physics, namely, gauge symmetry and symmetry
breaking. Applying these principles, Sheldon, Weinberg and Salam
discovered that electromagnetic and weak nuclear forces are actually two
aspects of a single electroweak force described by a common set of
equations -- the electroweak theory. Further, Grand Unified Theories
attempt to integrate electroweak theory with quantum chromodynamics.
These theories have yet to bear fruit both theoretically and experimentally.

     In order to achieve the final stage of unification, gravitational
interaction has to be incorporated with the other three forces. But this
involves the integration of the general theory of relativity with quantum
mechanics. That is to say, a quantum theory of gravity has to be
constructed. This, we saw, requires quantization of the gravitational field,
and so a quantum description of space-time along with mass-energy at the
Planck regime. Such a quantum theory of gravity has to be integrated in the
final theory -- the Theory of Everything which is projected to be the
unification of the four fundamental interactions into a single mathematical
framework. John Gribbin suggests:
       A fully united description of the Universe and all it contains (a
       ‘theory of everything', or TOE) would have to take gravity and
       spacetime into the quantum fold. That implies that spacetime
       itself must be, on an appropriate very short-range level,
       quantised into discrete lumps, not smoothly continuous.80

Thus, the unification of the four interactions of nature into the TOE would
progress as follows: electromagnetic and weak interactions are unified into
electroweak theory which is incorporated with quantum chromodynamics
to form Grand Unified Theory; and finally, GUT would be integrated with
quantum theory of gravity to constitute the Theory of Everything. In such a
project, the TOE would be the final stage in the unification of the four
interactions of nature. (See Figure 22)
Figure 22. The progressive unification of the forces of nature
However, some theories like string theory attempt to develop a unified
framework in which all the four interactions of nature are described. Such
theories are also called ‘Theory of Everything.' Hawking concludes:
“Ultimately however, one would hope to find a complete, consistent,
unified theory that would include all these partial theories as
approximations, and that did not need to be adjusted to fit the facts by
picking the values of certain arbitrary numbers in the theory.”81
     Figure 22 shows the different relations between, and the present status
of, various quantum field theories. Quantum electrodynamics and
electroweak theory are fully developed and confirmed by experiment with
an amazing degree of accuracy. Quantum chromodynamics is theoretically
developed, but strong experimental confirmation is not yet forthcoming. A
number of quantum theories of gravity and Grand Unified Theories are
proposed, but these are neither theoretically complete nor are they
supported by experiments. The final unified theory would be the Theory of
Everything. The dotted steps show that advances are made in theory, but
that experimental support is wanting.82

     In fact, the Theory of Everything would represent the state of the
universe at its beginning. Thus, the steps towards a final unified theory are
also in a real sense steps backwards to the moment of creation of the
universe. Hence, the ultimate developments in TOE involve an
understanding of physical processes at the origin of the universe. Physicists
believe that all four interactions -electromagnetic, strong, weak and
gravitational - were unified as one universal interaction at ultra-high
energies of the primordial universe. Such high energies would never be
produced in particle accelerators on Earth. The only time that such energies
existed was at the very beginning of the universe. That was just 10-43 sec
after the universe emerged from the purported Big Bang singularity.
Physicists believe that just after the Big Bang itself, there was an era,
called the ‘Planck epoch.' During this period, there was only one ‘super-
force' in which gravity was unified with other three interactions. At the
Planck time scale of 10-43 sec, distance scale of 10-33 cm, temperatures
above 1032 K and energies in excess of 1019 GeV, the four fundamental
forces were unified and the strength of gravity would have been equal to
the strengths of the other forces of nature. This is indeed the regime of the
Theory of Everything.

     In the last analysis, the project of constructing the Theory of
Everything involves inventing a gauge field symmetry which would explain
the unity and diversity of the four interactions. Pagels observes: “The royal
road to unification was now seen as uniting fields under the aegis of a
spontaneously broken symmetry. The notion of a broken symmetry
explained how it was possible that forces which were fundamentally
unified and symmetrical could be manifested so differently in nature.”83
Hence, the unification of the four fundamental interactions has to do with
inventing a gauge symmetry/field which integrates them all, and which
manifests their diversity in symmetry breaking. Barrow suggests:

       Moreover, to complete a fully unified picture of the Universe,
       we must do something about the fact that we have many
       different gauge theories which must be unified into a single
       description by embedding the different symmetries associated
       with individual theories into a bigger over-riding pattern, or a
       grand unified theory [a Theory of Everything].84

Barrow expresses the same idea differently:

       Each of the four forces of Nature is accurately described by a
       theory that derives from the assumption of a particular
       invariance under all possible changes. The quest for unification
       proceeds by seeking to embed the separate patterns preserved
       by the several forces of Nature within a single ‘Grand Unified'
       pattern into which the sub-patterns fit uniquely and
       completely.85

Thus in inventing the TOE, particle physicists attempt to construct a single
gauge field symmetry that incorporates all the four interactions of nature;
this symmetry breaks at appropriate times and the forces separate with
their different ranges and their associated quanta with varying masses.

     Now, we get a deeper comprehension of the Theory of Everything by
reflecting on some of its logical properties. This would also highlight some
flaws of this projected great theory. A number of renowned physicists have
adverted to the logical characteristics of TOE. First and foremost, unlike all
other scientific theories, it would be literally comprehensive, and it would
explain the whole range of physical reality. Hence, all other theories and
laws of science could be logically inferred from this master theory. Pagels
says:
       Such a physical law [Theory of Everything] would explain the
       origin of the universe, its contents and its destiny. All other
       natural laws could be logically deduced from this one law. If
       such a law were discovered, it would be the final triumph of
       physics: the logical account of the foundation of existence
       would then be complete.86

Davies shares a similar view:

       All the laws of physics, all the parameters in the standard
       model, the various constants of nature, the existence of three
       dimensional space and one dimensional time, the origin of the
       universe, quantum mechanics, relativistic spacetime and their
       causal properties — all will follow inexorably and inevitably
       from the unified theory. In that case, the Universe must exist
       necessarily as it is; it could not have been otherwise. Such a
       theory of everything purportedly provides a complete
       understanding of physical existence. Nothing is left
       unexplained; nothing of a fundamental nature is arbitrary or
       the result of chance.87

      Moreover, the Theory of Everything would eliminate all arbitrary
initial conditions, such as those of the Big Bang, and account for all free
parameters of physics, like the fundamental constants of nature and
velocity of light. Boslough points out: “One would not regard a theory as
complete if it contained a number of adjustable parameters such as masses
or coupling constants which could be given any value one liked such as
proton-neutron mass difference being about twice the mass of the
electron.”88 Likewise, Pagels observes: “Yet most physicists feel that the
ultimate theory should have no input parameters, no fundamental
dimensionless constants, and that all the masses of quarks, and all coupling
strengths, should be predicted by such a master theory.”89 The values of
these quantities would not be adjustable, but would be fixed by the TOE
itself. Furthermore, a unified Theory of Everything purportedly would
predict the actual numerical values of the physical constants and relate
them to one another. Barrow refers to this property as the ultimate logical
test of TOE: “If we find the Theory of Everything, it should tell us the
values of the fundamental constants — and this would be the ultimate test
of such a theory.”90 Pagels suggests that the final goal TOE is to invent a
theory without any free parameters or constants: “Ultimately the goal is to
find a master theory with no arbitrary parameters, so that every physical
constant can be calculated.”91 He calls this property the ‘parameter
reduction' of TOE. “Such parameter reduction is another goal of the
fieldunification program.”92 Incidentally, Einstein refers to a theorem,
supposedly the Theory of Everything, in which there would be no free
parameters and no arbitrary constants of nature:

       Concerning such [dimensionless constants] I would like to
       state a theorem which at present cannot be based upon anything
       more than upon a faith in the simplicity, i.e., intelligibility of
       nature: there are no arbitrary constants of this kind; that is to
       say, nature is so constituted that it is possible logically to lay
       down such strongly determined laws that within these laws
       only rationally completely determined constants occur (not
       constants, therefore, whose numerical value could be changed
       without destroying the theory).93

     The Theory of Everything then aims at providing a unique, self-
consistent description of physical reality. In its most ambitious form, it
seeks to combine all quantum field theories and physical laws into a single,
unified mathematical scheme, hopefully captured in a simple formula.
Such a theory purportedly would provide a complete description of the
universe in terms of a closed system of logical truths, and would provide an
ultimate comprehension of the totality of physical existence. Hence, the
final theory would have the character of logical necessity. Davies says: “We
would finally know the reason for existence; it had to be like this (or not
exist at all).”94 TOE would be the only logically self-consistent theory
which supposedly tells us why everything is what it is.

    However, these tall claims of the Theory of Everything are challenged
by Gödel's incompleteness theorem. According to this theorem, it is
impossible to construct a mathematical framework as a closed system of
truths; it would necessarily be incomplete. Since theories of physics,
including the TOE, employ mathematical systems, they would also be
similarly incomplete. Hence, no such system of physics can ever hope to
achieve completion and logical necessity, but would always remain
unfinished. Truly, at the core of any constructed Theory of Everything
would be found lurking the inevitable incompleteness on account of
Gödel's theorem.

     Gödel's theorem proves that deductive mathematical systems based on
axiomatic method have certain inherent limitations. According to the great
mathematician Kurt Gödel, such a formalized, consistent system with true
statements is essentially incomplete. No consistent mathematical system
can prove all of its propositions to be true within the system itself. That is
to say, if a mathematical system is consistent in so far as it does not contain
any contradiction, then it would be incomplete in as much as there would
be some true statements that cannot be proved using the axioms and rules
of that system. Hence, in any system of mathematics, there will always be
some statements which cannot be proved to be true within that system. In
short, Gödel proved that no mathematical system can be both consistent —
meaning without contradiction — and complete

          — meaning that all of its propositions can be proved to be true
     within the system itself.

      Since we take consistency to be the hallmark of a deductive system,
we would have to sacrifice its completeness, that is, the truths of some of
its propositions within the system. It follows, then, that mathematics would
never constitute a completely closed logical system of knowledge, but
always remain incomplete. Eminent Oxford mathematician Roger Penrose
says: “No matter how comprehensive we think we have been, there will
always be some propositions which escape the net.”95 Similarly, physicist
Douglas Hofstadter remarks: “The goal of total and pure truth is, however,
as unreachable by formal method as is the speed of light by any material
object.”96 Mathematician Morris Kline caustically remarks that
mathematicians had been “worshipping a golden calf — rigorous,
universally acceptable proof, true in all possible worlds — in the belief that
it was God. They now realize it was a false god.”97

     A number of reputed physicists and thinkers have commented on the
profound implications of Gödel's theorem for systems of physics in
general, and for a Theory of Everything in particular. Renowned particle
physicist Freeman Dyson has stated:

       Gödel's theorem implies that pure mathematics is
       inexhaustible. No matter how many problems we solve, there
       will always be other problems that cannot be solved within the
       existing rules. . . Because of Gödel's theorem, physics is
       inexhaustible too. The laws of physics are a finite set of rules,
       and include the rules for doing mathematics, so that Gödel's
       theorem applies to them.98

     Further, Gödel's theorem has devastating effect on the unique Theory
of Everything. Philosopher of physics Michael Redhead is forthright when
he says that “this amazing result [of Gödel's theorem] does strike tellingly
at the possibility of a theory of everything, at a very fundamental level.”99
Likewise, physicist Menas Kafatos and Robert Nadeau suggest:

       If no mathematical system, no matter how formal, can reach
       closure, then it follows no physical theory built, as all modern
       physical theories must be, on mathematical systems, can reach
       closure. This means, in short, that even if we do construct a
       ‘Theory of Everything,' and even if that theory could somehow
       coordinate within its mathematical framework an explanation
       of the phenomena of life and/or consciousness, this theory
       could not ‘in principle' claim to be a complete, final, or
       ultimate description.100

Davies states that a final theory which is consistent and complete is
impossible: “So the search for a genuinely unique Theory of Everything
that would eliminate all contingency and demonstrate that the physical
world must necessarily be as it is, seems to be doomed to failure on
grounds of logical consistency. No rational system can be both consistent
and complete.”101 Similarly, Barrow acknowledges: “There is no formula
that can deliver all truth, all harmony, all simplicity. No Theory of
Everything can ever provide total insight. For, to see through everything
would leave us seeing nothing at all.”102

     Apart from the devastating effect of Gödel's theorem, there is another
profound problem that bedevils the Theory of Everything. Davies calls it
the ‘fire-breathing actualizer.'103 This phrase has reference to Hawking's
famous allusion to ‘breathing fire into the equations.' He says: “Even if
there is only one possible unified theory, it is just a set of rules and
equations. What is it that breathes fire into the equations and makes a
universe for them to describe? . . . Why does the universe go to all the
bother of existing? Is unified theory so compelling that it brings about its
own existence?”104 Hawking's questions entail that even if physicists
construct a Theory of Everything, it cannot instantiate itself and create a
universe. Davies aptly asks: “Who, or what, promotes the ‘merely possible,'
to the ‘actually existing?”105 In other words, how is it that the merely
possible universe represented by the TOE happens to become the real
universe? “A Theory of everything cannot ‘officiate' at its own
instantiation of bringing about the Universe into existence.”106 Willem
Drees makes a similar point when he says: “A mathematical idea of getting
a universe from nothing does not give a physical universe, but only the idea
of a physical universe — assuming that there is a difference between the
Universe and a mathematical idea about the Universe. There has to be some
input of ‘physical reality.'”107 A genuine Theory of Everything must
therefore explain itself and also how the universe came into existence. Of
course, these problems have not deterred particle physicists from pursuing
their ultimate dream of constructing a Theory of Everything.

     As we close our discourse on “The Standard Model of Particle
Physics,” we have to admit that it is a magnificent creation of human
scientific creativity. It succeeds at least in partially unifying the
fundamental particles and forces of nature. More importantly, it has
provided particle physicists keys like gauge principle and symmetry
breaking to unlock the secrets of physical reality. These help to solve the
great riddle of unity in the great diversity of natural phenomena. We
conclude the present chapter with a quote from Pagels on the Standard
Model:

      There you have it: the standard model consisting jointly of
      quantum chromodynamics for the strong interactions of the
      quarks and the electro-weak theory for the electromagnetic and
      weak interactions of the quarks and leptons. It seems consistent
      with all experiments; but more tests remains to be done. . . .
      Most particle theorists take the Standard Model as gospel
      truth; it describes how to build a universe, and any future
      model must include it.108
                                    5
  THE QUANTUM WORLD AND THE OBSERVER
       It is not possible to formulate the laws [of quantum physics] in
       a fully consistent way without reference to consciousness.

              –Eugene Wigner, Symmetries and Reflections

     The present chapter and its contents may appear unorthodox and/or
mystifying to some physicists who hold on to the Newtonian ideal of
science. For them, scientific investigations should be an impersonal affair,
and the human observer should not be allowed to intrude into the
impassionate enterprise called science, especially physics. However, great
quantum physicists, like Niels Bohr, Werner Heisenberg, Max Born, John
von Neumann, Eugene Wigner and John Wheeler, have categorically
affirmed that the observer/consciousness has a role to play in quantum
physics. As Wigner's quote above implies, no account of quantum physics
would be complete without also referring to the place of
observer/consciousness in the quantum world. In the first chapter of the
present book, we mentioned that one of the principal motifs of quantum
physics is the relation between the quantum system and the observer.
Similarly, in the second chapter we indicated that the observer appears to
have a role in the collapse of the wave function, at least according to
Copenhagen interpretation. This is in contrast to Newtonian mechanics in
which the observer is a mere spectator of cosmic drama. Classical,
Newtonian physics has seemingly exiled observer/consciousness from
scientific enterprise; however, quantum mechanics appears to have re-
instated it and brought it in through the back door.

5.1. The Observer in the Classical World

      The role of the observer/consciousness in quantum mechanics is best
illustrated by contrasting its place in Newtonian theory. Newton's physics
makes two sets of assumptions regarding the mode of existence of
physical objects, and the effect of observation and measurement on them.
John Honner writes:

       Crucial to the success of the mechanist model is the
       assumption that the physical objects are entirely independent
       of the effects of observation, so that any measurements of the
       state of the system at any point in space and time do not
       interfere with its future pathways in space and time. A second
       assumption is that these independent physical bodies are
       permanent and that they maintain the properties we observe in
       them whether we are observing or not.1

We take up the second assumption first. Classical mechanics postulates
that physical objects exist permanently over time, independently of being
observed or interacted with. At any particular time, they have mass,
definite dimensions, position in space (both location and orientation) and
motion (both velocity and rotation) in time. In other words, material
particles and their aggregates have definite mass, places, times and
velocities independent of our observing them. These are all in fact part of
our common sense perception and understanding of physical objects in the
world around us.

     The first assumption mentioned above affirms that according to
Newtonian mechanics, physical objects exist independently of our
observation and measurement. Obviously, we come to know physical
systems by making measurements or observations. “A measurement is a
straightforward physical process of interaction between a measuring
instrument and a measured system, the outcome of which is directly
related to the feature of the system under investigation in a known way.”2
According to classical physics, observations either produce no significant
disturbance on measured systems, or else such disturbances are precisely
calculable. Physical systems exist and evolve independently of the
presence of observers as observers. “The old physics assumes that there is
an external world which exists apart from us. It further assumes that we
can observe, measure, and speculate about the external world without
changing it.”3 The existence and nature of physical objects and processes
in general are not directly dependent upon their being observed. Observers
as intelligent beings play no essential role in the measuring process.
Properties of physical objects, such as mass, position, duration and
velocity, measured by an observer exist independently of the observer. In
other words, physical systems - objects and their motions - are in no way
affected or disturbed by an observation. An object would be what it is
whether it is observed or not.

    Great physicists have acknowledged the independence of the
observed physical system from the observer in classical mechanics.
Heisenberg says:

       In fact, our ordinary description of nature, and the idea of
       exact laws, rests on the assumption that it is possible to
       observe the phenomena without appreciably influencing them.
       To coordinate a definite cause to a definite effect has sense
       only when both can be observed without introducing a foreign
       element disturbing their interaction.4

Max Born testifies:

       The generation to which Einstein, Bohr, and I belong was
       taught that there exists an objective physical world, which
       unfolds itself according to immutable laws independent of us;
       we are watching this process as the audience watches a play in
       a theater. Einstein still believes that this should be the relation
       between the scientific observer and his subject.5

Paul Davies and John Gribbin attest:

       In the pre-quantum era of physics, everyone assumed that the
       world ‘out there' existed in a well-defined state quite
       irrespective of whether, or how, it was observed. Admittedly
       the act of observation would intrude into that reality, for we
       cannot observe anything without interacting with it physically
       to some extent; yet it was always supposed that the interaction
       was purely incidental and could either be made arbitrarily
       small (at least in principle) or else be performed in a
       controlled way and so be taken precisely into account.6

Zukav shares a similar view: “The concept of scientific objectivity rests
upon the assumption of an external world which is ‘out there' as opposed
to an ‘I' which is ‘in here.' . . . According to this view, Nature, in all
diversity, is ‘out there.' The task of the scientist is to observe the ‘out
there' as objectively as possible.”7

      Such a belief in the separation of the observed physical object and the
observer resulted in a world view in which man and nature parted ways to
evolve and subsist by themselves. The world of nature was thought to be
an impersonal, mathematically ordered machine. Reputed historian and
philosopher John Randall summarizes the Newtonian mechanistic
conception of the world: “The world is a vast perpetual motion machine,
and every event in it can be deduced mathematically from the fundamental
principles of its mechanical action; the discovery of these mathematical
relations is the goal of science.”8 Zukav observes that such a mechanistic
view of the world correlates with the search after complete objectivity in
scientific knowledge: “The Great Machine is impersonal. In fact, it was
precisely this impersonality that inspired scientists to strive for ‘absolute
objectivity.'”9 The end result of such impersonal, mechanistic world view
was that it had no place for mind/consciousness. Errol Harris observes:

       But the Newtonian instauration provided no mechanics of the
       mind, and the celestial mechanics made no room for
       consciousness. The scientific observer viewed the world, as it
       were, from the outside, and within that world no provision was
       made for any consciousness. The observer, therefore,
       remained outside the purview of investigation and was
       presumed to make no difference to its results.10

Likewise, Davies says: “[T]he clockwork mechanism [the physical world]
carried on turning, completely oblivious of whether, or by whom, it was
being observed.”11 Philosopher of science Edwin Burtt comments on man's
experience in such an impersonal, mechanistic universe: “Man begins to
appear for the first time in the history of thought as an irrelevant spectator
and insignificant effect of the great mathematical system which is the
substance of reality.”12 The natural world was portrayed as a vast, self-
contained, mathematical machine, consisting of motions of material
particles and their aggregates in space and time, and man with his
purposes, feelings and aspirations was demoted as a mere onlooker of
cosmic phenomena.

     Thus, in the Newtonian, mechanistic world view, the physical world
was conceived as a vast machine, in which there was no room for
observer/human mind. Although the importance of observation was
stressed by scientists, the observer as observer had no role to play in
scientific enterprise. The observer was set apart from the scene that was
being surveyed, a scene viewed from the outside, as it were, through a
telescope or a microscope, or other instruments of observation. We
conclude with the words of physicist Alastair Rae:

       Ever since the beginning of modern science four or five
       hundred years ago, scientific thought seems to have moved
       man and consciousness further from the centre of things.
       More and more of the universe has become explicable in
       mechanical, objective terms, and even human beings are
       becoming understood scientifically by biologists and
       behavioural scientists.13

5.2. The Observer in the Quantum World

     In fact, until the advent of quantum physics, it was irrelevant to
discuss the role of observer/consciousness in physical sciences. But as the
renowned philosopher of science Karl Popper says, in the quantum
scenario “we have to regard ‘the observer' or ‘the subject' as particularly
important, because atomic theory takes its peculiar character largely from
the interference of the subject or the observer (and his ‘measuring
agencies') with the physical object under investigation.”14 In general, this
is known as the ‘measurement problem.'
5.2.1. The Observer and Measurement of Quantum Objects

     The measurement/observation of physical systems and the relation of
the observer to these systems were not problems for classical physics -
both Newtonian mechanics and the theory of relativity. But in quantum
theory, these have turned out to be disturbing questions to physicists.
Davies and Gribbin testify:

       Probably the most unsettling aspect of these studies [quantum
       physics] is the way that the observer seems to play a central
       role in fixing the nature of reality at the quantum level. This
       has long worried both physicists and philosophers. . . . But
       quantum physics presents a picture of reality in which
       observer and observed are inextricably interwoven in an
       intimate way. The effect of observation is absolutely
       fundamental to the reality that is revealed, and cannot be
       either reduced or simply compensated for.15

The role of the observer in the quantum world has to do first and foremost
with the measurement/observation of quantum objects. As we have seen,
according to the Copenhagen interpretation, when the measurement of a
quantum object is made by an observer, its wave function collapses, and
one of its possibilities turns into actuality.16 Ian Barbour clarifies the
meaning of observing in this context: “Observing consists in extracting
from the existing probability distribution one of the many possibilities it
contains. The influence of the observer, in this [Copenhagen] view, does
not consist in disturbing a previously precise though unknown value, but
in forcing one of the many existing potentialities to be actualized.”17

     Eminent physicists have supported the Copenhagen view that the
observer is deeply involved in the process of observing quantum objects.
Bohr and Heisenberg, the great founders of the Copenhagen school, have
categorically affirmed this position. Bohr says: “Now, the quantum
postulate implies that any observation of atomic phenomena will involve
an interaction with the agency of observation . . . . Accordingly, an
independent reality in the ordinary sense can neither be ascribed to the
phenomena nor to the agencies of observation.”18 Heisenberg remarks
about the intimate link between the observed system and the observer:
“This indeterminateness of the picture of the [quantum] process is a direct
result of the indeterminateness of the concept ‘observation'— it is not
possible to decide, other than arbitrarily, what objects are to be considered
as part of the observed system and what as part of the observer's
apparatus.”19 Davies and Gribbin opine: “The oddity of ‘the collapse of the
wave function' is that it seems to depend upon the activities of the
observer. If nobody looks, then the wave [function] never collapses. So the
behaviour of a particle such as an electron appears to vary according to
whether it is being watched or not.”20

     Obviously, the key concept in the ‘measurement problem' is the wave
function, the fundamental postulate in quantum mechanics; and so we can
comprehend the role of the observer in the process of measurement only
by understanding the implications of this notion. This can best be achieved
by contrasting wave function with the comparable concept in classical
mechanics, namely, the initial conditions.21 The wave function supposedly
‘represents' the ‘state' of a quantum particle, which is not an actual state,
but a superposition of all its possibilities. In contrast, the initial conditions
in classical mechanics comprise the actual state of an object. Take for
example, the case of a boy throwing a stone at a distant target. The state of
this object -- the initial conditions of the stone -- consists of quantities,
such as its stuff, mass, shape, weight, position and velocity. Another
example is tossing a coin. The initial conditions of the coin includes
quantities, like its mass, weight, shape, precise position, orientation in
space and motion at some instant of time.

     The important point to note here is that these initial conditions of
stone and coin are actual quantities, not mere possibilities as in the case of
the wave function of a quantum object. An observer is not needed in order
to transform their possibilities into actualities simply because all the
possibilities (quantities) of these physical objects are actually present. In
the case of the stone, its position and velocity are actual quantities which
can be precisely stated; and if it is thrown, the time it takes for hitting the
target can in principle be predicted with precision. Similarly, in the case of
the coin, it would be possible to know precisely its initial conditions, and
the outcome of tosses can be predicted at least in principle with certainty.
Hence, in classical physics, given sufficient information about the initial
conditions of a physical object and the related laws of physics, one can in
principle predict its behaviour with certainty. Moreover, in both cases -
throwing a stone and tossing a coin -- an observer merely registers the
trajectory of stone and the movement of tossed coin; his/her observation in
no way affects the motion of ball or of coin, but just records the hitting of
stone at the target and outcome of the tosses. A camera will equally do the
job of observing these objects - the thrown stone and the tossed coin.
However, certain knowledge of the movements of physical objects, like
thrown stone and tossed coin, is rarely achieved in classical physics; in
most cases, only probability is attained. The reason is that most often the
knowledge of initial conditions is incomplete or errors creep in. In
classical physics then the use of probabilities is due to practical
limitations of our knowledge of initial conditions and of other
determinants. That is the reason why predictions are only probable.

      However, the quantum scenario is in complete contrast with the
classical setting. Here the state of a quantum object is described by a wave
function. But unlike the initial conditions of a physical object in classical
mechanics, the wave function does not represent anything actual, but is a
superposition of all the possibilities of a quantum object. For instance, the
reality of an electron as particle or as wave, and its properties, such as
position and velocity, are not actual quantities, but evolve as an ensemble
of possibilities. The electron's wave function describes the superposition
of these possibilities; and Schrödinger's wave equation predicts the
probability of some of these possibilities becoming actual. These
equations allow us to calculate, for instance, the relative probabilities of
finding an electron at different locations on an image screen in double-slit
experiment. Since the wave function describes an ensemble of
possibilities, one can calculate only probability, not the actual outcome of
any particular possibility. Here probability is an inherent feature of an
evolving quantum object, not a result of incomplete knowledge as in
classical physics. Physicist Stephen Barr explains:
       There [in quantum theory] one cannot dispense with
       probabilistic concepts; they are woven into the very fabric of
       the theory. Even if one had all the information about a
       physical system which it were possible to have at one time, . .
       . one would still not be able to predict everything about the
       future behavior of that system with certainty. . . .

       There is, then, a profound difference in the way probability
       enters in the two frameworks [Newtonian mechanics and
       quantum physics]. In the classical framework, the use of
       probability is not in principle necessary, whereas in the
       quantum framework it is. In quantum theory the probability
       amplitudes are at the very heart of the mathematical
       description of physical reality.22

Similarly, Davies says: “Quantum mechanics is therefore a statistical
theory. But unlike other statistical theories (e.g. the behavior of stock
markets, roulette wheels) its probabilistic nature is not merely a matter of
ignorance of details; it is inherent.”23 Hence in classical physics,
probability does not play the fundamental role that it does in quantum
mechanics. The latter is intrinsically probabilistic in a way in which
classical physics is not.

     But supposing the wave function were to describe some actual states
of quantum objects as in classical physics, the observer would not have a
role to play in quantum theory.24 It is precisely the characterization of
wave function as an ensemble of possibilities, and the insertion of
probabilistic concepts into the very fabric of quantum theory that open
door to the entry of observer in the process of measurement/observation.
Thus, as long as the wave function of a quantum system like an electron is
undisturbed by external influences, that is, before it is measured/observed,
it evolves as a superposition of possibilities. The Schrödinger equation
predicts the probabilities of these possibilities becoming actual. This
means that if the probabilities of a quantum system are specified at one
time, their values at later times are in principle computable. But, when the
system is measured and observed, one of the possibilities represented by
wave function, and whose probable occurrence, among others, has been
predicted by Schrödinger equation, becomes actual, and the rest continue
to evolve as possibilities. This is in fact the collapse of the wave function.
Barr writes: “The point at which the transition is made between the
hypothetical and the actual is the point at which the observer makes the
measurement/observation that one hypothetical possibility did actualize,
and the others did not, that the measurement gave one result, and not the
others.”25

     Thus, the wave function of a quantum object, such as a photon or an
electron collapses, and a transition from possibilities to actuality takes
place when a measurement is made by the observer. Nobel Laureate
Eugene Wigner was one of the first physicists to recognize the influence
of observer/consciousness in quantum measurement. He says:

       The being with a consciousness must have a different role in
       quantum mechanics than the inanimate measuring device. . . .
       In other words, the impression which one gains at an
       interaction, called also the result of an observation, modifies
       the wave function of the system. The modified wave function
       is, furthermore, in general unpredictable before the
       impression gained at the interaction has entered our
       consciousness: it is the entering of an impression into our
       consciousness which alters the wave function . . . . It is at this
       point that the consciousness enters the theory unavoidably and
       unalterably.26

     Renowned mathematician and physicist John von Neumann critically
examined the entire chain of events involved in an experiment which
consists of the quantum system being measured, the measuring device,
such as a photographic plate on which the result is recorded, and the
human observer. He realized that all of these elements with the exception
of the observer are composed of the same subatomic particles. Only the
human observer by virtue of his/her consciousness is unique. Von
Neumann was forced to conclude that the ultimate outcome of a quantum
experiment is determined by human consciousness. Nick Herbert
comments on von Neumann's predicament:

       In desperation von Neumann seized on its [the chain of
       elements in an experiment] only peculiar link: the process by
       which a physical signal in the brain becomes an experience in
       the human mind. This is the only process in the whole von
