       Neumann chain which is not mere molecules in motion. Von
       Neumann reluctantly came to the conclusion that the human
       consciousness is the site of the wave function collapse.27

As physicist Max Jammer comments, it was “von Neumann's far-reaching
contention that it is impossible to formulate a complete and consistent
theory of quantum mechanical measurement without reference to human
consciousness.”28 Fritjof Capra shares a similar view: “In atomic physics
the observed phenomena can be understood only as correlations between
various processes of observation and measurement, and the end of this
chain of processes lies always in the consciousness of the human
observer.”29

     Now, the question is, can a physical system like a camera take the
place of an observer in the measurement of a quantum system? Physicist
Walter Heitler asks, “if it is sufficient to carry out a measurement by a
self-registering apparatus [like a camera] or whether the presence of an
observer is required.” He answers that such an apparatus is insufficient,
and that “the observer appears, as a necessary part of the whole structure,
and in his full capacity as a conscious being.”30 This means that a
recording device like a camera cannot function as an observer in the
measurement of a quantum system. Physicists, like Eugene Wigner, John
von Neumann, Frits London, Edmond Bauer and Rudolf Peierls, have
strongly supported this view.

     Let us examine whether a camera or any other recording device can
replace an observer. The camera and film are themselves physical systems
made up of subatomic particles similar to the particle they are supposed to
record. Consequently, the behaviour of camera and film can also be
described using equations of quantum theory, just as the evolution of the
particle in question is described. In principle, it is possible to write down a
very complex wave function that describes the whole complex system
consisting of the evolution of particle, camera and film. That wave
function will evolve in accordance with Schrödinger equation and would
yield only probabilities. The camera capturing on film the motion of
particle would be described in terms of these probabilities. Hence, the
camera ‘taking' snapshot would not produce a definite outcome. In other
words, the wave function that describes what happens to the whole system
of particle, camera and film, contains only probabilities of the evolution of
the complex system. But it would not tell us which of those probabilities is
actually realized.31

     The problem here is that by expanding the mathematical description
to include camera and film, these become part of the continuous evolution
of the wave function which describes their probabilities. The whole system
is then trapped in the realm of probabilities; there is nothing outside the
new, larger wave function causing it to collapse. For this to happen, there
has to be an observer outside the whole system. John von Neumann
envisaged an apparently unending chain of measuring devices, each
observing the preceding member of the series, but none ever bringing
about the collapse of the wave function. The chain can then only come to
an end with a conscious observer. Only with the measurement by an
observer will the entire pyramid of quantum ‘limbo' states collapse into a
concrete actuality. Barr says: “In short, the observer cannot be considered
part of the system that is being physically described and remain the
observer of it. . . . You [the observer] cannot be described completely by
the wave function and also collapse it.”32

     This means that in the mathematical description of the quantum
system to be measured, it is not possible to include the observer. For if one
attempted to describe the observer in the mathematics of quantum theory,
then the observer too would become a part of the quantum world of
probability. The observer would step out of the world of facts, so to speak,
and enter into the realm of possibilities and probabilities. However, the
role that the observer plays in quantum physics is precisely to allow an
escape from the realm of possibilities. Hence, if the observer were to
become part of it, the observer would not be able to play this role. In other
words, the original observer would have abdicated the function of observer
by becoming part of the quantum system being observed. The task of
observer would have to be taken up by someone else — someone outside
the mathematical description. The upshot of these considerations is that
the observer as observer is not describable by the mathematics of quantum
theory.33 We affirm with Barr: “The mathematical description of the
physical world given to us by quantum theory presupposes the existence of
observers who lie outside those mathematical descriptions.”34 Hence, we
may safely conclude that the observer or consciousness plays a
fundamental role in quantum mechanics. This position is accepted by the
significant number of physicists already referred, but they do not all have
the same idea about the role of consciousness in the measurement process.
However, that consciousness or mind is a fundamental reality which is not
reducible to physical description is clearly admitted by Eugene Wigner,
Fritz London, Edmond Bauer and Rudolf Peierls. Quantum physicists
Henry Stapp and Euan Squires have most forcefully argued in support of
this point of view.35

     Nevertheless, the role of mind in quantum mechanics is viewed with
great suspicion by a number of physicists. Eminent physicists, like
Einstein, and philosophers of science, like Karl Popper and Ernest Nagel,
have voiced strong objections against the intrusion of the
observer/consciousness into physics.36 We do not go in for a detailed
discussion of their positions, but just quote only Popper who is a very
acerbic critic of assigning a role to consciousness in quantum physics. He
states:

       In this introduction, [to his book Quantum Theory and Schism
       in Physics] I attempt to exorcize the ghost called
       ‘consciousness' or ‘the observer' from quantum mechanics,
       and to show that quantum mechanics is as ‘objective' a theory
       as, say, classical statistical mechanics. In the body of this
       volume, I shall attempt to substantiate my argument in
       somewhat greater detail, and to state my own understanding of
       these issues that have plagued quantum theory over the past
       fifty years, and my own alternative approach.

       My thesis in this introduction is that the observer, or better,
       the experimentalist, plays in quantum theory exactly the same
       role as in classical physics. His task is to test the theory.37

But Euan Squires counters:

       It is probably fair to say that most members of the physics
       community would reject ideas [about role of the observer].
       [However], their reasons would be based more on prejudice
       than on sound argument, and the proportion of those who
       reject would be much smaller if we considered only those who
       had actually thought carefully about the problems of quantum
       theory.38

As a matter of fact, the Many Worlds hypothesis is the only theory which
can serve as an alternative to the involvement of the observer/ mind in the
measurement process. But that is a huge ransom for rejecting the observer.
Barr says: “The price to be paid for eliminating the observer and the
observer's mind from the picture in this way, however, is the postulating of
an infinite number of branches of reality, with an infinite number of
versions of every person, that are completely unobservable to us [Many
Worlds hypothesis].”39

     We conclude with the statements of some physicists affirming the
role of the observer in the quantum world. Niels Bohr, the great founder of
the Copenhagen school, says: “Indeed, the finite interaction between
object and measuring agencies . . . entails the necessity of a final
renunciation of the classical ideal . . . and a radical revision of our attitude
towards the problem of physical reality.”40 Pagels concurs: “The ancient
idea that the world actually exists in a definite state has come to an end.
The quantum theory reveals a new message — reality is in part created by
the observer.”41 John von Neumann, we saw, has argued that the
measurement problem can only be resolved by appeal to human
consciousness. Referring to von Neumann's view, Robert J. Russell says:
“Since even the macroscopic apparatus must in principle be understood
quantum mechanically, only the conscious observer can actualize quantum
properties and, in this (strong) sense according to von Neumann,
consciousness creates the objective world.”42

5.2.2. The Observer and the Properties of Quantum Objects

      Here again we start our discourse on the relations between the
observer and the properties of quantum objects by comparing them with
corresponding relations in Newton's mechanics and Einstein's theory of
relativity. In classical physics and also in our ordinary experience, we have
said, measured/observed objects exist by themselves; and they have
properties independently of the measuring instruments or the observer. In
classical mechanics, the observer does not figure in any significant sense
of the term in the measurement process; rather the observer is effectively
excluded. The basic quantities of Newtonian physics, namely, space, time,
matter and motion, are thought to be absolute, existing independently of
the knowledge of the observer. “For the scientist, to say that a datum is
‘absolute' is to say that it is not linked to this or that observer, . . .”43
Further, the properties of physical objects also exist independently of the
observer. The properties, such as length, mass and duration, are not
influenced by the measurement of, or by the state of motion of, the
observer. These are absolute in so far as they remain constant always and
everywhere, whether they are observed or not, or whether the observer is
moving or not. In classical physics, these properties are thought to be
inherent attributes of physical systems being observed.

      Oddly enough, it was Einstein's theory of relativity which for the first
time in the history of physics introduced observer in a scientific theory in
an essential way. Harris suggests: “Quantum theory and Relativity finally
undermined the classical dichotomy, because they involved the observer
inextricably with what was observed.”44 With the advent of the theory of
relativity, there is no absolute standard of measurement, and every frame
of reference became relative to every other. Harris says: “When it had
been established that there was no absolute frame of reference, that every
frame was in motion relative to every other, the velocity of the observer
affected every observation and every measurement.”45 This means that
according to the theory of relativity, the properties of physical objects are
not intrinsic, but relative to the observer. Blanché tells us:

       The theory of relativity teaches us that these [length, duration
       and mass] are not properties inherent in things and physical
       phenomena, but only relations between them and a given
       observer. To ask what is the distance between A and B is a
       question as meaningless as to ask what is the position of a
       point -- unless a reference system is specified, or at least,
       unless such a referential is implicitly and indubitably
       understood.46

      Thus, the theory of relativity teaches that there is no absolute
standard of mass, length and duration independent of the observer; it all
depends on the state of motion of the observer. The great physicist Arthur
Eddington refers to the crucial role of human observer in the phenomenon
of length contraction in the special theory of relativity: “When a rod is
started from rest into uniform motion, nothing whatever happens to the
rod. We say that it contracts; but length is not a property of the rod; it is a
relation between the rod and the observer. Until the observer is specified,
the length of the rod is quite indeterminate.”47 Ian Barbour speaks in a
similar vein: “In relativity, all the basic properties of objects, such as
length, time, and mass, are relative to the observer.”48 Hence, we cannot
speak about the properties of the observed object without taking into
account the state of motion of the observer. Davies explains:

       The essential element injected into physics by the theory of
       relativity is subjectivity. Fundamental things like duration,
       length, past, present and future can no longer be regarded as a
       dependable framework within which to live our lives. Instead
       they are flexible, elastic qualities, and their values depend on
       precisely who is measuring them. In this sense the observer is
       beginning to play a rather central role in the nature of the
       world.49
In a deeper sense, space-time framework itself becomes relative to the
observer. Theoretical physicist Mendel Sachs writes:

       The real revolution that came with Einstein's theory . . . was
       the abandonment of the idea that the space-time coordinate
       system has objective significance as a separate physical entity.
       Instead of this idea, relativity theory implies that the space
       and time coordinates are only the elements of a language that
       is used by an observer to describe his environment.50

Similarly, Davies states: “Space and time are not fixed, but are in a sense
elastic; they can stretch and shrink depending on who is observing
them.”51

     In quantum theory, the link between the observer and the observed
system goes much deeper. This relationship is much more bizarre and
strange. A number of renowned physicists have testified to this effect.
Davies is forthright when he says: “Quantum theory requires the
incorporation of the observer into the physical world in a still more
essential way.”52 Some physicists hold that the properties of quantum
objects do not exist unless and until they are observed by the observer.
Thus, John Barrow and Frank Tipler opine that “many physical properties
of atomic particles did not even exist before the act of observation; the act
of observation was necessary to bring these properties into existence.”53
Likewise, David Bohm writes:

       But in the usual interpretation of the quantum theory, an atom
       has no properties at all when it is not observed. Indeed, one
       may say that its only mode of being is to be observed; for the
       notion of an atom existing with uniquely definable properties
       of its own even when it is not interacting with a piece of
       observing apparatus, is meaningless within the framework of
       this point of view.54

     This means that these properties are not inherent in quantum object,
but instead they are relations or interactions between the observed object
and the observer. Capra remarks:

       The crucial feature of atomic physics is that the human
       observer is not only necessary to observe the properties of an
       object, but is necessary even to define these properties. In
       atomic physics, we cannot speak about the properties of an
       object as such. They are meaningful only in the context of the
       object's interaction with the observer.55

Babu Joseph shares a similar view when he says: “Quantum mechanics has
shown that the properties of a physical system that are investigated by an
observer (knower), depend on the observer's state. Since the observer is
the agency possessing knowledge, this interaction may be described as a
‘knowledge-physical system' interaction.”56 Moreover, it is even difficult
to speak about the separate existence of a quantum object apart from its
properties. Rae suggests: “If all properties of an object, its mass, position,
energy, etc., are quantum in nature and do not have values until they are
measured, it is hard to see any meaning in the object's separate
existence.”57 This is in fact ‘quantum idealism.'

     Incidentally, the ‘subjective idealism' of the British philosopher
George Berkeley (1685-1753) appears to be very similar to ‘quantum
idealism.' Historians of philosophy, Gunnar Skirbekk and Nils Gilje
summarize his thought: “The first step in Berkeley's immaterialism
[idealism] is thus the rejection of the idea that matter is something
different from [its] properties; the second step is an interpretation of the
properties as sense impressions.”58 According to Berkeley's view, matter
does not exist apart from its properties which are known through our sense
impressions. Since sense impressions exist only when they are perceived,
matter too exists only when it is perceived. Philosophically, this position is
expressed as “esse est percipi;” that is, “to be or to exist is to be
perceived.” In simple words, it means that a physical object exists only
when it is perceived. In the present context, we can render this statement
as follows: the properties of quantum objects exist only when they are
observed; and since these objects have no existence apart from their
properties, quantum objects exist only when they are observed. In fact,
Heisenberg has stated as much when he said: “The conception of objective
reality . . . evaporated into . . . the mathematics that represents no longer
the behavior of elementary particles but rather our knowledge of this
behavior.”59 Some authors have explicitly identified Berkeley's thought
with quantum mechanics. Coveney and Highfield suggest: “It [quantum
theory] is the modern version of Bishop Berkeley's famous denial of the
existence of the tree in the quadrangle at New College, Oxford when he
turned his back on it.”60

     Now the question is, how can we substantiate the claim that
properties of quantum objects, such as an electron, depend on the
observer? The answer is that these properties are dependent on
experimental conditions; and that it is the observer who chooses
experimental set up. The observer decides which measurement set up is to
be chosen in order to determine the properties of an observed object. If the
experimental conditions are modified, then the properties of observed
object will also be different. Prigogine and Stengers remark:

       All description [of the measurement of a quantum system]
       thus implies a choice of the measurement device, a choice of
       the question asked. In this sense, the answer, the result, of the
       measurement, does not give us access to a given reality. We
       have to decide which measurement we are going to perform
       and which question our experiments will ask the system.61

Thus for instance, in double-slit experiment and photoelectric effect, the
observer's decision about observing photons determines their properties to
some extent. If the observer asks them a wave-question, they will give a
wave-answer; but if the observer asks photons a particle-question, they
will give a particle-answer. It is the observer who makes the choice as to
what to observe - wave or particle. We can demonstrate that light is a wave
in double-slit experiment, and that light consists of particles in
photoelectric effect. Both of these complementary aspects of light (wave
and particle) are necessary in order to understand the nature of light. It is
meaningless to ask what light really is. Light behaves as waves or as
particles depending upon which experiment we choose to perform, and
which question we ask. Wave and particle are properties of light as it
interacts with the observer. The wave-like behaviour that we observe in
doubleslit experiment is not a property of light; it is a property of our
interaction with light. Similarly, the particle-like character observed in
photoelectric effect is not a property of light, but a property of our
interaction with light. Thus, wave-like behaviour and particlelike
behaviour are properties of our interactions with light. Since these
properties are now known to belong, not to light itself, but to observer's
interaction with light, it is argued that light has no properties independent
of the observer.

     Similarly, the properties of position and velocity of quantum objects
too are dependent on experimental arrangements, and so, on the observer.
Heisenberg's uncertainty principle states that if the value of a physical
quantity is certain, then the value of its complementary quantity is
uncertain. Hence, it is impossible to measure with certainty both the
position and velocity of a quantum particle like an electron at the same
time. Consequently, when observing such a particle, we may choose to
measure either its position or its momentum. We can obtain either a
precise knowledge about the particle's position, but remain uncertain about
its momentum, or vice versa. The choice to measure either of the two
properties depends on the observer. John Wheeler says: “The position (or
momentum) of an object only acquires useful meaning through the
participatory act of observation.”62

     We wind up the present chapter on “The Quantum World and the
Observer,” with some quotes from eminent quantum physicists on the
profound involvement of the observer/consciousness in the quantum world
and in the universe itself. Great physicists, like Niels Bohr, Werner
Heisenberg, Max Born, Arthur Eddington, Andrei Linde and John
Wheeler, have strongly endorsed the view that the observed quantum
system and the observer are intimately related. According to Wheeler, the
most important feature of quantum mechanics is this deep relationship
between the two; and so he replaces the word ‘observer' by the word
‘participator': “To describe what has happened [measurement of a quantum
system], one has to cross out that old word ‘observer' and put in its place
the new word ‘participator.' In some strange sense, the universe is a
participatory universe.”63 Obviously, Wheeler implies that mind or
consciousness plays a fundamental role in quantum processes and the
universe.

     The reputed cosmologist Andrei Linde who proposed the theory of
chaotic inflation, asserted as much when he said: “Thus we see that
without introducing the observer, we have a dead universe, which does not
evolve in time.”64 He is reported to have gone further and suggested: “We
are together, the universe and us. The moment you say that the universe
exists without any observers, I cannot make any sense out of that. I cannot
imagine a consistent theory of everything that ignores consciousness. . . .
In the absence of observers, our universe is dead.”65 Arthur Eddington
voices a similar opinion:

       Recognizing that the physical world is entirely abstract and
       without ‘actuality' apart from its linkage to consciousness, we
       restore consciousness to the fundamental position instead of
       representing it as an inessential complication occasionally
       found in the midst of inorganic nature at a late stage of
       evolutionary history.66

Davies shares a similar view when he says:

       Perhaps more than anything else this conclusion carries the
       greatest significance of the quantum revolution, for unlike all
       the previous scientific revolutions, which have successively
       demoted mankind from the centre of creation to the role of
       mere spectator of the cosmic drama, quantum theory
       reinstates the observer at the centre of the stage.67

Apparently, Eugene Wigner goes so far as to affirm the supremacy of
consciousness: “It will remain remarkable in whatever way our future
concepts may develop, that the very study of the external world led to the
conclusion that the content of the consciousness is an ultimate reality.”68
Truly, unlike the classical mechanics which exiled consciousness from the
world, quantum mechanics appears to have placed it at the center of the
universe.69
                                      6
                THE QUANTUM COSMOLOGY
       Maybe the universe itself sprang into existence out of
       nothingness -- a gigantic vacuum fluctuation which we know
       today as the big bang.

                    –Heinz Pagels, The Cosmic Code

     Cosmology is physics applied to the universe as a whole. The Big
Bang theory is actually the end product of the application of Einstein's
general theory of relativity to the entire universe. From the general theory
to the Big Bang theory, it is just a small step. Similarly, it is a short
distance from quantum field theories to quantum cosmology. We had
discussed about the creation of particles and anti-particles when we dealt
with quantum field theories and quantum vacuum. Thence quantum
cosmology -- the quantum creation of the universe - is only a short
distance. Davies observes: “Combining the two extreme ends of physics -
cosmology, the study of the universe, and quantum mechanics, the study of
atomic and subatomic systems - is ambitious by any standard.”1 However,
such speculations, Hawley and Holocomb warn us, “can as yet be no better
than educated guesswork, and may prove to be nonsense.”2 Nonetheless,
some cosmologists have dared to engage in such bold ‘educated guesswork'
regarding the quantum origin and end of the universe. They have advocated
different versions of quantum cosmology. Renowned physicists Andrei
Linde, Alex Vilenkin and Edward Tryon have put forward theories, known
as ‘chaotic inflation' and ‘eternal inflation.' These theories suggest that our
universe originated as the outcome of a primordial quantum vacuum
fluctuation. The theory of quantum creation of the universe is
complemented by a theory of its quantum destruction - the Big Decay.

6.1. The Quantum Origins of the Universe
     No comprehensive discourse on the quantum origins of the universe
would be complete without also discussing about the theory of its Big Bang
creation. In particular, we have to explain how to conceptually ‘tame' Big
Bang singularity. Reputed physicists Stephen Hawking and James Hartle,
and John Wheeler have proposed theories as to how to ‘handle' this
supposed primordial state of the universe. These discussions take us to the
quantum creation of the universe - the quantum cosmology.

6.1.1. ‘Taming' Big Bang Singularity

      We start the saga of Big Bang creation with the concept of Big Bang
singularity. This idea, we saw, has its origin in the ‘singularity theorems'
invented by Stephen Hawking and Roger Penrose in 1964.3 They
demonstrated that every solution to Einstein's general theory of relativity
eventually develops a state of singularity. Singularity is a region in space-
time at which some physical quantity of a star, like density of mass/energy,
temperature or strength of gravitational field (curvature of space-time)
becomes infinite. Such a state of the star would be a black hole. Hawking
and Penrose further showed that our universe would have had a space-time
singularity at the beginning of its Big Bang expansion. Now, in the
framework of the Big Bang model, this state of singularity would be
reached by specifying the antecedent states of the present universe, and
working all the way ‘back' to its beginning. In the equations used to model
the origin of the universe, this beginning is referred by the time parameter
t; and in many cosmological models, there is a singularity at t = 0.
“According to the standard model, at time zero (by definition) the universe
had infinite matter density, infinite curvature and infinite temperature — a
state known as ‘singularity.'”4 In other words, the limit or the beginning of
succession of hotter and denser phases of the present universe is a point
where density and temperature are infinite. Cosmologists refer to it as
‘initial singularity,' or ‘Big Bang singularity,' and they call it the
‘beginning' of the universe at time zero.

     What is significant in this brief description of the Big Bang model of
the universe is the important role of time. Time decisively enters into the
conceptual framework of the Big Bang theory. The Big Bang singularity is
the regime of physical reality ‘where' time is supposedly zero, and it
represents the time ‘when' the universe purportedly originated. Hawking
tells us: “If the classical theory of general relativity was correct, the
singularity theorem showed that the beginning of time would have been a
point of infinite density and curvature [that is, singularity].”5 Singularity is
thus the end product of the general theory of relativity. According to this
theory, the density distribution of mass/energy in the universe determines
the curvature space-time. This relation between mass/energy and spacetime
has profound implications for the origin of the universe. For, if the general
theory is applied indiscriminately back to the beginning of the universe, it
predicts that space-time curvature would become infinite. Such an infinite
space-time warpage is supposedly Big Bang singularity at time zero.
Davies warns: “In the context of the big bang origin of the universe and the
possible presence of an initial singularity, the Planck time serves as a
warning: we should not trust general relativity when extrapolated to within
one Planck time of the origin.”6

     However, what is intriguing is that we do not encounter such a
singularity or a temporal beginning of the universe in Newtonian
cosmology; its conceptual structure cannot, and so does not, admit such a
supposed state of the universe. The reason is that according to Newtonian
mechanics, time does not enter into the constitution of the universe
although it is a category to describe cosmic phenomena. Newton speaks of
time as follows: “Absolute true and mathematical time, of itself and by its
own nature, flows uniformly, without regard to anything external.”7 Capek
comments:

       According to this view time flows no matter whether
       something changes or not; in its own nature time is empty and
       is only in an accessory and contingent way filled by changes.
       Changes are in time; they are not time itself. This distinction
       between time and concrete becoming is at the very foundations
       of classical physics.8

Time, like Newtonian space, is completely indifferent to all changes in the
universe; and so “no changes, whether observable or not, can be produced
by the mere flow of time.”9 Thus, time in no way affects changes in the
universe nor does the universe influence time. Hence, Capek speaks of “the
accidental and superficial character of time in the [Newtonian] mechanistic
scheme.”10

     Classical mechanics considered time “an ‘accident of accidents' which
was, so to speak, twice removed from the solid, fundamental reality of
matter and space.”11 Capek further observes: “Thus the lapse of time is a
mere accident: matter and its laws are the same whether we consider them
within cosmic aeons or within excessively minute intervals of time.”12
Philosopher-mathematician Alfred North Whitehead remarks about the
‘accidental' character of time:

       This fact that the material is indifferent to the division of time
       leads to the conclusion that the lapse of time is an accident,
       rather than of the essence of the material. The material is fully
       itself in any sub-period however short. Thus the transition of
       time has nothing to do with the character of the material. The
       material is equally itself at any instant of time.13

The Newtonian mechanics in effect thus eliminates time from fabric of
physical reality - of the universe.14 Since time is not essential but
accidental in the Newtonian universe, the universe would be static, not
dynamic. Such a universe would have no antecedent states, finally ending
up with a state having infinite density and infinite curvature as in the Big
Bang model; and so the universe would have no beginning with Big Bang
singularity at time zero.

      However, this whole scenario undergoes a radical change with the
advent of Einstein's theory of relativity. What is significant here is that
according to relativity, time enters in an essential way into the constitution
of the universe. Arthur Peacocke aptly remarks: “Time in modern
relativistic physics is an integral and basic aspect of nature. Space and time
have to be mutually defined in interlocking relations and both are related to
definitions of mass and energy, themselves interconvertible. So matter,
energy, and space-time constitute the created order.”15 Capek speaks about
the ‘ontological priority of time over space' in the theory of relativity.16 In
order to emphasize this priority of time, he refers to the unification of
space and time in the special theory as ‘time-space' rather than ‘space-
time,' as is customarily used. Capek writes: “Thus it is more accurate to
speak of time-space than of space-time.”17 He expresses the same idea
alternatively when he says that “the fusion of space and time is more
accurately characterized as a temporalization or dynamization of space than
as a spatialization of time.”18

      In short, the great accomplishment of the theory of relativity is
precisely this temporalization or dynamization of space. Such
dynamization of space is more evident in the general theory than in the
special theory. The special theory unified space and time into space-time or
rather time-space. The general theory, while retaining the fusion of space
and time effected by the special theory, removed the classical distinction
between space-time and matter, and “intimately related [them] in a single
package deal.”19 Thus, the general theory unifies space-time with
mass/energy and with it the integration of time into the structure of
physical reality is complete. Space-time and mass/energy are fused into
one single dynamic whole. Space, time, matter and motion -- the four
absolutes of Newtonian mechanics - are inextricably linked rendering them
relative. This linkage results in space-time curvature in the presence of
mass/energy; and such space-time warpage is called gravity. As the density
of mass/energy increases, space-time curvature/gravity also increases
inexorably. If this law is applied to the universe as whole, the resulting
model would result in Big Bang singularity at time zero. Hence, in the last
analysis, singularity is the final outcome of the relativistic unification of
space-time/mass/energy. The whole structure of the universe is rendered
dynamic. The space-time/mass/ energy regime of the universe is dynamic
and evolutionary.

     The foregoing reflections on the conceptual foundations of Newtonian
physics and the theory of relativity provide the right setting to discuss the
mechanics of ‘taming' Big Bang singularity. Obviously, the Big Bang model
describes a dynamic universe which had a beginning and will have an end.
According to this theory, we saw, there is supposedly a singularity in the
beginning of the universe at time zero. This means that in order to ‘handle'
singularity, we have to ‘flush out' or eliminate time zero from the Big Bang
model. As we have seen,20 the quantum field theory that ‘manages' the
singularity regime and Planck magnitudes is the quantum theory of gravity.
Coveney and Highfield aptly suggest: “Nevertheless, it is widely believed
by cosmologists that a successful quantum version of gravity would
transcend the singularity problems arising in general relativity, . . .”21 The
point here is that quantum effects begin to affect space-time domain at the
extremely minute Planck scales on account of uncertainty principle. Under
such scenario, space-time structure is not continuous and smooth, as in
Newtonian mechanics and the theory of relativity, but needs quantum
mechanical treatment. In other words, gravitational field (space-time
curvature) has to be quantized, and so a quantum description of space-time
along with mass-energy at the singularity regime and Planck dimensions is
required. William Stoeger speaks about the possible modification of space-
time structure at this setting:

       If this is correct, then space and time are not continuous in this
       primordial phase, but give way to some other more
       fundamental realities — very different from the space and time
       with which we are familiar. These quantum space-time
       realities, whatever they turn out to be, then yield or give birth
       to our space-time continuum once the temperature of the
       universe falls below 1032 degrees Kelvin.22

Stoeger further explains about the two modalities of time:

       Instead, there may have been some material quantum reality
       integrated with some quantized space-time reality, whatever
       that would have been, in which time as we know it was hidden,
       and from which it only gradually emerged. Time would then
       have a beginning and be finite in the past, but in its primordial
       ‘spatial' form, it might also be unbounded as well, and would
       have an origin which was different from that of created reality
       itself.23

From this sort of ‘primordial' time, the ‘real' time that we experience,
gradually emerges. This is the sum and substance of the ‘no boundary'
model proposed by Hawking and Hartle.

      In fact, the no boundary model is Hawking's method of ‘taming' Big
Bang singularity by ‘flushing out' time. John Gribbin observes: “Physics
can't cope with infinities, or singularities, but he [Hawking] has done away
with both by removing the ‘moment of creation' with its implication of a
state of infinite density in the beginning of the Universe.”24 At one stroke,
Hawking has eliminated infinities and Big Bang singularity, and he has
accomplished this by doing away with the moment of creation of the
universe altogether. According to him, the moment of creation is the
boundary of time, that is, time zero. This boundary represents the Big Bang
singularity which, we saw, is the result of applying the general theory of
relativity uncritically past Planck magnitudes. Gribbin remarks: “Hawking
has tried to do away with the singularity, improving general relativity by
including aspects of quantum physics in the description of the universe.”25
Hawking explains his proposal briefly:

       When quantum mechanics is taken into account, there is the
       possibility that the singularity may be smeared out and that
       space and time together may form a closed four-dimensional
       surface without a boundary or edge, like the surface of the
       Earth but with two extra dimensions. This would mean that . . .
       there would not be any singularities at which the laws of
       physics would break down.26

      The key feature of Hawking's no boundary model consists in the
proposal that Heisenberg's uncertainty principle blurs the distinction
between space and time at the Planck domain. The quantum uncertainty
relation predicts that space and time become ill-defined at the Planck
dimensions. But more than that, quantum fuzziness entails smearing the
separate identities of space and time. Thus, if we imagine going back in
time to the Big Bang regime of the universe, when we reach the Planck
time, quantum uncertainties would remove the distinction between space
and time; and concepts, such as ‘before' and ‘after,' lose their meaning. In
other words, the effect of uncertainty principle would modify the basic
distinction between space and time. To use Hawking's terminology, time is
‘smeared' into space and becomes ‘imaginary.' We might say that time
becomes ‘spatialized.' Davies explains this scenario:

       In daily life, space is space, and time is time - there is no
       ambiguity - even though space and time are nearly the same
       thing, being part and parcel of a unified spacetime. But in the
       quantum realm their distinct identities become blurred.
       Sometimes intervals of time behave like intervals of space, and
       vice versa: space becomes time-like and time becomes
       spacelike. The resulting identity crises -- quantum fluctuations
       in spaceness and timeness -- are exceedingly small: in fact,
       they are more or less restricted to Planck lengths and times.
       But in the context of the origin of the universe they could be
       immensely significant.27

     According to Hawking's theory, at the Planck scales there is no longer
time dimension, but only four space-like dimensions. The radical character
of this approach is that it regards time as truly spacelike in the ultimate
quantum gravitational domain of the Big Bang. As one approaches the
beginning of the universe, the conventional time disappears; and time
becomes indistinguishable from space as the effects of boundary conditions
are felt. Calculations suggest that this might be the state of affairs at the
beginning of the universe -- during the first minute fraction of a second.
The question then arises as to the geometry of the four-dimensional space
which has to somehow smoothly join onto the more familiar space-time,
once the quantum smearing effects subside. And we may also ask which is
the ‘real' time? Hawking argues:

       The singularity theorems of classical general relativity showed
       that the universe must have a beginning, and that this
       beginning must be described in terms of quantum theory. This
       in turn led to the idea that the universe could be finite in
       imaginary time, but without boundaries or singularities. When
       one goes back to the real time in which we live, however, there
       will still appear to be singularities. . . .
       This might suggest that the so-called imaginary time is really
       the fundamental time, and that what we call real time is
       something we create just in our minds. In real time, the
       universe has a beginning and an end at singularities that form a
       boundary to space-time and at which the laws of science break
       down. But in imaginary time, there are no singularities or
       boundaries. So maybe what we call imaginary time is really
       more basic, and what we call real time is just an idea that we
       invent to help us describe what we think the universe is like.28

Hawking thus ‘tames' Big Bang singularity by ‘flushing out' real time and
by the same token introducing imaginary time. He summarizes his position
as follows: “Only if we could picture the universe in terms of imaginary
time would there be no singularities.”29

    With help of two figures, Davies explains Hawking's method of
taming singularity by flushing out time.
  Figure 23. Space-time singularity (Courtesy: Paul Davies,
                     Goldilocks Enigma)

     Figure 23 is a schematic representation of the expanding universe.
Time is represented vertically and space horizontally. We observe that as
the universe is expanding, the radius of circle is becoming bigger at later
times. Conversely, in the past it is smaller, until at the beginning of time,
denoted by t = 0, it shrinks to nothing; this is the purported Big Bang
singularity at the beginning of the universe. This series of events is shown
in the figure schematically by the apex of an inverted cone. The region
below the cone, marked ‘nothing', which seems to lie ‘before' or ‘beyond'
the Big Bang, does not exist as a physical region in this model. Both time
and space start at the singularity.30




  Figure 24. The Quantum origin of the universe (Courtesy:
              Paul Davies, Goldilocks Enigma)

      Figure 24 displays Hawking's suggestion to eliminate singularity by
flushing out time. The effects of quantum uncertainty transform the
structure of cone near apex so that the infinitely sharp point, representing
space-time singularity, gets replaced by a rounded bowl. The radius of this
bowl is supposedly about one Planck length, extraordinarily tiny by human
standards, but not actually zero. The singularity has therefore been
eliminated in this illustration. Figure 24 shows that as we advance towards
(supposed) time zero, just before we reach the singular terminating event
(about one Planck time before it), time itself starts getting fuzzy, and
begins to adopt more and more space-like character. Thus, at the ‘base of
the bowl,' close to the beginning of the universe, time becomes space-like
as a result of quantum mechanical effects. Such is the concept of Hawking's
imaginary time. Imaginary time, we saw, behaves as if it were a dimension
of space in such a way that we can move forwards and backwards along it,
just as we can move right and left, or up and down in space. Time does not
change abruptly into space, but fades away in a continuous manner. Thus,
when visualized in imaginary time, the singularity and time zero are
eliminated.31
      Incidentally, it is significant that what produced Big Bang singularity,
and also what eliminated it, are the dynamics between space and time. The
theory of relativity in the last analysis, we said quoting Capek, involves a
temporalization of space rather than a spatialization of time. As Capek
suggests, relativity accords ontological priority to time over space. It is
such temporalization of space and ontological priority given to time that
produced singularity in the Big Bang model. However, these concepts are
in direct conflict with the principles of the no boundary model of Hawking
and Hartle. The fundamental principle in this model is the ontological
priority of space over time; but the space in question is not Newtonian, but
a four-dimensional manifold. Hence, it appears that the no boundary model
supposes spatialization of time rather than temporalization of space as in
the theory of relativity. By the same token, it has eliminated Big Bang
singularity and time zero which were the products of the general theory of
relativity. Obviously, Hawking's concept of space is very different from
Newtonian space; in a word, the former is four-dimensional while the latter
is three-dimensional, each with diverse, attendant properties.

     We conclude with Davies: “But the quantum effects of gravity should
dramatically alter all the stuff about singularities and the ultimate origin of
the universe. It was the realization of this that led to the creation of a new
field of study: quantum cosmology.”32

6.1.2. The Vacuum Creation of the Universe

     With our presentations of quantum theory of gravity and quantum
vacuum in the fourth chapter, and the foregoing narrative on taming Big
Bang singularity, we have hopefully prepared the ground for our discourse
on quantum cosmology -- the quantum origins of the universe -- often
dubbed ‘vacuum creation.' Andrei Linde has proposed a theory of quantum
cosmology called ‘chaotic inflation' or ‘eternal inflation.' Edward Tryon
and Alex Vilenkin also have developed similar models of chaotic inflation;
it is the application of quantum theory to the entire Cosmos. These
quantum cosmologists believe that our present universe came into
existence as a result of a colossal quantum vacuum fluctuation. Pagels
observes: “Maybe the universe itself sprang into existence out of
nothingness -- a gigantic vacuum fluctuation which we know today as the
big bang.”33 Physicists have employed, as we saw, a number of terms in
order to refer to the quantum vacuum: ‘superspace' (Davies), ‘super-
spacetime' (Gribbin), ‘the original mother Universe' (Katsuhiko Sato), and
‘Mother Spacetime' (Willem B. Drees).34 “Superspacetime, not a
singularity or an antecedent cosmic epoch, is the ultimate Antecedent
Universe in these theories [of quantum cosmology]; it produces all Big
Bangs, including our own. Maternal Superspacetime is the space beyond
our space, and the time before, during, and after our time.”35

     Now, what is the mechanism by which superspacetime generates
universes like our own? That mechanism is called ‘eternal inflation.'
Davies writes: “Eternal inflation therefore offers an inexhaustible universe-
generating mechanism, of which our universe — our bubble — is but one
product.”36 Other appellations for this mechanism are ‘vacuum fluctuation'
or ‘quantum fluctuation.' It is, as we saw, the quantum process by which
particles and anti-particles are created and annihilated. It is indeed a dance
between being and non-being. Pagels says: “The vacuum randomly
fluctuates between being and nothingness.”37 In quantum fluctuation,
virtual particles and anti-particles are constantly created, and they
annihilate each other by converting themselves into the energy of the
vacuum. The uncertainty principle permits to ‘borrow' energy required to
produce these particles and antiparticles from the vacuum itself; and the
‘debt' is quickly ‘repaid' when particles and anti-particles annihilate each
other. These are short-lived because the energy tied up in them has to be
repaid after an extremely minute fraction of a second - within the time
permitted by uncertainty relation. Such processes of creations and
annihilations have been confirmed by many experiments. Physicists have
observed similar quantum fluctuations in laboratories down to distances of
about 10-16 cm and over times of about 10-26 sec.

     However, at the Planck scales, quantum fluctuations would begin to
affect space-time itself. Space-time comes into being spontaneously and
uncaused through quantum fluctuations. Each such region of space-time
would be only about 10-33 cm across, and it generally survives for a mere
10-43 sec. (Planck scales) Like virtual particles, the quantum bubbles of
virtual space-time disappear almost as soon as they come into being.
Quantum vacuum would produce an infinite number of ‘bubbles' of
‘virtual' space-time which come into existence and disappear as permitted
by uncertainty principle. Gal-Or tells us: “Super-space at these scales may
be compared to a ‘carpet of foam' in which the millions of tiny ‘bubbles'
having different shapes are continually ‘bursting' as new ones are being
formed.”38 These tiny bubbles are called ‘child' or ‘baby' universes. The
entire cosmos may consist of an infinite and eternal set of such baby/child
universes connected to each other in a very complicated way. (See Figure
25) Davies suggests: “The ‘mother' universe which spawned ours is also
continuously inflating at a fantastic rate, and spewing out baby universes
for all it is worth. If this state of affairs is correct, it implies that ‘our'
universe is only part of an infinite assemblage of universes, although it is
self-contained now.”39 Similarly, Drees observes that according to this view,
“a mini-universe appears as a bubble on the ‘surface' of the mother and,
after evaporation of the connection, it becomes spatially disconnected.”40




       Figure 25. ‘Mother' universe and ‘child' universes
     Thus, an infinite number of universes are created by, and co-exist
within, infinite superspacetime which includes, but transcends the finite
space-time of our own universe. Such co-existing child universes ‘live and
move and have their being' within Mother Space-time without influencing
one another. The child universes are so far apart in Mother Space-time that
they cannot act causally on one another, even at the speed of light, except
possibly through worm holes.41 In infinite Superspacetime, an infinite
number of co-existent, child universes could all be infinitely far apart. Our
universe is one such bubble-child. After its spontaneous generation from
Mother Space-time, a child universe may expand or contract forever. Some
cosmologists identify creation of a child universe like ours with the Big
Bang. Davies observes: “In the theory of eternal inflation, the big bang is
no longer the ultimate origin of all physical things. Rather, it is the
beginning of the history of our ‘bubble', or pocket universe, embedded in
an eternally inflating superspace.”42 Similarly, Drees speaks of the origin of
our universe in biological terms of conception and birth: “The Singularity
might be something like birth or conception, being, as it were, the
beginning of time from the perspective of the baby or an event in time as
seen by the mother. As ‘conception' the Universe is like a bubble within an
embedding space-time.”43

     John Gribbin thinks that chaotic inflation is the ultimate theory about
the universe:

       The idea of chaotic inflation led to what is (so far) the ultimate
       development of the inflationary scenario. The great
       unanswered question in standard Big Bang cosmology is what
       came ‘before' the singularity. It is often said that the question
       is meaningless, since time itself began at the singularity. But
       chaotic inflation suggests that our Universe grew out of a
       quantum fluctuation in some pre-existing region of spacetime,
       and that exactly equivalent processes can create regions of
       inflation within our own Universe. In effect, new universes bud
       off from our Universe, and our Universe may itself have
       budded off from another universe, in a process which had no
       beginning and will have no end.44
     Davies sums up the products of eternal inflation: “What we have all
along been calling ‘the universe' is, in this theory [of chaotic inflation], just
an infinitesimal part of a single ‘bubble', or pocket universe, set amid an
infinite assemblage of universes — a multiverse — itself embedded in
inflating space that exists without end.”45

6.1.3. The Inflationary Universe

     Indeed, an enormously massive fluctuation of the quantum vacuum
could occur in principle creating a bubble/child universe. But it would be
exceedingly small, and would have an enormous gravitational field. The
result would be an extremely rapid collapse, snuffing the embryonic
universe out of existence as quickly as any pair of virtual particles
produced in quantum vacuum. Like virtual particles, the quantum bubbles
of virtual space-time disappear almost as soon as they come into existence.
Most of the bubble universes might turn into black holes on account of
their extreme gravity. However, many quantum cosmologists believe that
occasionally a minute blob of space-time, a virtual bubble universe of
unimaginably small size, might have avoided a rapid gravitational collapse
and become promoted to the real, astronomically large universe like ours.46
In order to achieve this extraordinary feat, the child universe must
somehow avoid the crushing barrier of gravity which tries to squeeze the
universe back to nothing. Somehow the nascent universe has to boost its
size from almost nothing to literally cosmic proportions of the present
universe. Something must trigger this process very rapidly during the split
second for which a quantum fluctuation is normally allowed to exist. What
is needed is a titanic, repulsive force which can overwhelm the attractive
grip of gravity and set the universe on a path of expansion. That is, there
should be a suitable mechanism in order to promote the virtual child
universe into the real universe similar to ours. That mechanism is called
‘inflation.' A tiny bubble of space-time burst spontaneously into existence
as a result of quantum fluctuation, whereupon inflation seizes it, and the
bubble swells to macroscopic dimensions. The nascent universe
exponentially inflates within an extremely small period of time. Gribbin
says: “In the tiny fraction of a second it exists, inflation can set to work and
blow the seed up into a full-size universe.”47
      According to the theory of inflation, the embryonic universe found
itself in an ‘excited' or ‘false' vacuum state. The vacuum could become
‘excited' and adopt a number of very different states of energy in the same
way as an atom can be excited to higher energy levels. The energy levels
leaps by huge amounts from one vacuum state to another. The excited state
of quantum vacuum is a state of high energy which physicists call ‘false
vacuum.' The false vacuum differs from ‘true vacuum' in so far as the false
vacuum possesses extremely high energy density of about 1074 gm/cm3.
(See Figure 26)




            Figure 26. False vacuum and true vacuum
      In this state, the excited vacuum would behave like a gigantic cosmic
repulsion force of such an enormous magnitude that it would cause
headlong expansion of the universe at a colossal rate. A typical region of
space would double in size in every 10-35 sec, that is, every hundred trillion-
trillion-trillionths of a second. The radius of the tiny universe would have
increased by a million million million million million times in only an
extremely minute fraction of a second; it would increase in size from 10-26
cm to about 10 cm. The minuscule universe would have expanded by a
factor of at least 1050 in volume. It was as if a coin, one centimeter in
diameter, suddenly blew up to the size of the Milky Way galaxy. The
universe would have gone on doubling every 10-35 sec so long as it
remained in the grip of the huge cosmic repulsive force. The inflationary
epoch lasted from 10-34 sec after the Big Bang to 10-32 sec.48 This pattern of
expansion is called ‘exponential,' and it results in an extremely rapid
increase in the magnitude of the universe. (See Figure 27)




                 Figure 27. Inflation of the universe
This stage of accelerated expansion is known as the ‘inflationary universe.'
Without the inflationary stage of expansion, the universe would have
expanded only tenfold during the interval between 10-34 sec to 10-32 sec after
the Big Bang, while the inflation would blow it out by a factor of 1050 in
volume. In contrast, the normal expansion of the universe from the size of
ten centimeters (that is, the size after inflation) to the present magnitude
during the fourteen billion years of its existence is just an expansion factor
of 1027, and appears quite modest.49

     The inflationary phase of frenetic and accelerated expansion of the
universe lasted only for an extremely brief duration -- from 10-34 sec after
the Big Bang to 10-32 sec. Like all excited states, inflationary state of the
universe is unstable. It is a general feature of any physical system that it
seeks out the state of lowest energy or ground state. This is true of excited
or false vacuum as well. Just as in the case of all excited quantum systems,
the excited false vacuum is unstable and would tend to ‘decay,' and attain
true vacuum of low energy level or ground state. The excited vacuum state
of the universe, being inherently unstable, decayed abruptly into true
vacuum, the lowest vacuum energy state or ground state which is the empty
space of the universe at present. The enormous reserves of energy locked
up in the false vacuum were, as a result, released in the form of heat and
particles of matter. This changeover from false vacuum to true vacuum is
called ‘phase transition.' The Russian cosmologist Igor Novikov says:

       The instability means that a phase transition becomes possible
       from the state of ‘false vacuum' with its huge density to a state
       in which the entire mass density (and the corresponding energy
       density) of the ‘false vacuum' transforms into the mass density
       of ordinary hot matter while the density of the true vacuum is
       zero or vanishingly low.50

The energy that was contained in false vacuum gives rise to an enormous
number of particles and anti-particles of ordinary matter, all having very
high energies. Once the inflation of the universe came to a halt, the cosmic
repulsive force also disappeared, and gravity assumed its usual attractive
role, acting as a brake on expansion. The universe continues to expand from
the initial impetus imparted by inflation, but at a steadily falling rate as at
present; and the expansion of the universe follows laws of evolution of the
standard model of the Big Bang theory.51

     After all these narrations of child/baby universes, our present universe
and mother universe (superspacetime), one may ask, which is the ‘real'
universe, or as it is usually asked, “will the real universe please stand up?”
In order to answer this question, some clarifications are called for. We start
with a working definition of the universe as ‘the totality or entirety of
physical reality.' When theories of chaotic inflation speak about mother
universe with the assemblage of child universes, they appear to refer to
such totality of physical reality. These physical entities are believed to be
beyond the boundaries of our present universe; they are regions of space
and time that are unobservable in principle. Apparently, the entirety of
physical reality is the mother universe together with child universes.
Gribbin reserves the term ‘Cosmos' to refer to this totality. On the other
hand, the word ‘universe' is used to signify our present physical world
which would be an insignificant region of this totality. Gribbin clarifies this
distinction:

       If we use ‘Universe' as the name for our own expanding bubble
       of space-time, everything that is in principle visible to our
       telescopes, then may be the term ‘Cosmos' can be used to refer
       to the entirety of space and time, within which (if the
       inflationary scenario is correct) there may be an indefinitely
       large number of other expanding bubbles of space-time, other
       Universes with which we can never communicate.52

Similarly, Davies refers to this entirety of physical reality when he writes:
“This [the entire cosmos] includes all of (possibly infinite) space, within
and beyond our horizon, plus all of its contents, . . . ”53

     In contrast to the Cosmos, our present universe represents “our own
expanding bubble of space-time, everything that is in principle visible to
our telescopes.” It refers to “the entire span of space and time accessible to
our instruments, now and in the future.”54 However, our universe is not
simply there, whole and entire, for us to observe. It had been expanding
ever since its origin in the Big Bang; it expands now, and will continue to
expand in the future. Since the time of the Big Bang 13.82 billion years ago
(14 billion in round terms), light can have travelled 14 billion light years,
and therefore we cannot see beyond this distance or boundary even in
principle, however powerful our instruments of observation are. This
boundary is called ‘visual horizon.' “The visual horizon is the limit of what
we can see or know directly via electromagnetic radiation.”55 Since we
cannot observe beyond this horizon, “the observable universe means
‘everything within this horizon'.”56 At the present epoch, this visual horizon
is about 14 billion light years in extent. That is to say, this region
constitutes the visible, observable universe for us.

6.2. The Quantum Destruction of the Universe

     Just as there is a theory of quantum creation of the universe, there is
also a hypothesis about its quantum destruction. Like quantum creation, the
quantum destruction is also the effect of the unpredictable behaviour of the
quantum vacuum. Referring to this cosmic catastrophe, Davies asks: “But
what about events of universe-wrecking proportions? Is it possible that a
convulsion can occur that would destroy the entire cosmos at a stroke — in
midlife, so to speak?”57 It is indeed frightening to think that it is an ever-
present possibility.

6.2.1. Energy Levels and Quantum Tunnelling

     The possibility of the quantum destruction of the universe through the
mechanism of a sudden decay of the quantum vacuum was proposed by two
reputed physicists Sidney Coleman and Frank De Luccia in 1980. It has to
do with the unpredictable behaviour of quantum vacuum, namely, vacuum
decay. Vacuum decay is based on certain fundamental assumptions of
physics, specifically, different energy levels, ground state of energy and
quantum ‘tunnelling.' First, one of the important concepts in physics is that
every physical system has an energy level which may be high or low.
Evidently, the higher the level is, the more the energy. For example, coal or
wood has a high level of energy; it can be burned, and the chemical energy
stored in it released as heat. The wood as ashes has now a lower energy
level than before its burning. Second, everything in the universe tries to
reach the ground state where it has as little energy as possible, and where it
is completely stable. A ball on a hill, for instance, is unstable, and has a lot
of potential energy. When disturbed, it will roll down the valley and lose its
potential energy in the process. The ball is now in its ground state and is
stable. Thus, if something has a lot of energy, it is unstable and would get
rid of it; it would reach its ground state and become stable. Everything in
our universe follows these two principles of energy levels and stability.

     Now, a quantum particle like an electron too has the lowest possible
energy level in the ground state and so is stable; in this state it is as close to
the nucleus of atom as possible. It leaves its ground state and jumps to a
higher orbit only if it becomes excited, that is, if it receives extra energy by
absorbing a photon. In the excited state, an electron has a higher energy
level than in ground state, and so it is unstable. In other words, if an
electron absorbs some energy from some other source (light for instance),
it will jump up to a state that is higher than its ground state. Since the
electron in this state is unstable, it would want to get back to its ground
state. But before it can return to this state of low energy, it must get rid off
its excess energy. The electron sheds this extra energy by emitting the
photon it absorbed, and then returns to the ground state and becomes stable.
(See Figure 28) That is, when the excitation ceases, the electron returns to
the lower, ground state, releasing energy in the form of




      Figure 28. Electron absorbing and emitting photon
     In quantum field theories, we saw, the quantum vacuum is the state
with lowest possible energy. Such a state is the true vacuum which is at
global minimum of energy -- lowest possible energy at the global level --
and so is stable; and it is commonly assumed that it coincides with the
physical vacuum state of our universe. However, it is possible that the
vacuum state of our universe is a configuration of quantum fields
representing a local minimum of energy, but does not coincide with global
minimum of energy. In this case, there exists a false vacuum at the local
minimum of energy (of our universe), which is not stable in contrast to the
true vacuum which is at the global minimum and is stable. This type of
false vacuum state is in fact an excited vacuum. In quantum field theories,
a false vacuum is a hypothetical vacuum that is not actively decaying; it is
not entirely stable, but ‘meta-stable.' It may last for a very long period of
time in that state (a property known as meta-stability), and might
eventually move to a more stable state -- a process known as ‘vacuum
decay.' Thus, higher energy states tend to decay into lower energy states,
and finally into ground state. An excited false vacuum with high energy
will try to decay to the lowest state of energy or true vacuum with lower
energy. The inflationary theory is based on the assumption that the very
early universe was in an excited or false vacuum state; during this period it
inflated frenetically, but in a very short time this state decayed to the
supposedly true vacuum and inflation ceased.58

      The third concept involved in vacuum decay is ‘quantum tunnelling.'
Quantum tunnelling is the phenomenon in which an object, usually an
elementary particle, tunnels through a potential barrier even though it does
not have sufficient energy to surmount the barrier. The central
characteristic of quantum tunnelling is that under certain conditions,
quantum particles are able to negotiate the obstacle of a potential barrier
without having sufficient energy to overcome it. But according to classical
mechanics, physical objects which do not have enough energy to get over a
barrier will not be able to do so. Particles attempting to cross over potential
barriers can be compared to a ball trying to reach the other side of a small
hill from the valley. A ball without sufficient energy to climb over the hill
would not be able to do so and go down to the other side. (See Figure 29)

      However according to quantum mechanics, quantum particles can,
with a very small probability, tunnel through the barrier to the other side,
even though they do not have sufficient energy to do so. This is called
‘quantum tunnelling.' Suppose a quantum particle, such as an electron, is
trapped before an insurmountable barrier of force; as in the foregoing
example, we can say that it sits in the valley of a hill. (This need not have
to be a real hill; it could be electric or nuclear force field.) In the absence
of the energy needed to surmount the hill or overcome the force barrier, the
electron appears to be trapped forever. But Heisenberg's uncertainty
principle, we know, permits energy to be borrowed from the vacuum for an
extremely short time provided it is paid back within that time. Hence, if the
electron can borrow enough energy to reach the top of the hill and get
across to the other side before having to pay the energy back (that is, within
the time allowed by uncertainty principle), it can escape from the valley. In
effect, it will have ‘tunnelled' through the barrier. Thus, if a quantum
particle is trapped in the valley of a hill, there is a small probability that it
can escape by borrowing energy and cross over to the other side of the hill;
it ‘tunnels' through the barrier. (See Figure 29) 59
               Figure 29. The Quantum tunnelling 60
     The probability of a quantum particle tunnelling out of the valley
depends very sensitively on both the ‘height' and ‘width' of the barrier. The
higher the barrier, the more energy the particle must borrow to reach the
top, and so according to the uncertainty principle, the shorter the duration
for the energy to be ‘paid back.' Hence, high barriers can be tunnelled
through, only if they are also thin, enabling the particle to traverse them
quickly enough to repay the energy loan on time. For this reason, the tunnel
effect is not noticed in daily life; macroscopic barriers are far too high and
wide for significant tunnelling to occur. On an atomic scale, however,
tunnelling is very common.

6.2.2. The Vacuum Decay

     Physicists Coleman and De Luccia have proposed a theory about the
total destruction of the universe through vacuum decay. Quantum
tunnelling purportedly functions as the mechanism for this ultimate
catastrophe. In figure 30, the present (supposedly false) vacuum state of the
universe corresponds to the base of valley A. The true vacuum, however,
corresponds to the base of valley B, which is lower than A. There would be
a small probability of state A decaying by tunnel effect into the truly stable
ground state B. That is, the higher energy state A of false vacuum would
tend to decay into the lower energy state B of true vacuum, but it is
deterred from doing so by the ‘hill' or force field that separates them.
Although the hill impedes decay, it does not entirely prevent it, on account
of the possible quantum tunnel effect; the system can tunnel through, from
valley A to valley B. If the present universe is the false vacuum state A,
and if it makes a transition to true vacuum state B, it would end up in
vacuum decay. If this theory is correct, then our universe is living on
borrowed time, hung up in valley A, but with an ever-present possibility
that it will tunnel into valley B at some arbitrary moment.61




  Figure 30. Quantum tunnelling from false to true vacuum
      The usual assumption is that the present state of the universe
corresponds to true vacuum with lowest state of energy. Coleman and De
Lucia consider the chilling possibility that the present vacuum state may
not be the true vacuum, but merely a long-lived, meta-stable, false vacuum
that has lulled us into a false sense of security because it has endured for a
few billion years. We know of many quantum systems, such as uranium
nuclei that have half-lives of billions of years. Suppose the present state of
the universe is in fact a false vacuum, it would decay. The ‘decay' here
refers to the catastrophic possibility that the present vacuum may suddenly
fail, and ground the universe into an even lower energy state.

     Coleman and De Luccia were able to model the decay of the present
vacuum state of the universe mathematically tracing the manner in which
the decay might occur. They found that the decay might start at a random
location in space in the form of a tiny bubble of true vacuum — ground
state of energy -- surrounded by unstable false vacuum which is the present
state of the universe. As soon as the bubble of true vacuum is formed, it
will expand at a rate that rapidly approaches the speed of light, engulfing
larger and larger regions of false vacuum and instantaneously converting it
into true vacuum. The energy difference between the two states is
concentrated in the ‘bubble wall' which sweeps across the universe spelling
destruction to everything on its path. The first moment we would know
about the existence of a true vacuum bubble would be when the wall has
arrived, and the quantum structure of our world suddenly changed. We
would not have any warning. Instantaneously, the nature of all subatomic
particles and their interactions would alter drastically; for example, protons
might immediately decay, in which case all matter would abruptly
evaporate. What is left would then find itself inside the bubble of true
vacuum; it would be a state of affairs very different from what we observe
at present. The most significant difference concerns gravitation. Coleman
and De Luccia found that the energy and pressure of the true vacuum would
create a gravitational field so intense that the region embraced by the
bubble would collapse in less than microseconds, even as the bubble wall
expands.62

      Vacuum decay would be the abrupt annihilation of everything, as the
bubble interior implodes into a space-time singularity. In short, it is an
instant Crunch. Coleman and De Luccia observe: “The possibility that we
are living in a false vacuum has never been a cheering one to contemplate.
Vacuum decay is the ultimate ecological catastrophe; in a new vacuum
there are new constants of nature; after vacuum decay, not only is life as we
know it impossible, so is chemistry as we know it.”63 Cosmologist Michael
Turner and physicist Frank Wilczek also have arrived at such a grim
conclusion: “From the point of view of microphysics, then, it is quite
conceivable that our vacuum is metastable . . . without warning a bubble of
true vacuum could nucleate somewhere in the Universe and move outwards
at the speed of light.”64

     Astrophysicist Piet Hut and cosmologist Martin Rees raise the
alarming specter that the formation of a catastrophic vacuum bubble might
be inadvertently triggered by particle physicists themselves in high energy
particle accelerators like the Large Hadron Collider (LHC). They speculate
that very high energy collision of subatomic particles might create
conditions, just for an instant in a very small region of space which would
stimulate vacuum to decay. Once such an event has occurred even on a
microscopic scale, the newly formed bubble of true vacuum would balloon
to astronomical proportions. However, as Hut and Rees point out, cosmic
rays which achieve higher energies than the particle accelerators have been
hitting nuclei in the Earth's atmosphere for billions of years without
triggering vacuum decay. Moreover, these speculations are based on the
validity of the quantum theories of gravity and quantum cosmology which
are yet to be established.65

    We conclude the final chapter of the present volume The Strange
World of Quantum Physics with a quote from Paul Davies:

       For thousands of years, human beings have contemplated the
       world about them and asked the great questions of existence:
       Why are we here? How did the universe begin? How will it
       end? How is world put together? Why is the way it is? For all
       of recorded human history, people have sought answers to such
       ‘ultimate' questions in religion and philosophy, or declared
       them to be completely beyond human comprehension. Today,
       however, many of these big questions are part of science, and
       scientists claim that they may be on the verge of providing
       answers.66

Indeed, quantum physics does make such claims.
                             EPILOGUE
       Because the theory of relativity did upset familiar notions, it
       was at first regarded as revolutionary. Nevertheless, this
       revolution affected only the outer boundary of reason, so to
       speak – its most superficial layer, the forms of sensible
       intuition [space and time]. . . . With quantum mechanics, it
       was not only our usual notions of space and time that were
       being questioned but also mental categories – those of cause
       and of substance; and even purely logical principles, such as
       those of identity and contradiction, appeared less secure.

         –R. Blanché, Contemporary Science and Rationalism

     The leifmotif of the present work The Strange World of Quantum
Physics is expressed by the word ‘strange' in the title. The book narrates
the saga of a very strange world - the microcosm of quantum objects and
fields. When scientists speak about this world and its various dimensions,
they invariably use words, like ‘weird,' ‘bizarre,' and ‘exotic.' It is the
counter-intuitive world of subatomic particles and interactions, so very
remote from the world of our everyday experience. Not only ordinary
mortals like us find quantum theory strange and weird, but also the great
quantum physicists themselves deem it bizarre. One of the works of
Richard Feynman has the title, QED: The Strange Theory of Light and
Matter. He wrote in this book: “[Quantum mechanics] describes nature as
absurd from the point of view of common sense. And it fully agrees with
experiment. So I hope you can accept nature as She is — absurd.”1 Niels
Bohr says: “Anyone who is not shocked by quantum theory has not
understood it.”2

     Bohr told Wolfgang Pauli after his presentation of Heisenberg's and
Pauli's non-linear field theory of elementary particles at Columbia
University in 1958: “We are all agreed that your theory is crazy. The
question that divides us is whether it is crazy enough to have a chance of
being correct.”3 Heisenberg has said: “I remember the discussions with
Bohr . . . and when at the end of the discussion I went alone for a walk in
the neighbouring park I repeated to myself again and again the question:
Can nature possibly be as absurd as it seemed to us in these atomic
experiments?”4 There are other eminent physicists who testify about
quantum weirdness. John Wheeler: “If you are not completely confused by
quantum mechanics, you do not understand it.” Einstein: “If it [quantum
theory] is correct, it signifies the end of physics as a science.” Roger
Penrose: “Quantum mechanics makes absolutely no sense.”5 Again another
quote from Richard Feynman: “On the other hand I think I can safely say
that nobody understands quantum mechanics.”6 Gary Zukav wryly asserts:
“Quantum physics is stranger than science fiction.”7

     Why do we ordinary people and great physicists alike say that the
world of quantum mechanics is strange? Obviously, what is strange is
extra-ordinary - in the literal sense of the term; and what is ordinary -
normal - is our world of common sense experience. As such, we may call
quantum physics ‘ab-normal'- literally. It represents a world which is out
