of the ordinary or normal. Hans Reichenbach calls such ordinary, normal
world ‘the zone of the middle dimension.' It is the macrocosm situated
between the megacosm - the world of the very large (galaxies and stars) -
and microcosm - the world of the very small (subatomic particles and
quantum fields).8 Newton's mechanics represents the physics of this
ordinary world or macrocosm. In contrast to this macroworld of common
sense experience, the physical objects and events of the megaworld and of
the microworld appear to us extra-ordinary and strange. The objects of
megacosm, like Big Bang singularity, black holes, dark matter, dark
energy, quasars and neutron stars, are indeed strange entities of wonder
and fascination for us. The theory of relativity deals with these objects of
the megaworld.

     However, as we plunge into the microcosm, still more bizarre objects,
such as photons, electrons, protons, neutrons, positrons, quarks, and
various quantum fields await us. We are puzzled by the concepts of
quantum physics, such as wave-particles, uncertainty relation, wave
functions, non-locality, quantum vacuum, creation and annihilation of
particles and anti-particles, quantum tunnelling and smearing of space and
time; these are all ideas beyond our wildest imagination. “If the concepts
and predictions of relativity are often considered difficult and counter-
intuitive, many of the basic tenets and implications of quantum mechanics
may appear absolutely bizarre and inconceivable, but they have been
repeatedly proven to be true, and it is now one of the most rigorously
tested physical models of all time.”9 Similarly, Greene suggests that
“quantum mechanics absolutely and unequivocally shows us that a number
of basic concepts essential to our understanding of the familiar everyday
world fail to have any meaning when our focus narrows to the microscopic
realm.”10 John Polkinghorne suggests that the weird characteristics of
quantum objects are founded on superposition principle:

       All these strange aspects of the quantum world (uncertainty,
       wave/particle duality, quantum logic) arise from what Dirac
       correctly identified, as the basic feature which distinguishes
       quantum from Newtonian physics, the superposition principle.
       This states that in the quantum world we can mix together
       possibilities which in the Newtonian world are for ever
       separate and distinct.11

      Counter-intuitive concepts are employed also in the theory of
relativity. R. Blanché tells us: “Nevertheless, this revolution [of the theory
of relativity] affected only the outer boundary of reason, so to speak -- its
most superficial layer, the forms of sensible intuition [space and time].”12
The unification of space and time in the special theory leads on to strange
physical phenomena, like contraction of length, dilation of time, and
mass/energy conversion. The integration of space-time with mass/energy
in the general theory takes us to bizzare astronomical phenomena, such as
black holes and Big Bang singularity. However, the radical transformation
of our everyday concepts effected by by quantum physics goes much
deeper. It affects the fundamental categories of our understanding, namely,
those of substance and causality. Blanché says further: “With quantum
mechanics, it was not only our usual notions of space and time that were
being questioned but also mental categories -- those of cause and of
substance; and even purely logical principles, such as those of identity and
contradiction, appeared less secure.”13 Fritjoff Capra remarks about the
demolition of the notions of substance and causality: “Quantum theory has
demolished the classical concepts of solid objects [substance] and of
strictly deterministic laws [causality] of nature.”14 Capek speaks about the
break down of the concept of substance with the advent of quantum
physics:

       The extent of the contemporary crisis in physics cannot be
       fully grasped if we do not realize that the very concept of
       substance or thinghood has become questionable. The term
       ‘object' misleadingly suggests the permanence of a certain
       thing - more specifically, a constant conjunction of observable
       properties. But we already know that the alleged
       microphysical ‘objects' are not permanent; furthermore, not
       even within a single instant can they be characterized as
       conjunctions of position and velocity.15

     Truly, quantum physics has transformed beyond recognition our basic
categories of understanding the world, namely, substance and causality.
This is borne out by concepts, such as wave-particle duality, superposition
principle and uncertainty relation. Polkinghorne observes about
superposition principle: “The superposition principle illustrates one of the
general effects of the discovery of quantum mechanics - that it has brought
about an extension of the limits of what is conceivable.”16 Further,
quantum theory apparently wrecked the principle of causality - that every
effect has a cause. That was the consequence of Heisenberg's uncertainty
principle. As we have seen, according to this principle, in any
measurement we can know simultaneously with certainty only half of the
conjugate quantities, like position and velocity, energy and time. Such
decrepit knowledge of measurements leaves causality in a shambles.

     Moreover, quantum physics has shaken the very foundations of the
citadel of reason - logic itself. Without logic, no meaningful
communication is possible, and without it every written and spoken
language turns out to be a meaningless chatter. “Quantum theory extends
the limits of the conceivable to a quite remarkable degree, even including
the limits of logic itself.”17 Quantum mechanics has seriously challenged
the fundamental logical principles, such as distributive law and
commutative law. Polkinghorne suggests: “Even logic finds a modification
in the quantum world. The distributive law of classical, Boolean logic does
not hold for subatomic particles, and a new quantum logic is required to
mirror their idiosyncratic character.”18 The distributive law says that if the
statement “A is at X or Y,” is true, then it is true that either “A is at X” or
“A is at Y.” That is not so if A is an electron. In that case, A may be in a
state which is a superposition of the state (A at X) and the state (A at Y).
That is a quite different state from either of those two possibilities
separately, as stated by distributive law. Quantum theory superposes these
two classical incompatibles. Polkinghorne suggests: “Quantum theory
permits the mixing together of what was classically immiscible.”19

     Similarly, quantum physics violates the commutative law of Boolean
logic, namely, (p x q) = (q x p). But classical, Newtonian physics does
obey this law because its variables for position and momentum of a
particle, for instance, are simple numbers. Thus, the position (q) of a
particle from a fixed point may be 5 feet (q = 5); its momentum (p = mass
of particle times its velocity) may be designated by 3 (p = 3). These
simple numbers 5 and 3 obey commutative law of multiplication: that is,
(3 x 5) = (5 x 3) = 15; the order of these numbers does not matter. Like
most people, physicists think that position and momentum of a particle
have definite values specified by simple numbers. But in the matrix
mechanics of quantum physics, the position of a particle is described by a
matrix -- not a simple number. The main idea of matrix mechanics
proposed by Heisenberg, and developed by Max Born and Pascual Jordan,
is that physical variables, like position q and momentum p of a particle,
are not simple numbers but matrices. These matrices do not follow
commutative law. This means that (p x q) need not be equal to (q x p). In
fact, such modification of Boolean logic calls for a different mode of
understanding necessitated by quantum objects and events.

    In fact, we find similar break down of ordinary logic, and the
consequent illogical statements in mythology and also in Buddhist koans.
The renowned anthropologist Claude Lévi-Strauss testifies:

       Mythology confronts the student with a situation which at first
       sight could be looked upon as contradictory. On the one hand,
       it would seem that in the course of a myth anything is likely to
       happen. There is no logic, no continuity. Any characteristic
       can be attributed to any subject; every conceivable relation
       can be met. With myth, everything becomes possible.20

     Myths are stories which communicate intuitive truths that cannot be
conveyed through rational discourse. The world of mythical story is a
domain far removed from our present world of space and time. Otherwise,
it would not be a story. Oftentimes, stories begin as follows: “Once upon a
time, in a country . . . .” This style of starting a story shows that events in
the narrative do not take place in our ordinary space and time. Once a
story thus begins, anything can happen in the world described by it. As
Lévi-Strauss has rightly said: “With myth, everything becomes
possible.”21 In fact, many of the concepts of quantum physics, such as
wave-particle, wave function, non-locality, anti-particle and quantum
vacuum, appear as characters in a mythical story. The idea of wave-
particle is similar to that of a round-square which in our common
understanding is an impossibility. Or just imagine the specter of an
electron in doubleslit experiment visiting the Andromeda galaxy before it
lands on the image screen!

     We observe similar break down of the logic of our ordinary language
in Buddhist koans. Zen Buddhists have developed a technique called the
‘koan' which, along with meditation, produces changes in our perceptions
and understanding. Koans employ illogical language in order to achieve a
mode of consciousness different from that of our ordinary mental state.
They are riddles or puzzles that Zen Buddhists use in order to unravel deep
truths about the world and about themselves. “Koans are carefully devised
nonsensical riddles which are meant to make the student of Zen realize the
limitations of logic and reasoning in the most dramatic way. The irrational
wording and paradoxical content of these riddles makes [sic] it impossible
to solve them by thinking.”22 They are designed precisely to stop thought
process and thus to prepare Zen practitioners for non-verbal experience of
reality. The main Taoist scripture, Lao Tzu's Tao Te Ching, is written in an
extremely puzzling, seemingly illogical style. It is full of intriguing
contradictions, and its compact and powerful language is meant to arrest
the Zen practioner's mind and throw it off its familiar tracks of logical
reasoning. In almost all the koans, the striking characteristic is illogical
words or absurd acts. All these koans have unique solutions which a
competent Zen master recognizes immediately. Once the solution is found,
the koan ceases to be paradoxical and becomes a profoundly meaningful
statement made from the state of consciousness which it has helped to
awaken. Zen students are told to think unceasingly about a particular koan
until they come to know its answer. The following are some typical koans.

     To a beginner, the Zen master will normally present one of the two
following koans: “What was your original face — the one you had before
your parents gave birth to you?” Another koan: “You can make the sound
of two hands clapping. Now what is the sound of one hand?”

     When the Zen master Daito saw the emperor Godaigo who was a
student of Zen, the master said: “We were parted many thousands of
kalpas ago, yet we have not been separated even for a moment. We are
facing each other all day long, yet we have never met.”23

     The following are some common examples of koans.

     “The non-activity works! O! work without deeds! What is great has to
be looked for in what is small. What is heavy weighs as long as it is light.”

     “Empty-handed I go, and behold, the spade is in my hand; I walk on
foot, yet on the back of an ox am I riding.

     When I pass over the bridge

     Lo, the water floweth not; it is the bridge that doth flow.”

     All these koans appear illogical and non-sensical puzzles from the
point of our ordinary logic; they break the rules of logic, and involve even
contradictions. But they cease to be illogical when they are solved through
years of meditation achieving another mode of consciousness; they have to
be understood in terms of a new awareness of reality.

     We can make similar remarks about quantum physics. The concepts
and principles of quantum mechanics break the rules of ordinary logic; it
employs a different kind of logic in which logical rules, like distributive
law and commutative law, do not hold. That is the reason why the concepts
of quantum physics appear strange and weird from the point of view of
common sense which follows these rules of logic. Further, quantum theory
employs imposing mathematical frameworks, like Heisenberg's matrix
mechanics and Schrödinger's wave mechanics which are very abstract and
remote from the mathematical structures of Newtonian physics and even
those of the theory of relativity. May we say that a proper understanding of
the strange, bizarre and exotic concepts and principles of quantum theory
requires a mode of comprehension similar to the mode of consciousness
achieved by Zen practitioners employing koans? Do not the mathematical
frameworks of quantum mechanics, such as matrix mechanics and wave
mechanics, constitute a modality of intellectual apprehension
fundamentally different from our ordinary mode of consciousness? It is no
wonder that from the point of view of our common sense (that is, language
and logic, employed in the ‘zone of middle dimensions'), the world of
quantum physics appears strange, bizarre and weird. Polkinghorne rightly
observes: “One of the most important lessons from the story of quantum
theory is the encouragement to be open to the totally unexpected, even to
the initially apparently unintelligible.”24

     We conclude the present volume The Strange World of Quantum
Physics with the personal testimony of the renowned quantum physicist
Heinz R. Pagels about his experience of the strange, exotic quantum world
as he pursued his studies of quantum mechanics: I had no sense of the
‘quantum weirdness' of the atomic world; it was the earlier theory of
special relativity, with its space contractions and time dilations, that
seemed bizarre to me. But as I continued my study my reaction reversed
— relativity seemed less bizarre and more in accord with common sense,
while the quantum theory seemed more and more ‘weird.' Pursuing the
mathematics of the quantum theory, I felt pushed beyond common sense
into unimagined areas. Later, I found out that my experience paralleled
that of the physicists who first discovered the new quantum theory. First
they discovered the mathematical equations of the quantum theory which
worked experimentally; then they pondered the equations and their
meaning for the real world, developing an interpretation which departed
radically from naive realism. As I realized what the abstract mathematics
of quantum theory was actually saying, the world became a very strange
place indeed.25

Truly, quantum physics presents before us a world that is strange, weird
and bizarre - beyond all our wildest imaginations.

51. The Planck time of 10 -43 sec is the time it takes light to ‘cross' the
    Planck length of 10 -33 cm which is some 10 -20 times the distance ‘across'
    a proton. The Planck length is one millionth of a billionth of a billionth
    of a billionth of a centimetre; or 100 billion billion times less than the
    size of an electron. To get a sense of this scale, if we were to magnify
    an atom to the size of the known universe, the Planck length would
    barely be the height of an average tree. Below this magnitude, quantum
    fluctuations in the fabric of space-time would become enormous.

    1985; London: Bantam Books, 1986), p. 186. Paul Davies and John
    Gribbin, The Matter Myth: Beyond Chaos and Complexity (Viking,
    1991; Penguin Books, 1992), p. 235.

40. Double-slit experiments primarily employ photons and electrons.
    Photons are generated by any means which produces light like
    flashlights. Electrons can be produced by an electron gun. Electron
    guns are devices that generate streams of electrons, and they are quite
    common. For example, our television sets have electron guns at their
    back, and television images are produced by guiding the electrons
    generated by this gun to appropriate locations on the screen.
41. Consider for example, waves produced in a pond when two stones are
    dropped in it at the same time. If two wave peaks overlap, the height
    of water wave at that point increases; it is the sum of the heights of the
    two individual peaks. But if a wave peak overlaps with a wave trough
    from the other, they cancel each other out.

      In the case of electromagnetic waves in the visible part of the
      spectrum, an increase in frequency corresponds to a change in colour
      from red to orange, yellow, green, blue, indigo and finally to violet.
      Frequencies higher than that of violet light are not visible and
      correspond to ultraviolet radiation, such as X rays; and frequencies
      lower than that of red are also not visible, and correspond to infrared
      radiation.
70.   Greene, The Elegant Universe, p. 103. Italics Greene's.
71.   Peter Kosso, Appearance and Reality (Oxford: Oxford University
      Press, 1998), p. 124.
72.   Kezwer, Meditation, Oneness and Physics, p. 127. Richard Dewitt,
      World Views (Oxford: Blackwell Publishing, 2004), p. 237.
      Some general observations about the nature of particles and waves are
      in place. There are two ways in which energy and information can be
      sent from one place to another, namely, by waves and particles. In the
      case of a particle-signal, physical bits of material stuff actually move
      from one place to other. But a wave-signal travels without any object
      moving from the source to the destination. A wave is a pattern of
      disturbance in the intervening medium. For example, two people, A
      and B, hold opposite ends of a tight string, and they move it up and
      down. In this way, they can send signals. Bits of string do not travel
      from A to B. In fact, none even moves in B's direction, but A
      communicates a disturbance created by him/her in the string in the
      shape of a wave. The moving shape is the wave, and the string is the
      medium. Waves can take on all sorts of shapes. They can be produced
      as a single, sharp pulse, a slow, broad pulse, a series of pulses at a
      certain frequency, and so on. [Kosso, Appearance and Reality, p.
      116.]
73.   Hooker, “The Nature of Quantum Mechanical Reality,” p. 73.
      Interference is the phenomenon in which two or more waves of the
      same frequency combine to reinforce or cancel each other. Diffraction
      is the bending of a wave around an obstacle. Interference and
      diffraction occur in all kinds of waves - ocean waves, sound waves
      and electromagnetic waves like light.

40. Incidentally, we note that in his general theory of relativity, Einstein
    likewise replaced the concept of gravitational force by that of space-
    time curvature. Thus, both quantum field theory and the theory of
      relativity have done away with the concept of force replacing it with
      subtler notions of particle interactions and space-time curvature
      respectively.
41.   ‘Virtual' means ‘real in essence, although not in actual fact.' For
      instance, some electronic gadgets give us the experience of virtual
      realities, such as driving a virtual car. It is a real car, but it is not actual
      because it does not exist. Thus, photons which effect exchange
      between electrons are called ‘virtual' to distinguish them from the
      ‘actual' particles that shine in light bulbs and create in us the sensation
      of light. Virtual particles are also called ‘messenger' particles or
      ‘force-carrying' particles, or simply ‘force-carriers.' The concept of
      virtual particle belong to quantum physics, not to classical mechanics.

102. The Newtonian space is flat, and its properties are described by

     Newtonian-Euclidean form of intellect.' This mode of understanding
     is adjusted to the ‘zone of the middle dimensions' which is situated
     between the megacosm of flying galaxies and the microcosm of

114. When Einstein heard about Edwin Hubble's discovery of the flight of
     galaxies and the consequent expansion of the universe, he eliminated
     the cosmological constant from his equations by setting its strength to
     zero. From the 1930s until the late 1990s, most physicists assumed
     the cosmological constant to be equal to zero. However, in 1998 it
     was discovered that the expansion of the universe is accelerating; this
     implies the possibility of a positive non-zero value for the
     cosmological constant.

118. Weinberg, Dreams of a Final Theory, p. 179. Ellipsis mine. Please
     note that we have already referred to this energy density of the
     universe: “In one cubic centimetre of empty space the amount of
     energy is much greater than the total amount of energy of all the
     matter in the known universe.” (See note no.107)
119. But the problem is that if the value of the cosmological constant were
     so enormous, (10 120 times bigger than its observed value), the
     universe, if at all it exists, would be totally different from the one in
     which we live; we would not find ourselves living in a universe just
     right for life. Many physicists have openly admitted this fact.
     Weinberg confesses: “With the total cosmological constant this large,
     space would be so radically curved that it would bear no resemblance
     to the familiar three-dimensional space of Euclidean geometry in
     which we live.” (Weinberg, Dreams of a Final Theory, p. 180).
     Similarly, Larry Abbott remarks: “If the vacuum energy density, or
     equivalently the cosmological constant, were as large as theories of
     elementary particles suggest, the universe in which we live would be
     dramatically different, with properties we would find both bizarre and
     unsettling.” [Abbott, “The Mystery of the Cosmological Constant,”
     Scientific American Vol. 3, No. 1 (1991): 72].

128. Pagels, The Cosmic Code, p. 223. The symmetry between matter and
     antimatter demands that for every particle there has to be an anti-
     particle with equal mass and opposite charge. The anti-particle of
     electron is positron, and it has positive electric charge, and many anti-
     matter versions, such as anti-protons, were discovered. The total
     number of anti-particles so far discovered is about eighty. There could
     be atoms of anti-hydrogen composed of an anti-proton and a
     positron. Whole anti-matter universes could exist; perhaps distant
     galaxies are made out of anti-matter.

5.   The name ‘boson' was coined by Paul Dirac to commemorate the
     contribution of Satyendra Nath Bose, an Indian physicist, in
     developing with Einstein, Bose-Einstein statistics which theorizes the
     characteristics of elementary particles. It was also Dirac who named
     the other class of elementary particles ‘fermions' in honour of the
     eminent Italian physicist Enrico Fermi.
6.   Half-integer is a number obtained by dividing an odd integer by two,
     like 1/2, 3/2, 5/2.

      solidity of matter, structure of atoms, rules of chemistry and stability
      of stars.

10.   The word ‘hadron' comes from the Greek adros, meaning ‘thick,'
      ‘robust,' ‘massive,' or ‘large.' It refers to the ability of the point-like
      quarks to bind together and form particles that are ‘thick' (hadron) in a
      certain sense. The word ‘quark' originally appeared in a single line of
      the novel Finnegans Wake by the Irish author James Joyce
      (18821941). The main character of the book is a publican, named
      Humphrey Chimpden Earwicker who dreams that he is serving beer to
      a drunken seagull. Instead of asking for ‘three quarts for Mister Mark,'
      the drunken bird says, ‘three quarks for Muster Mark.'

62.   A spinning top, or simply a top, is a toy with a thick body and a sharp
      point at the bottom; it is designed to be spun on its vertical axis,
      balancing on the tip due to the gyroscopic effect. Once set in motion, a
      top will usually wobble for a few seconds, spin upright for a while,
      then start to wobble again with increasing amplitude as it loses energy
      (angular momentum), and finally tip over and roll on its side. Tops
      exist in many variations and materials, chiefly wood, metal and plastic,
      often with a metal tip.

    Theology,” pp. 192-97.
    A word of caution about theories like chaotic/eternal inflation and also
    about concepts like quantum foam, mother/child universes, is in order.
    Davies warns that speculations about quantum space-time foam and
    chaotic inflation depend on the validity of quantum mechanics when
    applied to the whole universe. “Quite apart from the astonishing
    extrapolation involved in applying a theory of subatomic particles to
    the entire cosmos, there are deep questions concerning the meaning to
    be attached to certain mathematical objects in the theory.” (Davies, The
    Mind of God, p. 62) Similarly, Pagels cautions us that “all these models
    involve the stretching of current theories and concepts far beyond
    where they have been tested and hence are no more than imaginative
    guesses. . . . A whole community of very smart scientists may have
    talked themselves into a theory of the very early universe that in the
    future (with the wisdom of hindsight) will be seen as a fantasy based
    on incomplete information and imaginative extrapolation. Theory
    building, while it creates a framework for thought, is never a substitute
    for experiment and observation.” (Pagels, Perfect Symmetry, p. 366.
    Ellipsis mine). John Polkinghorne is very sceptical about speculations
    on quantum cosmology. He says: “Those who essay a quantum
    cosmology are necessarily skating on intellectual thin ice, however
    pretty the arabesques they perform.” [Polkinghorne, “The Mind of
    God? Symposium: Hawking's A Brief History of Time Reconsidered,”
    The Cambridge Review (March 1992): 1].

50. Novikov, Black Holes and the Universe, p. 160. The freezing of water
    into ice is an instance of phase transition. Liquid water is symmetrical,
    the same at every point and in every direction. However, when ice
    crystals form, they will have definite positions and will be lined up in
    some direction. This breaks the symmetry of water.
